Potentially revolutionary. Well-written and clearly argued, this book challenges some of our most basic assumptions about how we ought to proceed philosophically and is sure to shake up our tired self-confidence in our philosophical intuitions. A truly innovative book; I know of nothing like it.

Jeremy Wisnewski, Hartwick College, New York





Fischer develops the concept of philosophical therapy to new heights and depths. His analysis is insightful, comprehensive, and richly back-grounded in many episodes in the history of philosophy, from the genesis of the modern mind-world dichotomy to Wittgenstein’s later philosophy.

Dale Jacquette, University of Berne, Switzerland





The idea that some philosophical problems are due to ‘diseases of the intellect’ and need to be treated—like an illness—has been mooted before. But no one has developed this thought, nor given it systematic elaboration. Nor has anyone demonstrated by detailed historical analyses how fruitful this conception can be. Fischer’s book undertakes these tasks, and executes them with exemplary skill and conviction. His case studies of Locke, Berkeley, Ayer and Wittgenstein are enthralling, as are his methodical examinations of the nature of the mind, the attributes of material things, sense perception, and the phenomenon of sudden understanding.

P. M. S. Hacker, St. John’s College, Oxford





By debunking long-entrenched myths, this deeply absorbing book opens up a fresh approach: a therapeutic back-tracking through the intellectual genealogies that led us into philosophical problems we too easily take as given but may thus come to see through. An excellent, adventurous, provocative book.

Garry Hagberg, Bard College, New York





This lucid and fascinating book offers both penetrating diagnosis and effective cure of a crucial subset of philosophical problems.

Louis A. Sass, Rutgers University





This clearly written, insightful and well-argued book makes an important contribution to meta-philosophical discussion, both of the status of philosophical methods of argument and of the underlying sources of philosophical conviction. Philosophers as well as non-philosophers could learn much from this kind of detailed methodological study. A helpful and original book.





Paul Livingston, University of New Mexico

In 2006, I joined the School of Philosophy of the University of East Anglia, which first granted me leave and then provided an exceptionally congenial environment for the completion of an unconventional and ambitious project.

Norwich, May 2010





Introduction


Some Perplexing Discoveries




In this book, we will develop an account of philosophical thought that vindicates and facilitates a fresh approach to a wide range of philosophical problems: Much philosophical reflection is shaped by habits of thought of which we are largely unaware. At their root are ‘philosophical pictures’: simple models which have guided the development of our language, to which we unwittingly but systematically assimilate things more closely than we know to be appropriate, when lost in abstract reflection. When under the spell of such pictures, we are prone to systematically form ‘philosophical delusions’. The conflict of such delusions with reality, or among each other, gives rise to a wide range of philosophical problems.—If correct, these ideas vindicate and facilitate a revolutionary reorientation of philosophical work: the adoption of aims and the development of methods which turn much philosophy into a kind of ‘therapy’, guided by meta-philosophical insights into the nature and genesis of the problems that worry us.

Some of the pertinent aims were first explicitly adopted, and some of the relevant methods first mooted, by some thinkers belonging to the ‘Austro-British strand’ of 20th-century analytic philosophy. From the early 1930s onwards Ludwig Wittgenstein adopted the chief goal of liberating himself and others from a certain kind of intellectual unease or disquiet: ‘As I do philosophy, its entire [!] task is to shape expressions in such a way that certain disquietudes disappear’.1 ‘Thoughts at peace. That is the goal someone who philosophises longs for.’ Overcoming disquieting problems ‘is not a matter of pronouncing new truths about the subject of the investigation.’ According to Wittgenstein, it crucially involves ‘assembling reminders’ of familiar facts about this subject, which we knew all along.2 But to return to, or arrive at, the notions of common sense, a philosopher ‘has to cure many diseases of the understanding in himself’, and ‘the philosopher’s treatment of a question is like the treatment of an illness.’ Throughout the 1930s and 1940s, these and related ideas were explored by a number of philosophers influenced by Wittgenstein, most seriously by Friedrich Waismann, John Wisdom and B.A. Farrell.3 Simultaneously but independently, J.L. Austin’s classic lectures Sense and Sensibilia explicitly addressed the main aim of ‘dissolving philosophical worries’ by ‘exposing a wide variety of concealed motives’ for maintaining a ‘disturbing’ philosophical doctrine—instead of examining the arguments philosophers explicitly adduce for it.4

These ideas were ahead of their time and still strike many thoughtful philosophers as too puzzling to even cause offence. To make sense of them, one has to identify pertinent ‘diseases of the understanding’ and to understand, at least in rough outline, the aetiology of the ‘symptoms’ they cause in philosophy. Without such an understanding, ‘therapeutic’ methods cannot be properly developed, the very need for therapy in philosophy—the need, in philosophy, for something it is more helpful than misleading to call a ‘therapy’—remains unclear, and the sparks of a potential revolution lack the fuel to develop into flames.

The key to the identification of relevant intellectual ‘diseases’ is provided by findings about how philosophical reflection is shaped and pre-structured by—unsound—cognitive processes which are not under our direct control and of which we are typically unaware. Following up inspired hunches, the pioneers of therapeutic philosophy sought to expose ‘concealed motives’,5 including ‘false analogies’ and ‘simile[s] at work in the unconscious.’ 6 But the only model of ‘unconscious’ cognition they had at their disposal to develop these hunches, provided by Freudian psycho-analysis, did not readily lend itself to the analysis of philosophical thought, and scientific inquiry into the most pertinent kind of ‘automatic’ reasoning, viz. non-intentional analogical and metaphorical reasoning, did not commence until half a century later. By seizing on results of this scientific work, the present book develops a new conception of the nature of philosophical reflection and the genesis of philosophical problems—which certainly does not apply to all of philosophy but arguably to an important part. This conception will provide a fresh foundation for a revolutionary enterprise that previously hung largely in the air.

With the help of findings and concepts from cognitive linguistics and from cognitive and social psychology, we will analyse how philosophical thought is shaped by metaphors and analogies we are not aware of employing and are prone to misapply. We will analyse how such ‘philosophical pictures’ shape philosophical reflection, give rise to philosophical problems and motivate philosophical theories. We will examine the nature and genesis of classical and of idiosyncratic problems from the four core areas of theoretical philosophy (epistemology and metaphysics, and the philosophies of mind and language): problems about the nature of the mind and about the attributes of bodies, about sense-perception and about the phenomenon of sudden understanding. We will proceed from case studies on major thinkers from two key periods of philosophy who worked within different paradigms and traditions: We will study the early modern empiricists Locke and Berkeley, as well as Berkeley’s 20th-century heir, A.J. Ayer (the immediate target of Austin’s therapeutic efforts), and Wittgenstein (whom we will see to have been his own ‘patient’, in self-therapy). We will thus obtain a new conception of philosophical reflection and philosophical problems, and establish that it applies to a relevant cross-section of the subject. In conjunction with some concepts borrowed from clinical psychology, this new conception will allow us to re-analyse extant models of therapeutic philosophy, due to Austin and Wittgenstein, to explain where and why therapy is called for in philosophy, and to develop techniques to actually carry it out—with purely rational means which fully respect the intellectual prowess and integrity of the thinkers addressed.





1. THE DISCOVERY OF PHILOSOPHICAL DELUSIONS




The work whose results are presented in this volume evolved from a series of largely chance discoveries. The reader might find it convenient to have them briefly set out at the start. An anecdote has it that a radio-astronomer knocked his instruments until they stopped emitting noises he picked up by chance, found unintelligible and therefore immediately put down to a malfunction. The pulsars were then discovered by others, who picked up the same signals and tried to make sense of them. (To be fair, some of the requisite concepts had been developed only in the meantime.) The discoveries to be set out were made by a combination of luck and ‘not knocking the instruments’: by not dismissing unexpected and initially unintelligible observations out of hand as impossible or irrelevant but trying to make sense of them, where necessary by importing or adapting concepts from other disciplines.

The first discovery was the most unsettling. It was made the first Monday of December 2000, in the late evening. The previous summer, I had given a class on J.L. Austin’s Sense and Sensibilia, and was still trying to make sense of his puzzling approach of exposing ‘concealed motives’ for adopting a worrying doctrine, apparently without addressing philosophers’ stated reasons. Suspecting that this approach might be rendered intelligible by features of the particular work Austin was responding to, I turned to A.J. Ayer’s Foundations of Empirical Knowledge and analysed its sophisticated development and defence of the sense-datum doctrine of perception, the doctrine that whenever we see or otherwise perceive anything, all we perceive directly are ‘sense-data’ in our minds. That Monday, I had pulled together various strands of the analysis and found that the way in which Ayer maintained this doctrine had four peculiar features. A bit earlier, Wittgenstein’s comparison of certain philosophical errors to motives elicited in psycho-analysis (a comparison drawn in his so-called ‘Big Typescript’), had prompted me to take up Freud’s Introductory Lectures as bedtime reading. I had then continued with it for pleasure after losing hope that it might help my philosophical endeavours. That evening, I arrived at lecture XVI, where Freud presents the case history of a patient with delusions and gives four crucial features of such beliefs—exactly the same four features I had, some hours before, found Ayer’s belief in the sense-datum doctrine to possess! The cases were analogous precisely in the respects that mattered. Ayer’s belief was a delusion—at least by the lights of the definition used by Freud. A look at recent psychiatry textbooks quickly revealed that this definition was not idiosyncratic but perfectly in line with standard explanations still given today. So a perfectly competent philosopher maintained an influential philosophical doctrine as a matter of delusion, in a familiar literal sense of this term. This totally unexpected, unsettling and electrifying finding (to be presented in Chapter 6) had me engage in further reading (of literature in clinical psychology) and analysis (of Ayer’s texts), which increasingly confirmed the conclusion (though, needless to say, a lot more was involved than had first met my eye).

This discovery prompted me to study models of delusion formation and maintenance, and therapies for conditions involving delusions. I quickly found the conceptual framework of cognitive therapy most helpful, since it renders delusions intelligible as the result of, among other things, cognitive processes amenable to the philosophical tools of logical analysis.7 Aaron Beck’s seminal work on cognitive therapy, namely of depression,8 shows that intellectually perfectly competent subjects may spontaneously and continually make unsound inferences of certain characteristic types, which presuppose utterly unwarranted assumptions that are always variations of the same themes. Such spontaneous or ‘automatic’ inferences, which the subject cannot help making, take him via presuppositions he is unaware of relying upon to conclusions he finds intuitively compelling regardless of what warrant he has for them. When analysing a core part of Wittgenstein’s Philosophical Investigations, I found that Wittgenstein elicits inferences and conclusions of precisely this kind in his own reflections: things he finds it intuitively compelling to say, even though he has no warrant for them.9 He is inclined to spontaneously leap to these conclusions from premises which entail them in conjunction with always the same tacit assumptions— which he reflectively rejects (see Chapter 9). This phenomenon I found to recur in other texts, by Ayer and Berkeley—who tend to endorse the conclusions they leap to and find intuitively compelling (in contrast with the later Wittgenstein who is wary of such ‘intuitions’). I thus encountered the phenomenon of ‘autonomous habits of thought’ in philosophical reflection: In such reflection, thinkers unwittingly but systematically make spontaneous leaps of thought which tacitly presuppose always the same unwarranted assumptions the thinkers are not aware of relying upon, crucially including assumptions the thinkers explicitly reject(!), and which lead to conclusions the thinkers find intuitively compelling for all that. Such leaps may lead thinkers to form delusions and maintain them on the basis of the most specious ‘arguments’.

Like all other firmly maintained beliefs, delusions are reinforced by belief-bias effects well documented by experiments in cognitive and social psychology: Evidence consistent with the belief is noticed and remembered more easily and regarded as more important than evidence that speaks against it; the belief is presupposed in assessing potential evidence and in further reasoning. Both Ayer’s new and Berkeley’s classical arguments for the sense-datum doctrine provide ample evidence for these effects. In particular, I found the doctrine is presupposed, in part or full, in the very arguments adduced in its support—an extreme case of belief bias (Chapters 5–6).

Each of the thinkers I found to have autonomous habits of thought was thus led to find compelling claims apparently at odds with common-sense convictions. Such conflicts lead to the formulation of a characteristic kind of philosophical problems: a reconciliation problem which challenges us to reconcile what appears to be at odds. Philosophers often wonder ‘How is it (possible) that p?’ These questions do not fall out of the clear blue sky; philosophers ask them when they feel that there is some sort of problem or difficulty about the familiar fact that p. This is often due to an apparent conflict between p and an intuitively compelling philosophical claim which may be maintained as a matter of delusion. Such conflicts give rise to the sort of puzzlement in the face of familiar facts that Plato regarded as the starting-point of philosophical reflection.10 This constitutes the specifically philosophical motivation for pursuing many philosophical questions. Ayer is a case in point, as he devotes almost all of his Foundations to explaining how it is possible that we successfully communicate—as we clearly do every day—with expressions that ostensibly refer to publicly accessible material things located in physical space. The ‘problem’ is raised only by his delusional doctrine that all we ever directly perceive and can directly refer to are private and immaterial sense-data located in our minds. Problems whose specifically philosophical motivation is unsound in this way are philosophical ‘pseudo-problems’ which it is usually philosophically pointless to address.

To sum up, autonomous habits of thought have us see difficulties where there are none: In the grip of such habits, philosophers may come to espouse paradoxical claims in the absence of warrant, maintain them due to belief-bias effects and even as a matter of delusion, be puzzled by facts apparently at odds with these delusions, and labour to reconcile the former with the latter. This otherwise unsettling conclusion holds out the promise of a new approach that lets us overcome long-standing and maddening philosophical problems: By reconstructing and examining the lines of thought that motivate such a problem, we may identify moves which tacitly rely on assumptions its proponents explicitly reject, which are unsound by their own lights (rather than by the lights of that unsympathetic know-all, the philosophical critic). Where this is the case, we can overcome the problem by exposing it as a pseudo-problem conjured up in the grip of autonomous habits of thought.

So far we are dealing with a promise extended by a puzzle. Autonomous cognitive habits had previously been observed in conditions like depression or psychosis. I encountered such habits in philosophical reflection. But, surely, the autonomous habits of thought that shape philosophical reflection are not associated with emotional or psychotic disorders (or anything else with an entry in the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders, for that matter). So when and why do such habits arise in the radically different context of philosophical reflection?





2. THE DISCOVERY OF PHILOSOPHICAL PICTURES




The first intellectual stimulus was once more provided by Wittgenstein’s Big Typescript, which moots the idea that philosophical perplexities may be due to ‘false analogies’ which have been ‘accepted into language’ and then guide philosophers’ reflections in ways of which they are not aware. I did not find ‘false analogies’ in language philosophically relevant in my further enquiries, but the task of contributing to a volume on this text 11 shifted my attention to non-intentional analogical reasoning in which certain actual analogies get systematically misapplied, and this shift proved crucial. I started to analyse the unwitting use of analogy in key texts by Berkeley. Already George Pitcher (an eminent scholar who, in the 1960s and 1970s, worked on Wittgenstein, Berkeley and the philosophy of perception) had noticed various passages in which the mind is excessively assimilated to a place, and had attributed a ‘place-picture of the mind’ to Berkeley.12 Analysing Berkeley’s Three Dialogues, I found this assimilation the tip of an iceberg. Berkeley’s arguments involve a plethora of tacit assumptions. I found there is system to these assumptions: Some entail others. And the particularly fundamental presuppositions, which entail many others, posit analogies. They posit unduly strong analogies between abstract phenomena and simple tangible models: Against explicit better knowledge, Berkeley tacitly presupposes undue analogies between ‘inner’ and physical perception, and between qualities and parts of things; he presupposes that things perceived ‘in the mind’, like things perceived with our eyes, must be around to be perceived, and that qualities, like parts, are located in the things to which they belong (cp. Chapters 4–5).

At this point (September 2005), I started work at an Institute for Advanced Study and attended—purely because it was one of my few duties as a Fellow—the talk of a linguistics colleague. The speaker (Rudi Botha) mentioned in passing ‘conceptual metaphors’ which involve simple tangible models of just this sort. Scurrying to the library, I quickly learned that such models are involved in metaphorical extension, a major process of language development: In language after language, whole sets of related terms (e.g. manipulation terms) are initially applied only to concrete things, actions or achievements (as in “He grasped a stone to throw”) and then get extended to more abstract ‘targets’ (as in “He grasped the implications immediately”), in a systematic manner. In language after language, this process forges a plethora of structural analogies between the concrete and the abstract.

With these and some other bits of cognitive linguistics in mind, I set about analysing Galileo’s famous discussion of ‘secondary qualities’ as the likely beginning of at least one garden path leading to the sense-datum doctrine that was Berkeley’s starting-point. At crucial points, I found Galileo (and later Boyle and Locke) presupposing material analogies between models involved in metaphorical extension and their merely structurally analogous targets, and doing so against better knowledge, a knowledge the thinkers occasionally express explicitly (Chapter 3). Most of the further assumptions tacitly presupposed in the pertinent lines of thought could be derived from those basic presuppositions. A characteristic, recurrent, and systematic mistake in non-intentional analogical reasoning thus emerged as the source of philosophically relevant autonomous habits of thought: the overly literal application of conceptual metaphors, against better knowledge. Looking for an already familiar and suggestive label, I characterised these fundamental mistakes by attaching new content to Wittgensteinian talk of being ‘under the spell of philosophical pictures’ (Chapter 1).

Reading up on non-intentional analogical reasoning, I came across experiments which establish that when we lack knowledge of relevant context or detail, we are prone to unwittingly fill them in by falling back on familiar models: 13 We then unwittingly resort to familiar things which are structurally similar to the things at issue and spontaneously project properties of the former onto the latter; we do so without being aware of using the former as a model or engaging in any analogical reasoning; and we do so in the absence of warrant for crediting the targets with the properties at issue (Chapter 1.2).

In philosophical reflection we frequently lack knowledge of relevant context and detail. Think of how often philosophers consider phenomena ‘in the abstract’ or out of context, generalise too swiftly or extensively, pay little or no attention to concrete examples, or employ technical terms whose application to concrete situations remains unclear. It would therefore be no surprise if thinkers engaged in such reflection proved prone to unwittingly fall back on suitable models that come their way, namely on anything structurally similar to the object of their reflection. For a wide range of topics that have figured prominently in philosophical reflection, the process of metaphorical extension provides structurally analogous models and suggests them to us through the very terms we use. My previous logical reconstructions of philosophical lines of thought had revealed that some eminent philosophers continually make unsound inferences in non-intentional analogical reasoning. This finding now turned out to be perfectly consistent with experimental findings. Shortcomings that are characteristic of much—though certainly not all—philosophical reflection make thinkers prone unwittingly to misapply models offered them by their language, in non-intentional reasoning. From this source autonomous habits of thought may arise in philosophical reflection, entirely independently from the conditions in which such habits were first encountered (depression, psychosis, etc.).

When we presuppose material analogies where only structural analogies obtain, when we presuppose, for example, that to understand or ‘see’ someone’s point is to perceive some thing somewhere, with an organ of sense, we swiftly arrive at conclusions at odds with common sense or facts we acknowledge. In the grip of philosophical pictures and the autonomous habits of thought they fuel, we find such conclusions intuitively compelling for all that. In Boyle, Locke and Berkeley I found two kinds of response to such worrying conflicts between philosophical ‘intuitions’ and accepted facts: the deliberate construction of philosophical theories, and spontaneous glossing over. In the latter case, thinkers assert that the intuitively compelling claim is to be understood somehow differently, in a sense in which it does not conflict with the facts. Thus Berkeley, for example, explains that ‘when I speak of objects existing in the mind . . . I would not be understood in the gross literal sense, as when bodies are said to exist in a place.’ 14

Where salient conflicts cannot be papered over in this way, they occasion the explicit formulation of philosophical problems, such as various familiar problems about the mind and perception (Chapters 2 and 5). These philosophical problems, in turn, occasion the construction of philosophical theories which are required to ‘honour’ both our philosophical intuitions and the facts we accept and are intended to solve the problems raised by their apparent clash, usually by accounting for the facts in ways that appear to bring them into line with the intuitively compelling philosophical claims (rather than the other way round). Upon analysis, Locke’s famous theories of secondary qualities and sense-perception turned out to be of this type (Chapter 3), as did Berkeley’s idealism (Chapter 5) and Ayer’s phenomenalism (Chapter 6). In each case, non-intentional inferences of the sort that had raised the worrying problems recurred in the construction of the theory addressing them.

The conditions favouring non-intentional analogical reasoning and its characteristic mistakes and consequences obtain not invariably but frequently in philosophical reflection. The case-studies presented establish that even the most competent thinkers (including outstanding academics like A.J. Ayer and men of genius like Berkeley and Wittgenstein) are prone to such reasoning, with all those mistakes and consequences, when those conditions obtain. The lasting influence of the problems thus engendered further testifies to the extent to which these phenomena shape philosophical reflection. Quite possibly, a significant part—not all but a significant part—of philosophy is a struggle with philosophical pictures and their consequences.

The findings set out thus lead us to some hypotheses about the genesis of many philosophical problems and the nature of philosophers’ work on them: Philosophical pictures fuel autonomous habits of thought; under their spell we concoct and espouse philosophical delusions (absurd conclusions of non-intentional reasoning which we cannot help relying upon in further reflection, whether we want to or not). The conflict of such delusions with reality, or with each other, has us imagine problems where there are none. In response, philosophers typically devise theories shaped by the same pictures and habits that had them raise the ‘problems’. The result is a set of arguments, doctrines, problems and theories, which are unsound or unwarranted by their authors’ own lights, since they rely, ultimately, on presuppositions the thinkers explicitly reject. Where philosophers struggle with philosophical pictures without understanding their predicament, they attack imaginary problems with theories fired by the same imagination. Absorbed in a quixotic quest brilliant men ride against windmills on asses mistaken for chargers. Understanding our predicament is the first, and decisive, step towards a more productive response to problems which, though imaginary, are among the most long-lived and puzzling of our subject.





3. THE VINDICATION OF THERAPEUTIC PHILOSOPHY




This book will provide support for these hypotheses and explore their implications for philosophical work and method. A caricature of our subject’s development may help to place this effort into perspective. It is not entirely wrong to see philosophy in its present form as the result of a process through which scientific disciplines emerge from within the community of thinkers called ‘philosophers’. To the extent to which a range of notions and concepts, methods and techniques for formulating and addressing problems of a particular kind come to be developed and generally accepted by a certain number of such thinkers, a community of specialists emerges, and with the new scientific community often a new discipline is born: from geometry as philosophy’s arguably first off-spring, to psychology and linguistics as its latest. We have no reason to believe that this process has now come to an end, nor that it has left the subject of philosophy at this stage with one homogeneous class of problems, all of one and the same kind. There is, in other words, no reason to believe that it makes much sense to make sweeping assertions about ‘the nature and genesis of philosophical problems’. Accordingly, the present book is part of an effort to develop new notions and methods to understand and treat one class of such problems, which come to be formulated in one particular way: through non-intentional analogical reasoning of the kind we cannot help engaging in when we are under the spell of philosophical pictures.

Where philosophical pictures engender philosophical problems in the way outlined, at least the most basic ideas of a new approach to the problems are dictated by plain common sense: Instead of struggling to reconcile facts with delusions, we should try to liberate ourselves from these delusions and the problems they conjure up. Instead of rushing to ‘honour’ the claims to which we leap under the spell of philosophical pictures and then find intuitively compelling in the absence of warrant, we ought to establish carefully what warrant we can obtain for maintaining them. Where we cannot obtain any, we ought to give up the claim and abandon the ‘problems’ it created. The proper response to such an ill-motivated ‘problem’ resembles the atheist’s response to the problem of theodicy: ‘Since there is no reason to believe that there is a good, omniscient and omnipotent God (or any kind of Deity, for that matter), there is no reason to agonise over the question: Why is there so much suffering on Earth even though it is ultimately governed by a good, omniscient and omnipotent Deity?’ In other words, the proper response to an ill-motivated philosophical ‘problem’ is to show that it is ill-motivated: that it is raised only by a certain philosophical assumption and that this assumption is unwarranted (or worse). Such a demonstration resolves the problem precisely by showing that we ought not to answer the question that articulates it.

Many good questions about the reasons for human suffering remain for the atheist who has rejected the problem of theodicy: Why is there so much suffering in the world? Which psychological, economic, political and natural processes contribute to it, and in what ways? Similarly, many real questions may remain for us once we have rejected the claim that raises a particular philosophical problem: Once we have seen that we lack all warrant for the sense-datum doctrine of perception, we will no longer wonder ‘How is it possible for us to perceive physical objects, even though we can immediately perceive only immaterial sense-data?’ But there will still be plenty of good questions for us to pursue about the possibility of sense-perception: What conditions facilitate, and what conditions inhibit sense-perception? Which bits and structures of the brain facilitate perception of physical objects in the environment? How do human beings learn to recognise physical objects? Etc. We will, however, have got rid of one particularly maddening and peculiarly philosophical question, and be left in its stead mainly with questions which have come to be addressed—also or only—by other disciplines than philosophy (which arguably also deals with other kinds of problems which do not disappear in this way).

Establishing that a problem is ill-motivated is one thing; getting others or oneself to actually give up its pursuit may be another. Atheists’ critical arguments have done little to shake religious beliefs of the kind that raise the problem of theodicy. In a similar manner, if for different reasons, the claims we espouse under the spell of philosophical pictures will continue to strike us as intuitively compelling even once philosophical analysis has established that we have no warrant for them: After all, those claims are rendered compelling by non-intentional inferences and autonomous cognitive processes which are not under our direct control. Indeed, where non-intentional analogical reasoning is accompanied by belief-bias effects which have us interpret everything in the light of what we find intuitively compelling, and presuppose it in further reasoning, these claims may be maintained as a matter of delusion, and our belief in them will be unresponsive to direct challenges of the unsound evidence or arguments on which they are based. And as long as we maintain these claims, or even just find them intuitively compelling, we cannot give up pursuit of the problems raised by them without the disquieting impression of puzzling problems evaded rather than resolved. Thus it seems that some kind of ‘therapy’ needs to complement philosophical criticism: To enable us to actually abandon what we have seen reason to reject, without misplaced feelings of regret and worry, we need a kind of ‘therapy’ which enables us to shed our philosophical delusions and to distance ourselves from ideas that strike us as intuitively compelling even once we have seen them to be unwarranted.

Philosophers frequently think of therapy ‘in the usual sense’ as necessarily opposed to argument and assume that the use of non-argumentative methods must set therapeutic intervention apart from rational discourse.15 But this is not so. Even exemplary psychotherapies like cognitive therapy16 and rational emotive therapy17 for depression crucially involve argument and the assessment of evidence (to establish, for instance, whether the depressed patient really is as utterly inept as he thinks). Therapy may proceed by argument; therapeutic dialogue may be a form of rational discourse. What sets cognitive and rational therapy apart from other forms of rational discourse are not the (merely supposedly non-argumentative) means employed, but the ends pursued and the problems addressed—which are, anyway, the only things that the very different forms of psychotherapy have in common: Such therapy seeks, first and foremost, to change certain feelings or behaviours and addresses ‘emotional’ and ‘behavioural problems’ associated with illness or disease. Such therapy seeks, e.g., to remove unwarranted and disabling, distressing or disquieting emotions associated with depression.

In his Big Typescript, Wittgenstein repeatedly discusses the ‘disquiet’, ‘uneasiness’ and ‘unrest’ that intellectual problems can cause a thinker and evidently caused him.18 He explicitly adopts the removal of such feelings of intellectual disquiet as his paramount goal.19 At first sight, this is deeply puzzling (to put it politely): Of course philosophical like other intellectual problems may give us sleepless nights when we work on them intensely.20 But surely a philosopher’s most important task is to solve that problem— rather than to liberate himself from whatever feelings he may personally have about it. And even if we wished to attend to such feelings, their removal need not be explicitly advertised as a distinct goal: Even when an intellectual problem gives us sleepless nights, we automatically stop worrying about it, the moment we know its solution or resolution—and serious thinkers will only stop worrying about it the moment they have acquired such knowledge.

All this changes when, and only when, the worrying problems at issue are engendered by autonomous cognitive processes which are unsound. We have already seen that when the intuitively compelling conclusions of non-intentional inferences raise an ill-motivated problem, we will only stop worrying about the problem once we have not merely shown that the problem is ill-motivated but done something more to distance ourselves from the intuitively compelling claims that raise it. But there is also a more fundamental upshot. When a child is afraid that monsters might come in at night, we face a merely imaginary security problem; the only genuine problem we face and need to solve is the ‘emotional problem’ that consists in the child’s unwarranted feelings of fear. When unsound non-intentional inferences sweep us along, settle us with paradoxical delusions, and thus make us imagine and worry about problems where there are none (‘philosophical monsters’, as it were), the only genuine—rather than imaginary— problems we face and need to solve are the ‘emotional problem’ constituted by our unwarranted feelings of disquiet and the ‘behavioural problem’ that is constituted by the fact that we are wasting our time on ill-motivated questions we would rightly ignore if we were not under the spell of the pertinent philosophical pictures. When this happens, it is reasonable to explicitly adopt the aim of resolving emotional or behavioural problems as one’s paramount goal. Of course few philosophers are as acutely disquieted as Wittgenstein by the problems they are working on. For the solid majority, the ‘behavioural’ problems take precedence over the ‘emotional’ problems: We need to liberate ourselves, first and foremost, from the urge to engage in efforts we would rightly reject as pointless if we were not under the spell of a philosophical picture.

After discovering the existence of philosophical delusions, I went out of my way to stress that such delusions need not constitute symptoms of any disease. I did so, basically, for fear of arguing myself completely out of the job market and because the suggestion that the work—as opposed to the private life—of philosophers like A.J. Ayer might evidence any kind of non-somatic disease struck me as plainly incredible. It struck me thus prior to careful reflection which self-censorship had prevented. Years later, at a conference on philosophy as therapy (Liverpool, June 2008), I found myself surrounded by talk of philosophy as a cure for intellectual pathologies and, having obtained a permanent position in the meantime, without an excuse for not seriously exploring the notion. It quickly emerged that the autonomous cognitive processes I had reconstructed qualify as ‘pathological processes’, in a perfectly standard sense of the term, and that being under the spell of a philosophical picture meets a common job specification for ‘pathological conditions’. Being under the spell of a philosophical picture is constitutive of a ‘disease of the understanding’, a kind of intellectual altitude sickness that can befall any of us when we try to scale the heights of abstract reflection with undue haste and without due precautions (Chapter 6). When reasoning driven by philosophical pictures engenders unwarranted philosophical disquiet or pointless efforts to answer ill-motivated questions, we are hence dealing with problems of the kind addressed by non-somatic therapy: emotional and behavioural problems associated with some illness or disease. The discovery of philosophical pictures and their effects thus vindicates the project of therapy in philosophy which pursues the kind of aims and addresses the kind of problems that are characteristic of non-somatic therapy.

It also facilitates the development of new methods to do the job. To put an end to worries and pointless efforts engendered by ill-motivated problems we need to weaken the delusions that give rise to those problems. By definition, delusional beliefs are unresponsive to criticism of the evidence or arguments on which they are based. They were therefore regarded as unsuitable targets for cognitive or rational therapies until, in the late 1980s and early 1990s, cognitive behavioural therapists started to develop more indirect approaches. 21 The core idea was to give the patient insight into the largely autonomous cognitive and psychological processes that have him form and maintain delusional beliefs. Once a variety of behavioural exercises has weakened the psychological motivations that underlie the pertinent delusions, the subject then becomes increasingly able to question these beliefs, follow up the hypothesis that they may be due to the unsound processes discussed with him, and understand the beliefs in question as the results of autonomous cognitive and psychological processes which have overpowered him. The insight into the nature and ways of his pertinent thinking enables him to distance himself from its deliverances, in word, thought and deed.

I submit that philosophical, unlike paranoid or other psychotic delusions are typically formed and maintained due to purely cognitive processes and that further psychological motivation is not essential to them. Where such a motivation is absent, we can significantly weaken our delusions by coming to understand how we come to form and maintain them. This book’s account of how philosophical pictures shape philosophical reflection lets us understand just that: It lets us understand how competent thinkers can come to form and maintain philosophical delusions through non-intentional and automatic reasoning. This will help us to develop fresh methods for overcoming the problems thus raised, namely by coming to understand their nature and genesis (Chapter 7). We will find that to deal successfully with such problems is indeed ‘not a matter of pronouncing new truths about the subject of the investigation’ (as Wittgenstein put it). 22 But neither is it merely a matter of reminding oneself of familiar facts about the subject of the investigation (the mind, perception, understanding, etc.). Rather, it turns on acquiring a better understanding of the way we think about the subject of the philosophical investigation when engaged in such investigation. It turns on acquiring new or improved self-knowledge. The key to successful therapy in philosophy is improved self-understanding, namely such self-understanding as we may obtain through a novel epistemology of the subject, which takes into account autonomous cognitive processes and is informed by findings and concepts from various branches of cognitive science.





4. OBJECTIVES, STRATEGY AND STRUCTURE




According to standard explanations of the notion, a procedure qualifies as ‘therapeutic’ to the extent to which it is designed to cure illness or relieve the symptoms of disease.23 In the conditions addressed by exemplary psychotherapies, the symptoms at issue take the shape of emotional or behavioural problems.24 An activity is ‘therapeutic’ in the same sense as exemplary psychotherapies to the extent to which it is aimed at solving emotional or behavioural problems associated with processes or conditions we can describe as ‘pathological’, as ‘disease’ or ‘illness’.

In different ways, the ancient Stoics, Epicureans and Pyrrhonists made efforts to solve emotional problems which arise in everyday life and may be encountered by philosophers and non-philosophers alike; e.g., they sought to relieve disabling anxiety in the face of the uncertainties of life.25 Some of the philosophical counselling practiced today addresses similar emotional as well as behavioural problems, which are encountered prior to or independently of philosophical reflection. 26 I propose to say that philosophical efforts addressing such problems are constitutive of ‘philosophical therapy’. Wittgenstein and Austin, by contrast, pioneered efforts aimed at putting an end to intellectual disquiet and pointless efforts engendered by—and only by—philosophical reflection. Such efforts, to solve emotional and behavioural problems that arise in and through philosophical reflection, are constitutive of ‘therapeutic philosophy’. 27

This book is about therapeutic philosophy. It does not seek to give an overview over the various philosophical efforts that can aptly be described as ‘therapeutic’, in one sense or the other.28 Indeed, it is not even intended, primarily, as a contribution to the exegetical debates surrounding extant efforts in therapeutic philosophy, such as the work of the two pioneers mentioned. 29 Rather, this book deploys concepts and insights not yet available to earlier philosophers, with the main aim of providing fresh foundations for the enterprise, and of refining and developing ways of engaging in it: On the basis of a new account of philosophical reflection, inspired by Wittgenstein and informed by more recent cognitive science, this book will explain where and why therapeutic philosophy is called for, and develop methods for such therapy in philosophy—not the kind of set routines capable of mechanical execution that many philosophers intuitively associate with the term “method”, but structured yet flexible techniques of the kind used in cognitive therapy.

The term “therapy” implies that there is a disease or illness to be cured (namely, by the therapy). This implication cannot be cancelled simply by saying that the term is being used in a metaphorical sense: As we shall see (in Chapter 1), metaphorical extensions of established terms escape vacuity by preserving their core inferential relations to other terms. So, even if we use the term “therapy” metaphorically, it has to imply that there is some ‘disease’ or ‘illness’, in either a literal or a metaphorical sense, which is to be ‘cured’ (literally or metaphorically). To give a substantive rather than vacuous explanation of why therapeutic philosophy is called for, we must therefore (i) identify—literal or metaphorical—‘diseases’ or ‘illnesses’ from which philosophers suffer and (ii) explain why these diseases or illnesses have to be cured, in order to resolve or overcome familiar, and preferably important, philosophical problems.

In contrast to Austin’s pertinent efforts, Wittgenstein’s work has frequently been characterised as in some sense ‘therapeutic’. Indeed, a growing body of interpretations describe this work as ‘therapeutic’ in a sense that claims more than that it is ‘non-theoretical’ or ‘confessional’. While leading to ever more intriguing suggestions and provocative fresh ideas, this body of work has, however, not yet yielded a substantive explanation of what therapeutic philosophy is and why it is called for. Most of these interpretations develop in some way the theme that therapy is necessary ‘to help us work ourselves out of confusions we become entangled in when philosophising’.30 But they do not proceed to identify any ‘disease’ or ‘illness’: Either they do not identify processes that systematically engender, or conditions that systematically involve such confusions; or they fail to explain why these processes or conditions qualify as ‘pathological’ and as some sort of ‘disease’ or ‘illness’. A less popular but more straightforwardly therapeutic tradition squarely takes Wittgenstein to address emotional problems, namely individual philosophers’ feelings of worry and puzzlement rather than the problems and puzzles they worry about; as Gordon Baker puts it, Wittgenstein addresses ‘individuals’ troubled states of mind which have as their intentional objects particular conceptual confusions, tensions, paradoxes, or puzzles.’31 But so far no processes systematically generating such emotional problems have been identified and shown to actually occur and be pathological.

By examining how philosophical pictures and autonomous cognitive processes shape philosophical reflection, we will fill this gap: We will identify processes of non-intentional and automatic reasoning which are particularly likely to occur under conditions frequently met in philosophical reflection, which systematically have us form and maintain philosophical delusions, and which thereby raise familiar and disquieting philosophical ‘problems’ that in turn occasion pointless theorising. We will find that these processes are constitutive of diseases of the understanding—and shape not all of philosophy but much philosophical reflection. On the basis of these findings, we will explain the need for therapeutic philosophy and develop methods to practice it.

The new account of philosophical reflection which will emerge employs subject-relative notions like warrant (you may have warrant for a claim for which I have none), delusion (a paranoid conspiracy-theorist may, by accident, hold as a delusion what someone privy to well-kept secrets suspects with good reason) or non-intentional reasoning (where one thinker may deliberately reason to a conclusion another non-intentionally infers). There is therefore only one way of establishing our account’s accuracy and scope with the requisite rigour: We need to conduct case-studies on individual thinkers, preferably influential thinkers whose work has shaped significant parts of modern philosophy.

The influence of philosophical pictures is easiest to spot and acknowledge in unfamiliar settings and reflections that are either shaped by pictures that no longer hold us captive today or are couched in concepts we no longer use. We shall therefore proceed from case-studies on two early modern thinkers belonging to the lastingly influential empiricist tradition, namely Locke (Chapter 3) and Berkeley (Chapters 4–5), before turning to two protagonists of 20th-century analytic philosophy. The first is the logical empiricist A.J. Ayer, who re-developed Berkeleyan ideas with more sophisticated tools (Chapter 6) and became the target of Austin’s therapeutic efforts (Chapter 8). The other is Ludwig Wittgenstein, who, in the course of efforts to ‘cure in himself’ diseases of the understanding, examined his own non-intentional reasoning in some detail and thus left us with exceptionally extensive and detailed evidence for such reasoning (Chapter 9). All these philosophers struggled with a propensity to unwittingly misapply the two major conceptual metaphors of the mental, namely perception- and manipulation-metaphors for intellectual activities and achievements. We will develop our meta-philosophical argument through case-studies on these four thinkers, which follow up how the misapplication of these metaphors led to problems in epistemology and metaphysics and the philosophies of mind and language, namely problems about the mind and the attributes of bodies, about sense-perception and about linguistic understanding.

The book develops this argument in three steps, in three parts. The first part analyses how philosophical pictures shape philosophical reflection. It identifies and examines an autonomous cognitive process which is unsound and particularly likely to occur in philosophical reflection: the overly literal application of conceptual metaphors, against better knowledge, in non-intentional analogical reasoning, which then permeates further reflection through belief-bias effects. The second part analyses how this process—nicknamed the ‘pictorial process’—generates philosophical delusions, whose conflict with further convictions, or each other, gives rise to illusory but maddening problems. This establishes the need for therapy in philosophy. The third part explores extant and fresh methods to meet this need and actually practice therapeutic philosophy.

Each part consists of three chapters. Chapter 1 develops the concept of ‘philosophical pictures’ with the help of concepts and findings from cognitive linguistics and psychology, and uncovers the ‘pictorial process’ through a case study on two intuitions at the heart of the modern conception of the mind that, in the 17th century, came to displace earlier Aristotelian notions. Chapter 2 shows that the pictorial process can account for a whole cluster of intuitions that shape the classical conception of the mind as a private and transparent realm of inner perception, and gave rise to classical mind-body problems. These problems can be dissolved—though not completely overcome—by coming to understand their nature and genesis. Once we have thus been provided with an incentive to analyse in detail how philosophical pictures shape philosophical reflection, Chapter 3 analyses Locke’s closely related accounts of secondary qualities and sense-perception, and systematically takes stock of the effects of picture-driven reasoning, which we shall find to shape two conceptual frameworks of early modern philosophy, some puzzling doctrines about primary and secondary qualities, and various arguments, problems and theories involving those doctrines. Philosophical pictures can shape philosophical reflection at several levels and from beginning (concept-formation) to end (the theories that are to solve the picture-raised problems that set the agenda).

The second part shifts the focus from philosophical intuitions and intuitive reasoning to carefully crafted philosophical argument and examines how the processes and effects analysed lead competent thinkers to form and maintain philosophical delusions. To do so, it follows up how those Lockean accounts evolved into the lastingly influential sense-datum doctrine of perception, and considers the first systematic defence of the doctrine, due to Berkeley, as well as its most sophisticated development, due to Ayer. Chapter 4 shows that Berkeley was under the spell of the same philosophical pictures as Locke and that these pictures shaped the very methods and strategies of argument he employed. Chapter 5 shows that all his arguments for the sense-datum doctrine, including those that became part of sense-datum theorists’ standard repertoire, presuppose what is at issue, that the unsupported and paradoxical doctrine is absurd, in a strict sense of the term, and that the pictorial process and its accompaniments systematically lead competent thinkers to espouse and maintain such absurdities. Proceeding from an analysis of Ayer’s redevelopment of the sense-datum doctrine, Chapter 6 shows that these absurdities qualify as delusions and that the pictorial process that systematically generates them qualifies as ‘disease’ and constitutes a kind of intellectual altitude sickness that is likely to befall us when we lose ourselves in abstract thought. When we are under the spell of philosophical pictures, we are liable to surround ourselves with delusions which have us imagine problems where there are none.

These findings vindicate and facilitate the adoption of therapeutic aims and the development of therapeutic methods, for much philosophical work. Chapter 7 points out reasons to believe that the struggle with philosophical pictures and their offspring is central, and still central, to philosophical work, explains when and why philosophers engaged in this struggle ought to adopt therapeutic aims, and shows that, when applied on a larger scale, the approach to picture-raised problems we have developed (starting in Chapter 2) is actually up to the challenge of liberating us from philosophical delusions by letting us understand our predicament: This approach lets us understand how picture-driven reasoning leads us not just to endorse the particular belief at issue but shapes all our philosophical thinking about the subject, directly and indirectly (through belief-bias effects). The next two chapters then, first, show that Austin and Wittgenstein adopted the therapeutic aims thus vindicated and, second, deploy some concepts derived from cognitive therapy to extract further therapeutic methods from those two extant models of therapeutic philosophy. Chapter 8 analyses and vindicates Austin’s response to Ayer, in Sense and Sensibilia, and spells out when and how ordinary language analysis can be employed as therapy in philosophy. Chapter 9 analyses Wittgenstein’s way into and out of the problem about sudden understanding that is at the centre of the investigations of understanding and reading in his later main work. The analysis shows that in his Philosophical Investigations Wittgenstein is practicing what he is preaching, and elicits a subtle method of self-therapy that anticipates some guiding ideas of later cognitive therapy. We can overcome the problems we imagine under the spell of philosophical pictures by exposing the autonomous habits of thought that fuel those flames of fancy.

In brief: By showing that, and analysing how, philosophical reflection is shaped by philosophical pictures and, more generally, by autonomous cognitive processes of which we are largely unaware, we will provide new foundations for therapeutic philosophy, which allow us to vindicate its aims and to develop pertinent methods. We thus arrive at a new vision of philosophy, which applies not to all of the subject but to an important part: Where it is a struggle with philosophical pictures and the delusions they engender, philosophy is best practised as a kind of therapy, guided by the findings and theories of a novel metaphilosophy—a novel epistemology of philosophy—in something like the way in which cognitive psychotherapy is guided by the findings and theories of different branches of psychology.





Part I

Philosophical Pictures





1 Philosophical Pictures


The Birth of ‘the Mind’




Pictures rather than propositions, metaphors rather than statements, determine most of our philosophical convictions. At any rate, they determine many of our most fundamental philosophical convictions. Philosophers alive to the power of pictures have tended to focus on metaphors deliberately employed and pictures actually endorsed by the philosophers guided by them, like the picture of the mind as a repository of images or as always the most sophisticated machinery of the day—a mechanical clock, a telephone exchange, a computer.1 In this book, by contrast, we shall develop and vindicate the idea that philosophical reflection may be systematically guided, and misled, by metaphors and analogies that philosophers are not aware of relying upon. This idea will prove the key to a new understanding of the nature and genesis of many philosophical problems, which vindicates a radical reorientation of philosophical work.

At least part of the core idea it is built on was first mooted by Ludwig Wittgenstein, in his Big Typescript and in contemporaneous sources: ‘We encounter [philosophical problems] only when we are guided not by practical purpose in forming our sentences, but by certain analogies within language’ (BT 427). Many a ‘false analogy’ has been ‘accepted into language’ (BT 409). They guide us without our being aware of this: ‘To use [psychoanalysis’] way of putting things . . . [such] a simile [is] at work in the unconscious’ (VW 69). Such a ‘false picture’, applied to cases where there is nothing analogous to its crucial feature, is frequently at the bottom of philosophical perplexity (BT 428). We can sum this up by coining the notion of ‘being under the spell of a philosophical picture’. In first approximation:

We are under the spell of a philosophical picture when our philosophical reflection is guided by certain analogies within language, without our being aware of being guided by them.



I would like to show that philosophers often are, in this sense, under the spell of philosophical pictures. This is not a philosophical claim which purports to answer a philosophical question. Rather, it is a meta-philosophical claim about how philosophers reason about the topics about which they raise philosophical questions and problems, and construct theories to solve them. This meta-philosophical claim amounts to a hypothesis about how actual people in flesh and blood actually reason when actually engaged in a particular kind of intellectual activity. In brief, it is an empirical hypothesis. We shall develop the concept of ‘philosophical pictures’ in such a way as to turn our claim into a respectable hypothesis, amenable to empirical confirmation and disconfirmation. In this chapter, we will marshal enough evidence to warrant the claim’s adoption as a working hypothesis. In the next chapter we will come to see that, and why, the further pursuit of this hypothesis promises to be philosophically most rewarding.

To turn our basic claim into a respectable hypothesis, we will develop the notion of a philosophical picture with the help of some concepts and results from cognitive linguistics and cognitive psychology, which are in fact consistent with various Wittgensteinian strictures but were not yet available to Wittgenstein (who had to make do with Freudian psychoanalysis as the most advanced psychology of his day). Those new concepts and results allow us, first, to identify the relevant ‘analogies within language’ and, second, to explain how these analogies guide us without our being aware of it (how they are ‘at work in the unconscious’). This will allow us to see that, and how, an experimentally documented cognitive process interlocks with an empirically documented linguistic process and shapes philosophical reflection: It serially generates philosophical intuitions which are unsound by the lights of the very thinkers who feel bound to honour them. The most salient conflicts between such intuitions and other convictions give rise to illusory but maddening problems.

These philosophical problems can be overcome by coming to understand their nature and genesis: not by finding out new truths about their subject (about the mind, perception, meaning, etc.), let alone by constructing new philosophical theories about that subject (theories of the mind, perception, meaning, etc.), but by gaining new meta-philosophical insight into the way we think about this subject and come to raise those problems, when engaged in philosophical reflection. To build up to the conclusions that motivate this new approach to—one important kind of—philosophical problem, we will reconstruct some of the philosophical intuitions which shaped the early modern conception of the mind and trace how these intuitions gave rise to classical mind-body problems which still worry us today.





1. CONCEPTUAL METAPHORS AND

STRUCTURAL ANALOGIES




Metaphor (from Greek “meta-pherein”, i.e., ‘to transfer’) is the transfer of terms initially applied to things of one sort (the elements of the source domain), to things of a different kind (the elements of the target domain). The transfer is typically motivated by an actual or assumed analogy between the former and the latter. In the simplest case, this is a ‘material analogy’, where the source and the target share one or more particular properties. We think, for instance, that lions are strong and courageous and lambs innocent and docile, and accordingly say that Achilles is a lion and the small child a lamb, in case we think that they share one or more of those properties with these animals. Thus we apply an animal-predicate “F” (“is a lion”, “lamb”, etc.) to a human being in case Fs are exemplary owners of some properties (strength, courage, etc.) and we wish to credit the man at issue with one or more of those properties. Some contexts may leave it quite open which of the potentially pertinent properties get attributed; other contexts suggest some before others; and sometimes convention has rendered the metaphorical application of the animal-term practically synonymous with the attribution of one particular property: We say, “Achilles is a lion”, meaning that he is courageous like a lion—and might as well just have said, “Achilles is courageous”. Focus on these simple and obvious cases led philosophers for centuries to disregard metaphor as a stylistic device relevant mainly in poetry and rhetoric.

Far more common and important, however, are the less simple and salient cases of metaphor which linguists started to study only about thirty years ago:2 Frequently, we do not merely transfer one single term, in virtue of a material analogy, but transfer a whole set of related terms, forging a structural analogy. Indeed, such metaphorical extension is one of the major processes driving the development of language:3 We tend to conceptualise unfamiliar or abstract matters in terms initially applied literally to concrete or familiar things or actions, whose use we extend metaphorically to abstract or new contexts. Thus the verb “to grasp” started its career in talk about physical actions (‘He grasped a stone to throw’) and was then put to new use in talk about intellectual achievements (‘He grasped the implications of the claim’).4 For English, such metaphorical extension is well documented for a wide range of abstract domains of discourse, including talk about argument, communication, emotions, intellectual activity and economic development.5 A debate rages in cognitive linguistics about whether such extension occurs in all natural languages. But it is safe to say that it occurs in very many languages, often with comparatively slight differences between languages which are otherwise very different. For example, English and many Australian Aboriginal languages alike recruit a host of perception-terms for talk about intellectual activities and achievements; but where English employs terms for visual perception, most Aboriginal languages employ terms for auditory perception—where the Englishman ‘sees’ a point or implication, the Aborigine ‘hears’ it.6 Metaphorical extension is the single most important process whereby languages become equipped to deal with the abstract. Much of the ‘mental vocabulary’ of the major languages of philosophy (English and German, Latin and Ancient Greek) is recruited in this way from the domains of grasping and seeing, manipulation and perception.7

Typically, metaphorical extension is wholesale: The use of a whole set of related terms is extended from the initial (‘source’) domain to a new (‘target’) domain. Consider, for example, the plethora of perception-related terms recruited for talk about the intellectual accomplishment of understanding actions:

It is clear or obscure to me why you did what you did, according to whether or not I manage to see any reasons for acting that way. I may look for reasons where these are hidden or be blind to reasons that are in plain view. An illuminating explanation which throws new light on your action will let me see reasons I had previously overlooked, and thus get a fuller picture of these reasons, or at least let me catch some glimpse of them, where I was previously completely in the dark. For instance, a fresh look at the situation to which you responded may reveal threats in whose light your action no longer looks as out of character as it did at first sight. Those brighter than others will find it easier to make out such reasons, and point them out to those dim-wits who need to have the reasons shown to them. Now that his account has opened my eyes and lets me see your true reasons, I finally see your action in the right light. To see it aright, I had to look at it from another perspective.





More generally, terms initially applied in talk about visual search came to be employed, wholesale, in talk about goal-directed intellectual efforts: efforts to solve problems, answer questions, explain facts, events or actions, etc.

Metaphorical uses of words are commonly thought to involve poetic creativity or license (if only at the point of their inception). However, at any rate the wholesale extension now at issue involves more logical rigour than poetic license and a rather fettered sort of creativity. It is systematic almost to the point of being boring (or beautiful, depending upon your perspective). It derives metaphorical senses for ever more expressions from a basic mapping which maps an element (object, property or relation) of the source domain (here: visual search) onto an element of the target domain (here: intellectual effort). In the present source domain, the principal achievement is that of finding, which takes the shape of seeing what one is looking for. The principal achievements in the target domain are those of knowing and understanding. To understand something is to know its reasons or causes or whatever explains it. Seeing and knowing therefore are the most basic elements of the respective domains. The ‘basic mapping’ links them:





(1) S sees x → S knows x





Further mappings can be obtained by simple logical and modal operations. By replacing “sees” by “knows” in otherwise topic-neutral sentence-frames (‘base extension’), we obtain:





(2) S does not see x → S does not know x

(3) It is possible (for S) to see x, i.e., x is visible (for S)→ It is possible (for S) to know x

(4) It is not possible (for S) to see x, i.e., x is invisible (for S) → It is not possible (for S) to know x

(5) X makes it possible (for S) to see y, i.e., x makes y visible (for S) → X makes it possible (for S) to know y

(6) X makes it impossible (for S) to see y, i.e., x makes y invisible (to S) → X makes it impossible (for S) to know y

(7) S tries to get to see x, i.e., S looks for x → S tries to get to know x





The terms on both sides can also be adverbially qualified, as in:





(3’) It is easily possible (for S) to see x, i.e., x is readily visible (for S) → It is easily possible (for S) to know x



In cognitive linguistics, such mappings of several related ‘models’ (to the left of the arrows) onto several related ‘targets’ (to the right of the arrows) are called ‘conceptual metaphors’. For example, lines (1) to (7) and their progeny (like 3’ above or 8 below), constitute the conceptual metaphor ‘Intellectual Effort as Visual Search’.

Inferences known as metaphorical entailments8 seize on familiar facts about the source domain and on such mappings. Their conclusions yield further mappings which extend the conceptual metaphor, or new metaphorical senses which are ‘motivated’ by those mappings and ‘realise’ that metaphor. The source-domain facts can be either empirical or conceptual (consequences of definitions or explanations of meaning). For example: Until the mid-19th century, the adjective “evident” was literally used to mean ‘distinctly visible, conspicuous’. This explanation and mapping (3’)—treated as an implication—jointly entail a conclusion that introduces a still familiar metaphorical sense of this term:





If x is evident, then x is distinctly/easily visible.

If x is easily visible, it is easily knowable.

If x is evident, then x is easily knowable.



Or take the trivial empirical fact that when things are bright and shining they are (by and large) readily visible. Together with the same mapping, this yields the metaphorical sense of the adjective “clear”, applied until the mid-19th century to physical objects which are bright or shining (‘teeth as clear as ivory’):9 If a fact or cause, reason or consequence is clear, it is easily knowable (and if statements or explanations are clear, they are easy to understand, i.e. their meaning or content is easy to get to know).

Through such entailments, the same mapping may motivate the extension of several related terms. In early modern English, the term “obvious” was literally used to mean ‘situated in the way, positioned in front of or opposite to, facing’. When things are situated right in our way, they are readily visible. This trivial fact about the source domain and mapping (3’) jointly allow us to say that facts etc. that are easily knowable and ‘perfectly clear and manifest, such as common sense might suggest’ are obvious—a metaphorical use established since the mid-17th century:





If x is obvious (stands right in our way), then x is well visible.

If x is well visible, then x is easily knowable.

If x is obvious, then x is easily knowable.



In the same way, mapping (5) above facilitates several metaphorical entailments which seize on several different trivial facts about how things can be made visible, and thus motivate talk of people, investigations or explanations ‘exposing’, ‘unearthing’, ‘revealing’, ‘illuminating’ or ‘opening our eyes to’ reasons or causes, facts or events—whatever we try to get to know or understand:





If x sheds light on y, then x makes y visible (in the dark).

If x makes y visible (in any way), then x makes it easy to get to know y.

If x sheds light on y, then x makes it easy to get to know y.



In the same way, again, we can seize on mapping (6) and the truism that things are hard or impossible to see in the dark, to extend the verbs “obscure” and “obfuscate” with their related adjectives from their literal meanings ‘to make dark, to deprive of light or brightness’ and ‘to cast into darkness or shadow’, respectively, to the metaphorical senses we employ when criticising speakers for obscuring facts or implications with obfuscatory rhetoric which makes it hard to grow aware of those facts or implications.

To recruit a source-domain expression for use in the target domain, these simple metaphorical entailments each seize on one implication of the expression, which also serves as, or falls under, the antecedent of an extant mapping. Further mappings can be obtained by exploiting more than one implication (where available): If S ‘overlooks’ x, for instance, then (i) S does not see x, even though (ii) x is visible (from the position of S). (i) and (ii) are the antecedents of mappings (2) and (3), respectively. Those relations get mapped onto ‘S does not know x’ and ‘it is possible (for S) to know x’, respectively. A more complex metaphorical entailment thus yields the new mapping:





(8) S overlooks x → S doesn’t know x, though it is possible for him to know x



This mapping motivates saying that people ‘overlook’ implications, hints, points, risks, etc. they are in a position to, but fail to, appreciate. Another complex entailment lets us say that some people deliberately fail to notice what others may inadvertently overlook: When you deliberately close your eyes in front of something, you do not see that thing, even though it is visible, and do not want to see it. Our first three mappings take us to a conclusion that forges another new mapping





(9) S closes his eyes to x → S does not know x, even though he is in a position to get to know x, because he does not want to get to know x.



Accordingly, we say that people are prone to ‘close their eyes’ to inconvenient truths.

Base extension and metaphorical entailments preserve inferential relations between the terms extended. Regardless of whether we are talking about a sparrow on the roof or the implication of a hypothesis, that I ‘overlook’ it implies both that it is more or less ‘evident’ and that I fail to ‘see’ it even so, and that I ‘point it out’ to you implies that I ‘see’ it myself. In addition to inferential relations which are, like these, forged by semantic entailments, also relations forged by defeasible common-sense inferences get preserved: When a box is standing right in our way (i.e., is literally obvious), it will be easily visible—except of course if it is dark. This relation is preserved in the target domain, in which analogous defeasibility conditions obtain: An obvious implication is easy to see or discern—though not when it is obscured by obfuscating rhetoric. In this way, a multitude of implications, including subtle nuances of use, get preserved as the term gets extended from the source- to the target-domain. (Compare the different implications of “His remarks let me catch a glimpse of his intentions” and “His remarks made his intentions obvious.”)

This preservation of inferential relations between terms and, thereby, of relations between the domain-elements they stand for forges a structural analogy between source domain and target domain:

A is structurally analogous to B iff a set of elements of A can be mapped onto a set of elements of B, which stand in some of the same relations to each other.





The process of metaphorical extension systematically forges extensive structural analogies between perceptual activities and achievements and various intellectual activities and achievements including knowledge and understanding. More generally, the process systematically forges extensive structural analogies between simple concrete models and usually more abstract targets.

This is a sufficient condition for metaphor. We typically recognise the use of an expression as ‘metaphorical’ the moment we recognise such a structural analogy. To take a simple but compelling example, you will regard talk of ‘table-legs’ as just another literal use of the word “leg”—until you get to know that in German the bottom-ends of the legs are called the ‘feet’ of the table and someone suggests calling the top of the table that ‘stands’ on those legs its ‘back’ and the underside its ‘belly’. The proposal preserves the spatial relations that obtain between the legs, feet, backs and bellies of broad-backed four-legged animals like bears or cows. The moment you recognise the structural analogy between these animals and your kitchen-table, you will recognise talk of the latter’s ‘legs’ as metaphorical (at least when it is part of the proposed idiom). This allows us to link the cognitive linguists’ theoretical notion and our everyday recognition of metaphor:

A conceptual metaphor is a mapping of elements from a source- to a target-domain which preserves relations between the mapped elements (and thus forges a structural analogy).



The use of an expression is metaphorical if the expression is being applied to an element of an abstract or unfamiliar target domain on which a conceptual metaphor maps an element of a concrete or familiar source domain, to which the expression is or was applied as well.



Conceptual metaphors systematically forge structural analogies between simple concrete models and abstract targets which are frequently the object of philosophical reflection. To the extent to which those metaphors are ‘linguistically realised’, i.e., ‘motivate’ actual metaphorical uses of terms in the way outlined, these analogies can be said to have been ‘accepted’ or built ‘into language’. They do not quite fit Wittgenstein’s description of the ‘analogies within language’ that he takes to guide philosophical reflection: These structural analogies are not ‘false’ but do actually obtain. However, as we shall see, the moment we engage in philosophical reflection, we are prone to unwittingly but systematically mishandle such analogies, in what cognitive psychologists call ‘non-intentional analogical reasoning’, where they can be said to be ‘at work in the unconscious’.





2. NON-INTENTIONAL REASONING

AND PHILOSOPHICAL PICTURES




Much of our reasoning is analogical in nature; indeed, analogical inference is the motor of intellectual creativity, scientific and more mundane. We take some things to be analogous to others with a similar structure, and infer from the fact that something holds true of these models that it also holds true of those other things. Sometimes we do this deliberately and carefully.
