We will then consider the models and the things they are models of, known as their ‘targets’, will establish that models and targets share certain structural or relational properties, will infer the hypothesis that the targets might also share certain other, related properties of the model, and will finally test this hypothesis by further study of the targets at issue.10 At the other extreme, we may spontaneously and unthinkingly assimilate some things to others that are structurally similar to them, without being aware of using the latter as a model: We may unwittingly project further features of the models onto targets structurally similar to them. Since, by and large, we find it intuitively compelling to assimilate things in this way to the models we are not aware of employing, such non-intentional analogical reasoning may decisively shape or pre-structure our thoughts.

At present, such reasoning is only beginning to be studied in cognitive psychology. The pertinent work is emerging from a line of research into the role of analogy in text comprehension and interpretation.11 In the most pertinent experiments subjects were given a set of short stories to read and were later asked some questions about their content. Among ‘filler’ texts, each set included a pair of structurally similar stories (e.g. one story about the expeditions and findings of a zoologist and one about those of a marine biologist), where one text gave specific information at a point where the other remained vague or ambiguous (where the story about the zoologist relates a particular finding, the story about the marine biologist speaks only of an unspecified ‘discovery’). All subjects were given the same vague story, but different subjects were given different versions of the more detailed story (where in one version the zoologist discovers a new species, in another he discovers a new form of physical deformity). The experimenters wanted to find out whether subjects would fill in detail or disambiguate the crucial statements in the vague story by analogy to the more precise story they had read previously and, if so, whether they were aware of using the more precise story as a model. To find out, they asked subjects, after a ‘filler’ task, to indicate which of several statements in a list had been part of the stories they had read. The list’s key items were statements obtained by disambiguating or embellishing the crucial statements in the vague story, namely by analogy to the corresponding statements in the structurally similar model (such as, e.g., the statement that the marine biologist discovered a new species). In 72.5% of the choices concerning such key items, they were misattributed to the vague target story, compared with 25% misattributions otherwise. When subsequently asked whether they had found all stories completely intelligible on their own or had occasionally found themselves referring to earlier stories to make sense of later ones, 80% of subjects replied all stories were completely intelligible on their own. The experimenters concluded that subjects had employed the more precise story as a model in interpreting the vaguer target story, without realising they had done so. Reading speed measures were consistent with the assumption that the projection of properties from the model to the target occurred upon first reading rather than upon memory recall.

In more general terms, the conclusion is that people may unwittingly use or treat a structurally analogous object (here: a more detailed story with the same structure) as a model and spontaneously leap from the observation that it has a certain feature (here: that it relates the discovery of, say, a new species) to the conclusion that the target (here: a vaguer story) has the feature as well, while unaware of invoking any model or relying on any analogy. More generally, these experiments establish the occurrence of ‘non-intentional analogical inferences’:

A thinker makes a non-intentional analogical inference iff he spontaneously makes an inference that presupposes that some thing (the target) is in some respect like a structurally analogous other thing (the model), while unaware of presupposing this or invoking any model.





Crucially, such inferences are made irrespective of whether or not the subject’s beliefs and other propositional attitudes are consistent with the conclusion inferred.12

The participants in the experiments were not set any specific goal prior to reading, but were simply given passages ‘to read and remember’. Presumably, analogical inferences are more likely to be made non-intentionally when we are not engrossed in deliberate goal-oriented reflection. Second, the participants non-intentionally used a more detailed story or more fully known model to interpret a less detailed story or less fully known target. Presumably, the lack of knowledge of detail or context is what has us unwittingly fall back on pertinent models. Third, the participants seized in each case on a single structurally similar story, which was previously unfamiliar to them: an ‘ad hoc model’ seized upon when proffered. As likely as not, the propensity to spontaneously make analogical inferences is even stronger when the object of interest is structurally analogous to a suitably simple and already well familiar model, and extends to cases where such a model is not proffered but suggested by some salient cue. These three considerations take us to a hypothesis about when non-intentional analogical reasoning is likely to occur: Thinkers are prone to make non-intentional analogical inferences when (1) they pursue no specific goal, (2) they lack knowledge of relevant detail or context and (3) a structurally similar familiar model is available and suggested to them.

These conditions are frequently met in philosophical reflection: Frequently, philosophical reflection is not directed towards any specific goal. Often, it involves swift generalisation or general reasoning without detailed reference to specific examples, and is couched in technical terms defined without any such reference; this easily leaves thinkers adrift without much awareness of actually relevant detail or context. Third, we have just seen that conceptual metaphors forge structural analogies between concrete or publicly observable phenomena (objects, activities, achievements, etc.) which are familiar, and many more abstract phenomena which are common topics of philosophical reflection. Where these conditions are met it is reasonable to explore the hypothesis that philosophers thinking about such abstract phenomena unwittingly treat the source-domains of conceptual metaphors as models for their target domains, and spontaneously project properties of the former onto the latter.

Since conceptual metaphors preserve inferential relations, we can draw some of the same inferences from metaphorical talk as from literal uses of the pertinent expressions. If I failed to see an implication that was obvious, staring right in my face, I must have deliberately closed my eyes to it (unless I was momentarily blind). When I have closed my eyes to the problem, I failed to see it—and could not have pointed it out to you. Etc. But while competent speakers need not be able to classify this talk correctly as metaphorical and may take it to involve literal non-perceptual senses of the pertinent terms, they can readily identify fallacies which arise from mixing literal with what are actually metaphorical uses of those terms. In everyday contexts, we immediately see through fallacies of equivocation like: ‘I can see everything inside this room. But I cannot see any solution to this problem. Therefore the solution, if one exists, must be outside this room.’ Or: ‘My eyes work well; I just passed an eye-test. But I could not see the obvious implication of your statement. Therefore the eye-test must have tested other eyes than those with which I ought to have seen the implication that was right in front of my nose.’ Since we ordinarily see through such fallacies immediately, there is nothing inherently misleading about these well-established metaphorical uses of terms.

But even when metaphorical expressions do not mislead us, we may systematically make and countenance inferences which are just as bad, in non-intentional analogical reasoning. That we spontaneously make an inference which presupposes that the target is in some respect like the model, while unaware of presupposing this, implies that we may presuppose that the two are alike in ways in which we do not want to assimilate them, including ways in which we know the two to be different. For example, a thinker may unwittingly presuppose that ‘seeing’ implications is like seeing keys also in that it literally involves an object perceived somewhere, at some location, or in that it literally involves the use of an organ of sense. Non-intentional analogical reasoning may materialise in the shape of leaps of thought with presuppositions like:





(K) To know something is to perceive some thing that is literally located at some place, literally with an organ of sense.



Leaps of thought which rely on such presuppositions will of course be inconsistent with many of his other convictions and knowledge, conceptual and other. But since non-intentional analogical inferences are made irrespective of whether or not their conclusions are consistent with the subjects’ other beliefs and attitudes, thinkers may find even paradoxical conclusions intuitively compelling.

As we shall see, such leaps are made by philosophers who know full well that when applied to these intellectual achievements and activities, perception-terms are not used literally and do not have these literal implications. Later (in Chapters 4–5), we will follow up the way in which leaps with presuppositions like (K) drive the reflections of Berkeley, who acutely observes:

Nothing [is] more usual, than for philosophers to speak of . . . things existing in the mind. Nor is there anything in this, but what is conformable to the general analogy of language; most part of the mental operations being signified by words borrowed from sensible things; as is plain in the terms comprehend, reflect, discourse, etc. which being applied to the mind, must not be taken in their gross original sense. (3D 250)





I propose to use the notion of ‘being under the spell of a philosophical picture’ to capture this phenomenon, this kind of unsound inference which we may make again and again against our better knowledge, and which we are arguably particularly prone to make under conditions frequently occurring in philosophical reflection:

A thinker is under the spell of, or adheres to, a philosophical picture iff he systematically makes non-intentional analogical inferences which assimilate targets to models of a conceptual metaphor, in ways in which the thinker knows them to be different.



Let’s call such inferences ‘picture-driven’. Examples include leaps of thought which presuppose such obviously ‘false analogies’ as (K) above and (R) below. Let’s say that a thinker who unwittingly but systematically makes such leaps is in the grip of the perceptual picture of knowledge and the perceptual picture of reflection, respectively.

This notion of ‘philosophical pictures’ is very different from those previously linked to the project of a philosophical therapy. Thus the classical Wittgenstein commentary by Gordon Baker and Peter Hacker characterises a philosophical picture as a ‘proto-theory’ or set of claims which need not be explicitly stated but to which one is naturally led by widely shared propensities in philosophical thinking and which inform a wide range of philosophical theories.13 Our present notion, by contrast, does not refer to claims but to models, and serves to characterise not what we obtain due to certain cognitive propensities but these propensities themselves, namely the propensity to excessively assimilate certain targets to a certain model. This notion differs also from the one employed by more pronouncedly ‘therapeutic’ interpretations subsequently pioneered by Baker. There, a thinker is said to be under the spell of a philosophical picture in case he is in the grip of one of several equally valid ways of seeing things, without being aware or without acknowledging that he is; as a result, the thinker is free to adopt a new picture or carry on as before, once he has been made aware of the hold the picture has on him.14 And it makes no sense to try to refute the picture.15 Here, by contrast, the notion is used to characterise the propensity to make a certain kind of demonstrable mistake in non-intentional reasoning, which thinkers cannot help making against their better knowledge.16

Analogical inferences are never logically valid; they are always risky, and the conclusion is never guaranteed to be true when the premises are true. There is therefore no point in applying to such inferences the notion of ‘unsoundness’ that applies to deductive inferences and calling analogical inferences too ‘unsound’ in case they are logically invalid or proceed from false premises. But analogical inferences in which we unwittingly rely on supposed similarities between targets and models, which we know not to obtain, are unsound in a different—and stronger—sense: They proceed from a premise the thinker knows to be false. As a result, their conclusions are bound to be unwarranted by the lights of the thinker who infers them. Indeed, in addition to this epistemic, they are guaranteed to suffer from a semantic defect: Since such inferences project a feature of the model onto a target which does not share it, their immediate conclusions are guaranteed to be either false or meaningless.

For our purposes, it does not matter which of these—and other—explications captures more accurately what Wittgenstein meant by ‘philosophical pictures’ (and he may have had different things in mind in different works or passages). Rather, we need to find out whether the phenomenon we have characterised actually occurs, and matters, in philosophy. In the text-comprehension experiments we considered a moment ago, experimenters made available a pertinent model (a more detailed story), offered pertinent premises (the vaguer story), and checked whether the subjects leaped from them to a conclusion suggested by the model (the statement filling in detail by analogy). Instead of eliciting such leaps of thought (in controlled experiments or non-controlled surveys), we can study non-intentional analogical reasoning by analysing lines of thought which thinkers have already rehearsed, arguably under conditions facilitating such leaps, and laid down in philosophical texts. We can study non-intentional analogical reasoning in philosophy by analysing philosophical texts in which the results of such reasoning were set out in a polished shape that contains no inconsequential slips but preserves those lasting mistakes their authors were unable to see through.

Non-intentional analogical inferences lead to conclusions that the thinkers who make them are prone to find intuitively compelling. They may either proceed from stated assumptions or from premises not deemed worth stating (being either too trivial or too widely shared at the time). Where they proceed from stated assumptions, these inferences will show up in the text as gaps in the explicitly stated argument (argumentative gaps). Where they proceed from tacit premises they will typically materialise as philosophical intuitions. In the presently relevant sense,

Intuitions are non-perceptual judgements which thinkers (i) arrive at without being aware of making an inference, (ii) find intuitively compelling, and (iii) frequently maintain in the absence of argument (regarding them, perhaps, as sources of ‘intuitive insight’).





The attribution of non-intentional analogical inferences to thinkers unaware of making any analogical inference (or any substantive move whatever, for that matter) is then by inference to the best explanation: I propose to offer such attributions to explain philosophical intuitions and gaps in philosophical arguments, as set out in philosophical texts. Such attributions are part of accounts that explain why competent thinkers have certain—paradoxical—intuitions or go along with arguments that contain gaps that cannot be closed by invoking views they state elsewhere.

Psychological experiments establish that competent thinkers actually make non-intentional analogical inferences and do so under conditions which are frequently met in philosophical reflection. Empirical work in cognitive linguistics documents the actual occurrence of a linguistic process that systematically generates models of the kind thinkers unwittingly seize on in such inferences. To determine whether a particular philosopher actually makes non-intentional analogical inferences which employ models built into his language, we proceed in three steps: First, we identify pertinent explananda in his work: paradoxical intuitions and gaps in his argument which cannot be bridged convincingly by invoking any of his stated views. Second, we develop a cognitive reconstruction of those moves or intuitions, which is similar to a ‘rational reconstruction’ in that it explains how competent thinkers can or could come to make or have certain—alas unsound—moves or intuitions, namely as a result of cognitive processes for which there is independent experimental evidence and which demonstrably occur under conditions which are frequently met in philosophical reflection. We develop a cognitive reconstruction of the previously identified moves and intuitions, which shows how they could result from non-intentional analogical inferences (picture-driven inferences) and related phenomena to which we shall presently turn. Third, we need to show that this explanation of the pertinent moves and intuitions is superior to competing explanations, ideally refuting the latter. Where all three steps can be made, the conclusion that the thinker is under the spell of particular philosophical pictures and actually made the picture-driven inferences specified by the cognitive reconstruction is warranted as the best available explanation of why the thinker made the moves or had the intuitions at issue.

In the most interesting cases, we are dealing with paradoxical philosophical intuitions which many philosophers, perhaps entire generations of philosophers, regarded as too obviously true to merit argument—and therefore usually accepted in the absence of argument. In this chapter, we shall develop a cognitive reconstruction of a cluster of such intuitions and will refute the two most important alternative explanations, to support the hypothesis that those influential intuitions are actually due to the picture-driven inferences we shall reconstruct. Such reconstructions of unsound non-intentional inferences are easiest to grasp and accept where we share the intuitions at issue in part but not completely. In this and the next chapter, we shall therefore reconstruct a cluster of intuitions which initially shaped the modern concept of the mind that, in the 17th century, displaced earlier notions of Aristotelian provenance. In later chapters, we shall demonstrate the application of the new approach to particular thinkers, and will engage in increasingly detailed exegetical analysis of the works of two early modern philosophers, Locke and Berkeley, to develop cognitive reconstructions that explain some paradoxical claims and striking gaps in their arguments, which have puzzled commentators.





3. A COGNITIVE RECONSTRUCTION:

PHILOSOPHERS’ NEW ‘MIND’




We hypothesised that thinkers are particularly prone to make non-intentional analogical inferences under conditions which include familiarity with a model that is suggested to them and is structurally analogous to the target they think about. A conceptual metaphor can suggest suitable models to speakers of a language that realises it, and may well do so the more forcefully the more pertinent expressions are used simultaneously in literal talk about the source-domain and in metaphorical talk about the target-domain. While still alive today, the perceptual metaphor we considered was at this kind of peak in the 17th century, when practically all the still-common metaphorical senses of perception-terms were either first coined or already well established and when those same terms still retained their initial literal senses, many of which have become obsolete or archaic in the meantime. For example, the adjectives “evident” and “obvious” have lost their literal, perceptual sense completely, as has the noun “idea” which, from the mid-16th to the mid-18th century, was used in the sense of ‘image’ or ‘likeness’ (as when Locke uses it to speak of images in a mirror, in EHU II.i.25). The formerly common literal use of “to consider” to mean ‘to view attentively’ is now archaic. The pure perceptual sense of “to contemplate”, namely ‘to look at with continued attention, gaze upon, view, observe’, has been superseded by ‘to observe or look thoughtfully’, a mixture of the pure perceptual with a ‘mental’ sense, viz. ‘to view mentally’.

At this time, a new concept of ‘the mind’ displaced from philosophical discussion earlier concepts of Aristotelian provenance, viz. the notions of ‘rational’, ‘sensitive’ and ‘vegetative souls’. These notions pin down those clusters of ‘powers’ whose possession makes living beings the kind of beings they are: The ‘vegetative soul’ consists of those powers that make the difference between living beings and lifeless objects, viz. the powers of growth, nutrition and reproduction. The ‘sensitive soul’ is made up of those powers that distinguish animals from plants, namely the powers of perception, sensation, locomotion and a-rational desire (or appetite). The ‘rational soul’ or ‘intellect’, finally, consists of those powers that set human beings apart from other animals—the powers of reasoning and rational volition. The new concept of ‘the mind’ has a radically different rationale and extension: It was not introduced to identify powers that make the difference between different kinds of being, and the entity denoted was charged with the tasks of both the sensitive and the rational soul, with sensing and perceiving as well as with thinking and volition.

Let us consider two intuitions which—among others—shaped the new concept. The first is forcefully conveyed by terminological explanations prefacing Locke’s Essay Concerning Human Understanding (henceforward EHU):

‘By those denominations, I mean some object in the mind . . . such as it is there seen and perceived to be . . . By determinate, when applied to a simple idea, I mean that simple appearance, which the mind has in its view, or perceives in itself, when that idea is said to be in it’. (EHU p. 13, my underlining)





The term “the mind” here stands for two things: first, for a perceptual space in which objects of perception are located and, second, for a perceptual organ with which they are ‘viewed’. This organ, also called ‘the understanding’, is ‘like the eye’ and ‘makes us see, and perceive all other things’ (EHU II.i.1). It is here treated metonymically as the subject of perception (as we do in speaking of what ‘our eyes see’ when saying what we see with our eyes). While Locke feels torn between an immaterialist and a materialist conception of the mind (EHU IV. iii. 6, p. 542), the latter is, by most of his contemporaries, ‘thought to take up no [physical] space, to have no [physical] extension’ (EHU II.ix.1). The fundamental claim all parties take for granted is that



(I1) There is in us some sort of space and organ of perception (‘mind’), and things (‘ideas’) which we perceive in and with it.





The second core intuition is well conveyed by Locke’s distinction of two principal actions of the mind. Using the term “thinking” as a generic label for all kinds of cognition, Locke distinguishes: ‘perception, or thinking’ and ‘volition, or willing’ (EHU II.vi.2)—with the implication that, just as to will something is to perform an act of volition, to think something is to perform an act of perception. He explains that a man can get clear on what this action is simply ‘by reflecting on what he does himself, when he sees, hears, feels, etc. or thinks’ (EHU II.ix.2), treating thinking as one kind of perception along with seeing, hearing and feeling. Such perception only occurs when some sort of idea is in the mind (EHU II.ix.2–4). Hence:



(I2) To think (in the generic sense) is to perceive an idea in the mind, with the mind.

Locke is representative of 17th-century philosophers in that he states reasons for and against further developing these intuitions in one way or another (e.g., into a materialist or immaterialist conception of the mind), but provides no explicit argument for these two fundamental assumptions themselves, which he regards as obviously true (EHU I.i.8). On the other hand, we shall see (in the next section) that they are not part of common sense as implicit in (now or then) ordinary language, not the sort of thing anyone could have (then or now) believed all along, prior to philosophical reasoning or as the result of the mere exercise of linguistic competencies. Nor were these assumptions part of received academic wisdom (which can be built on without explicit discussion): Within the Aristotelian scheme of things, the workings of the ‘sensitive soul’ (sensation and perception) but not of the intellect (cognition) had been taken to involve inner spaces and organs of perception. I1 and I2 are distinctively philosophical and, at the time, fresh intuitions—which current scholarly consensus takes to have been shared, without explicit argument, by many early modern thinkers.17 We will now develop a cognitive reconstruction which explains how these common but otherwise apparently inexplicable intuitions can be generated by the cognitive process we have outlined: the overly literal application of conceptual metaphors, in non-intentional analogical reasoning, namely, the excessive assimilation of thinking, knowing, understanding, etc. to sense-perception.

At its root are analogical inferences which can be represented as deductions relying on tacit presuppositions like (K) above or (R) below:



(R) To think about something is to perceive some thing that is literally located at some place, literally with an organ of sense.



As premises these inferences take truisms which reflect the metaphorical uses of perception-terms motivated by the present metaphors. They let me say (metaphorically) that I think about or ‘consider’ an issue that has been ‘put before me’, and ‘look at’ it ‘from all sides’ and ‘with eagle-eyes’, in order to ‘discern’ all of its implications for our business. But—of course— none of these intellectual efforts (which form the target domain of the perceptual metaphors reconstructed) essentially involves the literal use of our eyes, or objects literally in our environment:



(T1) To think about something is not to perceive any thing that is literally located in our environment, and is not to literally use one’s eyes—or any other external sense-organ.



However, those prone to excessively assimilate thinking to visual perception will then find it natural to leap to a conclusion: If thinking about something does not involve seeing something located in our environment, around us, then it must involve seeing something located within us; and if it does not involve use of any external sense-organ, then it must involve use of some internal sense-organ. In other words: Together with (R)—and only together with (R)—the truism (T1) entails



(C1) To think about something is to perceive some thing (literally) located in us, (literally) by using an internal organ of sense.



Through this and analogous picture-driven inferences, with conclusions about other intellectual activities and achievements, the early modern concepts of ‘mind’ and ‘idea’ can be introduced:



(D1) “the mind” = def.: (a) the inner space in which we perceive the things we do not perceive with our five senses; (b) the inner organ of sense with which we perceive the objects in that space.



The things perceived in this inner space, with this inner organ, are called ‘ideas’. Again speaking metonymically of the organ as subject of perception, Locke, for instance, defines:



(D2) Whatsoever the mind perceives in itself . . . that I call idea. (EHU II.viii.8)



This much takes us to the conception of a perceptual space and organ crucially involved in cognition (and, perhaps, some other things) which is called ‘the mind’ and is placed quite literally in us: We thus come to posit senses and organs of inner perception in our heads, as Locke does in positing (without further argument) that ideas are ‘convey[ed] . . . to their audience in the brain, the mind’s presence room (as I may so call it)’, where they have to show up in order ‘to bring themselves into view, and be perceived by the understanding’ (EHU II.iii.1; cp. Chapter 3.5).

Like Locke (EHU IV.iii.6, p. 542), most philosophers attracted to this conception at the same time felt uneasy about it. The crucial conclusions leading up to it—C1 and its analogues—contradict convictions many thinkers are unable or unwilling to shed, such as



(T2) To think about something is not to perceive anything in our bodies, and is not to employ any bodily organ of sense.



A response to such contradictions for which there is evidence in early modern texts is the spontaneous reinterpretation of the problematic conclusions that thinkers find intuitively compelling. Thus many early modern thinkers explain that C1 is of course not to be taken literally (thus Berkeley in 3D 250; cp. EHU II.ix.10):



(C2) To think about things is not to perceive anything literally located ‘in’ us. It is to perceive something in an ‘inner space’ that is not literally, physically in us, with an ‘inner sense’ that is not to be confused with any bodily organ of sense.



But, crucially, this explanation does not retract the picture-driven idea that thinking is, quite literally, one kind of perception along with seeing, hearing and feeling, and with proper organs and perceptual spaces which must be somehow internal if they are not external. It does not tell us that to think about things is to ‘perceive’ them merely in the well-established metaphorical sense of some perception-verbs (like “look at” in “looking at a question from all sides”). Rather, it only rejects the suggestion that the pertinent spaces and organs of perception compete for space in our body with the organs of the other senses. (We will follow this up below, at the end of Section 5.)

To have a handy label for the typically spontaneous response illustrated by this explanation, we can speak of ‘sublimation’ (though without the term’s Freudian implications) and say that thinkers ‘sublimate’ conclusions which strike them, on the one hand, as intuitively compelling but, on the other hand, as too primitive or patently false to be taken seriously even for a moment.

A thinker sublimates a claim iff in the face of a conflict with claims he accepts he spontaneously places a new interpretation on the claim’s key terms, which apparently allows him to maintain its expression.





Frequently, sublimating explanations—like C2—merely tell us that the key terms are not to be understood ‘in a gross literal sense’ (to borrow words from Berkeley) but in some other way (which often is not spelled out). Such negative sublimation has thinkers spontaneously regard the mind defined by D1 as a non-physical space and non-bodily organ of inner perception.

By using the terms “mind” and “idea” to paraphrase the initial conclusion C1 or its sublimated variant C2, we obtain, respectively, a ‘materialist’ and an ‘immaterialist’ version of a specific instance of the intuition I2 we seek to explain:



(I2*) To think about things is to perceive ideas in the mind, with the mind.



Exactly parallel lines of thought lead from truisms about knowing and understanding, which reflect other conventional metaphorical uses of perception-terms, to analogous conclusions about these achievements: Like thinking-about, these achievements essentially involve perceiving ideas in the mind, with the mind—further specific instances of I2. To sum up the explanatory hypothesis thus obtained: Picture-driven inferences have thinkers posit a perceptual space and organ in us; negative sublimation removes the two from our bodies and turns them into immaterial ‘minds’.

Together with conclusions like I2*, the truisms that we sometimes think about, know or understand things then entail that there are such things as minds and ideas perceived in them. Indeed, the existence of minds then seems to be an utterly trivial consequence of the obvious fact that we sometimes think about, know or understand things. To convince yourself of the existence of minds and ideas all you need to do is to think a thought or indulge in recollections:

I suppose it will be easily granted me that there are such ideas in men’s minds; everyone is conscious of them in himself, and men’s words and actions will satisfy him that they are in others. (EHU I.i.8)





The existence of minds and ideas—the other intuition I1 we seek to explain— thus appears too obvious to merit philosophical argument.

This illustrates an empirically well documented phenomenon which is not restricted to abstract reflection but almost ubiquitous: belief bias.18 Quite regardless of their subject-matter, our loyalty to our extant beliefs, warranted and unwarranted, is commonly reinforced by several bias effects of which we are hardly ever aware: We tend to notice evidence that confirms our beliefs more readily than uncongenial evidence, attach more weight to the former than the latter, and accept argument, sound and unsound, more readily when it leads to a conclusion we believe true. At the heart of this cluster of bias effects is an interpretation bias, namely our potentially helpful propensity to spontaneously and immediately interpret new information in the light of extant knowledge—real and supposed. Such interpretation becomes problematic where we interpret statements and evidence in the light of merely supposed knowledge, unwarranted beliefs or unsound intuitions. Such interpretation usually makes these beliefs and intuitions appear more reasonable than they actually are and further inquiry seem less pressing. When thinkers spontaneously interpret, for example, truisms about people having thoughts or memories in the light of intuitively compelling conclusions like C1 or C2, they regard every single thought or memory they have as evidence bearing witness to the existence of the mind and thus come to take for granted that there is such a space and organ of inner perception, without asking for further evidence or argument.

We thus arrive at the hypothesis of a tri-partite process, which I propose to call the ‘pictorial process’: (1) Picture-driven inferences take thinkers to paradoxical conclusions they find intuitively compelling in spite of the con-flict with common sense. (2) They sometimes sublimate these conclusions in the face of this conflict. (3) The conclusions which they find intuitively so compelling they spontaneously want to save them then bias their equally spontaneous interpretation of further talk and evidence, which leads them to maintain the conclusion without further argument (or, in other cases, on the basis of obviously defective arguments). These three spontaneous, non-reflective moves may occur prior to all deliberate reflection and generate philosophical intuitions we feel compelled to honour—as it were, the surface pull of dark waters moved by powerful undercurrents of thought which quietly flow in directions we know to be wrong.





4. REFUTING A FIRST ALTERNATIVE:

RECOVERING THE ORDINARY ‘MIND’




Our working hypothesis, then, is that the pictorial process led early modern thinkers to have the intuitions I1 and I2 and to adopt and maintain them in the absence of argument. To show that this explanatory hypothesis offers the best available explanation of these intuitions, we need to refute at least the most plausible alternative explanations. We therefore now turn to the two (I think) most plausible and at any rate epistemologically most relevant competing explanations, before considering the arguably most familiar one (typically traced to Gilbert Ryle).

Biased interpretation of ordinary talk may obscure the paradoxical nature of the conclusions obtained through unsound non-intentional inference and its sublimation. It may have us think that I1 and I2 are pieces of common sense, as implicit in ordinary language, or result from mere— legitimate—extension of conceptual metaphors we employ every day. This cloaks two alternative explanations in an appearance of great plausibility. The first is this: Analytic philosophers like to elicit quite specific intuitions by inviting readers to imagine a certain case, scenario or situation, and to consider what is the right thing to say or judge in or about that situation (e.g., to judge whether the protagonist of a Gettier case really ‘knows’ a given proposition). These intuitions are commonly regarded as the deliver-ances of ordinary recognitional, classificatory and linguistic competences. If I1 and I2 are pieces of common sense, as implicit in ordinary language, then surely they can be put down to the exercise of these kinds of competences, even if they are not intuitive judgements about a specific case. I would now like to show that our two intuitions are no such pieces of common sense. To do so, we have to analyse another crucial conceptual metaphor, which motivates the major pre-philosophical use of the term “the mind” (but has, alas, not been analysed in any detail before).

Metaphorical conceptualisations of cognitive activities and achievements seize mainly on two source domains: visual perception and manual operation. The roots of that ordinary use of “the mind” lie in the development of a manipulation metaphor for feats and failures of remembering. We remember the things we manage to ‘retain’, are unable to remember those that ‘escape’ us, and may ‘recollect’ what we once ‘retained’ but then ‘escaped’ us. These terms obviously stand in the same inferential relations to each other, both when we are speaking about attempts to restrain a writhing animal (the earliest, now obsolete, sense of “retain”) or efforts to retain a fact. We are dealing with an embryonic conceptual metaphor.

‘The mind’ is born as we relax the requirement of physical contact: To remember something, you do not have to hold the writhing or fluttering animal with your hands or to your body; it is quite enough to keep it in your cage or corral, a confined space that belongs to you. We thus obtain a metaphor that uses spatial relations and movements to conceptualise feats and failures of remembering, including recall of past events, remembering dates and facts, and thinking of things to be done. The basic mapping from which we can generate all others, to obtain what I will simply call the conceptual ‘Metaphor M’ is:



(1) x is inside a space belonging to S → S remembers (or can readily think of) x



Base extension and complex metaphorical entailments generate further mappings. By simple negation:



(2) x is outside the space belonging to S → S does not remember x.



That x ‘leaves’ a bounded space in any way implies that it was once in that space but is now outside it. Hence we may derive, with mappings (1) and (2):



(3) x leaves the space of S → S forgets (no longer remembers) x



That x enters or ‘comes into’ a space implies that it was outside the space and is now in it. Hence, with the same two mappings:

(4) x enters the space of S → S remembers something he previously did not remember.



These four mappings motivate the use of a plethora of expressions including the noun “mind”: (1) We ‘keep’, ‘hold’ or ‘have in mind’ whatever it is we manage to remember, or (changing the bounded area into a container, the corral into a cage) ‘bear it in mind’. (2) Things ‘out of mind’ are not remembered or thought of: ‘out of sight, out of mind’. (3) Things that have ‘slipped (from) our mind’ or, to put it in now obsolete terms, have ‘gone’ or ‘passed out of mind’ have been forgotten. (4) Something ‘comes to mind’ when it occurs to us, especially upon reflection—prior to which we could not readily think of it.

These movements may be caused by subjects, yielding straightforward extensions of the conceptual metaphor: We may make something enter a space by bringing it into that space. Hence, with (4):



(5) x brings y into the space of S → x makes S remember something he previously did not remember



Thus something or somebody may ‘bring’ or ‘call’ things ‘to mind’, and somebody ‘puts’ something ‘into someone’s mind’ when he makes him think of something he previously did not. We can also bring people or things back into our presence by ‘recalling’ or ‘recollecting’ them from the more distant locations they occupied in the meantime. And we can move things in the reverse direction: That we ‘put’ something somewhere implies that we deliberately have it occupy that position. Hence, with mapping (2), when we ‘put something out of mind’, we deliberately do not remember or think of, but ignore it:



(6) S puts x out of his space → S ignores x



To sum up, the common-sense concept of ‘the mind’ is at home in a manipulation metaphor (of ‘holding’, ‘bearing’, ‘bringing’ and ‘putting’ things), built around the basic mapping of a spatial relation, namely spatial inclusion.

In metaphorical extension, terms literally applied to a concrete source-domain get extended to more abstract target-domains. The present metaphor motivates such extension of many manipulation-verbs and terms for spatial relations. It also allows us to extend the use of terms referring to physical spaces: The conceptual metaphor requires reference to some bounded space that is not hermetically sealed, where things can enter and leave, and be put in or out. Hence any term referring literally to some such physical space can be recruited through metaphorical entailment. We can therefore readily understand the picture Plato offers in his Theaetetus (197c ff.), of the mind as an aviary in which we ‘keep’ what we have learned, the facts we caught and caged like birds. Ordinary English exploits analogous entailments in recruiting a term referring to a suitable part of our body, viz. our head (with its several openings): If x is in my head, it is inside a space belonging to me (where things can be put or enter and leave). With mapping (1), this motivates saying that the things I remember ‘are in my head’, and mapping (4) motivates saying that what I could not retain (viz. ‘in my head’) ‘went in one ear and went out the other’. Further mappings allow me to say that I try to ‘put’ things ‘into’ someone’s else’s ‘head’ or ‘keep’ them in mine, and to tell people to ‘put out of their head’ things they should stop thinking of (forget about).

While equally ordinary, the term “the mind” is different: It has no application in the source-domain of the present metaphor and hence is not extended from it to the target–domain. It is only used as part of, or in conjunction with, metaphorically used expressions. Where it is an integral part of such expressions (e.g. “bear in mind”), the meaning of the whole (here: ‘to think of’) is not a function of the meaning of its constituent words. Where “the mind” is employed in conjunction with metaphorically used expressions which are independently meaningful, as in “His name has escaped my mind”, it can be deleted without loss or change of meaning (I might as well just say ‘His name has escaped me’). It then does not so much contribute to the meaning of the expression as highlight how the expression is being used: It is employed as a metaphor-marker, to highlight that the manipulation terms it complements are being used metaphorically. We can use it effectively to flag metaphorical use precisely because “the mind” has no use except in conjunction with metaphorically used expressions.

Since it has no prior literal use, it does not carry any baggage of inferential relations which might constrain its use as a metaphor-marker or as part of metaphorically used expressions. It adds nothing to the familiar metaphorical meaning of the expression it complements or completes: That we ‘retain something in the mind’ simply means that we retain it, in a familiar metaphorical sense of the verb—we remember it. “To bear x in mind” means ‘to think of x’, in the sense of taking x into account in one’s thinking; and that ‘I keep x in mind’ just means that I do not forget x in my thinking. In particular, none of these metaphorical expressions implies that when we remember or think of something, this thing (or anything else, for that matter) is at any particular location.

Metaphor M further evolves through mergers with other conceptual metaphors, which engender new metaphorical entailments. This is possible where metaphors cohere and their target-domains overlap. We actually merge Metaphor M with the perceptual metaphor previously discussed. Their target-domains overlap: Remembering often involves and sometimes crowns intellectual efforts. (We finally manage to think of something we tried to remember but could not think of readily.) Together with thinking-about, knowing and understanding, these cases of remembering thus fall into the target-domain of the metaphor Intellectual Effort as Visual Search. There are hence two equally well-motivated metaphorical ways of talking about these cases: We ‘come to see’ the facts and events that finally ‘come to our mind’. Since Metaphor M allows us to say that these facts and events, i.e. the things we remember, ‘come to’ or ‘leave’ just any non-hermetic bounded space, and since our visual field is such a space, the two metaphors cohere: There is a conditional link between an element of the source domain of the perceptual metaphor and the core element of the source domain of Metaphor M: If x is visible to S, x is inside a space belonging to S. This ‘source-link conditional’ can serve as the premise of a metaphorical entailment which motivates extending a source-domain term of the perceptual metaphor to the target domain of M. The conditional and M’s basic mapping (1) entail: If x is visible to S, S can remember or readily think of x. Accordingly, we say that we ‘can still see’ what we manage to remember, broadening the metaphorical use of “to see” which we already use to say that we manage to think of a solution. The further mappings of M then motivate saying that things we cannot remember (well) are (almost) invisible (as in ‘hazy memories’), that things we no longer remember have become invisible (‘fallen into the dark abyss of oblivion’), etc.

With some exceptions (like “retain”), the manipulation-verbs recruited by Metaphor M require completion by the phrase “in the mind” when used metaphorically. By contrast, the perception-terms recruited as outlined all have metaphorical senses of their own, on their own. When used in conjunction with them, “in the mind” is semantically redundant and merely used as a metaphor-marker. It can be used as such also when speaking about parts of the target-domain that do not overlap with that of Metaphor M: “I finally saw the solution in my mind” means the same as “I finally saw the solution”, i.e., that I finally ‘saw’ the solution, in a familiar metaphorical sense of that term—I finally managed to think of it. Used as a semantically redundant metaphor-marker, the phrase “in the mind” does not carry the implication that the solution ‘seen’ or thought of (or anything else, for that matter) is at any location.

Once picture-driven reasoning has led us to the philosophical conception we considered previously, belief-bias effects may have us read this conception into the ordinary talk we have now analysed. But the latter by no means implies the former. In all this ordinary talk, the phrase “in the mind” and its cognates are used either as an integral part of metaphorical expressions without semantically relevant inner structure, or as a metaphor-marker which indicates that the expressions it complements are being used in a familiar metaphorical sense. Either way, the phrase “in the mind” is semantically inert and carries no implications. Statements like ‘I finally saw the solution in my mind’ hence do not imply—I1—that there are in us any spaces of perception in which we perceive things, namely—I2—when we think. These philosophical intuitions are not implied by ordinary-language statements. They are not pieces of common sense as implicit in ordinary language. They cannot be put down to the mere exercise of ordinary linguistic skills or to related recognitional or classificatory skills.





5. REFUTING A SECOND ALTERNATIVE:

MISAPPLICATION, NOT EXTENSION




While our ordinary ‘mind-talk’ does not imply these intuitive assumptions, it is tempting to opt for a related explanation: It is tempting to think that the—legitimate—extension of the conceptual metaphors that motivate our ordinary ‘mind-talk’ might generate I1 and I2. It is of course clear from the start that this competing hypothesis can explain at most part of what we seek to explain: Like Locke, many philosophers who had these intuitions felt attracted to them on a straightforwardly literal understanding—they felt attracted to a materialist conception of the spaces and organs of perception they found it compelling to posit in us. And on such a literal reading, the present intuitions can be obtained from the metaphors discussed only through the overly literal application of the latter, not by their mere extension. We will now establish, however, that the mere extension of these metaphors cannot account for the present intuitions even on their weakest reading, while overly literal application of these metaphors can explain them on all their readings.

We can extend a conceptual metaphor through base extension and metaphorical entailments (Section 1). Base extension involves only topic-neutral logical and modal operations on a basic mapping. The basic mapping of a perceptual metaphor maps what we do or achieve with our external sense-organs (eyes or ears, etc.) onto a more abstract target domain. Topic-neutral operations on such a mapping will clearly fail to introduce the notions of inner spaces and sense-organs. Metaphorical entailments seize on extant mappings and conceptual or empirical facts about the source-domain. But the only locations, perceptual spaces and sense-organs figuring in the source-domain of a perceptual metaphor are locations in our physical environment, parts of that environment, and external sense-organs like the eyes in our face. Hence facts about them and about the concepts referring to them are the only empirical and conceptual facts metaphorical entailments can seize on. By merely extending a perceptual metaphor, we can therefore motivate ever more metaphorical talk of ‘external’ locations (describing implications as being ‘in front of our noses’), ambient perceptual spaces (a lecture ‘expanding my horizon’) and ‘external’ sense-organs (revelations ‘opening my eyes’), but no such talk of ‘inner’ locations, spaces and organs of perception. (Apparent counter-examples are discussed below.)

We can, however, further extend the metaphorical use of perception-terms by merging perceptual with other metaphors, in particular with Metaphor M. This move allows us to say that things are ‘seen’ or otherwise perceived ‘in the mind’. But even then, mere extension of metaphors does not motivate saying metaphorically that things are ‘seen’ or otherwise perceived ‘in us’, in some internal space of perception. The reason is this: We can develop Metaphor M through metaphorical entailments which seize on facts about any non-hermetic bounded space, outer or inner, and can, in particular, say that we ‘keep’ and ‘retain’ the things we remember ‘in the head’. (So far, so good.) We can also develop Metaphor M through merging it with perceptual metaphors, namely when the two cohere. (Still good.) But when we develop Metaphor M to motivate metaphorical talk of things being ‘kept’ and ‘retained’ inside us, namely in our head, it no longer coheres with metaphors invoking visual perception: If x is visible to S, x is inside a space belonging to S, namely his visual field. But this field does not even partially overlap with the inside of his head, and it is not the case that if x is visible to S, x is inside the head of S. The moment we opt for an inner space to ‘keep’ or ‘retain’ things in, we sever the previous link between the source domain of perception-metaphors and the source domain of Metaphor M. We are then left without any source-link conditional that could serve as premise of a metaphorical entailment (cp. Section 4). Hence we cannot motivate metaphorical talk of ‘seeing’ or otherwise ‘perceiving’ things ‘in our head’ or in any other ‘inner space’ by merging Metaphor M with perceptual metaphors. So we can metaphorically conceptualise remembering and thinking-of either as keeping things in an inner space or as seeing them. But we cannot metaphorically conceptualise these intellectual activities and achievements as seeing things in an inner space.

To understand a—merely—apparent counter-example, note that precisely because perceptual and inner metaphors fail to cohere, precisely because we cannot literally see things in our heads, we can effectively flag our talk of ‘seeing’, e.g., past events as being metaphorical by merging expressions motivated by the perceptual and inner metaphors that fail to cohere. This is what we do when we say, e.g., “I still see x in my head.” This combines “I still see x” and “x is in my head”. Used in the metaphorical senses we considered, the two mean pretty much the same, viz. ‘I still (manage to) think of/remember x’. While redundant in terms of content, the phrase “in my head” serves to indicate that the verb “to see” is being used in a metaphorical sense. Similarly, “I have x before my inner eye” results from a combination of “I have x before my eyes” and “I have x in my head” (into which an inner eye would look). The former means ‘I can see x’ in the given metaphorical sense, i.e., ‘I think of/remember x’—the same as “I have x in my head”. The adjective “inner” which thus gets inserted into “I have x before my eyes” therefore does not add or alter content or meaning. Hence it does not serve to attribute properties or relations to anything, in literal or metaphorical speech, but serves to indicate that speech is metaphorical. It is not used to attribute any property to an eye, and we do not say or imply that there is an eye literally or figuratively ‘in’ us. Rather, we indicate that the overall expression “S has x before his eye” is being used metaphorically. In the context of ordinary talk of intellectual activities and achievements, we use ‘inner’—like ‘mental’—epithets as metaphor-markers. For the above reasons, we cannot do otherwise.

When we extend metaphors through metaphorical entailments, inferential relations between pertinent terms get preserved. By contrast, when we use an expression (like “inner” or “in my head”) as a metaphor-marker to flag the use of another expression as metaphorical, neither it nor its constituents retain their inferential relations. (“I see x in my head” simply means ‘I see x in a familiar metaphorical sense of this verb’ and I am not entitled to draw an inference from the semantically irrelevant constituent “in my head.”). We can introduce talk of ‘inner’ spaces and organs of perception into discourse about cognition only through merging metaphors that fail to cohere and, hence, only through metaphor-markers and not through metaphorical entailments. By extending familiar conceptual metaphors, we hence cannot motivate metaphorical talk of ‘inner perception’ that would imply that, in suitably metaphorical senses—I1—there are ‘in’ us ‘spaces’ and ‘organs of perception’, which we—I2—use when we think. If we find these intuitions natural or compelling, this cannot be due to mere extension of the conceptual metaphors that motivate our ordinary mind-talk.

Like Locke, many philosophers who had these intuitions felt torn between a figurative and a literal understanding of these intuitions, and between an immaterialist and a materialist conception of the spaces and organs of perception they found it compelling to posit in us. While quite subtle argument is required to show that mere extension of conceptual metaphors cannot give rise to the intuition that there are ‘spaces’ and ‘organs of perception’ ‘in’ us, in a metaphorical sense of these terms, it is clear from the start that such extension cannot incline us towards the intuition that there are, quite literally, spaces and organs of inner perception in our physical bodies.

Accordingly, we have outlined a process whereby these intuitions are obtained not through extension but through overly literal application of the metaphors discussed (Section 3). The crucial first inference—from T1 to C1—is no metaphorical entailment: Its premise does not state a source-domain fact but explains the relation between elements of the source- and of the target-domain. Its other leg is not a mapping constitutive of a conceptual metaphor (like: If ‘S looks at x’ then S thinks about x) but a presupposition obtained only by applying this metaphor overly literally (like ‘If S thinks about x then S looks at something that is literally located at some place, literally with an organ of sense’).

The second step, negative sublimation, might then appear to flag a merely metaphorical understanding of the conclusion that to think about something is to perceive some thing located in us, by using an internal sense-organ. But the gloss—C2—does not tell us to understand the verb “to perceive” in the familiar metaphorical sense of a perception-verb: It does not state that to think about something is to ‘consider’ or ‘look at’ it, in any familiar metaphorical sense of these verbs, viz. to think of or about it. Instead of this truism, the gloss states the substantive claim that to think about something is to perceive something in an ‘inner space’ that is not literally, physically in us, with an ‘inner sense’ distinct from any bodily sense-organ. In other words: What we are told (by C2) to regard as metaphorical (in the first conclusion C1), is not the use of the verb “to perceive” but only that of the phrases “located in us” and “internal organ of sense”. This endows the verb not so much with a metaphorical as with an ill-defined hybrid sense, and reflects the fact that thinking was classified, without further ado, as a kind of perceiving, which differs from visual, auditory, etc. perception ‘only’ in involving a different sense-organ, and objects which are located neither literally around us nor literally within us. In both their ‘raw’ and their sublimated shape, the intuitions at issue result not from the—legitimate—extension but from the misapplication of conceptual metaphors.

Gilbert Ryle famously suggested that the classical conception of the mind rests on the category mistake of placing minds into the same category as bodies.19 We have now found reason to believe that it is only due to another, in a way more fundamental, category mistake that minds came to be posited in us, in the first place: Thinkers came to posit minds in us because they excessively assimilated intellectual to perceptual activities and achievements and thus placed cognition in the same category as visual perception, as Locke manifestly did when he characterised ‘perception, or thinking’ as one of the two main actions of the mind (EHU II.vi.2) and explained that a man can get clear on what this action is simply ‘by reflecting on what he does himself, when he sees, hears, feels, etc. or thinks’ (EHU II.ix.2)— treating thinking as one kind of perception along with seeing and the rest.

Many philosophers influenced by Ryle or Wittgenstein took the classical conception of the mind to rest on the category mistake of placing minds, thoughts and ideas into the same category as physical objects, and put this mistake down to an alleged propensity to take nouns to stand for such objects. However, there is evidence that we do not have such a general propensity. Many philosophers have found it intuitively compelling to credit the nouns “mind”, “thought” or “idea” with a physical or quasi-physical denotation. But very few have found this compelling for nouns like “debt” or “bank account” (see Chapter 2.3). We have now won through to an arguably better-justified hypothesis: We can explain the specific propensity to assimilate ideas to physical objects and minds to physical spaces as a consequence of the propensity to excessively assimilate intellectual to perceptual activities and achievements and place cognition into the same category as sense-perception. This category mistake has thinkers posit objects of inner perception which we perceive in us when we think, in something like the way in which we perceive physical objects when we use our eyes, and thus has them assimilate from the start the ‘ideas’ and ‘minds’ they have posited in us to such objects and their spatial environment.

The findings we have derived from cognitive linguistics and psychology let us understand why even the most competent thinkers may make this fundamental category mistake: Metaphorical extension has forged a structural analogy between visual search and intellectual efforts, and under conditions which frequently obtain in philosophical reflection, human beings are prone to unwittingly fall back on simple concrete models that are structurally analogous to the targets they are thinking about, and to spontaneously project properties from the former onto the latter.





6. CONCLUSION AND OUTLOOK: UNSOUND INTUITIONS




Let’s take stock. We have considered intuitions which shaped an influential conception of the mind. We have reconstructed how the overly literal application of conceptual metaphors, in non-intentional analogical reasoning, could generate those philosophical intuitions, which were typically accepted without further argument (Section 3). We then refuted the two most plausible alternative explanations: Those intuitions are not pieces of common sense, as implicit in ordinary language, early modern or current, which we could put down to the exercise of linguistic competencies (Section 4). Nor can the intuitions be obtained through natural and legitimate extension of the conceptual metaphors that motivate our ordinary ‘mind-talk’ (Section 5). Once we have excluded these and other explanations (which we have dismissed in passing), the explanation we have advanced emerges as the best available explanation, and inference to the best explanation allows us to conclude that whenever thinkers had the philosophical intuitions reconstructed prior to or in absence of explicit argument, these intuitions were generated by the pictorial process we hypothesised, viz. through the misapplication of conceptual metaphors in non-intentional analogical inferences.

We can further confirm this hypothesis, and show that the best available explanation is in fact good, by showing that the explanatory strategy on which it rests is generally successful: by showing that the pictorial process we have posited can explain not only the present but also a host of further philosophical intuitions and otherwise inexplicable gaps in philosophers’ arguments. Indeed, the strategy allows us to explain these intuitions and gaps as the results of cognitive phenomena (like non-intentional analogical reasoning) and psychological effects (like belief-bias effects) for which there is independent, experimental, evidence. In the next chapter, we shall show that the process we have identified can account for a whole cluster of related intuitions which are jointly constitutive of the lastingly influential conception of the mind as a private and transparent realm of inner perception which houses thoughts, beliefs and memories, as well as feelings and sensations of all kinds. In the third chapter, we will then show that a pictorial process involving a different philosophical picture can account for another cluster of philosophical intuitions and moves, including wild doctrines and patently incomplete arguments that have baffled commentators and have, so far, defied explanation.

The new explanatory strategy allows us to overcome a difficulty arising from the Freudian framework which was all Wittgenstein had at his disposal to develop the ideas about pictures and analogies from which we started. To simplify slightly, that framework does not contain sufficient resources to decide between mutually incompatible attributions of unconscious beliefs and desires to a subject. This difficulty is mitigated by the requirement that the patient finally come to acknowledge the attributions as correct. Wittgenstein transfers this idea to the unconscious analogical reasoning he took to be at the bottom of much philosophical perplexity:

One of the most important tasks is to express all false thought processes so true to character that the reader says, “Yes, that’s exactly the way I meant it”. To make a tracing of the physiognomy of every error.



Indeed, we can only prove that someone made a mistake if he acknowledges this expression as the correct expression of his feeling.



For only if he acknowledges it as such, is it the correct expression. (Psychoanalysis.)



What the other person acknowledges is the analogy I’m presenting to him as the source of his thought. (BT 410)





But since the analogical reasoning at issue is ‘unconscious’ or, more accurately, non-intentional and involves the use of models the thinker is unaware of employing, the acknowledgement of the thinker is not a necessary, let alone a sufficient condition for the attribution of such reasoning to him to be correct. With the means at Wittgenstein’s disposal, we cannot satisfactorily justify the attribution of such unconscious thought processes to thinkers.

The present explanation of why certain thinkers accept certain claims in the absence of argument thus fills an explanatory gap. Philosophically more importantly, it also enables us to soberly assess our warrant for intuitive judgements which we tend to accept without argument. We have explicitly refuted two explanations that would render such acceptance of I1 and I2 justified. Common-sense beliefs are privileged in that we are entitled to maintain them without argument until they are challenged with good reasons. But we have found that I1 and I2 are not pieces of common sense (and noted in passing that they were, at the time, not pieces of received academic wisdom, to which the same point would have applied). We have also seen that I1 and I2 cannot be obtained through—entirely legitimate—extension of the conceptual metaphors that motivate our ordinary ‘mind-talk’, something a competent speaker might well do without explicitly advertising it. The intuitive judgements at issue are hence warranted only if the thinker is able to explicitly back them up with argument. As long as he does not bother to adduce any argument to support them, those intuitive judgements will be unwarranted.

While irrelevant for the issue of whether the attribution of a non-intentional cognitive process to him is correct, the thinker’s acknowledgement of this process makes him aware of the fact that his intuitions are unwarranted. This happens in two steps. First, explanations like the one of I1 and I2 given above make us realise that we mistook for truisms what are in fact substantive claims whose truth we may not simply take for granted. They make us realise that our acceptance of these intuitively compelling claims is warranted only to the extent to which we manage to support them with sound reasons. Second, and once we have realised the need to give reasons, we may acknowledge the picture-driven inferences reconstructed as the only reasons we are able to give (now that we have got clear on how we came to have those intuitions). Picture-driven inferences are unsound by the lights of the very thinker who makes them: They rely on an assumption the thinker knows to be false; and since they project a feature of the model onto a target known not to share it, their (immediate) conclusion is guaranteed not to be true (but either false or meaningless). Once we have acknowledged them as the sole reason we are able to adduce to support those intuitions (which we previously accepted without giving any reasons), we thus come to see that we lack all warrant for accepting them.

At a more general level, the proposed explanation of intuitions I1 and I2 shows us that we need to be wary of the intuitions we have when doing philosophy. Such intuitions are frequently accorded some sort of evidentiary value; the intuition that p is often taken to support the assumption that p. Indeed, intuitions have traditionally been regarded as immediate insights, gained by the exercise of certain competencies, cognitive or linguistic. This has made conflicts between different people’s intuitions hard to adjudicate in a reasoned manner—it then seems that if you disagree with me, you or I are simply incompetent. Philosophers in the analytic tradition have frequently focussed on specific intuitions about particular cases, situations or scenarios, elicited by presenting these cases or situations and asking whether such-and-such is the right thing to say in or about them. It is plausible to put such intuitions down to the exercise of cognitive or linguistic intuitions. We have studied less specific and more fundamental intuitions. We could explain them as the result of non-intentional inferences. Disagreements about such intuitions, therefore, do not reduce us to table-thumping and mutual accusations of incompetence: The inferences that generate them can be assessed for soundness. In this chapter, we have identified a process of non-intentional inference which is systematically unsound: a process which first has us systematically assimilate targets to models of conceptual metaphors in ways in which we know the two to differ, and then has us presuppose the wild but intuitively compelling conclusions in further reasoning about the topic. We thus find that philosophical intuitions need not result from the exercise of a competence; they may be due to cognitive distortions, i.e., to recurrent, characteristic and systematic mistakes in non-intentional reasoning. They deserve not our blind trust but a second and third look—which, in many cases, will reveal that we lack all warrant for what we find too compelling to merit argument. Both at a more specific and at a more general level, the psychology of philosophy can thus lead us to epistemological conclusions.

In the following chapters, we will show that the process of non-intentional reasoning we have identified, viz. the ‘pictorial process’, can account for whole clusters of related intuitions. And we will see why such explanations are philosophically rewarding: The clash of the intuitions explained with other convictions gives rise to classical mind-body problems which still worry us today—and which we can overcome by coming to see through the intuitions that raise the problems.





2 Through Pictures to Problems


Minds and Bodies




For better or worse, intuitions are a pillar of philosophy. In particular thinkers in the tradition of twentieth-century analytic philosophy frequently base claims on specific intuitions about what to say in or about specific cases, situations or scenarios presented for the purpose. Such intuitions can be largely put down to the exercise of recognitional, classificatory or linguistic skills and have often been used to support philosophical claims about concepts like ‘knowledge’, which seem to play a central role in many familiar philosophical problems. In the previous chapter, we have embarked on the study of philosophically relevant intuitions of a different and perhaps more influential kind: We have started to examine intuitive judgements which are not about specific cases or situations and which have not been used to support conceptual claims but rather led philosophers to advance metaphysical or other ‘substantive’ claims without much, if any, argument. We examined the intuitively compelling claims that—I1—there are in us spaces and organs of perception (‘minds’), in and with which we perceive things (‘ideas’), namely—I2—whenever we think of or about something. We concluded that their champions advance and accept these claims because they are under the spell of philosophical pictures: because they excessively assimilate the targets to the models of conceptual metaphors, in non-intentional analogical inferences which they make unwittingly but systematically. In this chapter, we will further develop and defend this hypothesis and show that it is the key to the resolution of a wide range of philosophical problems.

We can develop and defend the hypothesis that particular intuitions are due to picture-driven reasoning by showing, first, that this hypothesis can account for them while alternative explanations cannot and, second, that it is part of a successful explanatory strategy, which can also explain further philosophical intuitions which are about the same topic or are had by the same thinker. In this chapter, we will show that the pictorial process we have made out can account also for further intuitions which, together with the two already discussed, make up what we can safely call the ‘classical conception of the mind’ which dominated early modern philosophy and still passes for common sense today. In the next chapter, we will show that the same process can also account for another cluster of once popular and highly influential intuitions, namely about secondary qualities and sense-perception as an operation of the minds thus posited in us. In the two following chapters, we will then turn from clusters of widely shared intuitions to the intuitions of a particular thinker, from ‘thematically related’ to ‘personally related’ intuitions, and show that the phenomenon of picture-driven reasoning can account for all the core intuitions that led Berkeley from generally popular views about sense-perception to his metaphysical idealism, and which have puzzled modern commentators. In the process, we will also widen the scope of our investigation: We will see that philosophical pictures shape not only philosophers’ intuitions but also their— our—problems, doctrines, arguments, theories and explanations.

We will now follow up how non-intentional reasoning in the grip of philosophical pictures led to a whole cluster of related intuitions, namely the intuitions that shaped the classical conception of the mind as a private (Section 1) and transparent realm housing our thoughts, beliefs, memories and their ilk (Section 2). By getting clear on their genesis, we can rationally assess such picture-driven intuitions. The clash of these unsound intuitions with further convictions systematically gives rise to ill-motivated but maddening philosophical problems, including mind-body problems which still worry us today (Section 3). We can resolve—though, alas, not completely overcome—such problems by showing that they are ill-motivated: by getting clear on our motivation for raising them and showing that this motivation is unsound (Section 4). Such ‘diagnostic analysis’ is facilitated by the systematic study of how philosophical pictures shape philosophical reflection (Section 5).





1. COMPREHENSIVE ANALOGICAL REASONING:

THE PRIVACY OF MINDS




The philosophical notion of a non-physical ‘inner’ space of perception involved in all kinds of cognition swiftly evolved into the classical conception of the mind as a (i) private and (ii) transparent realm of inner perception that (iii) houses thoughts, beliefs and memories, along with sensations of any kind and description. Each of these three constituent claims struck those who made them as intuitively compelling, in the absence of argument. Also these three intuitions can be explained by the ‘pictorial process’ we have seen to account for the most fundamental intuitions that have us posit minds in us: by picture-driven inferences leading to paradoxical conclusions which may get subjected to spontaneous reinterpretation (sublimation) and influence our further reasoning through various belief-bias effects.

When we engage in non-intentional analogical reasoning, we do so in a comprehensive rather than selective fashion: When we spontaneously project certain features of the source-domain onto the target-domain, we unwittingly but systematically presuppose in our reasoning about the former that its elements have properties or features which we actually know only the models in the source-domain possess. Once thinkers have unwittingly projected fundamental features from the source-domain of sense-perception and its mostly physical objects onto the target domain of cognition and everything else conceived of as an operation of the mind, they unwittingly seize on ever more familiar facts about the source domain in reasoning about the target domain, in whole series of non-intentional analogical inferences. These picture-driven inferences lead to several related conclusions which we find intuitively compelling in philosophical reflection. Some of these conclusions we maintain only upon sublimation; all of them are prone to be presupposed in further reasoning and spontaneous interpretation of ordinary talk and familiar facts. The tri-partite pictorial process we have outlined leads to whole clusters of related philosophical intuitions, including the three that shape the classical conception of the mind.

These three intuitions can be obtained through non-intentional inferences in which the mind that has been posited in us is assimilated to a literal, physical, space of, literally, sense-perception, namely in three fundamental respects in which the explicit (sublimating) explanations we have considered take the two to differ:

To be ‘perceived’ in the mind, as anywhere else, objects have to be present and suitably located relative to the pertinent sense-organs.

In the mind, as in all physical spaces, things ‘exist’ in the sense that they occupy a spatial position or place in it.

The mind, as any physical space, has itself a physical location: If it is ‘in’ us, it is located in our body.





Once these three fundamental features have been projected, against better knowledge, from the source–domain of sense-perception of objects in physical environments, onto the target–domain of cognition, conceived of as an operation of the mind, ever more familiar facts about the source-domain are recruited in further reasoning which presupposes (I) to (III) above.

Thus the following line of thought takes us from our first two intuitions—I2 and I1 above (Chapter 1.4)—to the intuition that the mind is a private realm (source-domain facts represented in italics):

Reasoning ‘Private’



(1) I perceive things in my mind (when thinking about, understanding, remembering something, etc.). (2) If a thing is perceived somewhere it is there (at that place). Hence [with I, from 1 & 2]: (3) The things I perceive in my mind are in my mind. (4) But one and the same thing can only be at one place at a time.
