Hence [with II, from 3 & 4]: (5) The things I perceive in my mind cannot at the same time exist outside my mind. (6) My mind is inside me. Hence [with III, from 5 & 6]: (7) The things I perceive in my mind can exist only inside me and nowhere else. (8) But others cannot look inside me, or perceive the things inside me. Hence [from 7 & 8]: (9) Others cannot perceive the things I perceive in my mind.





Negative sublimation (Chapter 1.3) of the penultimate conclusion (7) elevates the final conclusion (9) to the rank of metaphysical necessity:

The things that I perceive in my mind exist only inside me, but not literally in my body. But the only things others could possibly perceive inside me are the things in my body (cut open, x-rayed, etc.). Hence:





(I3) Others cannot possibly perceive the things I perceive in my mind.

All the inferences involved are unsound by the lights of thinkers who go along with the sublimating explanations we have considered and the immaterialist majority-view of the mind to which they give rise: The presuppositions (I) to (III) are all inconsistent with the definition of the mind as a figuratively ‘inner’ space without physical extension or location. This inconsistency reflects the same fundamental defect of non-intentional analogical reasoning in philosophy that materialised at an earlier stage in the shape of leaps of thought presupposing the likes of (R) and (K): the excessive assimilation of targets to simple concrete models, against thinkers’ better knowledge.

Picture-driven leaps may be followed by sublimation. Ever more analogical inferences take thinkers to further conclusions which are at odds either with common sense or with the first sublimating explanations given, such as the explanation that the mind is an inner space without physical extension or location. The latter, for instance, is incompatible with the conclusion of the first inference above, that the things perceived in the mind exist at a suitable location, namely at some place in the mind. But of course thinkers who found it intuitive to posit minds in which we perceive things we do not perceive with our five senses found it equally intuitive to add that these things are in the mind. This occasioned another round of sublimating explanations. Thus, Berkeley explains that the conclusion does not go beyond the premise:

[W]hen I speak of objects as existing in the mind . . . I would not be understood in the gross literal sense, as when bodies are said to exist in a place . . . My meaning is only that the mind comprehends or perceives them. (3D 250)





Locke probably relies on this kind of explanation in the passage we used to introduce the ‘new’ early modern conception of the mind, when he explains: ‘I mean that simple appearance, which the mind has in its view, or perceives in itself, when that idea is said to be in it’ (EHU p. 13) The idea ‘is said to be in the mind’ when it is perceived in the mind; the cautious formulation suggests that Locke here wants to treat this as a façon de parler: An idea can be said ‘to be in the mind’ just in case it is perceived there.

The notion that things are perceived in minds was introduced through sublimation of conclusions—like C1 above (Chapter 1.3)—that assimilate intellectual activities and achievements to visual or other sense-perception. It is therefore natural to explain the new talk of things perceived in the mind by reference to those familiar activities and achievements: That something exists in the mind means that it is perceived in the mind, and that it is perceived in the mind means that the subject understands it, thinks of it, remembers it, etc. Thus Berkeley, in the passage just quoted, uses the phrase “comprehends or perceives” as if the two, understanding and perceiving, came down to pretty much the same, and Locke explains and reasons:

[I]f these words (to be in the understanding) have any propriety, they signify to be understood. So that, to be in the understanding, and not to be understood; to be in the mind, and, never to be perceived, is all one, as to say, any thing is, and is not, in the mind or understanding. (EHU I.ii.5)





This explanation renders “x is in the understanding” synonymous with “x is understood” and suggests (though no more) that we regard “x is in the mind” and “x is thought of” as equally synonymous. These moves turn the phrases “in the understanding” and “in the mind” into integral parts of metaphorically used expressions without semantically relevant inner structure (Chapter 1.4). They thus deprive these phrases of all implications, including the presently problematic spatial implications.

The first kind of sublimating explanation, negative sublimation, failed to provide the expressions explained with any determinate content: For example, we were merely told that the mind is a space, but not in the literal physical sense, and that things are perceived in it, but not with any bodily organ of sense and not literally (physically) ‘in’ it either. The present explanations, by contrast, do provide talk of ‘inner perception’ with determinate meaning and content. But such ‘positive sublimation’ suffers from another flaw: Its champions ignore the explanations almost the moment they have given or invoked them. For example, Locke explains—in line with the present explanations—that when he speaks of ‘ideas’ (perceived in the mind), he simply means whatever we can think or reason about, or understand or remember (EHU I.i.8, quoted in the next section); in the very next sentence he then suggests that everybody is conscious of the things in his own mind, while having to infer the presence of ideas in others’ minds from their words and deeds—where he clearly thinks not of the public and frequently material objects or events we think of or remember but of the contents of inner spaces of perception secluded from others’ view (EHU I.i.8, quoted in Chapter 1.3). Indeed, already in giving the present sublimating explanation, Locke seems to waver in his resolve to treat talk of things ‘being in’ an inner space of perception as purely metaphorical: After explaining that ‘to be in the understanding’ simply is ‘to be understood’, he implicitly equates ‘being in the mind’ not with ‘being thought of’ in some suitably generic sense, but with ‘being perceived’.

Our account lets us understand why this is so. The present glosses belabour the symptoms of a fundamental cognitive process, a process of non-intentional reasoning to which we are prone in abstract reflection: In such reflection we are prone to excessively assimilate targets to their metaphorical models, against our better knowledge. We find it intuitively compelling to assimilate cognition to perception also in elementary ways in which we know the two to differ. This results in a tension between our intuitions and our knowledge. Explanations that try to resolve this tension by honouring our intuitions have to remain nebulous if they are not to be in open conflict with our knowledge. Explanations that try to resolve this tension by respecting our knowledge, by contrast, are simply ignored in further intuitive reasoning. No mere redefinition of terms allows us to steer a clear course between the Scylla of vacuity and the Charybdis of patent falsity.





2. FURTHER INTUITIONS:

THE TRANSPARENCY OF MINDS




Once his sublimating explanations have become part of a thinker’s standard repertoire, they may be seized on in non-intentional reasoning which also involves analogical inferences that tacitly rely on presuppositions at odds with those explicit explanations. A case in point is a line of thought that leads to the familiar idea that the mind is a transparent realm of whose dwellers the subject cannot fail to be aware (if he bothers to pay attention). Like the reasoning that led to the idea of the mind as a private realm, it recruits familiar facts about the source–domain of sense-perception (again in italics, below). In addition, it employs the two sublimating explanations we have just considered (underlined below):

Reasoning ‘Transparent’



(1) A thing is in my mind if and only if I perceive it in my mind. (2) When I perceive a thing somewhere, I know that it is there (at that place). Hence [from 1 & 2]: (3) When a thing is in my mind, I know that it is there, in my mind. (4) That a thing ‘is in the mind’ simply means that it is being thought of, understood or known, or remembered, etc. Hence [from 3 & 4]: (5) When I think of, understand or know, or remember something, I know that I think of it, know or understand it, remember it, etc., as the case may be.





This is the seminal version of the influential assumption that if a subject S believes (knows, remembers, etc.) that p, then S knows that S believes (knows, remembers, etc.) that p. The seminal version of this claim is maintained by Locke: ‘He that contemplates the operations of the mind cannot but have plain and clear ideas of them’ (EHU I.i.7). Since knowledge implies factuality—S can only be said to know that such-and-such if it is the case that such-and-such—the converse is trivial. We thus obtain an even stronger conclusion:



(I4) We know that we have a certain thought or belief when we have it, and vice versa.



This claim has been frequently maintained, often as a necessary a priori truth. Indeed, in this shape the present claim is the foundation on which some current theories of self-knowledge are built.1

Reasoning Transparent derives its conclusion from three premises. Two of these, viz. (1) and (4), are true by linguistic legislation, namely sublimating explanations of meaning. These assumptions are necessarily true and those that give them know a priori that they are true. The relevant instance of the remaining premise—(2)—can then easily be regarded as of the same sort: When I perceive a thing in my mind, I know that it is in my mind. ‘S perceives x’ carries the implication that S recognises x. And when we recognise an idea (thought, memory, etc.) as such, mastery of the concept of ‘idea’ (‘thought’, ‘memory’, etc.) would seem enough to ensure that we conclude that the thing we perceive is in our mind rather than in our environment. A conclusion entailed by necessary a priori assumptions is itself a necessary a priori truth. To sum up, the interplay of picture-driven inferences and sublimating explanations could explain how competent thinkers, first, come to have the intuition I4 and, second, immediately regard it as a necessary a priori truth.

Finally, such analogical reasoning can determine how we populate the inner realm of perception we may thus come to regard as private and transparent. When we talk of the mind today, we spontaneously populate it with thoughts, beliefs and memories. But this is not how early modern thinkers first populated it. Locke, for instance, introduces ideas, subsequently defined as ‘whatever the mind perceives in itself’ (EHU II.viii.8) by explaining:

[T]he word idea . . . serves to stand for whatsoever is the object of the understanding when a man thinks . . . [namely for] whatever it is, which the mind can be employed about in thinking. (EHU I.i.8)





In other words, the things we perceive in the mind, with the mind, are the things we think about, know, understand and remember. The present account lets us understand how one can arrive at this view: The conceptual metaphors we have reconstructed conceptualise thinking, knowing, understanding and remembering something as looking at or seeing that very thing, namely the event, object, person, fact, question or solution we think about, understand or remember. When we apply these metaphors too literally in the most straightforward fashion, we will presuppose, e.g., that to think about or ‘consider’ something is to perceive that very thing literally at some place, literally with an organ of sense, and will find it intuitively compelling to conclude that we perceive the things we think about in the mind, with the mind.

The things we think about are of course usually public and frequently physical in nature: You and I may think about the very same event, fact or problem, and we may both think about or remember the same physical object or person in flesh and blood. The sublimating explanations we have considered a moment ago let us say that these things are perceived, and exist, in our minds: According to these explanations, that a problem is in my understanding simply means that I have understood it, and that you are in my mind simply means that I think of you. So we may maintain without obvious problem that our minds contain the things we think of, understand or remember.

But non-intentional analogical reasoning may drive us to find it incredible that physical objects or people should dwell there. For one thing, they stand or walk around in our environment. And two non-intentional analogical inferences take us from our very first intuitions to the conclusion—(5) in ‘Reasoning Private’ above—that the things I perceive in my mind cannot at the same time exist outside my mind. The excessive assimilation of cognition to visual perception, in non-intentional analogical reasoning, thus takes us first to the conclusion that to think of people or other objects of thought is to perceive them in the mind, where they are when we perceive them with the mind, and then leads us to the conclusion that our mind cannot contain these ‘external’ objects but only private ideas.

Facing this apparent conflict, Locke and Berkeley simply leaped to the conclusion jointly implied by the two horns of the apparent dilemma: When our minds contain the things we know, reason or think about, but contain only ideas formed in the mind, then those objects of thought and knowledge are our own ideas:

Since the mind, in all its thoughts and reasoning, has no other immediate object but its own [!] ideas, which it alone does or can contemplate, it is evident that our knowledge is only conversant about them. (EHU IV.i.1)





Equally without argument, Berkeley asserts in the opening sentence of his Principles, designed to proceed from an uncontroversial starting-point, that ‘it is evident to any one who takes a survey of the objects of human knowledge, that they are . . . ideas’ formed in various ways in our minds (PHK 1). The overly literal application of perceptual metaphors can account for this perplexing intuition, which conflates ideas with their intentional objects, i.e., with the objects of thought and knowledge which they are ideas of.

An alternative response to the apparent conflict populates the mind with thoughts, beliefs and memories. It is a truism that when we think of or about something, we have or think thoughts about it. This provides us with two candidates for the role of object of perception in the mind: the thought and the thing it is about. Picture-driven leaps of thought and their sublimation may convince us that to think, or have some thought, about something is to perceive some thing in the mind, with the mind. Further non-intentional analogical reasoning—such as the first steps of ‘Reasoning Private’—may have us take for granted that the mind cannot contain the kinds of ‘external’ things that most thoughts are about. We are then left with only one candidate for the position of ‘object in the mind’: the thoughts about those external things. Analogous reasoning applies, mutatis mutandis, to knowing and remembering, and may lead us to regard beliefs and memories as things perceived in the mind. While the metaphorical idioms of ordinary English have us ‘keep in mind’ people, physical objects and public events, facts and dates, as well as things to be done, overly literal application of the pertinent conceptual metaphors in non-intentional analogical reasoning may have us, first, postulate quite different, essentially inner and nonphysical, minds in us and, second, populate them with thoughts, beliefs and memories of or about those ‘external’ things. Since thoughts, beliefs and memories are all of, or about, these things, we can then resolve the apparent conflict by saying, for instance, that to think of somebody (or ‘bear him in mind’) is to perceive in the mind a thought of that person.

This yields another positive sublimation, to which thinkers may resort in view of the conflict between the picture-driven conclusion that we perceive in the mind the things we think about and the equally picture-driven conclusion that the mind cannot contain such ‘external’ objects: It tells us that a thing we think about is perceived in the mind, in the sense that we perceive in the mind a thought of that thing. To sum up, a plethora of picture-driven leaps and the sublimation of some of their conclusions generate the intuitions that are jointly constitutive of the classical conception of the mind as a non-physical, private and transparent space of inner perception which houses thoughts and their ilk.





3. ILL-MOTIVATED PROBLEMS:

PUZZLES ABOUT MIND AND WORLD




Non-intentional analogical reasoning of the kind we have characterised as ‘picture-driven’ systematically generates whole clusters of philosophical intuitions. These intuitions are bound to be unsound: The non-intentional analogical inferences at their root rely on assumptions—like R or K (Chapter 1.2), or I to III (above in Section 1)—which the subject rejects and are therefore unsound by the subject’s own lights; their spontaneous reinterpretation saves some conclusions from obvious falsity but gives thinkers no right to maintain what they find intuitively compelling. Biased interpretation and other belief bias effects may then have thinkers espouse and maintain these intuitions in the continued absence of warrant. When under the spell of philosophical pictures, thinkers are prone to fall prey to whole clusters of unsound intuitions.

In picture-driven reasoning we systematically assimilate a target-domain to a source-domain, in respects in which we know the two to be different. Such reasoning is guaranteed to take us to conclusions (like C1 in Chapter 1.3) that are at odds with familiar facts and common-sense convictions (like T1 in Chapter 1.3), and may take us to conclusions which are inconsistent among themselves (like the parties to the conflict we encountered at the end of the previous section). We have seen how such conflicts are spontaneously glossed over by sublimating explanations. But as picture-driven reasoning takes us to ever more paradoxical or mutually inconsistent conclusions we find intuitively compelling, conflicts multiply beyond anyone’s ability to sweep them under the carpet through spontaneous reinterpretation. Where spontaneous responses no longer hide them from others and ourselves, such conflicts between philosophical intuitions and other convictions or intuitions become salient, and have us frame philosophical problems of a distinctive kind: Frequently eager to maintain both parties to such a dispute, we wonder how to reconcile what appears to be at odds. When there appears to be a ‘tension’ between a claim q they find intuitively compelling in philosophical reflection and some conviction p they are unable or unwilling to give up, philosophers tend to formulate ‘reconciliation problems’ by questions of the form:





How is it possible that p [namely, given that it is the case that q]?



Philosophers speak of a ‘tension’ when they take p and q to jointly entail a contradiction or otherwise unacceptable conclusion and thus request an answer that ‘reconciles’ p and q, namely shows against first appearances that they do not entail the dreaded contradiction or consequence.

A case in point is the ‘problem of other minds’ that is raised by the intuition that the mind is a private realm. The picture-driven ‘Reasoning Private’ takes us to the intuitively compelling conclusion—I3—that others cannot possibly perceive the things I perceive in my mind (Section 1). The sublimating explanations we have just analysed (Section 2) have us regard thoughts, beliefs and memories, etc., as things which subjects perceive in their minds—and, thereby, as things we get to know about through perception. We may then take our previous conclusion to entail that others cannot possibly get to know what thoughts, beliefs and memories I have, and wonder:

How is it possible that we often know what others think, believe or remember [namely, given that we cannot possibly perceive the things in their minds]?





The problem then unfolds as all the easy answers to this question are seen to fail. Similarly, the intuition generated by the next line of non-intentional analogical reasoning (‘Reasoning Transparent’), that ‘he that contemplates the operations of the mind cannot but have plain and clear ideas of them’ (EHU I.i.7), appears to be inconsistent with phenomena of self-deception and generates problems also whenever philosophers find it compelling to posit operations of the mind of which subjects manifestly fail to be aware (as Locke, e.g., does in EHU II.ix.8–9).

Reconciliation problems come in different shapes; they need not be expressed by questions of the above form. A case in point is the ‘ontological mind-body problem’, the most fundamental reconciliation problem generated by the intuitions we have analysed. Its seminal version is posed by the question: What kind of things are the things in the mind (thoughts and beliefs, memories and sensations, etc.)? In the words of a popular textbook: ‘What is the real nature of mental states and processes? In what medium do they take place, and how are they related to the physical world?’2 To understand what the problem is, we need to understand why we ask this question.

Questions of the form ‘What kind of things are Fs?’ are frequently asked in the course of exercises in ontological taxonomy. But this is clearly not so in the present case: Philosophers asking the present questions typically have no quarrel with the classification of beliefs or pains as ‘mental’. Nor do they wonder about whether beliefs are really states or whether having a pain is a process or an event. Which of several available logical or ontological categories the various ‘mental things’ fall into is not at issue.

When philosophers try to render the present question compelling to freshmen or a general audience, they typically contrast the things in the mind with the physical objects in the world:3 Beliefs and desires, thoughts and memories, pains and itches, hopes and expectations may be a rather motley crew. But they all, so the story goes, share some basic properties. They have a temporal extension and location (are dateable): I held my belief until you corrected my mistake; I entertained the thought for a few minutes while shaving, but then turned to other things; the stomach pain set in half an hour after dinner and lasted until I relieved myself, etc. But they have no extension or location in physical space: The pain in my stomach does not compete for space with the foodstuff being digested; the thought going through my head is not moving through it in the same sense as the blood pulsing through my brain, etc. A few further features are commonly added to obtain some such list of differences between mental and physical things as this:

Physical things Mental things

• have mass and physical extension and location • have neither mass nor physical extension or location

• fall under the laws of physics • do not fall under physical laws

• are public or ‘objective’ • are private or ‘subjective’



This contrast tends to make people feel uneasy: Thoughts and their ilk appear to be strangely non-embodied things existing in an ethereal medium. It is because we feel uneasy about this apparent nature that we ask: What is the real nature of mental things? In what medium do they exist?

This peculiar unease tends to be absent in analogous cases. Few people feel uneasy when reminded that debts and bank accounts have no mass or physical extension: that debts have no spatial but only a temporal extension (exist from the time they have been incurred until they have been repaid or remitted) and do not fall under the laws of physics (but obey only those of the Civil Code). While analogous to the above juxtaposition of physical and mental things, this suggestive formulation does not conjure up the disquieting idea of strangely non-embodied bank-notes due for return to ethereal vaults. This difference is, I submit, due to the fact that our abstract reflection about thoughts and sensations, in contrast with our reflection on debts and bank accounts, is driven by perceptual pictures. I.e.: Our language realises conceptual metaphors which take visual and tactile perception, respectively, as source–domain, and cognition and sensation, respectively, as targets. And in philosophical reflection we are prone to unwittingly but systematically assimilate these target- to those source-domains.

This is particularly straightforward in the case of sensations, to which we systematically extend terms from the source-domain of tactile perception: We say, in a well-established sense of these terms which we recognise as metaphorical the moment we are apprised of the process of extension, that we ‘feel’ sensations we have and are ‘numb’ when unable to ‘feel’ them, and we describe them with adjectives like “throbbing”, “piercing” or “sharp” which literally apply to objects we can literally feel with our fingers (unless they have gone literally numb from cold). We say we ‘feel’ pain ‘in’ the parts of our body that hurt, and the pain is said to ‘be in’ that part. But—of course!—to feel a pain in the stomach is not to perceive anything that is literally located in our body; a pain that can be said to ‘be in’ our stomach does not sit there between body-tissue and half-digested food. So, we conclude, it must be perceived in that non-physical inner space of perception, the mind, where it does not have to compete for space with physical things and material stuffs. While intuitively compelling, this inference is curious: In our familiar metaphorical idiom, there is no implication that a sensation ‘felt in’ the head or stomach is literally located in these body-parts—or anywhere else, for that matter. That a pain is not literally in our body hence does not imply that it must be located somewhere else. When, even so, we find it intuitively compelling to infer that since pains are not literally located in bodies they must be perceived in minds, rather than in the heads or stomachs in which they are commonly said to be ‘felt’ and ‘be’, we simply presuppose that—S—to feel a sensation is to perceive something that is located at some place not merely in that well-established metaphorical sense. Where competent speakers do this against their better knowledge, we are dealing with what we called a ‘picture-driven inference’, driven by what we may call the ‘perceptual picture of sensation’.

Above, we discussed the more involved cases of thought and memory, where picture-driven leaps place the objects of thought and memory into our bodies, from which negative sublimation may move them into immaterial minds as alternative spaces of perception. The merger of the phenomenal space of sensation with the perceptual space occupied by objects of thought marks the birth of the modern concept of the mind, which combines the domains of sensation and cognition which the previous, Aristotelian, scheme of things had assigned to different ‘souls’, viz. to the sensitive and the rational psyche, respectively. The picture-driven leaps discussed are the beginning of inferential avalanches of non-intentional analogical inferences, which rely on tacit presuppositions—first on the likes of K, R and S, and then on I to III—which take us to the full-fledged ‘new’ conception of the mind as a non-physical, private and transparent realm of inner perception, in which we perceive thoughts, beliefs, memories and sensations.

The right-hand side of the table above can be read as an expression of this classical conception. It can also be read as an expression of genuine truisms: In ordinary language, we do not literally credit thoughts, beliefs, memories or sensations with a position or extension in physical space. One of the presuppositions driving the picture-driven reasoning at issue—viz. (II)—has it that in the mind, as in our environment, things ‘exist’ in the sense that they occupy a spatial position or place. This takes us from that conceptual truism to the conclusion that thoughts, beliefs, memories and sensations occupy a position in some non-physical or ethereal space. The table above thus is either read as a record of the conclusions of picture-driven leaps of thought, or prompts such a leap, when it leaves us with the disquieting impression that thoughts and their ilk are strangely non-embodied things existing in an ethereal medium.

This impression is disquieting because it would appear to be incompatible with a scientific world-view which allots only physical objects a place in the universe. Accordingly, those who ask the present questions typically request an account of the nature of mental things (states, processes, etc.), which reveals that they do not exist (obtain, occur, etc.) in any non-physical or ethereal medium. This account is to reconcile various claims apparently at odds with it with our scientific world-view. The ontological mind-body problem is a reconciliation problem. Different philosophers champion different claims apparently in need of being reconciled with that world-view and thus address different versions of the problem. The ‘full-fledged’ version of the problem asks us to reconcile that world-view with the classical conception of the mind. A ‘minimalist’ version of the problem ‘merely’ asks us to reconcile the fact that we have beliefs, thoughts and sensations with that world-view.

In these, and in various ‘intermediate’ versions of the problem, either the claims to be reconciled with a scientific world-view or the impression of a conflict between the two parties is due to non-intentional analogical reasoning in which we excessively assimilate various targets to the model of visual perception of physical objects. In the previous sections, we have reconstructed (some of) the non-intentional reasoning that leads to the classical conception of the mind, which is indeed incompatible with a scientific world-view (and with common sense, properly understood). The truism that people have thoughts and beliefs, memories and sensations, on the other hand, is brought into apparent conflict with this world-view only by the picture-driven line of thought which proceeds from the leap to the conclusion—cp. C1—that to have thoughts, etc., is to perceive things somewhere. But of course neither—cp. T1—around us, in our environment, nor—cp. T2—somewhere inside our body, but—cp. C2—somewhere figuratively ‘within’ us. But this merely papers over the conflict without resolving it, and we cannot help presupposing that—(I)—to be perceived in the mind, as anywhere else, objects of perception have to be present and suitably located relative to the pertinent sense-organs. They have to have a spatial location. But we do not credit thoughts and beliefs, memories and sensations with any physical extension. Hence it seems that we can only have a thought, belief or sensation if we perceive objects which have some spatial location—they are somewhere—but lack physical extension. This is inconsistent with a scientific world-view which grants only objects with physical extension a spatial location or place in the universe. Through such intuitive reasoning, the truism that people have beliefs, thoughts and sensations appears to come into conflict with our scientific world-view, and to lead us straight into a puzzling problem.

Reconciliation problems are ill-motivated in at least two cases: in case the proponent lacks warrant for at least one of the claims that appear to be at odds, and in case he lacks warrant for the assumption that there is a tension between them. Picture-driven reasoning is bound to be unsound. Where its conclusions are not subsequently supported by sound argument, thinkers espousing them will lack warrant for them. In the different versions of the ontological mind-body problem either one of the assumptions to be reconciled or the assumption of a tension between truisms and our scientific world-view are due to picture-driven reasoning—and are hardly ever backed up by further argument. In its several versions, this classical mind-body problem is ill-motivated.

To sum up, we have considered the phenomenon of picture-driven reasoning, i.e., non-intentional analogical reasoning in which we apply conceptual metaphors more literally than we know appropriate. We have seen that this phenomenon can account for fundamental and consequential philosophical intuitions: intuitions that have shaped the modern conception of the mind and raised mind-body problems that still worry us today. Philosophical pictures can generate fundamental philosophical intuitions which clash with other convictions (or each other) and thus raise philosophical problems. Alas, these intuitions are unsound and the maddening problems ill-motivated.





4. RESOLVING PICTURE-DRIVEN PROBLEMS




This in itself disappointing finding vindicates a new approach to maddening problems: Where we have reason to believe that thinkers raising philosophical problems are under the spell of philosophical pictures, it is reasonable to explore whether those problems are ill-motivated. In other words: We can try to resolve philosophical problems by getting clear on our motivation for raising the pertinent questions and searching the lines of thought that motivate them for moves unsound by our own lights. We can try to resolve such problems by identifying and refuting their philosophical motivation.

We often have a specifically philosophical motivation for raising philosophical questions. Take questions of the form ‘How is it possible that p?’ and an example we will encounter in the next chapter: ‘How is it possible that people can attribute colour, smell or taste to physical objects?’ Taken out of context, it is utterly unclear what philosophers asking the question want to know. Do they want to know how the senses of sight, smell and taste have evolved? Or how people come to master colour-concepts, etc.? Or something else entirely? While an evolutionary biologist or a psycholinguist might use that interrogative sentence to ask one of those two questions, we will see that philosophers came to raise it due to lines of thought which suggested to them that physical objects do not in fact have any such ‘secondary qualities’ as we take them to have, so that our ordinary attributions of colour and taste to such objects are bound to be false or even meaningless. Something similar is of course true of the question ‘How is it possible that we know (as we often do) what others think, believe or remember?’ This question is at least partially motivated by a line of thought—‘Reasoning Private’—that suggests to us that we cannot possibly perceive the things in others’ minds, and by the thought that at any rate the most straightforward way of getting to know about thoughts and beliefs is to perceive them. In these and similar cases, certain lines of thought which suggest that it cannot be the case that p, that there is some sort of difficulty about its being the case that p, add a fresh problem about the subject under investigation— the perception and attribution of colours, our knowledge of others’ beliefs, etc.—to the problems other disciplines raise about it.

Where the lines of thought that suggest the existence of some such impossibility or difficulty are unsound, the specifically philosophical motivation for raising those questions is unsound. In such cases, we can resolve the problem by identifying and removing this motivation, that is, by reconstructing and refuting the pertinent lines of thought and thus showing that there is no such difficulty as we believed to confront us. In this case there is no specifically philosophical problem about the fact that p, in addition to, say, problems of biology or psychology.

In a sense, this suggestion merely takes one step further what is a common approach to problems motivated by philosophical paradox. A philosophical paradox is defined as an argument from—often apparently trivial— premises the proponents initially accept, via steps they find compelling, to a conclusion apparently at odds with common sense (whence ‘paradox’, from the Greek “para doxa”, meaning ‘against—common or established— opinion’). Where the conclusion of a paradox appears to be at odds with the common-sense conviction that p, philosophers tend to wonder: ‘How is it possible that p?’ Thus the sceptical argument from dreaming purports to show that we cannot acquire any perceptual knowledge about our physical environment and thus motivates the question; ‘How is it possible that we can acquire knowledge about the things around us, through our senses?’ Philosophers who address this question are typically not interested in the neurological prerequisites that make it possible for us to acquire perceptual knowledge, or about what information-processing tasks need to be successfully mastered by that neurological hardware if we are to acquire perceptual knowledge—or any other such scientific question. Rather, they want to address the challenge posed by sceptical arguments which we find intuitively compelling while refusing to believe their conclusions. Accordingly, the most common philosophical response to the question is to analyse these paradoxical arguments in the hope of identifying mistakes in them.

Many paradoxical arguments divide into two parts. All paradoxes purport to show that there is some sort of difficulty about p by arguing for a conclusion q that appears to entail or imply that not-p. Many paradoxes also seek to show that this difficulty cannot be overcome (that q indeed entails or implies not-p). The sceptical argument from dreaming, for instance, has it that you can acquire perceptual knowledge only if you can exclude the hypothesis that you are merely dreaming what you take yourself to see or feel. (As you look at it now, you know there is a book in front of you only if you can rule out the hypothesis that you are merely dreaming that you are reading this book.) This first step of the argument purports to establish that there is a difficulty or challenge that has to be overcome, if we are to acquire perceptual knowledge: We have to rule out the sceptical hypothesis that we are currently dreaming. The second step then argues that this difficulty or challenge cannot be overcome: that we cannot refute the hypothesis that we are only dreaming, because in this case we would have exactly the same experience. (You may have exactly the same experience when you are reading this book and when you are dreaming.) This argument provides a specifically philosophical motivation for asking: ‘How is it possible that we can acquire knowledge about the things around us, through our senses (viz. even though this requires refuting the dream hypothesis)?’

By identifying defective assumptions or inferences in different parts of the argument, we can either solve or dissolve the problem it raises:

We solve a problem if we provide a true justified answer to the question articulating it.



We dissolve the problem if we refute our reasons for asking this question and show we are justified in dropping it as pointless (or worse).





We solve the problem when we accept the first half of the paradoxical argument and the challenge it poses but refute the second half of the argument that purports to show that this challenge cannot be met and devise a way of addressing this challenge (e.g. when we can explain how to refute the dreaming hypothesis by establishing the coherence of our present with past experience and further knowledge or beliefs). This is of course the most common approach to problems motivated by paradox. By contrast, we dissolve the problem when we dig deeper and expose defects already in the first part of the argument, which appears to establish the existence of a challenge or difficulty (here: the apparent need to refute the dreaming-hypothesis).

Our analysis of how picture-driven reasoning raises philosophical problems suggests that the common approach to problems motivated by paradox can be fruitfully applied (i) more generally, to more problems than are commonly linked to paradox, and (ii) more thoroughly, to dissolve rather than merely solve the problems addressed. Typically, philosophers apply the term “paradox” only to arguments that are explicitly stated and lead to conclusions which we find it natural to draw from the premises but do not endorse for a minute. (Again, the conclusions of sceptical arguments are a case in point—we do not believe for a minute that we cannot possibly get to know, say, whether there currently is a book in our hands.) But also tacit lines of thought leading to conclusions we explicitly endorse satisfy the definition of the term. Our analysis has revealed such paradoxes: lines of non-intentional reasoning we are not aware of engaging in, where often only either the premise or the conclusion is stated explicitly and the latter may be explicitly endorsed, in spite of its apparent conflict with common sense. Such tacit paradoxes can motivate philosophical questions no less than their more prominent cousins.

Where these lines of thought are picture-driven, we are prone to make exactly the kind of mistakes we typically hunt for in familiar explicit paradoxes. Since we typically reject the conclusions of the latter, we assume that at least some of their assumptions must be false or some of the inferences made from them invalid. Since we initially accept the explicit assumptions and countenance the inferences made from them, we suspect that we must have made some ‘hidden mistake’: that at some step we tacitly relied on an inference-principle that is invalid or on a presupposition that is false, that we inadvertently placed an unduly substantive reading on a merely otherwise plausible assumption, or placed an unduly paradoxical or even meaningless interpretation on an otherwise unexciting or straightforward conclusion. The pictorial process we have outlined involves mistakes of precisely these kinds: Picture-driven leaps of thought systematically have us rely on tacit assumptions we explicitly reject and leap to wild conclusions we have no right to maintain. Various kinds of sublimating glosses obscure this defect without remedying it: Positive sublimation of picture-driven conclusions has us maintain claims for which we have no warrant, and negative sublimation has us cleave to conclusions to which we cannot even give proper meaning and content. Finally, when we do make an effort to explicitly justify what we find compelling, belief bias has us unwittingly presuppose what we mean to establish, and frequently read our conclusions into common-sense statements with which we then conflate them. In the cases we have considered so far, and in all the cases we will encounter as we proceed, picture-driven inferences are involved already in creating the impression of a challenge or difficulty. Where picture-driven reasoning raises philosophical problems, it is likely to go wrong from the very start and it is reasonable to try to dissolve, rather than solve, these problems.

In the sense explained, a problem may have both a solution and a dissolution, namely in case the paradox raising it goes wrong at both stages: If the paradox goes wrong in arguing that a conclusion q is incompatible with a common-sense conviction p, exposing and correcting this mistake puts us into a position to explain how it is possible that p, given that q. But if the argument also goes wrong already in arguing for q, this explanation has no philosophical point and is irrelevant except as a jeu d’esprit. Dissolutions render solutions irrelevant. (Dissolutions trump solutions.)

A major strand of twentieth-century analytic philosophy, commonly associated with Vienna, Cambridge and Oxford, and the period 1930– 1960, developed in various different ways the idea that philosophical problems ought to be not solved but dissolved, namely by showing that the questions articulating them are meaningless. (Hence a problem can only be either solved or dissolved, but never both.) These attempts tended to rely on controversial criteria of sense and were in many cases unable to make stick the charge of meaninglessness. For example, few philosophers perplexed by sceptical paradoxes could be convinced that they are unable to attach proper meaning or content to the question of how it is possible for us to acquire knowledge about things around us, through our senses. For problems that are actually (if, perhaps, unbeknownst to us) raised by— tacit—philosophical paradoxes, the present considerations give radically different content to the idea that we can dissolve philosophical problems, which makes the idea more widely applicable. By reconstructing and refuting the paradoxical lines of thought that lead us to ask the question, we expose and remove what philosophical motivation we had for asking it. We thus show that it is right to drop the question as philosophically pointless. The focus here is on defects not of the question articulating the problem but of the (paradoxical) conclusion motivating the question. The defects to be exposed include both lack of meaning and lack of warrant: It is utterly pointless for us to wonder how a certain supposed difficulty can be overcome both when we cannot give any content to the claim that there is such a difficulty (no meaning to the sentence purporting to articulate such a claim), and when we have no right to believe that there is one.

Warrant is of course a subject-relative notion: One thinker may be entitled to a belief for which another has no warrant. Accordingly, we can only expose certain thinkers’ lack of warrant (and not lack of warrant, tout court), and exposure of someone’s lack of warrant for a paradoxical conclusion will only dissolve that problem for him and those who share his relevant beliefs (and ‘disbeliefs’). And of course one person or generation may come to acquire warrant for a belief for which another had none. But this does not make for any practical difference between lack of warrant and lack of meaning: The latter too can be remedied, namely by innovative linguistic legislation (which may be well motivated); also the bounds of sense are moving frontiers. We can therefore safely borrow the philosophically well-established verb to sum up the approach suggested: Let us try to dissolve philosophical problems by reconstructing and refuting the lines of thought that motivate them.

The proof of this pudding lies in the eating. There is no reason to believe that every philosophical question we find worrying or perplexing will turn out to have been motivated by an unsound line of thought. Whether the approach allows us to cope with a particular question, we can find out only by applying it to that question or problem. It is not a cure-all but a good bet. In particular, it is a good bet wherever we have reason to believe that we are under the spell of philosophical pictures: We have seen that picture-driven reasoning systematically raises philosophical problems and that such reasoning involves steps that are unsound by the thinker’s own lights, at different stages of the pictorial process we made out.

A philosophical picture tends to be at the root of several philosophical problems and questions: It drives thinkers to maintain several conclusions which appear to be at odds with common sense, science—or each other. When in the grip of a philosophical picture, we thus tend to raise a whole series of subtly related reconciliation problems. Where philosophical pictures are indeed at the root of a philosophical problem, this problem is therefore likely to be just one branch of many growing from eventually the same roots. We hence stand to kill several birds with one stone, if we proceed not from the branch-tip but the root-end: if we do not start out with a particular problem and work our way ‘down’ through the reasoning raising it to the picture or pictures driving that reasoning, but rather start out with a philosophical picture and follow up how this picture—often in conjunction with others—shapes the formulation of philosophical terminology, claims, problems and theories (which generate new problems in trying to solve others). This leads us to the main idea of a new approach. Let us call it the approach of ‘systematic diagnostic analysis’: To dissolve a set of philosophical problems, we

reconstruct how one or more pictorial processes (picture-driven inferences, sublimation and belief bias) lead thinkers to maintain paradoxical conclusions in the absence of warrant, and then

inquire into the motivation of several philosophical questions about the topic at hand, with particular attention to apparent tensions in our beliefs and intuitions apparently at odds with common sense or with each other.





Where we find that a question is motivated by lines of thought which are unsound by our own lights and people are unable to come up with new and sound reasons for asking it, we can show that we are justified in rejecting the question as pointless.





5. DEVELOPING A DIAGNOSIS




This approach builds on the kind of cognitive reconstruction of philosophical intuitions and argumentative gaps we have started to develop in the previous chapter. Before we proceed further, let us get clear on how to devise and develop such a reconstruction, proceeding from such evidence as is provided by actual philosophical texts. Three kinds of moves are involved in the pictorial process we have posited: non-intentional analogical inferences in which thinkers unwittingly but systematically assimilate the targets to the models of conceptual metaphors, in ways in which they know the two to differ; spontaneous reinterpretation of conclusions thus obtained, to paper over their clashes with conceptual truisms thinkers respect; and various forms of biased reasoning in which conclusions thus obtained are either presupposed in leaps of thought or channel our attention. Together they form evidential icebergs. Sublimating reinterpretation may yield explanations authors explicitly give. These explicitly stated explanations are, as it were, the icebergs’ very tips—the first we will see of them. Biased interpretations and reasoning are right above and below the water-line—we can discern much of them when we have a close look, namely through logical reconstruction of explicitly stated lines of thought that reveal leaps of thought with a certain kind of presuppositions. Non-intentional analogical reasoning is the bulk submerged in dark waters—we reconstruct it through inferences to the best explanation, in the process of accounting for recurrent presuppositions, larger gaps in arguments and apparently paradoxical intuitions espoused without any argument.

We only start looking for such icebergs when we are in sufficiently cold waters—when we have encountered potentially pertinent explananda, namely apparently unsupported intuitions (like I1 to I4, in this and the previous chapter) and argumentative gaps (like the gaping hole in the middle of the ‘argument from conceivability’ we shall encounter in the next chapter). The first things to look out for then are those we just located in the icebergs’ tips:

1. Negative sublimating explanations provide first evidence for picture-driven reasoning: The fact that an author finds it necessary to stress that something is not to be taken literally indicates that this does not go without saying, that there is, in him or his audience, a propensity to take a metaphor more seriously than the author knows appropriate. For example, you have probably never found it necessary to remind yourself or explain to others that when you say you are ‘keeping’ someone ‘in mind’ you are not saying that the poor man is being kept in some place (and unable to go home). By contrast, Berkeley did find it necessary to explain:

[W]hen I speak of objects as existing in the mind or imprinted on the senses; I would not be understood in the gross literal sense, as when bodies are said to exist in a place, or a seal to make an impression upon wax. (3D 250)



2. Positive sublimating glosses do not wear their character on their sleeves in quite this way. Consider:

[W]hen I speak of objects as existing in the mind . . . My meaning is only that the mind comprehends or perceives them. (3D 250)



[I]f these words (to be in the understanding) have any propriety, they signify to be understood. (EHU I.ii.5)





These explanations purport to coin new expressions with a figurative sense: “x exists in the mind” and “x is in the understanding”. Whether they are sublimating glosses, i.e. introduced to paper over conflicts between picture-driven conclusions and truisms thinkers cannot deny, is something we can find out by considering how these expressions are used, once given: by considering whether we can replace the new expressions by the more familiar terms declared synonymous with them (and, e.g. replace “x is in the mind of S” by “S understands x”) without turning a prima facie plausible claim into a palpable falsehood. We thus find, e.g., that right after defining ‘ideas’ as whatever we understand or think about, Locke ‘presume[s]’ it obvious ‘that there are such ideas in men’s minds; everyone is conscious of them in himself, and men’s words and actions will satisfy him that they are in others’ (EHU I.i.8)—which is not the least bit obvious if the things ‘in the mind’ or ‘in’ a part of it are the mansions we dreamily think of and the financial situation we have understood.

Sublimating glosses may figure prominently in the explanation of core terms of a philosophical terminology (such as the terminology of ‘minds’ and ‘ideas’ or of ‘substances’ and ‘accidents’, which we will encounter in the next chapter). Where this happens, the use of that terminology constitutes prima facie evidence for widespread adherence to philosophical pictures among the thinkers who employ those terms.

3. Explicit comparisons and classifications which place into the same category things we would ordinarily put into entirely different categories offer further prima facie evidence that thinkers who advance them without argument or further explanation are prone to excessively assimilate different things. Locke, for example, compares the understanding, defined as the ‘power of thinking’ (EHU II.vi.2), with an organ of perception: ‘The understanding, like the eye . . . makes us see, and perceive all other things’ (EHU I.i.1). Even more suggestive is the fact that he explicitly places the models and targets of perceptual metaphors into the same category: He contrasts the two actions of the mind ‘Perception, or Thinking’ and ‘Volition, or Willing’, implying that to think something is an act of perception, just as to want something is an act of volition (EHU II.vi.2) and writes ‘What perception is everyone will know better by reflecting on what he does himself, when he sees, hears, feels, etc. or thinks, than by any discourse of mine’ (EHU II.ix.2), thus treating thinking as a mode of perception alongside seeing, etc.

Where we have come across such prima facie evidence, it is reasonable to consider whether the thinker might be misapplying a conceptual metaphor in non-intentional analogical reasoning. The first task is to verify whether the things apparently assimilated to each other figure in the target- and source-domains of conceptual metaphors which are at least partially realised in the language, natural or technical, used by the thinker at issue. Locke assimilates to each other thinking and sense-perception, and ‘minds’ and bounded spaces. We thus need to verify whether the more concrete things (here: sense-perception and bounded spaces) figure in the source-domains of conceptual metaphors whose target-domains contain the less concrete counterparts (here: thinking and minds).

To get started, we need to identify a set of terms whose use got extended from the source to the target domain (say, from perception to various cognitive activities or achievements). On this basis, we have to define a mapping of elements from the source-domain onto elements of the target-domain (like the mapping ‘Intellectual Effort as Visual Search’ in Chapter 1.1), and to establish that this mapping preserves inferential (or other) relations between the elements involved in it. For detailed explanations of how to proceed from here to the proper reconstruction of conceptual metaphors, the reader is referred to the specialist literature in cognitive linguistics.4 In the present case, we find that Locke’s early modern English realises conceptual metaphors which map perceptual onto intellectual activities and achievements (Chapter 1.1), while things are more complicated with bounded spaces and minds: Much ordinary talk of the latter is motivated by a conceptual metaphor—Metaphor M—which maps spatial relations onto feats and failures of memory, but does not map anything onto minds as distinct elements of the target domain, in addition to those feats and failures which are attributable to human beings (Chapter 1.4).

We then need to see whether the intuitions and argumentative gaps we intend to explain can be accounted for by the mere extension of these metaphors. Where we find that we cannot (as in Chapter 1.5), we should see whether those intuitions etc. can be obtained by their overly literal application (as in Chapters 1.3 and 2.1–2.2). Where this kind of cognitive reconstruction is successful, we have obtained a working hypothesis that is to be explored for strength and assessed in comparison with competing explanatory hypotheses.

In rough approximation: The more otherwise unintelligible claims or inferences we can account for as the result of the overly literal application of the given metaphors, and of the further steps of the pictorial process, the stronger our hypothesis becomes. Careful logical reconstruction of the text may reveal ever more claims and argumentative leaps in need of an explanation. Next we determine whether our cognitive reconstruction can be extended in a way that can account for the assumptions bluntly assumed or quietly presupposed. Whether these are ‘otherwise unintelligible’ then depends upon what other explanations are proposed and stand up to scrutiny.

The most obvious strategy for obtaining such alternative explanations is to search the given and related texts for arguments stated elsewhere in support of the claims or presuppositions to be explained. The existence of such arguments will weaken the case for attributing picture-driven reasoning to the given thinker if, and only if, the ‘new’ argument does not itself contain unsound moves that can be best explained as the result of picture-driven reasoning. In the next chapter, for example, a cognitive reconstruction of picture-driven reasoning will provide the (to my knowledge) first explanation of why Galileo, Boyle and Locke found compelling the ‘argument from conceivability’ which commentators so far despaired of understanding. Looking for alternative explanations, we will encounter the ‘argument from science’ which appears to independently support a part of its conclusion— and will find that this argument presupposes this latter conclusion from the start and throughout. Hence our explanation will prevail unchallenged.

Such explanations are of immediate philosophical relevance because they facilitate the assessment of the warrant thinkers have for the claims and inferences thus explained. The thinkers whose epistemic situation you and I are most interested in probably are you and me, respectively; the most interesting application of the present approach is to one’s own case. Here, we may complement the analysis of textual evidence, and generate further evidence, through self-experimentation similar to a kind of self-scrutiny to which Wittgenstein submitted himself (Chapter 9).

I engaged in such experimentation in formulating first hypotheses about the development of the classical conception of the mind: I had noticed that when wanting to take up or refer (back) to the claim that the mind is a non-physical inner space, I frequently paraphrased it as the claim that our thoughts, beliefs, and pains are non-physical and private things (such paraphrases—which paper over a major step—were subsequently edited out of the text). That is, I observed in myself a tendency to leap from the claim that the mind is a non-physical inner space to the claim that the things in the mind are non-physical and private (further presupposing that thoughts and beliefs are things ‘in the mind’), and to regard this move as entirely trivial. So I asked myself why this move should be right, resolved to give whatever answer would occur to me promptly, regardless of how embarrassingly simple it might be (to exercise no self-censorship), and jotted down what answers occurred to me. They were all variants of the same two lines of thought:

Certain things are perceived in minds. Clearly, the things perceived in minds are or exist in minds. But the mind is an inner space without physical extension. So the things that are or exist in the mind have no such extension either: The things perceived in minds have no physical extension.



The things I perceive in my mind are in my mind. So they cannot simultaneously exist outside my mind. Since my mind is inside me, the things I perceive in my mind can exist only inside me. So they are bound to be hidden in me and you cannot perceive the things I perceive in my mind.



I then tried to work them up into logically valid arguments, in two steps. First, I (i) filled in assumptions that struck me as pertinent truisms, until (ii) I obtained a line of thought I found intuitively compelling. In the first case, I obtained:

(1) Certain things are perceived in minds. (2) If a thing is perceived somewhere it is there (at that place). (3) Hence the things perceived in minds are in minds. (4) The mind is a space without physical extension. (5) Things that are in others cannot have a bigger extension than the container they are in. (6) Hence, by (4) & (5), things that are in the mind have no spatial extension either. Hence, by (3) & (6): (7) The things perceived in minds have no spatial extension.





In the second case, I obtained a version of ‘Reasoning Private’ (Section 1). Then I determined how, and how literally, I had to interpret the key expressions (“perceive”, “without extension”, “is in”, etc.) in order to obtain a valid (though not necessarily sound) argument. I then cast these interpretations into the mould of assumptions presupposed—and thus obtained I to III (Section 1). These assimilate the target-domain of cognition and other supposed operations of the mind to the source-domain of visual perception of physical objects, in ways inconsistent with (sublimating) explanations I was spontaneously inclined to give. I thus came to conclude that I am, to some extent, under the spell of perceptual pictures of the kind we have attributed to Locke.

Where the moves an author treats as trivial strike us as equally compelling when reading his work, where we (have come to) spontaneously employ the same terms in our own reflections, and where we are inclined to spontaneously give the same (sublimating) explanations of them as we later find the author to give in passing, such self-experimentation can also be used to generate hypothetical attributions of particular philosophical pictures to that other author. Such working hypotheses can then be tested by going through the steps set out above. Conversely, where we share the intuitions of earlier philosophers, their cognitive reconstruction based on textual evidence can help us to make sense of what wild reasoning we may elicit from ourselves when engaging in the kind of self-experimentation outlined. To sum up, textual analysis complemented by self-experimentation allows us to formulate and test cognitive reconstructions of philosophical intuitions and argumentative gaps.

Such reconstructions provide the basis for systematic diagnostic analysis that helps us to dissolve philosophical problems by identifying and refuting what philosophical motivation we have for raising them. We have thus won through to the basic ideas of a new approach to at any rate one class of philosophical problems: Where such problems are raised only due to ultimately picture-driven reasoning, we can dissolve them through systematic diagnostic analysis based on cognitive reconstruction of philosophical intuitions and argumentative gaps. We will now show that some important problems of metaphysics, epistemology and the philosophy of perception belong to the relevant class. Important philosophical problems in several core areas of philosophy are raised in the grip of philosophical pictures and can be dissolved by coming to understand their nature and genesis and the way in which we think about the topics at issue when engaged in philosophical reflection.





3 Pictures’ Effects


From ‘Secondary Qualities’

to ‘Perceptions’




Philosophical reflection is decisively shaped by non-intentional and automatic reasoning—by inferences we are not aware of making. The problems, arguments and theories philosophers explicitly state, develop and debate are visible waves on seas whose waters are largely moved by deep currents of thought beyond our direct control, flowing in depths into which we do not normally peer.

Proceeding from a body of experimental work done over the last four decades in cognitive and social psychology, various cognitive scientists have developed the distinction between two kinds or ‘systems’ of cognition.1 Their ‘dual process accounts of cognition’ distinguish between cognitive processes which are automatic, unconscious, associative and fast, and processes which are controlled, typically conscious, serial and comparatively slow. These processes are taken to be cued by different content and contextual triggers. Controlled processes, they maintain, are prone to be triggered mainly (though not exclusively) by the encounter of novel, complex or unusual cases, exceptions, logical complexities like negation, counterfactuals and hypothetical reasoning as well as by perceived high stakes, self-consciousness and the expectation that one will have to justify one’s responses. Automatic processes, by contrast, pertain, first, to the execution of routine or simple tasks, where they are particularly likely to be triggered by time pressure, distraction or the need to do different things at once, as well as pertaining, second, to reasoning about familiar matters where it may be triggered when the stakes are perceived to be low or no goal is being pursued. Much philosophical reflection is about perfectly familiar things, things we do or encounter every day, and is not directly geared towards any particular goal. In brief, in philosophy the conditions are frequently right for inferences of the automatic, unconscious, associative and fast kind.

The most obvious potential cognitive outcomes of automatic or unconscious inferences are intuitions: non-perceptual judgements we make without being aware of drawing any inference and find intuitively compelling—frequently to the point of deeming further argument for them unnecessary. In the course of the last ten years or so, 2 philosophers have grown increasingly interested in exploring the ways in which automatic or unconscious reasoning shapes such intuitive judgements in philosophy, frequently taking their cues from dual process accounts of cognition. We have embarked on an inquiry roughly of this kind, though with a slightly different scientific inspiration, less theoretical baggage and a wider philosophical scope: While previous philosophical work along these lines has been inspired by accounts shaped by research into cognitive heuristics and biases, we have developed ideas launched by work specifically on analogical and metaphorical reasoning. We did so without using the conceptual frameworks of extant dual process accounts of cognition (which have various theoretical commitments I prefer to steer clear of). Instead, we have built up to a new notion which remains neutral between, and open to different theoretical interpretations: the notion of ‘being under the spell of philosophical pictures’. With the help of this notion, we have examined non-intentional analogical reasoning in which thinkers assimilate targets to models of conceptual metaphors, in ways in which they actually know the two to be different. We have examined how such ‘picture-driven’ reasoning generated not only some influential philosophical intuitions which shaped the modern (post-Aristotelian) concept of the mind, but also some classical mind-body problems which still worry us today. In this chapter, we will systematically review the further effects picture-driven reasoning has at several stages of philosophical reflection.

Picture-driven inferences are easiest for us to spot in reasoning couched in conceptual frameworks in which we are not at home ourselves. Their novelty—to us—makes it less likely that non-intentional inferences are triggered, and the pertinent moves will not strike us as so natural that we just go along with them without realising that a substantive step is involved. In this chapter, we will therefore linger in the 17th century and follow up an important intellectual development which was facilitated by the concept of ‘the mind’ we have reconstructed and which was couched in the terminology of ‘substances’ and ‘accidents’ that was in general use until the mid-18th century but is entirely alien to us today. We shall follow up the early modern revival of the lastingly influential distinction between ‘primary’ and ‘secondary qualities’, and the evolution of the latter notion into the sense-datum doctrine of perception whose development, accommodation and refutation has been the core concern of modern philosophy of perception, for over three centuries. We will follow up this influential development in order to examine how picture-driven reasoning can shape philosophical reflection at several stages: We shall examine how philosophical pictures have shaped the formation of major conceptual frameworks of early modern philosophy (Section 1) as well as the formulation or development of crucial philosophical doctrines (Section 2) and arguments (Section 3), problems and theories (Section 4), and of speculative explanations (Section 5). At several points, our new explanation will let us solve puzzles that have perplexed modern commentators, and thus establish the explanatory potential of the novel cognitive reconstructions we are developing.





1. PICTURE-DRIVEN CONCEPTS:

‘SUBSTANCES’ AND ‘ACCIDENTS’




In the previous chapters, we have encountered the process of metaphorical extension and considered how the extension of perception- and manipulation-terms to more abstract, intellectual, activities and achievements provided ordinary English with a core part of its mental vocabulary. The process drives not only the development of natural language but also of philosophical terminology. A case in point is the ‘substance’/‘accident’-terminology of ultimately Aristotelian provenance which was in general use in Scholastic and 17th-century philosophy. Qualities (and some other things) are said to ‘exist’ or ‘be in’ the ‘substances’ (the things or stuffs) that possess them. The ‘substances’ are said to ‘stand under’ or ‘support’ those qualities (as pillars support a bridge), and these may ‘join’ or ‘leave’ them. These terms were initially applied literally to tangible things, in particular to material parts of whole physical objects.

The development of this terminology crucially involves the extension of all these terms from this concrete to more abstract fare: to qualities (etc.) of things. This new use endows these terms with new metaphorical senses, in which they are equivalent to our current everyday talk of things ‘having’, ‘acquiring’, and ‘losing’ qualities (used below to indicate the new senses “joins”, etc. have when extended to qualities, etc.):





Qualities as Parts

Parts/wholes Oualities/things (stuffs)

x joins y → y acquires x

x is in y → y has x

x leaves y → y loses x

y supports/stands under x → The thing made of y has X



This extension preserves the inferential relations between the terms involved: A physical part that joins a whole body will be in it until it leaves it; a quality that joins (i.e. is acquired by) an object will be in it (i.e. possessed by it) until it leaves it (i.e., until the object loses it). That a part leaves a whole implies that it was in it before; that a quality leaves an object (i.e., that the object loses the quality) implies that the quality was in it before (i.e. that the object possessed it before), etc. In general: Both in the initial sense in which they apply to parts and in the new sense in which they apply to qualities, these terms stand in the same inferential relations to each other. The above mapping, ‘Qualities as Parts’, hence preserves relations between the mapped elements; it is a conceptual metaphor (Chapter 1.1).

By preserving the inferential relations between the terms involved, this conceptual metaphor forges a structural analogy between a simple concrete model and a more abstract target, namely between tangible parts of physical objects, on the one hand, and qualities of things, on the other. Structural analogies between the concrete and the abstract facilitate non-intentional analogical inferences in reflection about the latter: When thinkers are not pursuing a practical goal and are unaware of relevant context or detail, they are prone to unwittingly fall back on the tangible model when thinking about the target, and to spontaneously project properties from the former onto the latter, without realising that they are doing this or are making any use of a model (Chapter 1.2). In this way they may excessively assimilate the target to the model, against their better knowledge: They may unwittingly project properties they know the target does not share with the model and presuppose that the two are alike in ways in which they know them to be different. In the previous chapters, we have seen how thinkers have unwittingly misapplied perceptual models in this way and presupposed, e.g., that to know or ‘see’ something is to perceive some thing that is literally located at some place, literally with an organ of sense. When misapplying the current model in this manner, they will presuppose:



(M) Qualities are things literally located at some place, namely in the objects to which they belong.



Let’s say that a thinker is under the spell of the part-whole or mereological picture of qualities iff he unwittingly but systematically makes inferences which presuppose M, i.e. inferences ‘driven’ by that picture.

We have seen that new philosophical concepts of ‘the mind’ and ‘ideas’ were introduced through inferences driven by perceptual pictures, which proceeded from truisms which reflect the metaphorical senses of perception-terms (Chapter 1.3). Similar inferences driven by the mereological pictures lead to definitions introducing the two principal technical senses of the term “accident”. The first definition was meant to capture a truism which arises from the conceptual fact that in saying that a quality ‘is in’ an object, in the new metaphorical sense of this expression, we are precisely not locating it in space, let alone referring to any object: A quality can be said to ‘be in’ objects, namely the objects to which it can be attributed, but cannot be said to ‘be anywhere’ else. That is:



(T1) A quality cannot ‘be anywhere’ except ‘in’ objects.



Aquinas meant to capture this conceptual truism by means of a definition that follows from it in—and only in—conjunction with M, viz. the proposition that qualities are objects which exist at some place or other:



(C1) A quality is a thing that can only exist in other objects and not ‘of itself’ or on its own, outside other objects.



In other words, qualities are existentially dependent things which necessarily require a ‘host’ object to exist in. Similar leaps led to analogous conclusions concerning quantities, relations and a few other things, so that this existential dependence was used to define not the notion of qualities, but a more general term:

(D1) An accident is a thing that by its very nature must exist in another thing. Accidens =def.: res cuius naturae debetur esse in alio. (Aquinas)



This defines the ‘wide’ sense of the term (‘accidens praedicamentale’), in which it marks the opposite of substances. Boyle explains:

As substance is commonly defined to be a thing that subsists of itself and is the subject of accidents . . . so an accident is said commonly to be id cuius esse est inesse [that whose being is in-being]. (OFQ 21)



The term “substance” then is used equivocally to refer to what makes up the object together with the accidents which inhere in it, and to the object itself. (Compare the use of the term “bridge” to refer both to what is supported by the pillars and to the whole of which also the pillars are a part.) In the wide sense in which it simply marks the opposite of ‘substance’, the term “accident” covers the remaining nine of the ten Aristotelian categories: qualities, quantities, relations, locations and times, actions and passions, disposition (the arrangement of parts) and ‘rainment’.

In its second, ‘narrow’ sense (‘accidens praedicabile’), the term applies to contingent, as opposed to essential qualities (which alone were called ‘properties’ at the time). In this sense,

an accident ‘is then defined [as] “that which may be present or absent without the destruction of the subject”—as a man may be sick or well, and a wall white or not white, and yet the one still a man, the other a wall’. (OFQ 21)





Like the previous definition, this one is derived from a colourful interpretation (‘Whiteness may be present or absent without the destruction of the wall’) of conceptual truisms it is evidently meant to capture (such as ‘A wall may be white or not white, and yet be a wall’). And, again, the interpretation of the truism is entailed by it in—and only in—conjunction with M. As this idea is not made explicit, we can conceptualize this move as a leap of thought which tacitly presupposes—a part of—M and proceeds from the truism T2 to be captured to the conclusion C2 conflated with it:



(T2) A contingent quality of an F is a quality which the F may have or fail to have, without ceasing to be an F.



And: The qualities of a thing of kind F exist somewhere in (‘are present in’) that F. Hence:



(C2) A contingent quality of an F is a quality which may be present in the F or absent from it, without it ceasing to be an F.



Thinking of qualities as parts of things then has thinkers interpret this conclusion as one about the non-essential parts of an object whose removal merely damages but does not destroy that object. This step takes us to the common formulation taken up by the above quote:



(D2) An accident is what may join or leave a thing without its destruction. Accidens =def.: quod accedit vel decedit absque rei corruptione. (Goclenius)
