The idiom of qualities ‘joining’, ‘being in’ and ‘leaving’ objects is obtained by metaphorical extension motivated by the conceptual metaphor ‘Qualities as Parts’. Metaphorical extension of terms initially applied to concrete things is a major process equipping language to deal with the abstract. In many cases, it provides us with all the equipment we have to talk about an abstract phenomenon. The abstract relation of qualities to the things that have them is a case in point: If we don’t want to talk metaphorically of qualities ‘joining’ and ‘leaving’ things ‘in’ which they are, we have to speak, equally metaphorically, of things which ‘acquire’ and ‘lose’ the qualities they ‘possess’ in the meantime. When both sets of expressions are used in the metaphorical senses motivated by the respective conceptual metaphors, they can be used interchangeably: That a quality ‘is in’ an object simply means that the object ‘possesses’ it, and vice versa. Hence we can substitute one expression by another in a sentence, without changing its meaning, let alone its truth-value, wherever the present idiom is used with the metaphorical senses motivated by the conceptual metaphor ‘Qualities as Parts’.

But we cannot make such substitutions in the sentences that articulate the philosophical dichotomy of things that can ‘subsist of themselves’ and things which are existentially dependent upon such substances. Take, for instance, the sentence “A quality is a thing that can only exist in other things and not of itself, outside other objects”. It is clearly not intended to say that a quality is a thing that can only be possessed by other objects, i.e., to deny that qualities can themselves have qualities. And it is quite unclear how to render its second half (“ . . . and not [exist] of itself, outside other objects”) in the idiom of possession rather than existence, without changing the topic completely. We may conclude, therefore, that the dichotomy of substances and accidents cannot be obtained by mere metaphorical extension of “joins”, “leaves” and “exists in” but only by the overly literal application of the conceptual metaphor motivating this extension, that is, through inferences driven by the mereological picture of qualities, such as those we have spelled out.

Picture-driven inferences take thinkers to conclusions at odds with truisms they are unable or unwilling to give up. The conclusions of inferences driven by the mereological picture are at odds with the conceptual truism



(CT) Qualities (etc.) do not stand in any kind of spatial relations.



A common response to such conflicts is sublimation (Chapters 1.3 and 2.1): Thinkers who find both sides to the dispute compelling spontaneously reinterpret the key terms of the picture-driven conclusions in a way that appears to render the latter consistent with the pertinent truisms. Accordingly, definitions of the term “accident” are typically accompanied by a gloss which deprives the key phrases “is in” and “exists in” of all spatial implications and thus appears to render definitions like D1 consistent with truisms like CT:



(E) That is said to be in a subject which has these three conditions: that, however it (1) be in another thing, (2) is not in it as a part, and (3) cannot exist separately from the thing or subject wherein it is. (Boyle: OFQ 21)

This explanation amounts to what we called a ‘negative sublimation’, which explicitly tells us that the pertinent terms are not to be understood in a gross, literal sense, but in some other sense, which frequently remains unexplained. Thus, now we are merely told that qualities (1) are in, indeed, (3) must be in other things, and (2) are in them in another sense than their parts—and nothing is added to explain the other, non-literal, sense in which qualities can be said to ‘be in’ objects. Such purely negative explanations are frequently accompanied by positive reinterpretation which may remain tacit. In particular when discussing concrete examples many philosophers momentarily employ the key terms in the metaphorical senses motivated by the pertinent conceptual metaphors and thus use the expression “qualities in things” simply as equivalent of “qualities of things”. Where this practice is not consistent, but serves to reinterpret claims previously obtained only due to unduly literal interpretation of such talk, we are dealing with positive sublimation, which we can logically reconstruct as application of rules like



(R) Say “Quality Q is in object O” iff Q is a quality of O.



While negative sublimation tends to remain purely negative, positive explanations like R get swiftly ignored and are not applied consistently. As we shall see (in the next section), thinkers sometimes even apply them only to one part but not another, of one and the same sentence—striking cases of merely partial sublimation.

These defects of sublimation are, I think, not due to ineptitude, but to the fact that the expectations thinkers maintain in the grip of the philosophical picture are hard (or even impossible) to satisfy simultaneously: On the one hand, thinkers in the grip, e.g., of the mereological picture hanker after a ‘substantive’ sense of “Q exists in O”, which carries at least some of the spatial implications of the literal sense of this phrase. On the other hand, explanations like E are motivated by the reluctance to completely part with the established practice of attributing qualities, which does not provide for any such implications. So the sense in which “exists in” applies to qualities ‘in’ or of objects is to be both substantive and intellectually acceptable by the lights of the established practice. Depending upon how substantive a thinker wants this sense to be, on the one hand, and how attached to established practice he is, on the other, he may impose requirements which jointly rule out any positive explanation of “exists in” he can think of—if they are not outright inconsistent. Thus he may find himself reduced to purely negative explanations of the expression, while unwilling to consistently restrict himself to the metaphorical sense which is patently free of spatial implications. Thus the hankering after a substantive sense may prevent users of the substance/accident-terminology from just seizing on the Rule R they often speak in accord with, to explain the sense in which qualities rightly can be said to ‘be in’ objects, and may push them at many points to use this phrase in ways not licensed by that rule. The defects of sublimation are signs of a conflict between philosophical pictures and common sense, which settles us with requirements that are hard to square and makes us try to square the apparent circle through verbal tricks.

To sum up, we have reason to believe that the concepts of ‘substance’ and ‘accident’ were obtained through picture-driven inferences—like the equally important concepts of ‘mind’ and ‘idea’ we have examined in a previous chapter (Chapter 1.3). These fundamental concepts were commonly paired up in a straightforward manner: Picture-driven reasoning has us swiftly conclude that the things we perceive in the mind are or exist in the mind (Chapter 2.1). In positive sublimation, early modern philosophers explain that “exists in the mind” simply means ‘is perceived in the mind’ (ibid.). Ideas, the things we perceive in the mind, thus appear to be of such a kind as to ‘exist’ in, and only in, the sense that they are perceived, namely in the mind, with the mind. It appears, in other words, that ideas cannot exist independently from minds which perceive them or outside the minds in which they are perceived. Hence they satisfy the definition—D1—of ‘accidents’ as things which by their very nature cannot exist independently but only in other things. At the same time, minds were held to satisfy the definition of ‘substances’: First, accidents (namely ideas) exist in them. Second, they are generally thought, at the time, to ‘subsist independently’: Commonly employed metonymy treats these supposed organs as subjects of perception, while equally common sublimating explanations stress their immaterial character (Chapter 1.3). In the intensely religious 17th century, these immaterial subjects of perception were typically conflated with the ‘souls’ religious teaching posited ‘in’ us as immaterial subjects of perception and volition, 3 which can exist independently and actually do so once death has separated them from the mortal bodies to which they have been harnessed in life. Souls bequeathed their existential independence on minds. Thus the new (post-Aristotelian) ‘minds’ were introduced as substances (e.g. EHU II.xxvii.2) and ideas as accidents which exist in them.

In this section and the previous chapters, we have found that the two most fundamental pairs of concepts employed in early modern philosophy are generated by picture-driven reasoning. This finding provides the key to a series of puzzles about lastingly influential doctrines couched in these terms, which have notoriously perplexed commentators. To establish the explanatory power of our hypothesis that philosophical pictures drive philosophical reflection, we now turn to two puzzling doctrines that lie at the root of the lastingly influential distinction between ‘primary’ and ‘secondary qualities’ and, ultimately, of the major modern philosophical theories of perception.

The development of the terminology of “substantia” and “accidens“ took place when Western scholars rediscovered Aristotelian ways of thinking and enriched their Medieval Latin with the idioms and expressions required for taking them up. It is plausible to assume that throughout the reign of this terminology (roughly, from the 13th to the early-18th century) much philosophical reflection was driven by the mereological picture of qualities we have seen to shape this conceptual framework. We shall now consider what happened when, in the 17th century, these Aristotelian concepts outlived Aristotelian metaphysics and were used by the ‘natural philosophers’ who resurrected Democritean atomism: By interpreting their ‘new’ metaphysical vision in the light of the philosophical picture they had inherited from their immediate predecessors, they got to conceive of ‘primary’ and ‘secondary qualities’ and to endorse two striking doctrines about them.





2. PICTURE-DRIVEN DOCTRINES: PRIMARY QUALITIES




The most important of these natural philosophers are Galileo, who first revived these Democritean concepts, and Locke, who established them in the philosophical mainstream, along with Boyle, who is generally regarded as Locke’s main source of inspiration on this topic, and Descartes, who influenced Boyle. We shall consider the three thinkers at the root of the more lastingly influential empiricist tradition (Galileo, Boyle and Locke), with a focus first on Locke and then on Galileo.

The distinction between primary and secondary qualities is commonly regarded as one between determinable rather than determinate qualities, where several determinate qualities like ‘red’ and ‘white’ (or ‘square’ and ‘round’) fall under the same determinable quality, like colour (or shape, respectively). The precise content of the distinction is a matter of debate: A thorough survey reviewed no less than twenty-two different distinctions that have been drawn under this heading, attributed no fewer than fifteen to early modern authors, and suggested that individual thinkers may have been running together up to six different distinctions under these labels.4 I propose to focus on what I regard as the most fundamental of the distinctions that can be credibly attributed to all three protagonists mentioned, from which the other distinctions attributable to them can be derived. This is the metaphysical distinction between—primary—qualities which really exist in the physical bodies to which we commonly attribute them and— secondary—qualities which do not really exist in such bodies (or at any rate not as distinct accidents). The mechanical properties of size, shape, motion and texture (along with the accident of number) are assigned to the first category. Colours and tastes, smells and sound, heat and cold are placed into the second. For instance, ‘sweetness and whiteness are not really in manna [a sweetish dried tree exudate]’ (EHU II.viii.18). By contrast, the ‘real original, or primary’ (EHU II.viii.23), or ‘primary, and real qualities of bodies’ (EHU II.viii.22) ‘may be called real qualities, because they really exist in those bodies’ (EHU II.viii.17).

This apparently straightforward metaphysical distinction rests on doctrines about primary and secondary qualities, respectively, which are nothing less than mind-boggling. Primary qualities are ‘real’ because they really exist in bodies. And the qualities that really exist in bodies are the mechanical properties of the ‘parts’ or ‘particles’ that make them up (sic). Thus Boyle declares:

There is in the body to which . . . sensible qualities are attributed nothing of real and physical but the size, shape, and motion or rest, of its component particles, together with that texture of the whole which results from their [i.e. the particles’] being so contrived as they are. (OFQ 31, my italics)





Locke seems to agree (cp. EHU II.viii.10):

The particular bulk, number, figure, and motion of the parts of fire, or snow, are really in them, whether any one’s senses perceive them or no: and therefore may be called real qualities, because they really exist in those bodies. (EHU II.viii.17, my italics)





Galileo, finally, gives a causal account of taste- and smell-sensations, according to which these causally depend only on the sizes, shapes, number and velocities of certain insensible particles (SAG 29), and then declares:

[T]hat in external bodies, to excite in us the smells, the tastes, and the sounds, there are required other things than sizes, shapes, multitudes, and movements swift or slow, I do not believe. (SAG 30)





Clearly, the ‘sizes, shapes, multitudes, and movements’ situated ‘in external bodies’ are those of the insensible particles that make them up; these primary accidents are ‘in external bodies’—in contrast to the tastes and smells that merely ‘seem’ to us to ‘reside in’ those bodies (SAG 28). Presumably, Galileo too agrees with Boyle and believes that:



(PQ) The only qualities to ‘really exist’ (or ‘be something of real’) in a sensible body are the size, shape, and motion of the insensible particles that make it up.

This would seem to imply that the primary qualities of ordinary, medium-sized physical objects are the mechanical properties of the imperceptibly tiny particles that make them up—which is precisely what Locke asserts when introducing the distinction between ‘primary’ and ‘secondary qualities’:

[T]heir [i.e. the objects’] primary qualities . . . [are] the bulk, figure, texture, and motion, of their insensible parts. (EHU II.viii.10, my italics)





It is tempting to try to make sense of this by invoking the once popular distinction between determinable qualities like colour and shape, and the determinate qualities that ‘fall under’ them, as ‘green’ and ‘white’ fall under ‘colour’ and ‘round’ and ‘square’ fall under ‘shape’. The present sentence could express a claim only about the determinable qualities which physical objects share with the ever smaller parts into which they can be broken up (EHU II.viii.9). And of course both a visible physical object and an invisibly small molecule or atom have some size and some shape. But the claim PQ which would seem to imply the present conclusion is one about the determinate qualities of the parts or particles: ‘The particular bulk, number, figure, and motion of the parts of fire, or snow, are really in them’ (EHU II.viii.17, my italics).

Particles are held to move even when a body they make up is at rest; the atoms that make up a round silver coin presumably have the same ‘figure’ or shape as those that make up a slender silver hair pin; and there would seem to be all the difference in the world between the ‘bulk’ or extension of an atom and that of a coin or a pin. The apparent implication of PQ therefore is plainly mad. Even so, Boyle and Locke explicitly and repeatedly endorse PQ. The only at least prima facie pertinent argument they share, their ‘argument from science’, proceeds from the—different—hypothesis that colour-, smell-, taste- and heat-sensations are causally dependent upon nothing but the motions, shapes and sizes of tiny particles acting on our sense-organs, and is to establish the—different—conclusion that the ‘sensible qualities’ of colour and heat, etc., do not really exist in physical bodies (at any rate not as distinct accidents). But neither Boyle nor Locke (nor Galileo) states any argument to support his doctrine PQ that that ‘[t]he particular bulk, number, figure, and motion of the parts’ of physical things, and no other qualities, ‘are really in’ these objects. Unable to make any sense of this doctrine, modern commentators regularly resort to dismissing its statements as ‘slips’. But the supposed slips are repeated and, in the case of Locke, remained uncorrected through several thorough revisions of the text containing them. So persistent and crass a ‘slip’ would be little less puzzling than the explicitly championed view commentators are, even so, reluctant to attribute to our authors. We might as well accept the texts as they are and address the question they confront us with: Why did these eminent thinkers maintain this apparently mad view?

I submit that Galileo, Boyle and Locke all got to espouse their doctrines about primary and secondary qualities due to positive sublimation of a view they arrived at by interpreting the metaphysical vision they shared, in the light of the mereological picture. The metaphysical vision at the heart of their ‘Corpuscular philosophy’, taken up from Democritean atomism, is that of a universe (the created universe) as ultimately made up of nothing but insensible particles, corpuscles, or atoms, which have only certain basic—mechanical—qualities: size, shape and motion (plus whatever qualities things must necessarily possess in virtue of having these). This vision, explicitly spelled out by Boyle (OFQ 18–21), already informs the causal accounts of sensations which Galileo presents after introducing the distinction (SAG 29–31). It translates into this assumption about any particular sensible body:



(1) Nothing exists in a body in addition to the insensible particles that make it up.



Thinkers in the grip of the part-whole picture of qualities will be prone to interpret this metaphysical core tenet of the Corpuscular philosophy in its light: In their reasoning, they will be inclined to treat qualities of sensible bodies as body-parts which unsuccessfully compete for space with the bodies’ constituent particles and must therefore submit to entering the particles (if they are not to leave the body and thus cease to belong to it). Less poetically and more precisely, such thinkers will make a leap of thought presupposing the by-now-familiar assumption M, in a form that takes into account that sensible bodies are constituted of insensible physical particles:



(2) The qualities of a sensible body really exist somewhere in it: either in the insensible particles that make it up or in addition to these. By (1) & (2):



(3) If a quality really exists in a sensible body, it exists in the insensible particles that make up the body (and not independently from, outside of, these particles).

Since this conclusion is at odds with the conceptual truism that qualities do not stand in any spatial relations, philosophers who find (3) compelling will spontaneously reinterpret, or ‘sublimate’ it. Positive sublimation is by the rule R presented above (in Section1). Partial application of R, to the present conclusion, yields the puzzling doctrine PQ; repeated application yields the even more puzzling implication of the latter that Locke explicitly maintains:



(4) A quality ‘exists in’ a (sensible or insensible) body iff it is a quality of that body. (Rule R) Application to only the consequent of (3) yields:



(5) If a quality really exists in a sensible body, it is a quality of the insensible particles that make up that body.



In other words: (PQ) The only qualities that really exist in a sensible body are the qualities of the insensible particles that make it up. Another application of Rule R, to the antecedent of (5), then yields Locke’s apparently mad claim that the ‘primary or real qualities’ of sensible objects are the qualities of their insensible parts (EHU II.viii.10).

While deeply puzzling to us today, the more basic and popular idea (5)— a.k.a. PQ—in fact provides an attractive solution to the quandary we took to account for the characteristic defects of negative and positive sublimation (Section 1): We put these defects down to the conflict between, on the one hand, the hankering of thinkers in the grip of the mereological picture after a substantive sense in which qualities can be said to ‘exist in’ things and, on the other hand, their reluctance to lapse too far from what is intellectually respectable by the lights of the established practice of attributing qualities, which does not allow for any substantive spatial sense. This conflict is resolved neither by the negative sublimating explanation E—which remains too lofty—nor by the positive sublimation R—which removes all spatial implications. The present conclusion (5), by contrast, seems to provide the key to an innovative sublimation (a third kind of sublimation to be added to the two discussed above): The conclusion suggests a new sense of “exists in” which is both sufficiently substantive and intellectually respectable. The qualities of the insensible particles are of course not spatially in them. But these particles are spatially in the sensible bodies they make up. Hence some qualities ‘really exist’ in a body in the quasi-spatial sense that they are qualities of the particles that exist in that body, in the literal, spatial sense. This is, I think, one major motivation for the distinction between ‘primary and real qualities of bodies’ which ‘are really in them’ (EHU II.viii.17), and the ‘secondary and imputed qualities’ (EHU II.viii.22) which are imputed to bodies even though they do not really exist in them (and can be attributed to them only as ‘bare powers’) (see Section 4). At any rate, such innovative sublimation renders the present conclusion, which is so puzzling to us today, attractive to thinkers in the grip of the part-whole picture of qualities.

To sum up, Locke and Boyle explicitly maintain a paradoxical—indeed, an apparently mad—doctrine about ‘primary qualities’ and do so without any argument. The moves we have posited, namely picture-driven inference and sublimation, can explain the acceptance of this doctrine in the absence of argument: These non-intentional and spontaneous moves take thinkers from the core tenet of atomism to this paradoxical doctrine, which thus appears to follow directly from the core tenet of the ‘Corpuscular Philosophy’ that goes with the ‘New Science’. This creates the wrong impression that the latter implies the mad doctrine, and its apparent madness appears to bear witness to nothing but exhilarating scientific progress which once more corrects uneducated common sense.





3. PICTURE-DRIVEN ARGUMENTS:

SECONDARY QUALITIES




The core doctrine about what Boyle and Locke came to call ‘secondary’ or ‘sensible qualities’ is no less striking than their doctrine about primary qualities. As revived by Galileo, the ancient doctrine of secondary qualities states bluntly that these accidents do not really exist in external bodies but in us:



(SQ) Colours and tastes, smells and sounds, heat and cold, are sensations (sic).



Spelled out more fully, the claim is that in contrast with the ‘primary accidents’ of size, shape, motion or rest, number, location and contact (SAG 28), which really exist in the external bodies to which we commonly attribute them, the other ‘qualities’ mentioned merely seem to us to exist in the objects we perceive around us and in fact reside exclusively in us:

[T]astes, odours, colours, etc., so far as concerns the subject in which it seems to us they reside, are nothing but mere names for something which resides exclusively in our sensitive body, so that if the living animal was removed, all of these qualities would be annihilated and abolished from existence. (SAG 28)





Smells, tastes and sounds are subsequently said to be ‘excite[d] in us’ (SAG 30); sounds and heat are ‘produced in us’ and ‘ma[d]e felt in us’ (SAG 30, 31); in brief, the so-called sensible qualities are held to be sensations we feel in our sensitive bodies—which perish with the living animal.

Boyle and Locke concur that they are ‘perceptions of impressions we receive’ or, more simply, ‘perceptions in us’:

We teach . . . that . . . there being men in the world, whose organs of sense are contrived in such differing ways that some sensory is fitted to receive impressions from some, and another from other sorts of external objects . . . the perceptions of these impressions are by men called by several names, as heat, colour, sound, odour. (Boyle: OFQ 51).



[R]ightly considered, these qualities of light and warmth . . . are perceptions in me when I am warmed, or enlightened by the sun. (Locke: EHU II.viii.24)





And as with light and warmth, so with taste and colour: These ‘perceptions’ are sensations or ‘ideas’ caused in us by external objects (EHU II.i.3), like the sensations of pain and feelings of sickness we have when eating too much manna (a sweetish dried tree exudate formerly used as a laxative):

Sweetness and whiteness are not really in manna; [they] are but the effects of the operations of manna . . . on the eyes and palate; [just] as the pain and sickness caused by manna, are confessedly nothing, but the effects of its operations on the stomach and guts. (EHU II.viii.18)





Colours and tastes, smells and sounds, heat and cold are sensations or perceptions produced in us—and, perhaps, other living animals—when external objects act on our sense-organs.

This claim is bound to strike today’s reader as puzzling, if not outright mad. It has struck modern commentators as so unacceptable that they decided that Boyle and Locke must have maintained an entirely different claim, namely that colours and their ilk are not sensations in us but dispositions of bodies to cause such sensations. This decision, which has become the cornerstone of an almost unquestioned orthodoxy,5 obliged commentators to dismiss the numerous statements to the contrary as ‘slips’6 and deplorable ‘confusions’,7 to claim that Locke ‘forget[s] his official doctrine about secondary qualities’ within a few pages of stating it,8 or to explain that the supposedly confused passages really mean something entirely different.9 Even the most thorough study of the topic, which is in various ways critical of the orthodoxy, curtly dismisses ‘the view that says bluntly that, for Locke, secondary qualities are . . . ‘in the minds of perceivers’’, does so only a few lines after acknowledging that Locke ‘used such words as “colour” and “red” for ideas of secondary qualities’, and proceeds to propose an interpretation requiring the assumption that ‘in spite of appearances, Locke does not intend texture to be a primary quality’,10 where the ‘appearances’ in question are created by the repeated and entirely unambiguous classification of texture as a primary quality (EHU II.viii.10 & 14).

These moves are dissatisfactory. They also stand in marked contrast to the responses of early modern philosophers who were still more familiar with the philosophical concerns and the conceptual framework of the 17th century: Reid and Berkeley credit Locke and the champions of ‘secondary qualities’ with precisely the view they actually state: that ‘colour, sound, and heat, are not any thing in bodies, but sensations in the mind’ (Reid),11 that is, that ‘secondary qualities . . . are sensations existing in the mind alone’ (Berkeley: PHK 10).12 Let us therefore try to make sense of what appears mad to us (though evidently not to better placed philosophers): Let us try to explain why Galileo, Boyle and Locke maintained that colours and tastes, smells and sounds, heat and cold are sensations or perceptions in us.

In contrast with PQ, the present doctrine SQ is supported by explicit argument. But the apparently pertinent arguments merely wrap a puzzle in an enigma. The arguments ‘from science’ (given by Galileo, Boyle and Locke) and ‘from relativity’ (added by Locke) argue for the related but distinct doctrine that colours and their ilk do not really exist in physical bodies and are—as we shall see in the next section—almost circular in that they rely on the conclusion SQ which apparently follows from that doctrine (see below). Since our three protagonists all ignore two further—unsound— arguments advanced by Descartes, this leave us with just one argument for the present doctrine SQ which does not take for granted what it ought to show, namely the notorious ‘argument from conceivability’.

Galileo puts it as follows:

[a] Whenever I conceive of any piece of matter or corporeal substance, I feel myself necessarily compelled to conceive along with it, that it is bounded and shaped by this or that shape, that in relation to others it is big or small, that it is in this or that place at this or that time, that it is in motion or at rest, that it touches or does not touch another body, that it is one, few, or many—and by no stretch of the imagination can I separate it from these conditions. [b] But I do not at all feel myself compelled to conceive of it as necessarily accompanied by such further conditions as being red or white, bitter or sweet, having sound or being mute, or possessing a pleasant or unpleasant fragrance. [c] On the contrary, if the senses were not supplying us, perhaps reason or imagination on their own would never get there. [d] I think, therefore, that these tastes, odours, colours, etc., so far as concerns the subject in which it seems to us they reside, are nothing but mere names, and reside exclusively in our sensitive body . . . ’ (SAG 27–28)





The argument can be conveniently paraphrased by invoking the distinction between determinable qualities and the determinate qualities that fall under them:





Corporeal substances necessarily have some shape, size, location, motion, contact and number. I.e.: The determinable qualities of shape, size, etc. are necessary or essential qualities of such substances.

Corporeal substances need not have any colour, taste, smell or sound. I.e.: The determinable qualities of colour, taste, smell, and sound are not necessary or essential qualities of such substances.

We do not know a priori that corporeal substances possess such contingent or inessential qualities.

Therefore (sic) colour, taste, etc. are only sensations existing in our sensitive bodies, and do not exist outside such bodies.





The puzzling claim that these qualities only exist in us is thus treated as a ‘simple corollary’ of the observation that they are not conceptually inseparable from bodies.13

As commentators noted, the move from the three premises to the conclusion is a startlingly obvious non sequitur:

The fact that our very general concept of a material body is that of a space-occupant, and that this concept implies shape, size, spatio-temporal location, capacity for motion and contact with other bodies . . . does not imply that material bodies are not coloured, fragrant, noise-emitting or flavoured. At most it implies that we can conceive of a colourless, soundless, odourless, and tasteless material object.14





Even the (to my knowledge) most sympathetic commentator to date acknowledged:

So there is a sense in which Galileo’s conclusion is right as well: shapes are more real than colours in the sense that they are more immediately linked with our concept of reality. [But] Galileo is still wrong in concluding from the conceivability of colourless bodies that colours are not intrinsic features of material objects; they might in fact be intrinsic features without necessarily being so.15





More precisely, the non sequitur consists in the move from the premises (b) and (c) to the conclusion (d). While the present argument does not—like some of the arguments we will consider later—simply presuppose what it is meant to establish, its core move presupposes a tacit assumption that is frankly breathtaking:



(A) If a (determinable) quality is not an essential property of material bodies, or cannot be attributed to such bodies a priori, then it is a sensation or perception in us.



In other words: Material bodies have only essential properties which we can determine by a priori reflection on the concept or essence of ‘material bodies’; if you have to use your eyes, or any other organ of sense, to find out about it, the alleged quality of the body in fact is a sensation in us. This assumption patently is not one we may simply take for granted. In fact, it seems mad (indeed, so mad that no commentator to date bothered to seriously discuss it). This confronts us with a puzzling question: Why did three eminent thinkers, three of the fathers of the world-view of early modern science, go along with this breathtaking assumption—and the apparently mad conclusion to which it took them?

This problem becomes more tractable the moment A is broken up into two assumptions that jointly entail it:



(A1) Only such accidents really exist in external physical bodies as such bodies necessarily have and are a priori known to possess.



(A2) If an accident does not (really) exist in external physical bodies, then it exists in us (as a sensation).



At first sight, these two assumptions do not look less puzzling than the original. But we can obtain the first by following up the picture-driven line of thought we have just found to lead to the puzzling doctrines about primary qualities (Section 2). The second then follows by an inference to the best explanation in response to a problem thus engendered.

That brief line of thought consisted of an inference driven by the mereo-logical picture of qualities and its positive sublimation. These two steps took us from the core tenet of atomism to the conclusion



(5) If a quality really exists in a sensible body, it is a quality of the insensible particles that make up that body.



A natural assumption manifestly shared by Galileo, Boyle and Locke then leads from this conclusion to A1. At a time at which the latest technological breakthrough consisted not in Wilson cloud chambers but in telescopes, it was natural to assume that the only qualities we can reasonably attribute to insensible particles we can never get to observe are those essential qualities of physical bodies that we can determine through a priori reflection on what qualities a physical body must have simply in virtue of being a physical body. Accordingly, Galileo attributes to the ‘tiny particles’ he invokes in his mechanist explanations of smell, taste, sound and heat (SAG 29–31) only those qualities he had a moment earlier declared himself ‘compelled to conceive’ corporeal substances as having (SAG 27). And Boyle explicitly deduces the qualities of the insensible corpuscles that ultimately make up all bodies, in unabashedly a priori reflection (OFQ 18–21): He derives all of them through purely a priori reasoning from the two assumptions that ‘there is one catholic or universal matter common to all bodies, by which I mean a substance extended, divisible, and impenetrable’ (OFQ 18), and that ‘[we see] diversity . . . in bodies’ (ibid.). Locke, finally, introduces the primary qualities he goes on to equate with the bulk, figure, texture and motion of the sensible bodies’ insensible parts as qualities ‘such as . . . the mind finds inseparable from every particle of matter’ (EHU II.viii.9). Thus Galileo, Boyle and Locke all maintain:



(6) Insensible particles have precisely those qualities that are essential qualities physical bodies are a priori known to possess. By (5) & (6):



(A1) If a quality really exists in a sensible body, it is an essential quality physical bodies are a priori known to possess.

The second assumption, A2, is that if an accident does not really exist in physical bodies, it must exist in us—provided it exists at all. While this may strike modern readers as rather surprising, it actually is a rather trivial consequence of the conceptions of ‘substances’ and ‘accidents’, ‘minds’ and ‘ideas’ we have analysed earlier (in Section 1): If an accident exists at all, it must exist in some substance or other. Minds qualify as ‘substances’, leaving us with a dichotomy of two kinds of substances: bodies and minds—where Locke, with many of his contemporaries, further distinguishes between finite minds and the infinite mind of God: ‘We have the ideas but of three sorts of substances: 1. God. 2. Finite Intelligences [or Finite Spirits]. 3. Bodies’ (EHU II.xxvii.2). If an accident does not exist in the latter, it must therefore exist in one of the former. If we can perceive the accident, as we manifestly perceive colours and tastes, sounds and smells, the accident in question surely exists in our own mind (rather than that of a categorically different being, into which we cannot peer). It must be the sort of accident that exists, and is perceived, in our minds; it must be an idea of some sort, an idea of perception, or a sensation.16

We will encounter this kind of inference to the best explanation in many other arguments: If an accident we perceive does not exist in physical bodies that surround us, it must exist in our minds and be perceived within us. If colour does not exist in physical bodies, it must be an idea we perceive in our minds. If heat does not exist in physical bodies, then it must be a sensation located in our minds (or sensitive bodies). Etc. Galileo, for a start, makes an inference of this kind three pages later, in the course of his subsequent ‘argument from science’, subsequently taken up by Boyle and Locke, where he arguably leaps from PQ to SQ, presupposing A2:

[T]hat in external bodies, to excite in us the smells, the tastes, and the sounds, there are required other things than sizes, shapes, multitudes, and movements swift or slow, I do not believe. And I judge that if the ears, the tongues, and the noses be taken away, the shapes, numbers, and movements remain, but not the smells nor the tastes nor the sounds, which outside the living animal I do not believe they are other than names. (SAG 30)





We will encounter the same presupposition again and again in Berkeley’s defence of ‘perceptions’ (Chapter 5), and similar dichotomous thinking in classical arguments for sense-data popular until at least the mid-20th century (Chapters 6.1 and 8.2).

To sum up, there is textual evidence that Galileo, Boyle and Locke share all the assumptions that lead, through the picture-driven reasoning reconstructed, from the core tenet of atomism—(1) above (Section 2)—to A1, as well as the assumptions that lead to A2, which is presupposed in much philosophical argument, including the argument with which Galileo follows up the argument from conceivability. These two assumptions jointly entail the tacit assumption A that bridges the gap in this argument, between the explicit premises that colours and their ilk are no a priori attributable essential properties of physical bodies and the explicit conclusion that those accidents exist in us. We may infer the hypothesis that Galileo—and all other early modern thinkers who found the argument from conceivability compelling—went along with it because they found themselves unable to derive a priori that physical bodies should have colours and tastes, smells and temperature, in addition to size and shape and motion, found assumption A compelling due to the picture-driven reasoning rehearsed, and therefore leaped from their first finding to the conclusion that colours and their ilk must exist in our minds. We have thus obtained the (to my knowledge) first explanation of why perfectly competent thinkers provided or went along with the apparently wild argument from conceivability.

In the first chapter, we have developed the concept of ‘philosophical pictures’ with the help of findings about non-intentional analogical reasoning and conceptual metaphors, in such a way that adherence to such pictures can be attributed to thinkers on the basis of inferences to the best explanation which explain why those thinkers espouse apparently paradoxical claims either in the absence of all argument or on the basis of arguments which sport stunning gaps that cannot be filled by invoking views their champions state elsewhere (cp. Chapter 1.2). Our analysis of the doctrine PQ provided us with an explanation of the first kind, that of the argument from conceivability for the doctrine SQ yielded an explanation of the second kind.

Our explanations warrant the conclusion that Galileo, Boyle and Locke espoused the claims and the argument discussed due to non-intentional reasoning which is unsound. Where the claim or the conclusion of the argument are not subsequently supported by sound (further) argument, but are in conflict with common sense and therefore in need of such support, their champions lack warrant for them. This happened to Locke, who does not provide any argument for either PQ or SQ but, in a fine display of belief bias, presupposes the latter in the arguments ‘from science’ and ‘from relativity’ which he provides to support the related claim that colours and the other ‘secondary qualities’ do not really exist in physical objects (next section). In a nutshell, the two doctrines about primary and secondary qualities illustrate two ways in which picture-driven reasoning may lead a thinker to maintain unwarranted claims, namely either in the absence of warrant or on the basis of a patently unsound argument. Let’s now turn to the further consequences of such ultimately picture-driven claims: to the problems they appear to raise and the theories philosophers construct in response.





4. PICTURE-DRIVEN PROBLEMS AND THEORIES:

LOCKE ON SECONDARY QUALITIES




In picture-driven reasoning we assimilate targets to models of conceptual metaphors in ways in which we know the two to differ. Such reasoning systematically takes us to intuitively compelling conclusions at odds with other convictions. Such clashes confront us with the task of remedying an apparent inconsistency in our beliefs. We have either to refute and abandon one of the parties to the clash or show against first appearances that these parties are not inconsistent. In the latter case we address, implicitly or explicitly, what we dubbed a ‘reconciliation problem’ (Chapter 2.3). PQ and SQ give rise to several problems of this kind.

According to PQ, the only qualities to ‘really exist’ (or ‘really reside’ or ‘be something of real’) in a sensible body are the size, shape, and motion of its constituent particles. This confronts us with two problems. First, we ordinarily attribute these qualities—like so many others—to the whole sensible body. Being accidents, they only exist to the extent to which there are other things in which they exist. So where do these properties of the whole body exist, if only the properties of the insensible constituent parts really exist in it? This problem is not explicitly addressed by our protagonists. Presumably, they think that the mechanical properties of a body necessarily result from the sizes, shapes and motions of its constituent parts and thus ultimately of the particles that make it up—and infer that any quality that necessarily results from those that really exist in a body is bound to be in it, as well, even if it should not, perhaps, be counted as an additional or extra quality. Thus Boyle states that the size, shape and motion of the body’s component particles are something ‘of real and physical’ in the body, and then adds the afterthought that the same goes for the ‘texture of the whole which results from their [i.e. the particles’] being so contrived as they are’ (OFQ 31). This kind of reasoning places all the mechanical properties of the whole body ‘into’ it, without the need to count them as extras: All these qualities of the whole necessarily result from the qualities of its elementary parts (cp. EHU II.viii.9, 22).

A second problem arises from trivial observation: We only need to open our eyes or use any of our other senses to appreciate that there are not only sizes and shapes but also colours and tastes, smells and sounds, heat and cold in the world. PQ appears to imply that they do not exist in physical bodies. As accidents, they do have to exist in some kind of substance. So in what other kind of substance do they exist? Without being explicitly stated, this question is answered by SQ: The colours we see and the odours we smell, etc., exist in minds. This idea, however, merely gives rise to a fresh problem: We ordinarily attribute such accidents as qualities to physical objects, and are convinced that these attributions are meaningful and often correct. But how is it possible to speak of qualities ‘of’, or ‘in’, an object, when the relevant accidents exist only in our minds? All three problems are ill-motivated in the sense we have explained (in Chapter 2.3): The thinkers perplexed by them lack warrant for at least one of the claims to be reconciled. The picture-driven line of thought we have reconstructed confronts us with a whole series of ill-motivated reconciliation problems, where the ‘solution’ to one may well just raise another.

Locke’s response to the third problem is frequently regarded as his ‘theory of secondary qualities’, encapsulated in his first explanation of ‘secondary qualities’:

Secondary qualities ‘in truth are nothing in the objects themselves, but powers to produce various sensations in us by their primary qualities, i.e. by the bulk, figure, texture, and motion of their insensible parts, as colours, sounds, tastes, etc.’ (EHU II.viii.10, cp. 23)





So even though colours, sounds and tastes are sensations and do not really exist in physical objects, these can be said to have such qualities in case they have the power to produce the pertinent sensations in us.

The notion of ‘bare powers’ Locke employs here is radically different from the modern notion of disposition with which it is commonly conflated by modern readers:17 An object possesses a disposition also when it does not exercise it. (A lump of sugar is soluble in water even when it is not placed in water and therefore is not in the process of dissolving.) But Locke does not just explicitly deny that physical objects have a colour when it is too dark for them to cause any colour-sensations in us, but even finds this obvious. He does so in discussing an experiment on the phenomenon of inorganic luminescence which attracted much attention in the wake of the discovery of the ‘Bolognian Stone’ (barite) which glows in the dark. His experiment concerns porphyry, which proves less spectacular: ‘Hinder light but from striking on it, and its colours vanish’; they vanish the moment the light is gone so that the stone ‘no longer produces any such ideas in us’—whence ‘tis plain [!] it has no colour in the dark’ (EHU II.viii.19).18 But the ‘colours’ we can attribute to physical bodies are powers to produce ideas in us. In Locke’s sense of the term, an object clearly has the ‘power’ to cause certain colour-ideas in us as long, and only as long, as it actually produces such ideas in us.

Locke’s concept is part of a dichotomy long since forgotten, which divides all cases of causation into two classes: Causal effects are either ‘effect[s] of bare power’ or are caused through the ‘communication of a quality which was really in the efficient’ (EHU II.viii.25)—as when a billiard ball hits another and sets it into motion, by passing some of the accident of motion in it on to the other. According to Locke, common sense assumes such a ‘communication’ when we find both the ‘efficient’ and the ‘patient’ to change qualities in corresponding ways (one ball is set into quick motion while the other slows down). Indeed, he holds, common sense resorts to this sort of explanation, whenever we cannot make an observation that excludes it, namely, whenever we cannot observe a change in the patient, which conspicuously fails to be accompanied by a corresponding change in the efficient:

[W]hen we see wax or a fair face receive change of colour from the sun, we cannot imagine, that to be the reception . . . of any thing in the sun, because we find not those different colours in the sun itself. (EHU II.viii.25)





That is: Since we observe that only your face, but not the sun, changes in colour when you get sunburn, we do not put this change of colour in your face down to a communication of colour from the sun.

When trying to explain our own colour-, taste- or heat-sensations, by contrast, we cannot make any such observation which rules out causation through a communication of accidents (ibid.): In this case we cannot make such an observation because we can only perceive ideas and their changes in us but cannot observe independently any changes in external objects (next section). Therefore, and because there is no ‘conceivable connexion’ between bulk, figure and motion of bodies and those sensations or ideas, common sense, according to Locke, imagines that our colour-sensations are caused by communication of a colour-quality which really exists, in addition to those primary qualities, in the object which has the power of producing those ideas in us (EHU II.viii.25): According to such alleged common sense, the accident of colour is communicated from one substance in which it really exists, the external body, to another, our mind. One and the same accident then exists first in external bodies and subsequently in our minds—(a) below—without changing, so that having entered the mind the accident still ‘perfectly resembles’ its former self in the body—(b) below, where “idea” is throughout Locke’s cover-all term of art for whatever we perceive in our minds (including ‘perceptions’ and sensations) (EHU II.viii.8):

Flame is denominated hot and light; snow white and cold; and manna white and sweet, from the ideas they produce in us. [a] Which qualities are commonly thought to be the same in those bodies, that those ideas are in us, [b] the one the perfect resemblance of the other, as they are in a mirror; and it would by most men be judged very extravagant, if one should say otherwise. (EHU II.viii.16)



Against what he thus regards as the common-sense position, Locke rehashes the argument from science previously given by Galileo (SAG 28–29) and Boyle (e.g. OFQ 23–24). To understand the key passages, recall that Locke conceives of colours and their ilk as being sensations or perceptions or, in his cover-all sense, ideas: Colours and tastes are ideas caused in us (EHU II.viii.18, cp. II.viii.24). When Locke speaks of ‘ideas of colour’, etc. this is therefore to be understood as analogous to our talk of ‘sensations of pain’, where “of” means ‘namely’: We have certain ideas, namely colour-ideas, just as we have certain sensations, namely pain-sensations, and there is no necessary implication that the colour-idea has an intentional object distinct from it (i.e. that it is the idea of such an object), just as there is no necessary implication that the pain-sensation or—same thing—‘idea of pain’ is somehow of a pain distinct from it.

Sensations or ideas are accidents, and it is a legitimate question whether these accidents also inhere in corporeal substances. The present argument takes for granted that the heat we feel and the colours we see are sensations or ideas in us, and seeks to show that these accidents do not really exist in the bodies from which they supposedly get communicated to our minds:

[H]e, that will consider, that the same fire, that at one distance produces in us the sensation of warmth, does at a nearer approach, produce in us the far different sensation of pain, ought to bethink himself, what reason he has to say, that his idea of warmth, which was produced in him by the fire, is actually in the fire; and his idea of pain, which the same fire produced in him the same way, is not in the fire. Why is whiteness and coldness [being posited] in snow, and pain not, when it produces the one and the other idea in us; and can do neither, but by the bulk, figure, number, and motion of its solid parts? (EHU II.viii.16, further fleshed out in EHU II.viii.18)





The argument is this: To explain heat-sensations, common sense posits a distinct accident of heat in the bodies that cause them in us, and assumes this accident to be communicated to us from there (assumes that ‘the idea of warmth . . . is actually in the fire’). To explain our pain-sensations, however, common sense does not posit any distinct accident of pain or painfulness in the external bodies (pins or fires, etc.) that produce them in us. But science—supposedly—shows us that both kinds of sensation get caused in the same way, through the local motion of insensible particles, and depend upon the number, size and figure of these particles. Therefore, there is no more reason to posit a distinct quality of heat (or colour or cold) in bodies than there is to posit a distinct accident of pain or painfulness in them. We ought to conclude that the accidents of colour and heat, etc. do not really exist in bodies and only exist in minds.

Hence physical bodies do not cause such perceptions or sensations in us by ‘communicating’ to our minds accidents which really are in those ‘effi-cients’. But those bodies clearly cause these perceptions. Given the present dichotomy, these colour-ideas and tastes-sensations hence must be effects of bare power:

Tis true, the things producing in us these simple ideas [which we call whiteness and sweetness], are but few of them denominated by us, as if they were only the causes of them; but as if those ideas were real beings in them. For though fire be called painful to the touch, whereby is signified [meant] the power of producing in us the idea of pain; yet it is also denominated light [i.e. luminous] and hot, as if light [luminosity] and heat, were really something in the fire, more than a power to excite these ideas in us; and therefore are called qualities in, or of the fire. But these being nothing, in truth, but powers to excite such ideas in us, I must, in that sense, be understood, when I speak of secondary qualities, as being in things; or of their ideas, as being in the objects that excite them in us. (EHU II.xxxi.2)



In other words, when we call a fire ‘painful’, we mean to ‘denominate it only as the cause of the idea’ or sensation of pain it produces in us: We merely say that it actually causes pain. (Compare: No matter how strong, a blow is called ‘painful’ only if it actually hurts someone.) When we call fire ‘hot’ or ‘luminous’, by contrast, we do not mean to ‘denominate it only as the cause of the ideas’ of light and heat it produces in us; we do not mean to say merely that fire produces light-ideas or heat-sensations in us. Rather, common sense allegedly thinks the light and heat are ‘real beings’ in the fire and therefore calls them ‘qualities in, or of the fire’. But, in truth, these so-called qualities are nothing but powers to excite ideas in us, that is, they are ‘powers barely’ (EHU II.viii.24), namely bare powers like the fire’s power to produce in us the idea or sensation of pain: When calling a fire ‘hot’ we ought to mean no more than when we call contact with it ‘painful’, namely, that it actually produces the pertinent sensations or ideas in us. To sum up, a body has the bare power to produce, e.g., certain sensations when and only when it currently produces these effects. And this is how Locke wants to be understood when speaking of sensible qualities as being ‘in’ the things that produce those sensations or ideas in us. (Whence there are no colours in the porphyry when it is dark.)

Even though no accident of colour, etc., really exists in bodies, ‘secondary and imputed qualities’ (EHU II.viii.22, my underlining) can therefore be imputed to, or said to ‘be in’ bodies as bare powers, namely as a mere façon de parler, meaning neither more nor less than that the body currently produces pertinent sensations in sentient creatures, not through a communication of those qualities, but in some other way. The suggestion—the core part of Locke’s ‘theory of secondary qualities’—that secondary qualities ‘in truth are nothing in the objects themselves, but powers to produce various sensations in us . . . by the bulk, figure, texture, and motion of their insensible parts’ (EHU II.viii.10) therefore provides an elegant solution to the problem of reconciling the fact that we can attribute colours and tastes to physical bodies with the philosophical doctrine—SQ—that these accidents really exist only as sensations or ideas in our minds.

While an elegant solution to this problem, the claim that a physical body can be said to be red or hot only in the sense that it is currently causing certain sensations is completely at odds with common sense, as actually implicit in ordinary language. The argument for it is based on the ‘argument from science’ which takes for granted the best part of the claim that is to be reconciled with the attribution of ‘secondary qualities’ to physical bodies: The colours we see, the odours we smell, the heat we feel, etc., are sensations or ideas in us. The argument then tells us that these accidents do not exist in physical bodies as well and hence cannot be caused in us by ‘communication of real qualities’ but must be the effects of bare powers. The theory that solves the problem thus ultimately rests on the very picture-driven—and unwarranted—claim that raises the latter. Picture-driven reflection leads philosophers to raise ill-motivated problems and build unwarranted theories on the intuitively compelling claims that raise the problems.





5. PICTURE-DRIVEN EXPLANATIONS:

LOCKE ON PERCEPTION




Locke’s ‘theory of secondary qualities’ is an example of a philosophical theory meant to explain familiar facts in a peculiar way, namely to explain how these actual facts are as much as possible in view of an apparent conflict with intuitively compelling philosophical claims—which may be rendered compelling only by picture-driven inferences. His account of sense-perception or ‘external sensation’ exemplifies theories that are to explain merely supposed facts which we posit as the result of picture-driven inferences. This account is built on and around a seminal version of what came to be known as the ‘sense-datum doctrine of perception’.

This doctrine is a trivial consequence of the present theory of secondary qualities with its core claim—SQ—that colours, tastes, smells, etc., are sensations or ideas in our minds. Whenever we perceive them, we hence perceive ideas in the mind. In the first chapter, we reconstructed the intuition—I2—that to think of or about things is to perceive ideas in the mind, with the mind. The core doctrine about ‘secondary qualities’ implies an analogous claim that proved even more popular in the long run:



(I5) To see colours, hear sounds, smell odours, taste tastes and feel heat or cold is to perceive ideas in the mind, with the mind.



A few truisms then take us on to the seminal version of the sense-datum doctrine: Whenever we see anything at all, we see (at least) colours (if only the black of the night in which you cannot make out any objects). Whenever we hear or smell anything at all, we hear or smell (at least) sounds and odours, respectively (without, perhaps, knowing what emits them). Whenever we taste anything at all, we perceive (at least) a taste (if you do not, you can chew as much as you like and still cannot be said to taste the object you feel in your mouth). Hence, by (I5):



(SD*) Whenever we see, hear, smell, taste or feel anything, we perceive ideas in our minds.



Ideas perceived then and thus are called ‘perceptions’:

Perceptions are ideas we perceive (i) in the mind, (ii) through use of our five senses.





‘Perceptions’ are the 17th-century ancestors of the ‘subjective sense-data’ of the 20th century. The crucial difference between the two is that sense-data are regarded as bearers of various qualities like colour or shape: 20th-century philosophers speak of the ‘silvery elliptical sense-datum’ we perceive when looking at a coin from the side. By contrast, perceptions are colour or heat, and Locke speaks, e.g., of ‘these qualities of light and warmth, which are perceptions in me when I am warmed, or enlightened by the sun’ (EHU II.viii.24). Several such ‘simple ideas’ can be ‘combined’ into a ‘compound’ or ‘complex idea’ (EHU II.xii.1). Thus, colour-ideas can be combined with ideas of shape, which we perceive together when looking at a coin. So we can perceive in our minds a complex idea combining silver colour and elliptical shape. But such ideas are not, like sense-data, (mental) objects with qualities but compounds of colour- and shape-ideas, etc. This is due to the conception of ideas as accidents (as belonging to the same category as qualities) and minds as substances (as belonging to the same category as bodies) in which the ideas exist (as qualities exist in bodies): This allows only for accidents to exist in minds, and leaves no room for substances which support these accidents and are distinct from the mind. But only things distinct from the mind can exist in this internal container, or be perceived by that inner organ of sense. As Locke notes, ‘the understanding, like the eye, whilst it makes us see, and perceive all other things, takes no notice of itself’ (EHU I.i.1). Therefore the present conception leaves no room for things which have qualities and can be perceived in and by the mind. Accordingly, perceptions will evolve into sense-data only upon the demise of the conceptual framework of substances and accidents.

The intuition—I2 (Chapter 1.3)—that to think of or about things is to perceive ideas in the mind, with the mind, and the present intuition-based doctrine that whenever we see, hear, smell, taste or feel anything we perceive ideas in our minds, jointly give rise to a very wide notion of perception. Locke takes both cognition and sense-perception to involve the exercise of one and the same faculty, namely the ‘power’ of ‘Perception, or Thinking’ (EHU II.vi.2), which is a ‘faculty [i] of the mind, [ii] exercised about our ideas’ (EHU II.ix.1), that is, a faculty of [ii] perceiving ideas, [i] by means of that inner organ of sense. To get clear on what perception is, a man just has to observe ‘what passes in his own mind’, namely ‘when he sees, hears, feels, etc. or thinks’ (EHU II.ix.2, both italics mine). The use of one’s senses and the use of one’s wit involve the exercise of one and the same faculty: perception. And such ‘perception’ always is perception of ideas in one’s mind.

Since we employ our wits and our senses throughout our waking lives, there clearly are minds and ideas perceived in them—this was the very first intuition we considered (I1, Chapter 1.3) and have now accounted for more fully. In further reasoning about the minds thus posited in us, thinkers are then prone to unwittingly assimilate them to models from the realm of visual perception and its physical objects, in some fundamental respects in which they know models and targets to differ; once this has happened, they seize on ever more familiar facts about those models in reasoning about the mind (Chapter 2.1). When we seize, for example, on the familiar fact that a thing needs to be around to be perceived, so that if it is perceived somewhere, it must be there (at that place), we naturally infer that if an idea is perceived in the mind, it must be or exist there, in the mind (Chapter 2.1).

This inference takes us from the intuition that there are minds and ideas perceived in them to a guiding question of Locke’s Essay: How did the ideas which ‘tis past doubt, that men have in their minds’ (EHU II.i.1) get to be there? I.e.: ‘How comes [the mind] to be furnished [with ideas]?’ (EHU II.i.2) In Book I of his Essay, Locke argues that there are no innate ideas. Hence all ideas that are in the mind must have been produced within or come in from without. In Book II, Locke sets out to explain how this happens: His account of ‘reflection’ (EHU II.i.4) or ‘internal sensation’ (EHU II.xi.17) is to explain how ‘the mind [itself] furnishes the understanding with ideas’ (EHU II.i.5). The account of ‘sensation’ (EHU II.i.3) or, more specifically, ‘external sensation’ (EHU II.xi.17) is to explain how ‘perceptions of things’ (EHU II.i.3) are ‘received from foreign things’ (EHU II.vi.1). It is to explain the supposed fact that our minds contain ideas which have in some way or sense come in from without.

Surprisingly enough, this account builds on the kind of alleged commonsense explanation that Locke explicitly rejects at any rate for secondary qualities: Idea-accidents come into the mind-substance by being communicated to them from corporeal substances. Locke presents the first version of the account without arguing for it (EHU II.iii.1). It can be obtained through non-intentional analogical reasoning seizing on ever more familiar facts about the kind of medium-sized physical objects we typically perceive with our five senses (facts stated in italics below):

(1) Ideas that come from external objects enter the mind from without. (2) Objects can enter an enclosed space from without only through openings in its enclosure. Hence: (3) The ideas that enter our minds from without enter through openings in our bodies.





Obviously, we cease to see colours the moment our eyes are shut, no longer hear sounds the moment our ears are plugged, and no longer smell odours the moment our noses are clogged; the moment these openings are unobstructed, we perceive them all again. Since—(I5)—to see colours, etc., is to perceive ideas in the mind, this entails that we perceive colour-ideas in our minds when, and only when, our eyes are open, sound-sensations iff our ears are open, smells iff the openings of our noses are unobstructed. Given the previous conclusion, the most straightforward explanation of this ‘fact’ would seem to be that



(C1) The colour-, sound-, and smell-sensations we perceive through the senses come from foreign things which communicate them to us, through the openings of our eyes, ears, and noses, respectively, into the mind where we perceive them.

This is the core of an account slightly fleshed out by further analogical reasoning of the same kind:

(4) Openings in my body do not allow you to look into my mind. (5) Through openings you can see what lies directly behind them. Hence: (6) The mind does not lie directly behind openings in our bodies. (7) An object that, through openings, enters a container that does not lie directly behind those openings must pass through some sort of conduits that take them from the former to the latter. Hence: (8) If ideas enter the mind through openings in our bodies, they must pass through conduits connecting the openings with the mind.





By (C1), some ideas, namely the colours, sounds, tastes and smells we perceive through our senses do enter the mind through openings in our bodies, namely our sense-organs. In line with the present conclusion, Locke from the start likens the host of perceptions we ‘receive’ to a stream being channelled through pipes or ‘conduits’ from a source: Perceptions are ‘derived by [our senses] to the understanding’ (EHU II.i.3), where the verb is used in the then current sense of ‘conducting a fluid from a source or into a channel’ or conduit. (C1) and the present conclusion entail that



(C2) After passing through the openings constituted by our sense-organs, perceptions are conducted through conduits that link those organs to the mind in which they are perceived.



Some findings of early modern physiology then suggest what these conduits are, and where they lead: Our several sense-organs are linked by nerves to the same further organ: the brain. Since nerves and the brain are thus found to be linked to the sense-organs in the way in which the posited conduits and inner viewing space of perceptions are supposed to be, it seems reasonable to infer that—(C3)—those nerves must be the conduits of perceptions and the brain the space in which they are viewed.

Conclusions C1 to C3 add up to Locke’s first account of how the mind comes to be furnished with colour- and other ideas from without (external sensation):



(ES) Thus light and colours . . . come in only by the eyes: All kinds of noises, sounds, and tones only by the ears: The several tastes and smells, by the nose and palate. And if these organs, or the nerves which are the conduits, to convey them from without to their audience in the brain, the mind’s presence room (as I may so call it) are any of them so disordered, as not to perform their functions, they have no postern [i.e. small rear gate] to be admitted by; no other way to bring themselves into view, and be perceived by the understanding. (EHU II.iii.1)



In other words: External objects communicate to us various accidents, including colours, sounds, tastes and smells, as well as ‘those, belonging to the sense of touch’, namely ‘heat and cold, and solidity’, ‘smooth and rough’, ‘hard and soft, tough and brittle’ (as Locke adds in the very next sentence). All those accidents enter our body through the openings of our sense-organs and are guided through nerve-conduits into a place in our head, the brain or mind, where they can be perceived by our inner eye, the understanding. Thus one and the same accident is first in bodies and then in minds. Elsewhere, Locke distinguishes between ‘ideas . . . as they are . . . perceptions in our minds; and as they are modifications of matter in the bodies that cause perceptions in us’ (EHU II.viii.7). Here, he asserts that they come from these bodies, through the openings of specific sense-organs, into our minds, where we perceive them.

This is of course the very explanation of our ideas and sensations that the argument from science seeks to refute, as far as colours and sounds, tastes and smells and heat and cold are concerned. And it is clear from the start that—T1—the things we see and hear do not touch our minds, like billiard balls hitting each other, to pass on accidents, and—T2—that we do not see any accidents flying through the air, either (which is anyway tricky, since they have to inhere in substances). It is therefore clear from the start that those accidents cannot literally be ‘conveyed’ or ‘communicated’ from physical objects to our minds. Already when first introducing the notion of external ‘sensation’, Locke therefore explains:

[W]hen I say the senses convey [those ideas, we have of yellow, white, heat, cold, soft, hard, bitter, sweet] into the mind, I mean, they from external objects convey into the mind what produces there those perceptions. (EHU II.i.3)





This explanation amounts to a sublimating explanation (cp. Chapters 1.3 and 2.1): That an idea is ‘conveyed’ from an object to a mind simply means that something that produces the idea in the mind is conveyed to it from the object. This explanation removes the patent conflict between the apparently common-sensical account of ‘external sensation’, on the one hand, and two truisms—T1 and T2—and the Corpuscular philosophy of early modern science, on the other. It does not, however, provide Locke with any shred of evidence or justification for the account of external sensation we obtain when interpreting the first statement in the light of this explanation:



(ES+) When we perceive bodies through our senses, (as yet unspecified) things come from the bodies through specific openings into our bodies, where nerves convey them to the brain, where they produce ideas of colour and smell, etc. The things that produce colour-ideas come in only through our eyes; the things that produce odour-sensations come in only through our noses, etc.

Subsequently, Locke gives precisely one argument to support accounts along these lines (EHU II.viii.11–13). The argument proceeds from the assumption that the only way in which we can conceive bodies to cause effects is by impulse (EHU II.viii.11). The idea, widely shared at the time, that bodies cannot operate upon and affect other matter without mutual contact led many early modern thinkers to reject as unintelligible the notion that physical bodies (like planets) might exert gravitational or other forces on objects (like other planets) at a distance from them. While the reconstruction of this idea is beyond our present scope—it would require too long an excursion into the history of science—note that it can be obtained by a picture-driven inference from the idea that all genuine causation is by ‘communication of qualities which really exist in the efficient’. If we presuppose that—M—qualities are things that exist literally somewhere in space, we will infer that since qualities can only exist in substances, they cannot move from one corporeal substance to another through a void: The communication of accidents from one body to another requires their mutual contact. The one accident which mechanical explanations take bodies to communicate is motion. Hence the only way in which a body can cause effects is by touching another thing (substance) and communicating the accident of motion to it.

The argument then derives the sought account from the apparent implication that if bodies produce ideas in us, they must do so by impulse, by transferring motion to our minds, and from the two truisms mentioned previously: Since—T1—external objects do not touch our minds, are ‘not united with our minds, when they produce ideas in it’, but at most with our sense-organs (as food touches the palate), ‘tis evident that some motion must be thence continued by our nerves . . . to the brains or the seat of sensation, there to produce in our minds the particular ideas we have of them’ (EHU II.viii.12). ‘And since [T2] the extension, figure, number, and motion of objects of an observable bigness may be perceived at a distance by the sight’, when these objects do not touch any part of our body, ‘tis evident some singly imperceptible bodies must come from them to the eyes, and thereby convey to the brain some motion, which produces these ideas, which we have of them in us’ (ibid.). Thus Locke infers that ‘the ideas of . . . [primary and secondary] qualities are produced in us . . . by the operation of insensible particles on our senses’ (EHU II.viii.13) from a couple of truisms and the assumption that bodies can be conceived to cause effects only by impulse.

To sum up, Locke’s account of external sensation—ES—is to explain the supposed fact that the mind contains ideas which come in from without. Arguably, Locke accepts this explanandum as a fact only due to picture-driven inferences and initially obtains the explanatory account through picture-driven reasoning. He bases the one explicit argument he subsequently adduces for it on an assumption that can be obtained through such reasoning. Quite possibly, this account illustrates how picture-driven reasoning can shape speculative explanations from beginning to end: by providing a bogus explanandum, suggesting a wild explanation, and providing the premises of what argument is subsequently adduced in its support. At any rate, we have seen reason to conclude that picture-driven reflection drove Locke to take the first two of these three steps.

In this chapter, we have shown that, and considered how, philosophical pictures shape philosophical reflection at several stages: They shape the formation and development of philosophical concepts, doctrines, arguments, problems, theories and explanations. So far, we have focused on intuitively compelling claims advanced without much, if any argument. In the next chapter, we will focus specifically on sustained philosophical argument and consider how such pictures shape it at several levels: how they can determine the general argumentative strategy for establishing a certain claim, methods deployed in constructing pertinent arguments, and the arguments themselves. Both where we leap to conclusions without argument and where we take care to argue for our philosophical claims, we are driven by unsound processes of non-intentional reasoning which vitiate our best efforts as long as we fail to grow aware of them: They systematically have us espouse and maintain absurd doctrines based on the most specious of arguments—frequently as a matter of delusion. These findings will take us from the approach of problem-dissolution we have outlined in the previous chapter to the full-blown project of philosophy as therapy.





Part II

Philosophical Delusions





4 The Power of Pictures


Berkeley’s Approach




Under the spell of philosophical pictures, philosophers come to adopt paradoxical claims in the absence of warrant. Belief-bias effects have them maintain these claims in the continued absence of warrant. The clash of these claims with common sense, acknowledged facts, or among each other, gives rise to illusory problems. To solve them and resolve the apparent conflicts, thinkers construct unwarranted philosophical theories, in the grip of the same pictures that had them formulate their ‘problems’. This is, in the roughest of outlines, the account of nature and genesis of philosophical problems and theories which we have developed, so far, by reconstructing core claims and moves which led to the lastingly influential division of the world into a colourful realm of inner perception called ‘the mind’ and an austere external realm of physical objects devoid of colour and sound, taste and smell, heat and cold. We now turn to an equally influential doctrine about sense-perception which purports to explain how the chasm between these two realms can be bridged.

While the claims and moves we considered up to now were typically made on the basis of strikingly swift and rough argument or of no argument at all, we now turn to carefully crafted philosophical arguments. Having studied how non-intentional and automatic reasoning can lead us to espouse and maintain claims without much, if any, argument, we will now examine how such reasoning shapes the construction of philosophical arguments. To do so, we will consider the classic arguments for the sense-datum doctrine of perception. We shall consider its first systematic defence, due to Berkeley (Chapters 4–5), and its arguably most sophisticated development to date, due to A.J. Ayer (Chapter 6). We shall find that unsound processes of non-intentional and automatic reasoning, specifically, reasoning driven by philosophical pictures and belief-bias effects, can shape and vitiate philosophical argument at several levels (Chapter 4) and lead brilliant men to maintain doctrines which are absurd (Chapter 5), as a matter of delusion (Chapter 6). The findings will let us understand where and when, why and what for therapy is required in philosophy.

In the first of his Three Dialogues, Berkeley wants to show that all sensible things exist in the mind.
