On one reading he espouses from the very start to the very end, ‘sensible things are those only which are immediately perceived by sense’ (3D 175, cp. 3D 262). So we are dealing with a version of the sense-datum doctrine: All we ever immediately perceive when we employ our senses are ideas. To defend this doctrine, Berkeley pursues a comprehensive argumentative strategy and develops, in its pursuit, a series of arguments which includes what became classical arguments for the sense-datum doctrine (which we will consider in the next chapter). We shall now analyse how the ground was prepared for these classical arguments, by careful analysis of the pertinent parts of the first of Berkeley’s Three Dialogues (henceforward: 3D).1

After examining his ‘strict’ notion of sense-perception (Section 1), we shall analyse his general argumentative strategy (Section 2), an ‘introspective’ method he employed in some of his core arguments (Section 3), and some of these arguments, namely his preferred (but today forgotten) arguments for the sense-datum doctrine: his criticism of ‘abstract ideas’ (Section 4) and the arguments that build on it (Sections 5–6). We shall find that his strategy, method and arguments are motivated by the same pictures that drove Locke’s reflection about perception. Philosophical pictures may shape not only what philosophers intuitively think or to what conclusions they spontaneously leap but also the way they argue when carefully constructing arguments in support of the claims they find compelling—including the very methods for constructing and the strategies for deploying philosophical arguments. Unsound non-intentional reasoning may shape a thinker’s effort to support his claims by argument at every stage and level—and lead him to confidently maintain what he has no reason to believe. 2





1. UNWITTING LINGUISTIC INNOVATION:

BERKELEY’S ‘STRICT’ NOTION OF PERCEPTION




In abstract reflection, philosophers often unwittingly start to use familiar words in new ways. In particular, philosophers frequently want to argue at a very general level and need general or generic terms for this purpose. For example, philosophers who want to speak and reason at one go about seeing and hearing, tasting and smelling, and the achievements of the sense of touch, would like to employ one generic term to cover the exploits of all five senses. In such a situation, thinkers often resort to a familiar term. Thus, the verb “to perceive” came to be used to cover all five ‘modes of perception’, in a sense in which “x perceives y” is to mean neither more nor less than ‘x sees or hears or tastes or smells or feels y’.

We may think that this is an established and familiar sense of the verb. But this is not so. In its established use, “to perceive” has got (in some constructions) implications which “to see” or “to hear” do not share (in the same constructions). Thus, both “I perceived that Fred was in the crowd” and “I perceived Fred in the crowd” are ordinarily taken to imply that I recognised Fred in the crowd; the first implies that I knew that Fred was in the crowd, the second that I knew that it was Fred I perceived. Similarly, both “He perceives that a swallow is flying overhead” and “He perceives a swallow overhead” are ordinarily taken to imply that the subject recognises, and knows, that the thing overhead is a swallow. Accordingly, it is unidiomatic to say either ‘I perceived Fred right away but recognised him only later’ or ‘I perceived a swallow and later learned it was a swallow’. By contrast, only the constructions “x sees that p” and “x hears that p” imply such knowledge. If, failing to recognise Fred, I do not know he is in the crowd, I indeed cannot be said to ‘see that Fred is in the crowd’, even when looking straight at the man. But “x sees y” and “x hears y” carry no such implication: ‘I saw Fred straightaway but recognised him only later’ is as acceptable as ‘The explosion of the secret ammunitions depot could be heard by anyone within twenty miles’—including the vast majority who had no idea that that is what the loud bang was. The same goes, mutatis mutandis, for “x smells y”, “x tastes y”, and “x feels y”. Hence “x perceives y” ordinarily has implications, namely of recognition and knowledge, which “x sees or hears or tastes or smells or feels y” does not have. Ordinary language contains no general or generic use of the verb in which it is synonymous with this disjunction. Since ordinary language is geared towards specific practical pursuits, this is perhaps no surprise. At any rate, the introduction of the present generic use amounts to a linguistic innovation.

This innovation easily leads to unforeseen consequences. As we have just seen, we need to drop the implications of recognition and knowledge, if we want to use “x perceives y” as a mere shorthand for the disjunction of the five more specific ‘verbs of perception’. In particular, when we do not even realise that we are putting the familiar expression to a new use, we are, however, unaware of this necessity, fail to make the changes necessary to stay in line with the established use of these further terms, and end up unwittingly modifying their use instead. For example, many philosophers of perception continue to take “x perceives y” to imply recognition and knowledge, also when using the verb in the new ‘generic’ sense in which mere seeing or hearing imply perceiving. This transfers the ordinary implication of recognition and knowledge from perceiving to mere seeing and hearing. (If x sees y, then x perceives y. If x perceives y, then x recognises y. Therefore: If x sees y, then x recognises y.) Accordingly, many philosophers take “x sees y”, “x hears y” and the rest to imply recognition and knowledge, in their ordinary and established senses: You see Fred only if you recognise him, see the swallow only if you realise that it is a swallow you see, can be said to ‘hear’ the explosion only if you realise that such an explosion is what you hear, etc. Thus unwitting linguistic innovation is followed by spontaneous transfer of implications from the supposedly generic to the more specific terms and has us credit these with supposedly ordinary implications they do not in fact have.

By crediting them with these supposedly ordinary implications, we turn the achievements of seeing, smelling and tasting, hearing and feeling things into epistemic achievements, which involve a knowledge one could acquire by using either only one’s senses or also one’s reason: I may be able to tell by just looking, or may have to infer, that it is Fred or a swallow I see, that it is the explosion of a secret ammunitions depot I hear, etc. The moment we think that such knowledge is required for ‘seeing’ and ‘hearing’ Fred, the swallow, or the explosion, we may want to pin down the contribution our senses make to this achievement. We may then say that strictly speaking we see or hear or otherwise perceive by sense those things which we get to perceive by using only our senses and not our reason. Since inferences are drawn by reason, we strictly speaking see or hear only what we see or hear without making any inference. We thus come to conceive a supposedly strict or proper generic notion of sense-perception as ‘seeing or hearing, etc., without inference’. In actual fact, this notion is quite different from the ordinary English notion of ‘seeing’ or ‘hearing’: The present notion requires both that the subject who sees or hears something possesses certain knowledge and that he acquires this knowledge without inference. Ordinary English requires neither. We are dealing with a new notion, conceived only in philosophical reflection, and incorrectly regarded as merely rendering more precise the—supposedly—loose use ordinarily made of perception-terms.

We find Berkeley employing this new notion when explaining what we can be said to hear or see ‘in truth and strictness’ (3D 204) or in ‘strictness of speech’ (NTV 45). Berkeley thinks that we frequently make pertinent inferences automatically, as when we see or hear only one feature of a thing and immediately know what kind of thing it is, since we know from past experience that this feature is possessed by things of that kind (NTV 45-–46). To exclude all such automatic inference or ‘suggestion’ from past experience, he rules that ‘those things alone are actually and strictly perceived by any sense, which would have been perceived, in case that same sense had then been first conferred on us’ (3D 204). Berkeley clearly employs this supposedly strict (‘in truth and strictness’) or proper notion of ‘seeing or hearing, etc. without inference’—for a start, in the very first argument of the First Dialogue, where he responds to a question about which things may ‘properly be said to be sensible’, by inferring a conclusion about what the senses perceive ‘in truth’, from the fact that they ‘make no inferences’ (3D 174).





2. TACIT PRESUPPOSITIONS: THE STRATEGY OF BERKELEY’S FIRST DIALOGUE




In the first half of the dialogue (3D 175–191), Berkeley patiently pursues one general strategy to show that sensible things exist only in the mind. This argumentative strategy is motivated by an ‘opening argument’ that has perplexed commentators (3D 174-–175). The argument proceeds, first, from the truism that



(0) Sensible things are ‘those things which are perceived by the senses’



(though these need not be the houses, mountains and rivers common sense takes us to perceive). It proceeds, second, from a definition of the technical term “x immediately perceives y”:





Those things are perceived immediately which are perceived without the intervention of others.

The senses make no inferences. By (1) and (2) (sic):

In truth the senses perceive nothing which they do not perceive immediately. By (3) and (0):

Sensible things are those only which are immediately perceived by sense.

We do not immediately perceive by sight any thing beside light, and colours, and figures, nor by hearing any thing but sounds, nor by the palate any thing beside tastes, nor by the smell any thing beside odours, nor by the touch more than tangible qualities. In brief: We immediately perceive nothing beside sensible qualities. (un-argued assumption—sic) By (4) and (5):

Sensible things therefore are nothing else but so many sensible qualities or combinations of sensible qualities.





To show that sensible things exist only in the mind, Berkeley accordingly proceeds to discuss one determinable ‘sensible quality’ after the other, and seeks to show of each that it exists only in the mind. In a departure from Locke’s terminology, all immediately perceivable qualities, both secondary and primary, are ‘sensible’, and Berkeley’s discussion will conclude with the examination of extension, motion, and the tangible quality of solidity (3D 188–191).

Commentators have notoriously been puzzled by two—to us—patent shortcomings of the argument that motivates this strategy: the inference from apparently unrelated premises—(1) and (2)—to the first conclusion, and the assumption, without further argument, of an incredible claim: Pace (5), we would like to think, we do see something beside light, and colours and ‘figures’, namely physical objects which have those colours and shapes, and emit or reflect that light. Similarly, when a coach comes rattling by (Berkeley’s favourite example), we may see and hear the coach, and not merely its colour and shape, and the sound it makes. And usually there is nothing to ‘intervene’ between us and the physical objects (coaches, etc.) we see or hear. We shall see that at both points, in making his first inference and in taking for granted this incredible assumption, Berkeley presupposed what he sets out to show: His entire strategy for showing that the things we immediately perceive by sense exist in the mind presupposes from the start that the things we immediately perceive by sense are sensations or ideas. We are dealing with the phenomenon of belief bias (cp. Chapter 1.3), writ large: belief bias which vitiates both the general argumentative strategy and—as we shall subsequently see—the individual arguments that implement it.

Consider the argument’s first inference. When drawing a conclusion— (3)—about what the senses perceive ‘in truth’ from the premise—(2)—that they ‘make no inferences’, the argument is clearly employing the above notion of perception by sense ‘in truth and strictness’, that is, of ‘perception without inference’. But the conclusion derived is that in truth the senses perceive nothing they do not ‘perceive immediately’, that is, by the previous definition (1), ‘perceive without intervention’. These two notions of perception, ‘without inference’ and ‘without intervention’, are independent and the inference seems to be invalid.

Berkeley’s initial explanation of ‘immediate perception’ suggests how and why he takes the two notions to be related. He introduces the notion of ‘immediate perception’ by defining ‘mediate perception’ as perception ‘not without the intervention of other’ things, and explains this definition through examples (3D 174). In these, things we perceive either ‘signify or suggest to the mind’ things which are imperceptible—as when letters in a book suggest to my mind the notions of God, virtue, truth, etc.—or they allow us to infer the existence of some supposedly imperceptible cause—as when ‘I see one part of the sky red, and another blue, and . . . my reason does thence evidently conclude there must be some cause of that diversity of colours, yet that cause cannot be said to be . . . perceived by the sense of seeing’ but only by reason (ibid.).

These examples may leave us wondering why the letters we are said to perceive should ‘intervene’ between us and the notions they suggest. Nor is it clear why the ‘diversity of colours’ should ‘intervene’ between us and the rising morning sun that presumably causes it (cp. 3D 171). (If still below the horizon, the rising sun is obscured by the Earth, rather than by the colours of the sky; if above the horizon, it is clearly visible in the blue and red— and hence clear—sky.) An answer is suggested by the fact that the things we are said to perceive have precisely the features commonly attributed to ideas of perception: They ‘signify’ or represent other things and are thought to be caused by them. Furthermore, Berkeley will go on to argue that the colours, sounds and heat he here says we perceive without perceiving their causes are in fact ideas or sensations in us. In framing the present examples, Berkeley presumably takes for granted that

When using our senses we perceive ideas in our minds (qua spaces of inner perception), which intervene between the mind (qua organ of inner perception) and whatever physical things (if any) they are caused by and represent.



This version of the early sense-datum doctrine—SD* in Chapter 3.5— appears to render the notions of ‘perception without inference’ and ‘perception without intervention’ co-extensive: We infer the presence of the things we take to populate our environment, from the presence of the intervening ideas we take them to cause, in our minds. Physical objects thus are perceived ‘with the intervention’ of ideas (i.e. mediately) and ‘with an inference’ (i.e. by reason). Since the mind is a transparent medium (Chapter 2.2), the ideas in it, by contrast, are perceived both without the intervention of anything else (immediately) and without any inference (by sense, strictly speaking). Accordingly, Berkeley treats the two notions as co-extensive: ‘Whatever we perceive, is perceived either immediately or mediately: by sense, or by reason’ (3D 205).

Berkeley states or relies on the several elements of the present doctrine, both in the immediate sequel and when introducing the distinction between ‘immediate’ and ‘mediate perception’ in his first published work. In the latter, he regards it as obvious that we immediately perceive by sense (sight) ideas in our minds and assumes that by their intervention we mediately perceive other things:

We must bear in mind that there are two sorts of objects apprehended by the eye, the one primarily and immediately, the other secondarily and by intervention of the former. Those of the first sort neither are, nor appear to be [!], without the mind, or at any distance off’ but are obviously in the mind. (NTV 50)





In the First Dialogue, namely between Berkeley’s spokesman Philonous and the student of natural philosophy Hylas, the examples we have just considered are followed by the opening argument. In justifying its first step—from (1) and (2) to (3) above—Hylas assumes that the things we immediately perceive by sense are ideas, that these ideas are caused in the mind and that their causes are to be inferred:

[I]n truth the senses perceive nothing which they do not perceive immediately: for they make no inferences. The deducing therefore of causes or occasions from effects and appearances, which alone are perceived by sense, entirely relates to reason. (3D 174–175)





Hylas uses the term “occasion” in the then common technical sense, meaning ‘an inactive unthinking being [like a physical object], at the presence whereof God excites ideas in our minds’ (3D 219–220). The ‘effects and appearances’ in question are therefore ideas caused or excited in our minds (with or without Divine intervention). Already Locke uses the term “appearances” to refer to ideas perceived in minds: ‘By determinate, when applied to a simple idea, I mean that simple appearance, which the mind has in its view, or perceives in itself, when that idea is said to be in it’ (EHU p. 13). Locke uses “appearance” also in a more precise sense, to refer to the two-dimensional ‘variety of shadow or colour’ which he takes the senses to introduce into the mind, where the judgement or reason supposedly transforms them into three-dimensional representations (EHU II.ix.8). In the philosophical parlance of the time, ‘appearances’ are ideas.

On the basis of Hylas’ assumptions, the first conclusion of the opening argument—(3) above—can be inferred as follows, and arguably is inferred thus, in the present passage: Whenever we perceive anything at all by sense, we immediately perceive ideas in our minds. From these effects and appearances we deduce what objects in our environment cause or occasion them in our minds. So we only get to know about those external objects by making inferences. But, in truth and strictness, we perceive by sense just what we perceive without making any inferences. In truth, therefore, ideas ‘alone are perceived by sense’ (rather than by reason). In the transparent medium of the mind, ideas are perceived immediately: First, they are perceived immediately in Locke’s intuitive sense of being perceived in the mind, by the mind (EHU II.viii.8), which Hylas seems to use, for example, when contrasting the things ‘we immediately perceive, and that which exists without us’ (3D 182). Second, ideas are perceived immediately in Berkeley’s new sense of being perceived without the intervention of other things. ‘In truth’, therefore, ‘the senses perceive nothing which they do not perceive immediately’. Let’s retain: At bottom, the reason for which we are invited to agree that, strictly speaking, we perceive by sense only what we perceive immediately, is that whenever we perceive anything by sense, we immediately perceive ideas in our minds, and perceive these alone without inference. This much Berkeley himself is convinced of from the start and throughout—‘by our senses we perceive [immediately, of course] only the ideas existing in our minds’ (3D 247)—and takes to be common ground with his adversaries, ‘the materialists’ (3D 248). Arguably, this is his reason for maintaining the first conclusion of his opening argument.

The second major step of the argument proceeds from the puzzling assumption—(5) above—that we immediately perceive by sense only certain qualities, and not the things that have them. Later in the dialogue (3D 204), Berkeley tries to reconcile this assumption with our ordinary way of speaking of ‘seeing a red-hot bar of iron’ (and not merely its red colour) or of ‘hearing a coach’ (and not merely the sound it makes). In an earlier discussion of the latter example, Berkeley indicates why he finds it ‘evident, that in truth and strictness, nothing can be heard but sound’ (3D 204):

Sitting in my study, I hear a coach drive along the street; I look through the casement and see it; I walk out and enter into it; thus, common speech would incline one to think I heard, saw, and touched the same thing, to wit, the coach. It is nevertheless certain, the ideas intromitted by each sense are widely different and distinct from each other; but having been observed constantly to go together, they are spoken of as one and the same thing. (NTV 46)





The reason why I can only hear the sound of the coach but not the coach (as ‘common speech would incline one to think’) is that the ideas ‘intromitted’ by each sense, that is, the ideas allowed to enter, let in, or admitted by it, namely into the mind, are distinct from each other. In fact, whenever things are ‘being let into the mind [in] two ways’ (NTV 48), they are distinct objects of (inner) perception (NTV 49).

What Berkeley finds ‘certain’ here is the essence of Locke’s account of how the mind comes to be furnished with ideas from without (‘external sensation’, Chapter 3.5):

There are some ideas, which have admittance only through one sense, which is peculiarly adapted to receive them. Thus light and colours . . . come in only by the eyes: all kinds of noises, sounds, and tones only by the ears: the several tastes and smells, by the nose and palate. (EHU II.iii.1)





From this and the core claim that whenever we perceive anything at all by sense, we ‘immediately perceive by sense’ ideas in our minds, namely ideas coming in from without, Berkeley presumably infers that we immediately perceive by sight only those ideas that come into the mind by the eyes, that we immediately perceive by hearing only those ideas that come in by the ears, and so on and so forth. The present conclusion is the puzzling assumption at issue: ‘[W]e immediately perceive by sight’ nothing ‘beside light, and colours, and figures . . . by hearing [nothing] but sounds’ etc. (3D 175). Together with the conclusion that ‘in truth the senses perceive nothing which they do not perceive immediately’, this assumption entails the more succinct claim that ‘in truth and strictness, nothing can be heard but sound’ (3D 204, cp. 3D 174).

In the conceptual framework of the time, colours, figures, sounds, tastes and smells fall into place as accidents, things which can only exist in others (Chapter 3.1). These other things, which ‘subsist of themselves and are the subject of accidents’ are substances; physical objects and minds alike fall into this category. The objects of immediate perception, which alone qualify as ‘sensible things’, thus are distinct accidents which come into the mind through the various senses. One might call these things either ‘ideas’ or ‘qualities’, depending upon what substance—mind or matter—they are in, but Berkeley calls them ‘qualities’ also when asserting that they exist in the mind (e.g., in 3D 187–188). Several such accidents can combine in the mind to form a complex idea. Hence we can immediately perceive sensible qualities and their combinations. By (natural philosophers’) common opinion, accidents can also inhere in material or corporeal substances and thus make up a physical object. But the only things we can immediately perceive are sensible qualities. ‘It seems therefore, that if you take away all sensible qualities, there remains nothing sensible’ (3D 174). We can immediately perceive only these accidents but not the substances they supposedly exist in: ‘Sensible things therefore are nothing else but so many sensible qualities, or combinations of such qualities’ (3D 175)—and do not include substances.

Accordingly, Berkeley sets out to establish that sensible things exist only in the mind by showing that the ‘sensible qualities’ (i.e. the qualities we perceive immediately and by sense, strictly speaking) are in the mind:



(SD+) The qualities we perceive by sense (without inference) and immediately (without intervention of anything else) are ideas we immediately perceive in the mind.



This is the early sense-datum doctrine with a twist. The latter (SD* in Chapter 3.5) states that whenever we perceive by sense a quality (or anything else, for that matter), we immediately perceive an idea in the mind. The present twist is that the idea we immediately perceive is identified with the quality, as perceived immediately. As we have just seen, Berkeley takes for granted Locke’s ‘raw’ or ‘unsublimated’ account of sense-perception or ‘external sensation’: Whenever we perceive a quality by sense, this quality has come, through some sense, into our mind, where we perceive it (ES in Chapter 3.5). The idea we perceive in the mind thus is a quality that has come in from outside.

To establish that sensible things exist only in the mind, Berkeley seeks to show—SD+—that each kind of quality which ‘we immediately perceive by our senses’ (3D 180) is an idea in the mind. To show this, he ‘goes through all the qualities by name one after the other’ (3D 199) and ‘examine[s] them in order’ (3D 179): He considers, in turn, temperature, or heat and cold (3D 175–199), tastes (179–180), odours (180–181), sounds (181–183), colours (183–187), figure and extension (188–190), motion (190), and solidity, or hardness or resistance (191).

We have found that the argument that motivates this strategy tacitly presupposes, first, the early sense-datum doctrine—SD*—that whenever we perceive anything by sense, we immediately perceive ideas in our minds and, second, Locke’s account of external sensation—ES—which already comprises the conclusion—SD+—to be established by considering each quality in turn. We shall find that these doctrines are tacitly presupposed also by the several ‘quality-specific’ arguments that are to establish them. Berkeley takes these claims for granted, from the start and throughout.

We have seen (in Chapter 3.5) that these claims can be obtained through picture-driven lines of thought. We will see that Berkeley’s ‘quality-specific’ arguments rely on various old acquaintances: not only on the doctrines he seeks to justify but also on some of the picture-driven conclusions we have seen to lead up to them. We will thus have reason to believe that Berkeley was under the spell of the same philosophical pictures as Locke, was led to in many ways similar views about sense-perception, and found them so compelling that he unwittingly presupposed them in the very arguments he constructed in their support: adherence to philosophical pictures (Sections 3–4) culminating in belief bias (Sections 5–6). Indeed, his adherence to philosophical pictures has Berkeley employ, at key points of his argument, a patently unsound ‘introspective’ method to which we now turn.





3. PICTURE-MOTIVATED LINGUISTIC INNOVATION:

AN INTROSPECTIVE METHOD




Under the spell of philosophical pictures which include the perceptual pictures of knowledge and of reflection, we posit in us a space and organ of inner perception and take for granted that knowing, understanding, remembering, thinking-about and thinking-of all involve perceiving things in this ‘mind’, with this ‘mind’ (Chapter 1). The moment those important intellectual activities and achievements have thus been assimilated to each other, philosophers tend to press a supposedly suitable familiar term into service as a generic label for them. An early modern favourite is the term “thinking”: To ‘think’ is to perform any intellectual activity which essentially involves perceiving things in the mind, with the mind. In this new, supposedly generic, sense the verb then applied, without further reflection or other ado, to all activities which we ordinarily speak of as involving some kind of ‘thinking’. As a result, all these activities are then taken to essentially involve perceiving ideas in the mind, with the mind.

Thus Locke introduces the new generic sense when he declares ‘perception, or thinking’ to be one of the ‘two great and principal actions of the mind’ (EHU II.vi.2), then resolves to use the term “thinking” for ‘that sort of operation of the mind about its ideas, wherein the mind is active’—in contrast with ‘bare naked’ or sense-perception, which is purely passive (EHU II.ix.1)—and thus comes to treat remembrance, recollection and contemplation as well as reverie, dreaming and ‘ecstasy’ as ‘modes of thinking’ (EHU II.xix.1), adding that there are many further such modes besides (EHU II.xix.2). Locke thus seems to take for granted that the new generic label applies to all intellectual activities which we ordinarily speak of as involving some kind of ‘thinking’—and that all of them involve perceiving things in the mind. Above (Section 1), we have seen that the mere craving for generality can lead to unwitting linguistic innovation followed by spontaneous projection or transfer of ordinary implications. We now see that adherence to philosophical pictures can lead to equally unwitting linguistic innovation, followed by spontaneous projection of picture-driven implications. This extends the scope of the mind beyond the target-domains of perceptual metaphors.

Imagining is a case in point. It is a kind of ‘thinking’ in an ordinary sense: The feats of imagination include thinking that things might be thus-and-so under certain circumstances, thinking how things might then be, thinking of things that might be, and thinking of what might come, etc. The process outlined may lead thinkers under the spell of the perceptual pictures we have considered to take for granted that imagining essentially involves inner perception of things in the mind and ‘operations about ideas’ (PHK 2).

In particular once we have adopted them, picture-driven conclusions we find intuitively compelling get presupposed in the interpretation of talk about the topic at issue. Such biased interpretation has us populate the mind with various different familiar things: the thoughts we have when we think, the pains we feel, the memories we have, and the fancies of our imagination. Thus a motley crew gets subsumed under the technical term “idea” (qua what is perceived in the mind). This is nicely illustrated by Berkeley’s explanation of how to best follow his arguments, where he equates ‘attaining a train of thoughts’ with framing and considering a stream of ideas:

Whoever therefore designs to read the following sheets, I entreat him to make my words the occasion of his own thinking, and endeavour to attain the same train of thoughts in reading, that I had in writing them. By this means it will be easy for him to discover the truth or falsity of what I say. He will be out of all danger of being deceived by my words, and I do not see how he can be led into an error by considering his own naked, undisguised ideas. (PHK I25)



The reader is asked to frame and consider the same stream of ideas in his mind that Berkeley perceived in his, when writing down his thoughts. Given its prominent position—these are the closing words of the Introduction to the Principles of Human Knowledge (henceforward: PHK I)—we need to take this request seriously.

In further reflection about the mind, we tend to assimilate some of its contents to others, or all ‘ideas’ to some. In particular, when common sense, as implicit in ordinary language, appears to credit some of the things we have placed in the mind with features which an intuitive line of reasoning about the mind attributes to all its contents, we are prone to assimilate all these things to the former models, in ‘paradigmatic assimilation’. The most basic line of reasoning about the mind, which leads to the introduction of the early modern notion, has thinkers conclude that the things in the mind are perceived (i) at some location, (ii) with some organ of sense (Chapter 1.3). Ordinary language, current or early modern, takes none of the things commonly placed in the mind to be somehow ‘perceived’ both at some location and with some organ of sense. But it credits some of them at least with some sort of location: It credits corporeal sensations with a place in the body at which they are ‘felt’—though not ‘with’ any organ of sense. And it speaks of our ‘seeing’ or ‘having images’ ‘before’—though not with—‘the inner eye’ in some, though not all cases of remembering and imagining: When imagining what the consequences of higher inflation would be, we typically spell them out verbally and without visualising anything in any way; but when trying to imagine what she would look like in that dress, we will often frame such an ‘image’. Similarly, I do not visualise anything when remembering when the battle of Waterloo was fought, but can still ‘see’ my grandmother’s Christmas tree ‘before my inner eye’. Thus many philosophers assimilate all ‘ideas’ or things perceived in the mind, with the mind, either to corporeal sensations like pains (in some contexts) or to imagination- or memory-images (in others). The latter assimilation arguably informs already the choice of the label “idea”, which was still used in the literal sense of ‘image’ in the 17th century. The two different models are then frequently treated as if they amounted to pretty much the same, as Berkeley does in speaking of ‘ideas or sensations, call them which you will’ (3D 215).

In the present passages, where ‘pure’ philosophical reflection is at issue, Berkeley seems to think of ideas, first and foremost, as images: The ‘naked, undisguised ideas’ we are to ‘consider’ (PHK I25) are to be considered ‘divested of words’ and made an object of ‘attentive perception’ (PHK I22). Presumably, they are mental images. To avoid unnecessary misunderstandings and mistakes, Berkeley enjoins the reader to try, at several key points of his argument, to form the same mental images he, Berkeley, had in setting it down. This is to provide plain and incontrovertible proof for some of his philosophical claims:

[S]o long as I confine my thoughts to my own ideas, divested of words, I do not see how I can easily be mistaken. The objects I consider, I clearly and adequately know. I cannot be deceived in thinking I have an idea which I have not. It is not possible for me to imagine, that any of my own ideas are alike or unlike, that are not truly so. To [i] discern the agreements and disagreements there are between my ideas, to [ii] see what ideas are included in any compound idea and what not, there is nothing more requisite, than an attentive perception of what passes in my own understanding.’ (PHK I22)





The first lines rely on the picture-driven conclusion that the mind is a transparent medium (Chapter 2.2). The final lines refer to a method Berkeley employs at core points of his argument; repeatedly, Berkeley invites the reader to replicate thought-experiments: We are to frame certain ideas, so as to be able to simply see by considering them whether certain philosophical claims are true or false.

Some potentially pertinent moves fit Berkeley’s present words only in a metaphorical sense of the latter. For example: When, in the later Dialogues, Philonous asks Hylas how pleasure and pain can ‘agree to . . . unthinking [i.e., unperceiving] corporeal substances’ (3D 180), Hylas presumably is meant to ‘discern the disagreement’—(i) above—between the ideas of pleasure and pain and that of insensitive bodies. But it is hard to see what kind of image, mental or other, could make one aware of the mistake Berkeley takes to be involved in crediting such a body with pleasure or pain. By contrast, we shall see in the next section that when Philonous asks Hylas to ‘frame’ certain ‘ideas’ (e.g. 3D 177, 194), namely in order to determine— (ii) above—what is and what is not included in a given idea, Berkeley does want his reader to form certain mental images.

Unwitting picture-motivated linguistic innovation followed by spontaneous projection or transfer of picture-motivated implications leads thinkers who are under the spell of perceptual pictures of intellectual activities to assume that various kinds of ‘thinking’, including imagining and conceiving, essentially involve perceiving ideas in the mind. Paradigmatic assimilation then has them think of such ideas, first and foremost, as images: To conceive or imagine something is to form an image of that thing. Hence if we cannot form an image of some thing that satisfies the description “ . . .”, then a thing which is . . . is inconceivable (cp. PHK I13). The latter term usually carries the implication of impossibility. Hence, one may conclude, if we cannot form a certain image, then a certain kind of thing cannot possibly exist.

At some key points of his argument, Berkeley draws precisely this wild conclusion: He tries—and fails—to form certain mental images, invites the reader to try as well, expects him to do no better, and infers from our apparent inability to form such images that certain things cannot possibly exist. The process we have reconstructed in this section can explain why Berkeley proceeds in this manner and believes that these thought-experiments provide straightforward and compelling proof of substantive metaphysical claims. Let’s now consider how he employs this ‘method’ in arguing for two fundamental claims: first, the claim that all sensible qualities have to exist at the same place—so that if the secondary qualities exist only in the mind, as already Locke and others had maintained, then the primary qualities cannot exist anywhere else, either (Section 4); second, the claim that the secondary qualities of heat and cold, taste and smell, do indeed exist only in the mind (Sections 5–6).





4. FAMILIAR PICTURES:

BERKELEY AGAINST ABSTRACTION




Berkeley’s defence of these two claims relies on a demonstration that we cannot form ideas by ‘abstraction’. Berkeley presents this demonstration in the introduction to the Principles, as a prerequisite for understanding his subsequent arguments (PHK I6–I13). The demonstration is to refute an account Berkeley takes to ‘have occasioned innumerable errors and difficulties’ (PHK I6) and attributes to Locke (PHK I11, I13), presumably among many others. Berkeley agrees with its first statement but rejects the bulk of the account (introduced below with the words ‘But we are told’):

It is agreed on all hands, that the qualities or modes of things do never really exist each of them apart by itself, and separated from all others, but are mixed as it were, and blended together, several in the same object. But we are told, the mind being able to consider each quality singly, or abstracted from those other qualities with which it is united, does by that means form to itself abstract ideas. For example, there is perceived by sight an object extended, coloured, and moved: this mixed or compound idea the mind resolving into its simple, constituent parts, and viewing each by itself, exclusive of the rest, does frame the abstract ideas of extension, colour, and motion. (PHK I7)



This exposition is couched in the terminology of qualities and other accidents existing in objects or substances, whose development was driven by a philosophical picture we have become familiar with: the ‘part-whole’ or ‘mereological picture’ of qualities (Chapters 1.2 and 1.5). The first claim, which Berkeley endorses, arguably is so uncontroversial at the time because it is conflated with an actual truism, T1 below, interpreted in the light of the mereological picture:



(T1) Any sensible object has got several sensible qualities: colour, size, shape, motion, etc.



(M) Qualities are things literally located at some place, namely in the objects to which they belong (like parts). By (T1) and (M):



(C1) Several sensible qualities exist somewhere in the same sensible object.



This picture-driven conclusion is at odds with a conceptual truism we have already encountered (in Chapter 3.1):



(CT) Qualities do not stand in any kind of spatial relations: They have no location (neither in the object nor anywhere else) and do not stand in spatial relations to each other (or anything else).



The usual response to such conflicts is sublimation, for example in the shape of a gloss that indicates that something is not to be taken quite literally (Chapters 1.3 and 3.1). This backpedalling seems to occur here after a further conclusion is inferred from the conflicting parties, namely from the picture-driven conclusion and a truism reflecting (CT):



(T2) A quality is not here in an object and another over there (above or beside it). By (T2) and (C1):



(C2) Several sensible qualities exist not at specific but at diffuse locations in the same object, inextricably mixed and blended together.



The “as it were” in Berkeley’s statement of this conclusion indicates that something here (though it is not clear quite what) is not to be taken literally (while it is left unclear how we are to understand it instead). We are, it seems, dealing with a conclusion driven by the part-whole picture, which strikes its proponents, including Berkeley, as so compelling that they maintain it in the face of a conflict with conceptual truisms, upon negative sublimation.

This reasoning also leads to a related but distinct conclusion, a claim Berkeley regards as rather obvious:

I appeal to any man’s experience, whether the visible extension of any object does not appear as near to him as the colour of that object; nay, whether they do not both seem to be in the very same place. (NTV 43, cp. 3D 194)





What is in fact obvious is that objects which have a colour also have a visible extension. By (M) above, an object that looks to have two qualities, looks to have two qualities somewhere in it. Since—(CT) above— qualities do not stand in any spatial relations, we do not want to say that, say, the visible extension appears further away from (or closer to) us than the colour. And since—T2 above—a quality is not here in an object and another over there, the two visible qualities must be inextricably mixed and ‘coexist’ throughout the object (3D 194), the claim that they also look or seem to share ‘the very same place’ is intuitively more compelling than the suggestion that they look or seem to be at different places.

While Berkeley goes along with the opening sentence of the present passage—PHK I7—he denies that we can frame ideas by abstraction, in the proper sense of that term (PHK I10), that is by ‘precision or mental separation’ of ideas (PHK I9). Such ‘mental separation’ is meant to work in either of two ways: First, we are taken to perceive by sense an object with several qualities, then resolve the complex idea of it into its constituent parts, and thus obtain abstract ideas specifically of one quality and no other—even if this quality can never exist without others (PHK I7). Second, we are held to observe several determinate qualities (like red, blue or white), leave out all differences between them, retain ‘that only which is common to all’, and thus frame ‘an idea of [e.g.] colour in abstract which is neither red, nor blue, nor white, nor any other determinate colour’ (PHK I8).

To show that we are not capable of either feat, Berkeley proceeds from a self-test of what he is able to imagine:

[F]or myself I find indeed I have a faculty of imagining or representing to myself the ideas of those particular things I have perceived and of variously compounding and dividing them [e.g. of dividing the idea of a man’s body and thus imagining individual parts like hands or eyes]. But then whatever hand or eye I imagine, it must have some particular shape or colour . . . And it is equally impossible for me to form the abstract idea of motion distinct from the body moving, and which is neither swift nor slow, [neither] curvilinear nor rectilinear; and the like may be said of all other abstract general ideas whatsoever. (PHK I10)





It is of course easy to imagine a hand without imagining it to be pale or tawny or any other particular colour: When, in making up a story, we say, ‘Suddenly a hand grabbed him’ and proceed without giving any further details about that hand, we are doing just that. Many commentators have— rightly, we shall argue—concluded that Berkeley takes for granted that to imagine something is to form an image ‘before the inner eye’. For of course an image of a hand or any other body must ‘give it some colour’ (PHK 10); even though we can in fact think of each quality by itself, we indeed cannot ‘view each quality by itself’ (cp. PHK I7). Similarly, the ordinary English concept of ‘motion’ allows us to say that a body moves without implying anything about what path it describes or how fast it moves; we clearly have, and hence can form, a general concept of motion which ‘leaves out . . . that which is peculiar to each’ particular motion and ‘retain[s] that only which is common to all’ motions (as the account to be refuted has it, PHK I9). By contrast, we cannot form an image of motion except by visualising an object which moves, and moves at a determinate speed, in a particular way (rotates, or falls, etc). In the first sentence quoted, Berkeley explicitly equates imagining something with ‘representing to oneself the idea of’ that thing. Arguably, he simultaneously assimilates such ideas to images, and forming ideas to forming mental images. I.e.: He explicitly makes the first, and probably also the second of the two moves we have reconstructed above (Section 3).

As a result, Berkeley concludes that at any rate he (‘I’) is incapable of the exploits at issue:

I deny that I can abstract one from another, or conceive separately, those qualities which it is impossible should exist so separated; or that I can frame a general notion by abstracting from particulars in the manner aforesaid. (PHK I10)





In view of an exegetical controversy, note that Berkeley does not adduce the fact that certain qualities cannot exist separately as a reason why he cannot conceive them separately. In the first line Berkeley presumably means those qualities which cannot ‘really exist’ separately ‘in nature’ (cp. 3D 194, see below). What he denies is the view that even when qualities cannot be instantiated separately in nature, ideas of these qualities can be conceived separately in the mind, a view he previously put into the mouths of his opponents with the words: ‘Not that it is possible for colour or motion to exist without extension: but only that the mind can frame to it self by abstraction the idea of colour exclusive of extension, and of motion exclusive of both colour and extension’ (PHK I7). To show this mental feat impossible, Berkeley does not invoke the fact his opponents, he states, deem compatible with it, but performs a self-test which establishes precisely what he concludes: that he (‘I’) is incapable of this and a related feat. ‘Whether others have this wonderful faculty of abstracting their ideas, they best can tell’ (PHK I10): Each reader has to replicate Berkeley’s self-test: to ‘look into his thoughts, and there try whether he has, or can attain to have, an idea that shall correspond with the description’ at issue (PHK I13). This is to reveal that we cannot frame anything that would qualify, for example, as an ‘idea of colour exclusive of extension’ (cp. PHK I7) or as an ‘idea of colour in abstract which is neither red, nor blue, nor white, nor any other determinate colour’ (PHK I8). While it is not as obvious as Berkeley takes the present point to be that the general concept of ‘colour’ cannot be described thus (it is, after all, the concept not of any determinate colour but of colour in general), it does seem obvious that any image of colour must be of (something of) some determinate colour. Presumably, Berkeley’s—sole— support for his present conclusion consists in tests of what mental images can be formed.

From this conclusion and the conception of qualities as ‘coexisting parts’ we considered a moment ago, Berkeley proceeds to derive a metaphysical conclusion: The so-called ‘primary and original qualities’ must be where the ‘secondary qualities’ are, which already Locke had assumed exist only in the mind (Chapter 3). The argument is briefly sketched in the New Theory of Vision (NTV 43), concisely stated in the Principles (PHK 10), and developed at slightly more length in the First Dialogue, in this exchange between Philonous (P) and Hylas (H):

P: . . . try if you can frame the idea of any figure, abstracted from all particularities of size, or even from other sensible qualities. H: Let me think a little—I do not find that I can. P: And can you think it possible, that should really exist in nature, which implies a repugnancy in its conception? H: By no means. P: Since therefore it is impossible even for the mind to disunite the ideas of extension and motion from all other sensible qualities, does it not follow, that where the one exist, there necessarily the other exist likewise? H: It should seem so. (3D 194)





Inserting tacit assumptions (below, as throughout, in square brackets) until we have obtained a logically valid argument, and spelling out the implications merely hinted at, we obtain:

We cannot frame the idea of any [extended and moving] figure abstracted [i.e. separated] from all other sensible qualities [like colour].

When we cannot frame the idea of some thing, then this thing implies a repugnancy in its conception.

That which implies a repugnancy in its conception cannot possibly exist in nature. By [2] and (3):

When we cannot frame the idea of something, it cannot possibly exist in nature. By (1) and [4]:

Extension and motion cannot possibly exist in nature separately from other sensible qualities (like colour).

When we cannot frame the idea of an extended and moving figure separated from all other sensible qualities, then it is not only impossible that the qualities of extension and motion exist in nature separately from all other sensible qualities but impossible even for the mind to separate (disunite) the ideas of extension and motion from the ideas of colour, etc. By (1) and [6]:

It is impossible even for the mind to disunite the ideas of extension and motion from the other sensible qualities.

When it is impossible even for the mind to disunite some things from others, then the former necessarily exist where [at the same place as] the latter exist. By (7) and [8]:

Where the other sensible qualities exist, there necessarily also extension and motion exist.





The argument is to justify the strong conclusion that all sensible qualities, primary and secondary alike, must necessarily exist at the same place. The weaker conclusion that they actually exist at the same place is evident the moment we open our eyes: ‘Besides, if you will trust your senses, is it not plain all sensible qualities coexist, or to them [i.e. to your senses], appear as being in the same place?’ (3D 194, cp. NTV 43)

Recall the claim from which Galileo proceeded in arguing for ‘secondary qualities’ (Chapter 3.2): ‘When ever I conceive of any piece of matter . . . I feel myself necessarily compelled to conceive along with it, that it is bounded and shaped’ and has all the other primary qualities as well, but do not have to conceive of it as having colour, heat, smell, or taste’ (SAG 27–28). The physicist’s concept of a physical body implies that it has the primary qualities, but not that it has any others (which it may possess as a matter of empirical rather than of conceptual fact). So, already in Berkeley’s time, physicists had explicitly formulated a concept or notion of a body which can be ‘framed’ without ‘giving bodies some colour or other sensible quality’. By contrast, it is plausible to think that without giving a body some colour we cannot form an image of it, neither on paper nor in the mind. Here—in (1)—as before, Berkeley presumably employs the term “idea” in the then common sense of ‘image’ and proceeds from an experiment, whose results, he thinks, anyone can replicate, namely by trying whether he can form a mental image which answers to a particular description (here: ‘idea of a figure without any determinate size or other sensible quality’).

Our analysis of the reasoning that leads up to the present conclusions provides us with significant evidence of adherence to philosophical pictures. In this section, we have seen that the lines of thought leading to the weaker conclusion that primary and secondary qualities actually exist in the same place and the related conclusion that all sensible qualities are inextricably mingled together (C2 above, PHK I7) involve leaps of thought driven by the mereological picture of qualities. And also the argument for the stronger conclusion speaks of qualities as being ‘separated’ or ‘inseparably united’ (PHK 10) or impossible to ‘disunite’ (3D 194), and relies on presuppositions—like [8] above—which apply these terms to qualities in a quite literal sense, with the spatial implication that qualities which are ‘inseparably united’ or ‘impossible to disunite’ must exist in the same place, either in material bodies or in that space of inner perception, the mind. In the previous section, we have explained how adherence to perceptual pictures of intellectual activities may lead thinkers, first, to assimilate ideas to images—as Berkeley does again in (1) above—second, to assume that all imagining and conceiving involves the forming of images—as Berkeley assumes in his ‘refutation’ of abstract ideas (PHK I10)—and, third, to take for granted that if we cannot form certain images certain things are incon-ceivable—[ 2] above—and are so in a sense which implies that—(3) above— those things are impossible.

We have thus obtained further evidence that Berkeley, like Locke before him, adhered to the mereological picture of qualities and to perceptual pictures of intellectual activities and achievements. We have already seen (in Chapter 3.5) how lines of thought driven by these pictures lead to the early sense-datum doctrine and the account of ‘external sensation’ we then found Berkeley to rely on (Section 2). Such core beliefs tend to be justified, if at all, by arguments which presuppose either the conclusions to be established or crucial consequences derived from them (strong belief bias). We now turn to Berkeley’s preferred arguments for the sense-datum doctrine— and will find them a case in point.





5. BELIEF BIAS: THE PLEASURE/PAIN ARGUMENTS




Berkeley makes one brief (NTV 44) and one sustained effort to justify his fundamental doctrine—SD+—that the qualities we perceive by sense (without inference) and immediately (without intervention of anything else) are ideas we immediately perceive in the mind, and the conclusion that these objects of immediate perception exist only in the mind. He makes the sustained effort in the first half of the First Dialogue (3D 175–191), where he seeks to show this for one quality after another (Section 2)—and discusses both secondary and primary qualities, in spite of the ‘shortcut’ afforded by the arguments we have just considered. For most qualities he gives more than one argument to establish that the doctrine applies to it. Most arguments belong to one of three types: ‘arguments from science’, ‘arguments from relativity’, and ‘pleasure/pain arguments’ building on ‘anti-abstractionist arguments’ (for short: ‘PP/AA-arguments’).

The first two are descendants of the arguments for the claim that colour and the other secondary qualities do not exist in physical bodies, previously rehearsed by Galileo, Boyle or Locke (Chapter 3.4). Berkeley arguably rehearses them only due to their potential popularity, for persuasive effect rather than justification, and does not rely on them himself: At the beginning of the Second Dialogue, he viciously criticises the ‘arguments from science’ and the ‘scientific’ explanations of sensations these are based on (3D 209–210, cp. 3D 242). And elsewhere he states that the ‘arguments from relativity’ do not manage to establish that sensible qualities exist in the mind (PHK 15, cp. PC 265). By contrast, Berkeley does seem to have espoused arguments of the third type, on which we therefore now focus: They rely on the prominently presented considerations about abstraction we have considered a moment ago; and they are given pride of place in the Dialogues, where the first ‘quality-specific’ arguments are of the PP/ AA-variety.

Berkeley offers such arguments about intense heat (3D 176), intense cold (178), tastes (179–180), and smells (181). When present to an intense degree, these qualities are indeed associated with certain bodily sensations, namely ‘annexed to . . . pleasure and pain’ (3D 191): Think of the burning pain you may feel in your hand when you hold it close to a fire, of the unpleasant burning sensation you may feel in your nostrils when exposed to a strong acid smell, or of how a cheese with a strong taste may cause a stabbing or tingling sensation on your tongue. The pleasure/pain arguments consider qualities as ‘things we immediately perceive by our senses’ (3D 180). Also known as ‘arguments from identification’, they explicitly assume that the present qualities, as immediately perceived by sense, are ideas or sensations we perceive in ourselves, namely that they are identical with those actual bodily sensations: Those qualities ‘are so many pleasing or displeasing sensations’ (3D 181). The arguments then merely bring out the apparently trivial consequence that these qualities can exist only in the mind and not in an unperceiving being or material substance. Thus Hylas sums up the argument about heat (developed on 3D 176):

[B]ecause intense heat is nothing else but a particular kind of painful sensation; and pain cannot exist but in a perceiving being; it follows that no intense heat can really exist in an unperceiving corporeal substance. (3D 177)





The crucial initial assumption then is supported by the ‘anti-abstractionist argument’ we shall consider in the next section.

In the case of temperature, the assumption is supported also by another kind of pleasure/pain argument: By establishing that intense heat, as immediately perceived by sense, is a particular kind of painful sensation, this argument is to show that intense heat, as immediately perceived by sense, is a sensation or idea. The argument for this very specific instance of the ‘embellished sense-datum doctrine’—SD+ —is developed in an exchange which immediately follows the argument Hylas sums up in the passage we have just considered:

H: I fear I was out in yielding intense heat to be a pain. It should seem rather, that pain is something distinct from heat, and the consequence or effect of it. P: 1Upon putting your hand near the fire, do you perceive one simple sensation, or two distinct sensations? H: But one simple sensation. P: 2Is not the heat immediately perceived? H: It is. P: And the pain? H: True. P: Seeing therefore (2)they are both immediately perceived at the same time, and (1)the fire affects you only with one simple, or uncompounded idea, it follows 3that this same simple idea is both the intense heat immediately perceived, and the pain; and, consequently, 4that the intense heat immediately perceived, is nothing distinct from a particular sort of pain. (3D 176)



This argument presupposes what it is meant to show. When you put your unprotected hand near the fire, you perceive the heat outside your body immediately and by sense, strictly speaking: As we would ordinarily judge, you perceive that heat without the intervention of any other thing or quality; and you do not have to infer the presence of this heat from anything else (as you might have to infer the presence of a fire next door from the heat you feel through the wall). Provided the heat is intense, you feel, at the same time, a sensation of pain. This pain you are assumed to perceive in the transparent medium of your mind, whose contents you perceive immediately, without intervention of anything else. So you immediately perceive both a heat outside your body and a pain within yourself. In fact, this pain is the only sensation you then feel, and we may well regard it as ‘simple, or uncompounded’. But it does not follow from this that the heat is identical with this sensation. This only follows if we tacitly assume in addition that the heat we perceive by sense and without intervention of anything else is a sensation or idea. Which is of course what the argument is really meant to show. Only by presupposing an instance of the sense-datum doctrine—SD+—can we infer from the present premises the conclusion that is meant to support this doctrine:

When you put your hand near the fire, the fire affects you only with one simple, uncompounded sensation or idea. (1 above)

When you put the hand near the fire, the heat and the pain are both immediately perceived at the same time. (2 above)

The objects you then perceive immediately—both the pain and the heat—are sensations or ideas which something else—viz. the fire— affects you with. (tacit assumption) By (2) and [3]:

When you put the hand near the fire, the fire affects you at the same time with the sensations or ideas of heat and of pain. (tacitly inferred conclusion) By (1) and [4]:

The sensation of heat and the sensation of pain are the same simple, uncompounded sensation or idea. (3 above) Therefore:

The heat immediately perceived is nothing distinct from a particular sort of pain. (4 above)





With the tacit assumption [3], Berkeley presupposes that when we put the hand near the fire and perceive the heat through the pertinent sense, we perceive the heat immediately, without intervention, but do so only in the mind, where the heat perceived is present as an idea or sensation—the specific instance of the sense-datum doctrine SD+.

When we combine the two ‘pleasure/pain arguments’, we obtain one overall argument, which Hylas sums up nicely for the case of intense cold, of which ‘the same’ is said to be true as of intense heat (3D 178): ‘An intense degree of cold is a pain; for to feel a very great cold, is to perceive a great pain: it cannot therefore exist without the mind’ (3D 178). In proper order:





To feel a great cold is to perceive a pain. For this reason:

An intense cold is a pain. Therefore:

An intense cold cannot exist without the mind.





The first assumption, which is not supported by explicit argument, is entailed by the kind of observation we have just considered with respect to heat, and an instance of the early sense-datum doctrine (SD* in Chapter 3.5): To feel a cold is to (immediately) perceive a sensation or idea in the mind. But, we observe, when we feel a great cold we feel a pain, and this pain is the only sensation we then feel. Therefore this pain must be the sensation or idea to (immediately) perceive which in the mind is to feel that cold. That is: To feel a great cold is to perceive this pain. But even if perceiving a pain thus is constitutive of feeling a great cold, it does not follow that the sensation perceived within is the quality we ordinarily take to be perceived without. Presumably, therefore, Berkeley here proceeds from the tacit assumption of the ‘embellished’ sense-datum doctrine—SD+—that the cold we perceive by sense and immediately is a sensation or idea we immediately perceive in the mind. Having concluded that everything we perceive by sense is perceived immediately (Section 2), he thus assumes that the cold we feel is a sensation we perceive in the mind when we feel that temperature. Together with the present observation, this entails both that to feel a great cold is to perceive a pain and that an intense cold is a pain. Arguably, Berkeley again presupposes the very doctrine—SD+—he means to establish.

To sum up: Together, the pleasure/pain arguments are meant to support the doctrine—SD+—that the qualities we perceive immediately and by sense are sensations or ideas, and that they exist only in the mind. To establish this, these arguments seize on cases of sense-perception where the perception of certain qualities is indeed associated with characteristic sensations. By inferences which proceed from a trivial observation and the very doctrine to be established, the present arguments then conclude that certain qualities we perceive immediately and by sense must be these familiar sensations, that these familiar sensations are examples of the perceived qualities/ideas at issue. Biased reasoning thus provides us with bogus examples of ‘sensations immediately perceived by sense’, which in turn appear to bear out the doctrine at issue—a clear case of belief bias. To see whether it is pernicious in the present case and has Berkeley endorse conclusions for which he has no warrant, we need to turn to the ‘anti-abstractionist arguments’ on which Berkeley ultimately bases the present conclusions.





6. PERNICIOUS BELIEF BIAS:

THE ANTI-ABSTRACTIONIST ARGUMENTS




The pleasure/pain arguments about intense heat and cold (3D 176–178), tastes (179–180), and smells (181), all build on the same two ‘anti-abstractionist’ arguments, which immediately follow the exchange about intense heat quoted above:

P: 1Again, try in your thoughts, Hylas, if you can conceive a vehement sensation to be without pain, or pleasure. H: I cannot. P: 2Or can you frame to yourself an idea of sensible pain or pleasure in general, abstracted from every particular idea of heat, cold, tastes, smells, etc.? H: I do not find that I can. P: 3Does it not therefore follow, that sensible pain is nothing distinct from those sensations or ideas, in an intense degree? H: It is undeniable; and to speak the truth, I begin to suspect that 4a very great heat cannot exist but in a mind perceiving it . . . A very violent and painful heat cannot exist without [i.e. outside] the mind. (3D 176–177)



This conclusion—tacitly along with analogous conclusions about intense cold, tastes, and smells, etc.—is derived independently from two premises (1 and 2 above). Hylas satisfies himself of the truth of these two premises by ‘trying in his thoughts’ to engage in two kinds of ‘abstraction’. Arguably, these are the two ‘proper acceptations of abstraction’ of which Berkeley had declared himself incapable (PHK I10, cp. Section 4 above): Arguably, the present premises are to be established by trying, respectively, to ‘conceive separately’ two things—‘vehement sensations’ and ‘pain or pleasure’— ‘which it is impossible should exist so separated’ in nature and, second, to ‘frame a general notion by abstracting from particulars’ (cp. PHK I10). Let’s consider these two efforts in turn.

As we have seen, early modern philosophers are prone to think of ‘ideas’ as either images or corporeal sensations like pains (Section 3). In his most extensive discussion of abstraction (PHK I6–21), Berkeley thought of ideas, first and foremost, as mental images, and of ‘conceiving ideas’ as forming mental images (Section 4). Now he thinks of ideas as sensations, and of ‘conceiving ideas’ as imagining or calling up sensations: The ‘vehement sensations’ at issue (in sentence 1 of the above quote from 3D 176–177) are ‘those sensations or ideas’ which the conclusion refers to (in 3), namely ‘particular idea[s] of heat, cold, tastes, smells, in an intense degree’ (in 2). An ‘idea of heat, in an intense degree’ is a ‘vehement sensation’ of heat.

Just as Berkeley found himself unable to form an image that would satisfy the description of ‘idea of colour exclusive of extension’ (Section 4), I submit, Hylas is now made to find himself unable to call up a sensation that would qualify as ‘an idea of great heat (or intense smell, etc.) without pain, or pleasure’. A moment ago, Hylas had agreed that when putting his hand near the fire he is affected only with one simple idea or sensation. What Berkeley has him ‘try in his thoughts’ presumably is to imagine or call up such sensations, sensations of the sort we in fact usually have when exposed to things that are very hot or cold, or have an intense smell or taste. Since the only sensation we actually have in these cases is one of pain, or pleasure, he duly imagines only sensations which are painful or enjoyable and thus ‘finds’ that he cannot ‘conceive’ a pertinent sensation ‘to be without pain, or pleasure’. In other words, he cannot ‘separate’ these sensations from pain, ‘even in the mind’. He therefore concludes—(3) above— that the sensations he considered are nothing distinct from pleasure or pain and—(4) above—exist in the same place, the mind. Indeed, the conclusion sought—invoked by the initial “again” in (1)—is that ‘the intense heat immediately perceived is nothing distinct from a particular sort of pain’ (3D 176), and is paraphrased when attained—in (4) above—as: ‘A very violent and painful heat cannot exist without the mind’. The step from (3) to this conclusion presupposes a specific version of the doctrine—SD+—to be established: that the violent heat, the intense heat immediately perceived, is a sensation of the kind Hylas imagined, namely the painful sensation we in fact usually have when exposed to things that are very hot.

The second argument (the move from 2 to 3 above) relies on Berkeley’s denial ‘that I can frame a general notion by abstracting from particulars in the manner aforesaid’ (PHK I10), with which he rejects accounts like this: ‘[T]he mind by leaving out of the particular colours perceived by sense, that which distinguishes them one from another, and retaining that only which is common to all, makes an idea of colour in abstract which is neither red, nor blue, nor white, nor any other determinate colour’ (PHK I8). Mutatis mutandis, the same goes for ‘abstract ideas of the more compounded beings’, say of man or animal (PHK I9). Since we are now dealing with ideas that are ‘simple, or uncompounded’ (3D 176), the case of ‘sensible pain or pleasure in general’ must be analogous to that of ‘colour in abstract’ (rather than that of ‘the complex idea of man’). Just as the particular colours perceived by sense are different determinate qualities (red, blue, white) falling under the same determinable (viz. colour), so heat, cold, tastes, smells are different members of the same genus. And just as one cannot form by abstraction ideas of ‘colour in abstract which is . . . no[t] any determinate colour’, one cannot, Berkeley now claims, form an idea of that genus, ‘abstracted from every particular idea of heat, cold, tastes, smells’ (3D 177).

He refers to that genus-idea as ‘an idea of sensible pain or pleasure in general’: The genus that heat, cold, tastes, and smells are presupposed to belong to is that of ‘sensible pain or pleasure’. Presumably, Berkeley uses the label “sensible pains and pleasures” to distinguish those pains and pleasures which we may have when employing our five senses from those corporeal sensations (like head- or stomach-aches) which we have irrespective of whether or not we use any of those senses. The present assumption—(2) above—thus presupposes that at any rate intense heat, cold, tastes and smells all are sensations of a specific sort: sensible pains or pleasures. The second ‘anti-abstractionist argument’ thus presupposes from the start that intense heat, cold, tastes and smells are sensations. Since the present discussion is throughout about ‘things we immediately perceive by our senses’ (3D 180), the claim presupposed is, more precisely, an instance of the ‘embellished sense-datum doctrine’ (SD+) to be established: that intense heat, cold, tastes and smells, as immediately perceived by sense, are sensations or ideas. Both the ‘pleasure/pain arguments’ and the ‘anti-abstractionist arguments’ they build on presuppose what they are to show; strong belief bias vitiates the entire strand of argument.

To sum up: Berkeley adheres to at least some of the same philosophical pictures as Locke, including the mereological picture of qualities and perceptual pictures of intellectual activities and achievements (Sections 3–4), which lead to the adoption of the sense-datum doctrine of perception; second, he maintains a version of this paradoxical doctrine as a matter of strong belief bias: He presupposes it in the ‘opening argument’ which motivates the general strategy he employs to marshal arguments in its support (Section 2) and in those of the individual arguments marshalled that he seems to have thought his best (Sections 5–6).

Philosophical pictures thus shape philosophical reflection more profoundly than we have previously noticed. In previous chapters, we considered what we may call ‘intuitive reasoning’: single leaps of thought which materialise in the shape of philosophical intuitions (where the premises remain tacit) or single-step arguments (where the premises are explicitly stated). We now see that picture-driven reasoning shapes not only such spontaneous leaps of thought but also the deliberate construction of arguments, and does so at several levels: Directly, through picture-driven inferences, and indirectly, through biased reasoning presupposing picture-driven conclusions, such reasoning shapes also deliberately constructed arguments of several steps (Sections 5–6) and can suggest methods for constructing such arguments or providing evidence (like Berkeley’s adoption of introspection as a supposedly error-free method for establishing truths, metaphysical and other, Sections 3–4) as well as strategies for marshalling arguments (like Berkeley’s grand strategy for establishing the sense-datum doctrine, Section 2). Philosophical pictures may influence not only what philosophers spontaneously or intuitively think but also how they reason deliberately, what they accept as reason or evidence, and how strong a reason they think it is.





5 Self-Perpetuating Absurdity


Berkeley Defends ‘Perceptions’




Our loyalty to our extant beliefs, warranted and unwarranted, is commonly reinforced by various belief-bias effects of which we are hardly ever aware: We tend to notice evidence that confirms our beliefs more readily than uncongenial evidence, attach more weight to the former than to the latter, and accept argument more readily when it leads to a conclusion we believe true. In deductive reasoning, people are significantly more likely to accept unsound arguments when they lead to conclusions they already believe true.1 They are also prone to unwittingly presuppose assumptions whose truth they take for granted. These two tendencies come together when we argue for a conclusion whose truth we take for granted all along: We may then presuppose the claim in the course of the very argument meant to support it and accept the argument thus rendered unsound.

We do not often adduce deductive arguments to support claims whose truth we take for granted. But, while otherwise uncommon, this situation does arise in philosophical reflection. It arises, for example, when we are under the spell of philosophical pictures: when non-intentional reasoning has us adopt paradoxical claims we find intuitively compelling and wish to defend in view of their conflict with further convictions, and when we resort for the purpose to the form of argument philosophers tend to favour, namely deductive argument. Once we have adopted picture-driven conclusions in the absence of warrant, we may thus come to maintain them in the continued absence of warrant, namely on the basis of arguments which presuppose, more or less subtly, what they are meant to show. In this way, the unwarranted claims we espouse under the spell of philosophical pictures can come to permeate our reasoning and perpetuate themselves by spawning arguments that tacitly presuppose them but lend them an air of intellectual respectability. Like mountaineers who manage to survive in the thinnest air on oxygen they carry themselves, these intuitively compelling claims manage to support themselves by the most specious of arguments which grow from those very roots.

The sense-datum doctrine of perception is one of these masters of self-sufficient survival. In his first systematic defence of the doctrine, Berkeley repeatedly employs three kinds of arguments. In the previous chapter, we considered Berkeley’s preferred first kind of arguments, his arguments from identity (a.k.a. ‘pleasure/pain arguments’ based on ‘anti-abstractionist arguments’). In this chapter, we will consider the other two arguments, which Berkeley had reservations about but which proved lastingly popular: arguments from science (Section 1) and arguments from relativity (Section 2), as well as the supposedly decisive ‘Master Argument’ Berkeley finally adds to this stock (Section 3). In the next chapter, we will examine the arguments from illusion which were the mainstay of sense-datum theorists in the 20th century. All these arguments presuppose, in one way or another, the very doctrine they are meant to establish. Berkeley is unable to reconcile this unwarranted doctrine with common sense (Section 4). This renders the doctrine absurd, in a strict sense of the frequently over-used term (Section 5). Picture-driven reasoning and belief bias thus contribute to a process— the ‘pictorial process’ (Chapter 1.3)—whereby the most competent thinkers systematically come to espouse and maintain philosophical doctrines which are plainly absurd.

By saying that we perceive things ‘immediately’ Berkeley means that we perceive them without the intervention of other things; that we perceive things strictly speaking ‘through our senses’ means that we perceive them without also using our reason, namely without making any inference (Chapter 4.1). For the reasons we have reconstructed (in Chapter 4.2), Berkeley takes for granted that the things we perceive, strictly speaking, through our five senses are immediately perceived, and that the only things thus perceived are various qualities. This shapes his strategy for showing that all the things we can perceive immediately or strictly speaking through our five senses are ideas we perceive in our mind: He considers in turn each of the ‘sensible qualities’ we immediately perceive by sense and shows that it exists only as an idea or sensation in the mind. For each quality, he offers one or more arguments of the three main kinds distinguished. We now turn from his ‘arguments from identity’, to the lastingly influential arguments from science and relativity.2





1. ARGUMENTS FROM SCIENCE:

ASSUMING THE CONCLUSION




The ‘arguments from science’ presented by Berkeley descend from the ‘argument from science’ employed by Locke (Chapter 3.4). Locke sought to refute a view he attributed to common sense, namely the view that sensations of heat and colour—in contrast with sensations of pain—are produced in us by a ‘communication’ or transfer of these accidents (heat and colour, but not pain) from physical objects or corporeal substances in which they really exist. Against this, Locke argued that science reveals pain- and heat-sensations to be produced in us in roughly the same way, namely through the local motion of insensible particles, so that there is no more reason to posit a distinct accident of heat in external bodies than there is to posit a distinct accident of pain or painfulness in them.

Berkeley’s arguments from science are of two kinds. The first, the argument from analogy, is very similar to the Lockean argument.
