It is developed in this exchange between Philonous (P) and Hylas (H), about heat and cold, in response to Hylas’ objection that it is ‘absurd . . . to say, there is no heat in the fire’:

P: To make the point still clearer; tell me, whether in two cases exactly alike, we ought not to make the same judgement? H: We ought. P: When a pin pricks your finger, does it not rend and divide the fibres of your flesh? H: It does. P: And when a coal burns your finger, does it any more? H: It does not. P: Since therefore you neither judge the sensation itself occasioned by the pin [viz. the pain], nor anything like it to be in the pin; [i] you should not, comfortably to what you have now granted, judge the sensation occasioned by the fire, or any thing like it, to be in the fire. H: Well, since it must be so, [ii] I am content to yield this point, and acknowledge, [iii] that heat and cold are only sensations existing in our minds. (3D 179)





In contrast with Locke, Berkeley here considers only a quite specific case of heat-perception in which the hot object causes us pain (a coal burns your finger). This sensation is ‘occasioned by the fire’ and one may indeed infer from the—false—assumption that both a pin that pricks us and a coal that burns us act on us in the same way (‘rend and divide the fibres of our flesh’) that the sensations of pain caused by them ought to be explained in roughly the same way, so that if we do not posit an accident of pain or painfulness in the pin occasioning pain in us, we also—[i]—ought not to posit an accident of pain or painfulness in a flame or piece of coal burning us. But Hylas draws a rather different conclusion in yielding the point—[ii]—that no such accident as heat is in the fire and acknowledging—[iii]—that heat and cold—at any rate as immediately perceived by sense (3D 180)—are only sensations existing in our minds.

There are two ways to advance from the first to the latter conclusions. If Hylas takes “the sensation occasioned by the fire”—in [i]—to refer to the one sensation actually caused by the fire burning us, namely the pain, his move to [ii] and [iii] is rendered valid only by the tacit assumption that heat, at any rate as immediately perceived by sense, is identical with that pain. This is of course the conclusion of the second pleasure-pain argument, which Hylas adopted two pages earlier (3D 177). This argument, in turn, presupposes that heat, as immediately perceived by sense, is a sensation or idea something else affects us with (chapters 4.5–4.6). Alternatively, one may interpret the present talk—[i]—of ‘the sensation occasioned by the fire’ in the light of this assumption, namely as referring to some not further specified ‘sensation of heat’ occasioned in us by the fire (which may or may not be a pain). In this case, the argument explicitly assumes without providing further reasons that heat, at any rate as immediately perceived by sense, is a sensation caused in us. (The argument then ‘merely’ brings out that this accident is not communicated from, and does not also exist in, external objects, just as the accident of pain is not communicated from, and does not exist in, external objects.) That is: On one reading, the argument tacitly relies on the assumption that heat, as immediately perceived by sense, is a sensation caused in us; on another reading it explicitly proceeds from this assumption.

The argument from analogy proceeds from cases in which another body is supposed to act mechanically on our body (to ‘rend and divide the fibres of our flesh’) and thereby to cause an exemplary sensation (pain) in us; the argument then assumes that the pertinent case of perception is analogous, namely in that when we perceive something in the pertinent sense-modality (i) another body acts on parts of ours, (ii) does so in ways which can be described and explained in mechanistic terms, and (iii) thereby causes us to have some sensation.

The second kind of argument from science, the causal argument, proceeds from a supposedly scientific ‘proto-theory’ of perception in the pertinent sense-modality. Such an account gives—minimal—content to the three elements of the present analogy and specifies which (parts of) external bodies act on which parts of ours, what kind of mechanical action is involved, and which sensations are thus brought about. Berkeley presents full-blown causal arguments concerning sound (3D 181–182) and colour (3D 186–187), and hints at such an argument for solidity (3D 191).

The argument about colour proceeds from the following ‘proto-theory’ of visual perception:

[E]xternal light is nothing but a thin, fluid substance, whose minute particles being agitated with a brisk motion, and in various manners reflected from the different surfaces of outward objects to the eyes, communicate different motions to the optic nerves; which being propagated to the brain, cause therein various impressions: and these are attended with the sensations of red, blue, yellow, etc. (3D 186)





Like the argument from analogy, the philosophical argument that proceeds from this account relies on the assumption that the pertinent sensible qualities are identical with the sensations external things affect us with: The present argument assumes that the ideas or ‘sensations of red, blue, yellow, etc.’ supposedly caused in us do not take these colours as their intentional objects but are identical with them. Together, and only together, with this assumption—(0) below—the final part of the present account entails the first explicit premise of the subsequent philosophical argument (3D 186–87):



[0] The sensations of red, blue, yellow, etc. are these particular colours, as immediately perceived by sight. I.e.: Colours, as immediately perceived by sight, are sensations.

The light does no more than shake the optic nerves, and consequent to each motion of the nerves the mind is affected with a sensation, which is some particular colour [as immediately perceived by sight].

These sensations [can] have no existence without the mind. By (1) and (2):

Colours, as immediately perceived by sight, cannot exist without the mind.

Colours in the vulgar [i.e. pre-philosophical] sense are immediate objects of sight. By (3) and (4):

Colours in the vulgar sense cannot exist in (‘agree to’) any but a perceiving substance.





Like Locke’s argument from science and Berkeley’s previous arguments, this argument assumes from the start that—(1)—the pertinent qualities, as immediately perceived by sense, are sensations, and ‘merely’ argues that those qualities cannot exist outside the mind. Indeed, like the first pleasure-pain argument (Chapter 4.5), it states this crucial assumption quite explicitly and merely adds the apparently trivial inference from it and the second assumption that sensations cannot exist outside a ‘perceiving being’ or ‘mind’.

Berkeley develops these arguments from science without believing in them, presumably for rhetorical effect. At any rate, he goes on to deliver the most scathing criticism of them. In the First Dialogue, the ‘scientific’ accounts of perception on which the causal arguments are based are only put into the mouth of Hylas and not of Berkeley’s own spokesperson Philonous. At the beginning of the Second Dialogue, Berkeley criticises them viciously (3D 209–210). In particular, he notes that those accounts do not render intelligible ‘the connexion . . . between a motion in the nerves, and the sensations of sound or colour in the mind . . . or how [it is] possible these should be the effect of that’ (3D 210, cp. 3D 242). This criticism is fair. Already Locke had found no ‘conceivable connexion’ between bulk, figure, and motion of bodies and colour-, taste- or heat-sensations in us (EHU II.viii.25) and resorted to invoking the help of the Lord, in his only ‘explanation’ of how the former can produce the latter in us:

Let us suppose at present, that the different motions and figures, bulk, and number of such particles, affecting the several organs of our senses, produce in us those different sensations, which we have from the colours and smells of bodies . . . [an example follows]. It being no more impossible, to conceive, that God should annex such ideas [i.e. colour- and smell-sensations] to such motions, with which they have no similitude; than that he should annex the idea [i.e. sensation] of pain to the motion of a piece of steel dividing our flesh, with which that idea [i.e. sensation] has no resemblance. (EHU II.viii.13)



Since ‘arguments from science’ have been rather popular among later champions of the sense-datum doctrine, it is worth keeping in mind that these arguments also suffer from an even more fundamental defect: They assume or presuppose this very doctrine from the start. As stated by Berkeley, they assume or presuppose it with its Berkeleyan twist: Whenever we perceive anything by sense we immediately perceive sensations or ideas occasioned in us—which are identical with the several ‘sensible qualities’.





2. ARGUMENTS FROM RELATIVITY:

RELYING ON THE CONCLUSION




Most of the arguments that implement Berkeley’s general strategy for showing that all the things we perceive immediately by sense are ideas in the mind are ‘arguments from relativity’. In the First Dialogue he develops one or more of these arguments for all but one of the qualities discussed: temperature (3D 178–179), taste (3D 180), odour (3D 181), colour (3D 185– 186), figure and extension (3D 188–189), motion (3D 190) and solidity (3D 191) (while sound is the odd one out). These arguments rely on a variant of the ‘Phenomenal Principle’ which later champions of the sense-datum doctrine continue to rely on:



(P’) If a sensible quality F can be attributed to or said to ‘inhere in’ it at all, then an external object has the quality F (inhering in it) if it looks F or smells F or tastes F or feels F or, in brief: ‘seems’ or ‘appears’ F, to some observer.



(If a coin, for example, can be credited with colour and shape at all, then it is silvery and elliptical if it looks silvery and elliptical.) Second, these arguments employ one of two ‘reality assumptions’:



(R1) If a quality really inheres in an object, the quality does not change without a change wrought in the body itself.



(R2) If a quality really inheres in an object, no other determinate quality falling under the same determinable is in the object at the same time. (E.g.: If an object really is silvery, no other colour is in it.)



The arguments then proceed from various uncontroversial premises: Objects feel different under different conditions (3D 178–179); they look different from different perspectives or distances (3D 185, 189), under different lighting (3D 186) or upon a change to a person’s eyes (3D 185); things may taste different to different people or upon a change to the palate (3D 180); they smell differently to different creatures (3D 181); objects look different upon the use of visual aids (3D 185, 189), etc. With the help of principle P’ the arguments then derive from these premises conclusions at odds with the ‘reality assumptions’ and conclude that the sensible quality at issue does not really inhere in external objects and can only exist in that other kind of substance: the mind.

This is nicely illustrated by the argument about colour. After pointing out, for instance, that ‘in the jaundice . . . all things seem yellow’ (3D 185), Philonous infers by P’ that if colours were really inherent in external bodies, their colours would change (viz. to yellow) upon the onset of the jaundice, that is, ‘upon a change happening in the humours of the eye’ which is not accompanied by any change in or to the bodies themselves, and this is incompatible with the first ‘reality assumption’:

[I]n case colours were real properties or affections inherent in external bodies, they could admit of no alteration, without some change wrought in the very bodies themselves: but is it not evident from what has been said, that . . . upon a change happening in the humours of the eye, or a variation of distance, without any manner of real alteration in the thing itself, the colours of any object are either changed, or totally disappear [!]? (3D 185–186)





In response, Hylas concedes that ‘there is no such thing as colour really inhering in external bodies’ and adopts the position that colour ‘is altogether in the light’ (3D 186).3 (The causal argument considered above then makes Hylas explicitly acknowledge the final conclusion that colours in the ordinary or vulgar sense are sensations which only exist in the mind.) Or consider the argument about taste, which relies on the second ‘reality assumption’:

P: [N]othing can be plainer than that divers persons perceive different tastes in the same food, since that which one man delights in, another abhors. And how could this be, if the taste was something really inherent in the food? H: I acknowledge I know not how. (3D 180)





The difficulty baffling Hylas is that a good and a bad taste cannot simultaneously inhere in the same stuff. But since the same food tastes good to one man and bad to another, Principle P’ has us conclude that both a good and a bad taste are in the food, if taste can be said to inhere in physical stuff at all. Hence taste cannot inhere in physical stuff but can only ‘exist in . . . a perceiving substance or mind’ (3D 181).

The principle P’ employed in these arguments is patently, strikingly unsound: Already small children learn to distinguish between how something looks from a certain distance or in a particular light, and the colour, shape or size it actually has, and know better than to always infer from the fact that something looks small to them that it actually is small. Of course a tall tree is tall even when it looks small from a mile’s distance (cp. NTV 44), and a white cloth looks blue but still is white when you shine a blue light on it (say, in ‘the experiment of the prism’ Berkeley refers to, in 3D 186). Pace Berkeley (3D 186) and Locke (EHU II.viii.19), the red and white colours of porphyry (Chapter 3.4) do not ‘totally disappear’ or ‘vanish’ the moment we extinguish the light; the stone still is red and white when everything looks dark. And as for colour so for taste: The wine still has its usual taste (its taste has not ‘totally disappeared’), when I have a cold strong enough to prevent me from discerning it, and food need not be sour when it tastes sour to me (e.g. when I still have a sour wine’s aftertaste in my mouth). (Apart from that, I may of course enjoy a taste that you ‘abhor’.) So why does Berkeley rely on the patently unsound principle P’ and, indeed, regard the conclusions thus reached as every bit as ‘evident’ as Locke had found them ‘plain’ (EHU II.viii.19)?

When offering the present arguments, Berkeley proceeds from the view that the sensible qualities he discusses are sensations or ideas we are affected with and perceive in the mind. The arguments from relativity about temperature, taste, and odour are all preceded by pleasure-pain arguments which make Hylas grant this point. Thus the above argument about taste is preceded by the concessions that ‘sweet taste [is] a particular kind of pleasure or pleasant sensation’ (3D 179), that ‘bitterness [is] some kind of uneasiness or pain’ (3D 180) and that ‘these qualities, as perceived by us, are pleasures or pains’ (3D 180). The argument from relativity is then developed in response to the protest that, all the same, ‘it sounds oddly, to say that sugar is not sweet’ (3D 180). That is: The protagonists have agreed that taste as perceived by us is a sensation we perceive in the mind when exercising our sense of taste. The issue between them is whether the same accident, the very taste ‘we immediately perceive by our senses’ (3D 180), namely as a sensation in our mind, whether this very accident can inhere in physical objects as well as in the mind in which we perceive it. The argument from relativity is to rule out this possibility. Similarly, Hylas holds all along the view that impressions in the brain ‘are attended with the sensations of red, blue, yellow, etc.’ in the mind (3D 186), that is, that colours are sensations the mind is affected with. Probably, the present argument from relativity, like the arguments from science, simply takes for granted that the colours we immediately perceive by sense are sensations or ideas perceived in the mind and is to settle the follow-up question of whether the accidents we perceive in the mind also inhere ‘in any outward object’ (3D 185).

Presumably, Berkeley individuates sensations or ideas of perception (‘perceptions’, cp. Chapter 3.5) by the pertinent qualitative properties (as they are often called), much as we commonly individuate corporeal sensations. Such sensations are individuated by reference to how they feel: When it feels piercing, it is one pain; when it feels dull, it is another. They are said to have disappeared and, perhaps, been replaced by others if nothing feels that way anymore: When we first have a piercing and then a dull pain, one pain vanishes and is replaced by another sensation. Berkeley’s argument about colour makes the analogous assumption that a colour ‘totally disappear[s]’ when nothing looks that way to you anymore. More generally, the idea is that when we would ordinarily say that an object or stuff looks or tastes different to us, we perceive different colour- or taste-sensations: When something looks white to you, you have one colour-sensation or -perception; when it looks yellow to you, you have another. When something tastes good to me, I have one taste-sensation; when it tastes bad to me, I have another. And so on.

Suppose the present arguments are to settle the question of whether the same accident I perceive in my mind also inheres in the outward object. By the present assumptions, this is the case if the same colour it looks to me also inheres in that object, if the same taste it has to me also inheres in that object, etc. In other words: If the same quality that I perceive immediately in my mind inheres in a physical body at all, then that body has that quality inhering in it that it looks or tastes or smells or feels to me to have. Thus we arrive at Principle P’. I therefore submit the hypothesis that Berkeley reasons in line with this otherwise patently unsound principle because he, first, takes for granted from the start that the qualities we immediately perceive by sense are immediately perceived in the mind, as an idea or sensation, and, second, individuates these sensations or ‘perceptions’ by reference to their qualitative properties.

We can confirm this hypothesis by turning to Berkeley’s slightly earlier New Theory of Vision. Here Berkeley presents two arguments which employ a more straightforward version of the Phenomenal Principle. In a nutshell: The things I see are small when they look small to me. More generally:



(P) A sensible (perceivable) object has a sensible quality F if it looks or otherwise appears F to an observer.



The previous variant is obtained by assuming that sensible qualities (qualities we perceive by sense, strictly speaking or immediately) can be attributed to, and only to, sensible objects (objects we perceive by sense, strictly speaking or immediately), and hypothesising that external objects may be sensible, that is (by the present assumption), that sensible qualities can be attributed to them: (P’) If a sensible quality F can be attributed to an external object at all, then it has that quality if it looks or otherwise appears F.

The arguments in the New Theory clearly are to establish not ‘merely’ that the accidents we perceive in the mind do not also inhere in external objects, but also the sense-datum doctrine those arguments take for granted: They are to show, first, that the ‘immediate objects of sight’ are not identical with the physical bodies we take to surround us but are ideas perceived in the mind (an instance of the sense-datum doctrine) and, second, that these ideas do not resemble anything ‘placed at a distance’ (the familiar conclusion that, if there are external physical bodies, they do not resemble our ideas and, in particular, do not have any such qualities inhering in them as we immediately perceive).

The first argument is this:

Suppose, e.g., that looking at the moon I should say it were fifty or sixty semi-diameters of the earth distant from me. Let us see what moon this is spoken of: it is plain it cannot be the visible moon, or anything like the visible moon, or that which I see, which is only a round, luminous plane of about thirty visible points in diameter. For in case I am carried from the place where I stand directly towards the moon, it is manifest the object varies, still as I go on; and by the time that I am advanced fifty or sixty semi-diameters of the earth, I shall be so far from being near a small, round, luminous flat that I shall perceive nothing like it; this object having long since disappeared, and if I would recover it, it must be by going back to the earth from whence I set out. (NTV 44)





The first conclusion is supported as follows: (1) The visible moon, or that which I see, is only a round, luminous plane of about thirty visible points in diameter. (2) There is no luminous flat object of this size at the position at which the moon is judged to be. Therefore: (3) That which I see cannot be any object rightly judged to be at that position.

The first premise is unsupported by any argument. Quite clearly, it is inferred in line with P from an obvious fact it is then conflated with: When we look at, and see, the moon in the sky, it looks small, round, luminous and flat. By P, the thing we perceive by sight, the ‘visible moon’ (as Berkeley calls it) actually is small, round, luminous and flat. Like the arguments from relativity in the Dialogues, the reasoning applying this phenomenal principle is preceded by argument to the effect that the pertinent objects of perception are sensations or ideas: ‘For at this time it seems agreed on all hands . . . that colours, which are the proper and immediate object of sight, are not without [but within] the mind’—and ‘visible extension’, figure, and motion are ‘in the very same place’ as colour (NTV 43, cp. Chapter 4.4). The present passage is to give ‘a fuller explication of this point’ (NTV 44). That is: Again, the Phenomenal Principle is applied only once it has been suggested that the qualities we immediately perceive by sense are immediately perceived in the mind. And, again, the objects of sense (now only: sight) are individuated, like sensations, by reference to their qualitative properties (here: by reference to how things look to someone) and, like sensations, are said to have ‘disappeared’ when nothing has those properties anymore (when nothing looks that way anymore). Presumably, the objects of perception to which Principle P is applied are all along taken to be ideas or sensations (‘call them which you will’, 3D 215).

Indeed, in the very next sentence the first premise of what is clearly intended to be an analogous argument explicitly assumes that what we perceive by sight are ideas:

Again, suppose that I perceive by sight the faint and obscure idea of something which I doubt whether it is a man, or a tree, or a tower, but judge it to be at the distance of about a mile. It is plain I cannot mean that what I see is a mile off, or that it is the image or likeness of anything which is a mile off, since that every step I take towards it the appearance alters, and from being obscure, small, and faint, grows clear, large, and vigorous. And when I come to the mile’s end, that which I saw first is quite lost, neither do I find anything in the likeness of it. (NTV 44).





The pertinent ideas are now individuated with reference not only to how things look but also to further ‘qualitative’ properties: how ‘faint and obscure’ or ‘clear’ and ‘vigorous’ they are. Such an idea is then called an ‘appearance’: Like Locke before him, Berkeley arguably comes to conflate ideas and appearances by assuming that ideas are what we see immediately in our minds whenever we use our eyes and individuating these ideas by reference to how things appear to us (cp. Chapter 4.2). And this leads him to apply the Phenomenal Principle to the objects of perception (strictly speaking or immediate) which he takes to be ideas in the mind, from the start. The present arguments thus confirm our hypothesis: The sense-datum doctrine that all we immediately perceive by sense are ideas in our minds motivates the adoption of the otherwise unsound Phenomenal Principle.

At any rate in the absence of independent argument for the sense-datum doctrine, the arguments which employ the Phenomenal Principle are hence unsound (see also Chapter 6.1). Where such arguments are employed to support the sense-datum doctrine of perception, they also suffer from the same fundamental defect as the ‘arguments from science’ we previously considered and rely from the start on the very doctrine they are to support. They do so in a subtle way. The arguments from science assumed what they are to show, explicitly or tacitly, by making inferences that are valid only if that claim is added to the premises. The present arguments rely on it in an even less conspicuous way: They employ principles of inference which appear sound to the arguments’ proponents only due to a line of thought proceeding from the claim at issue. When we find it intuitively compelling, a philosophical doctrine may permeate—and vitiate—the arguments adduced to support it, in profound ways that are not exposed by mere logical reconstruction of the argument but only by inquiring into the motivation for the inference-principles it is found to employ.





3. BERKELEY’S ‘MASTER ARGUMENT’:

STRONG BELIEF BIAS




The Phenomenal Principle is employed also by the ‘arguments from illusion’ which became the most popular arguments for the sense-datum doctrine of perception, within 20th-century analytic philosophy. We will consider these arguments in the next chapter, in a currently up-to-date form. For now, let’s turn to what Berkeley regarded as his most decisive argument, which he finally adds to the various arguments from science, relativity and identity (PP/AA) which he gives for one quality after another, without necessarily believing in all of them. This ‘Master Argument’ requires some stage-setting.

The sense-datum doctrine is usually presented as a doctrine about what we ‘immediately’ perceive by sense, sometimes as one about what we perceive ‘strictly speaking’ by sense, and occasionally as one about what we perceive by sense, tout court. Berkeley swiftly advances from the first, via the second, to the third version. Recall (from Chapter 4.1) that, arguably due to unwitting linguistic innovation and spontaneous transfer of ordinary implications, Berkeley attributed epistemic implications to the notions of ‘seeing’, ‘hearing’, ‘feeling’ etc., and thus came to distinguish between perception by sense, strictly speaking, where the relevant knowledge is acquired by using only the senses, and perception by reason, where also inferences are made. Recall also (from Chapter 4.2) that Berkeley presupposed from the start a version of the sense-datum doctrine, namely that whenever we employ our senses we perceive ideas in the mind, from which we need to infer their possible causes, and concluded that, ‘in truth and strictness’, we perceive by sense only ideas which intervene between us and whatever causes them; ‘in truth the senses perceive nothing which they do not perceive immediately’ (3D 174) or ‘without the intervention of others’ (cp. 3D 173). This conclusion then takes us from the ‘standard’ sense-datum doctrine that whenever we exercise our senses we immediately perceive ideas in the mind, to the conclusion that whenever we employ our senses all we strictly speaking see or feel or otherwise perceive by sense are ideas in the mind—from which the qualification ‘strictly speaking’ quickly gets dropped (after all, why should we bother about loose talk?):

You allow the things immediately perceived by sense to exist nowhere without the mind: but there is nothing perceived by sense, which is not perceived immediately: therefore there is nothing sensible that exists without the mind. (3D 215, cp. 3D 203)





Let’s call this conclusion the ‘strong sense-datum doctrine’: All sensible objects, all things we perceive by sense, are ideas or combinations of ideas, which exist in the mind (and nowhere else).

Such things as houses, mountains and rivers are perceived by sense. By the present doctrine, these things therefore are ideas in the mind: ‘For what are the forementioned objects but the things we perceive by sense, and what do we perceive besides our own ideas or sensations . . . ?’ (PHK 4) This implication of the strong sense-datum doctrine is not to be confused with the metaphysical doctrine of idealism, that there are only ideas and minds to harbour them and no such things as physical bodies which exist outside the mind. For the present claim leaves open the possibility that in addition to the houses and mountains we perceive by sense there are distinct objects we cannot perceive, which exist outside the mind. We can therefore regard the present trivial consequence of the strong sense-datum doctrine as a variant of this epistemological doctrine: The houses, mountains, rivers, etc. we perceive by sense are ideas which exist in the mind (and nowhere else).

As we have seen (in Chapter 4.2), a line of thought presupposing the ‘standard’ version of the sense-datum doctrine led Berkeley to the conclusion that we can perceive by sense only qualities but not the substances in which they inhere, so that the only sensible objects are (combinations of) sensible qualities (3D 175). So Berkeley maintains, more specifically, that houses, mountains, rivers, etc. are combinations of qualities which are ideas in the mind: ‘Thus, for example, a certain colour, taste, smell, figure and consistence having been observed to go together are accounted one distinct thing, signified by the name apple. Other collections of ideas constitute a stone, a tree, a book and the like sensible things’ (PHK 1). He maintains the strong sense-datum doctrine as the claim that the houses and trees we perceive are combinations of ideas which exist only in the mind.

This claim motivates the main argument of the First Dialogue which is meant to establish it (Chapter 4.2): Berkeley shows for each sensible quality in turn that it is an idea and exists only in the mind (3D 175–191). Once he has patiently executed this strategy, Berkeley finally presents an argument which struck him as so overwhelmingly plausible that he was willing ‘to pass by all that has been hitherto said, and reckon it for nothing . . . and put the whole upon this issue’ (3D 200). As deployed in the Dialogues, his notorious ‘Master Argument’ supports the strong sense-datum doctrine, namely by arguing for the even stronger conclusion that it is inconceivable that ‘any mixture or combination of qualities, or any sensible object whatever, [should] exist without the mind’ (3D 200)—easy though it may seem to conceive or ‘imagine’ this (cp. PHK 23):

Hylas: What more easy than to conceive a tree or house existing by itself, independent of, and unperceived by any mind whatsoever? I do at this present time conceive them existing after that manner. [He is ‘thinking of a tree in a solitary place, where no one was present to see it’.] Philonous: How say you, Hylas, can you see a thing which is at the same time unseen? H: No, that were a contradiction. P: Is it not as great a contradiction to talk of conceiving a thing which is unconceived? H: It is. P: The tree or house therefore which you think of, is conceived by you. H: How should it be otherwise? P: And what is conceived, is surely in the mind. H: Without question, that which is conceived is in the mind. P: How then came you to say, you conceived a house or tree existing independent and out of all minds, whatsoever? H: That was I own an oversight . . . P: You acknowledge then that you cannot possibly conceive, how any one corporeal sensible thing should exist otherwise than in a mind. H: I do. (3D 200)





This argument has baffled commentators no less than Galileo’s puzzling argument from conceivability (though it received a lot more attention). To get started, we need to spell out properly the core argument (with tacit assumptions and conclusions in square brackets):

When you conceive or think of a sensible object like a tree or a house in a solitary place, the tree or house you think of is conceived by you.

That which is conceived is in the mind. By (1) and (2):

When you conceive or think of a sensible object like a tree or a house in a solitary place, this object (house or tree, etc.) exists in the mind. Hence:

When you conceive or think of a sensible object like a tree or a house in a solitary place, you do not [think of or] conceive a house or tree which exists independently and out of the mind.

You conceive it possible for a sensible object to exist without the mind only if you conceive a sensible object which exists out of the mind. By (4) and [5]:

When you conceive or think of a sensible object like a tree or a house in a solitary place, you do not conceive it possible for a sensible object to exist without the mind.





Thus we fail even when ‘we do our utmost to conceive the existence of external bodies’ (PHK 23) and are manifestly unable to conceive it possible for a sensible object to exist without the mind. It is inconceivable that the objects we perceive by sense exist without the mind.

The argument turns on two crucial assumptions, one explicit, one tacit. The tacit assumption—(5)—is that conceiving that a sensible object exists without the mind requires conceiving a sensible object that exists without the mind. Such ‘conceiving’ is tantamount to ‘imagining’ (PHK 23). In brief, the assumption is this: Imagining that some thing is thus-and-so requires imagining a thing that is thus-and-so. Previous explanations account for this assimilation of imagining-that to imagining an object: Once imagining was taken to consist in perceiving and operating with ideas in the mind, these ideas were assimilated to certain models which common sense appears to credit with a suitable location: the images we are commonly said to ‘see before the inner eye’ when imagining objects (Chapter 4.3). Thus all cases of imagining are assimilated to those cases where we form and see images ‘before the inner eye’. In all such cases (also when we picture, e.g., events) we have to picture objects (as well): When I imagine what she would look like in that dress, I picture her in that dress (turning around, perhaps), and hence have to picture both her and the dress. When imagining-that gets assimilated to those cases of imagining where we form mental images, it therefore appears to require visualising objects and, thereby, imagining objects. You imagine or conceive that a tree exists out of the mind only if you form the image of, and thus imagine, a tree which exists out of the mind.

The argument that this apparently trivial feat is beyond us turns on the explicit assumption—(2)—that the things we imagine or conceive are in the mind; therefore—(4)—you cannot imagine or conceive things which exist out of the mind. The key term here is ambiguous: On the one hand, ‘to conceive’ is ‘to think of’ something, presumably in the generic sense in which the latter covers all intellectual activities taken to essentially involve perceiving ideas in the mind (the picture-motivated generic sense explained in Chapter 4.3). In this sense, the verb takes the things (trees or houses, etc.) ‘thought of’ as objects: ‘As I was thinking of a tree in a solitary place, where no one was present to see it, methought that was to conceive a tree as existing unperceived or unthought of, not considering that I myself conceived it [viz. the tree] all the while’ (3D 200, my italics). On the other hand, we are taken to ‘conceive ideas’, which is to mean the same as ‘to frame ideas’: ‘But now I plainly see, that all I can do is to frame ideas in my own mind. I may indeed conceive in my own thoughts the idea of a tree, or a house, or a mountain, but this is all’ (3D 200, my italics). Accordingly, there are two different readings of the crucial assumption (2): ‘The things (trees, houses, etc.) of which we think are in the mind.’ Or: ‘The ideas we frame are in the mind.’

On the first reading, the assumption is about sensible things. On this reading, (2) therefore comes down to the assumption that the things we perceive by sense (houses, trees, mountains, etc.) are ideas in the mind— the strong sense-datum doctrine, i.e., the very claim the present argument ultimately is to support. On this reading, the Master Argument Berkeley champions explicitly assumes the strong doctrine it is to support, just as the more popular causal arguments which he rejects (for different reasons) explicitly assume the standard sense-datum doctrine they purport to establish (Section 1). If Berkeley gives the crucial assumption (2) the second reading, ‘The ideas we frame are in the mind’, the strong sense-datum doctrine is not explicitly asserted but tacitly presupposed in the move to the first conclusion: To get from the first premise, that when you conceive or think of a sensible object (house or tree, etc.) the object you think of is conceived by you, and the second premise, that the ideas we frame are in the mind, to the conclusion that—[3]—when you conceive or think of a sensible object (house or tree, etc.) this sensible object exists in the mind and—(4)—not independently and out of the mind, we have to presuppose that the sensible object (house or tree, etc.) you conceive or think of is an idea you frame in the mind. To sum up: Depending upon the reading Berkeley gives to a crucial assumption, his Master Argument either explicitly assumes or tacitly presupposes that sensible objects like houses and trees are ideas in the mind—the strong sense-datum doctrine the argument was adduced to support.

Such fallacies may be manifestations of strong belief bias, where a conviction a thinker firmly maintains all along ‘creeps into’ arguments he subsequently formulates to justify that conviction to others’ satisfaction. This requires that the thinker was all along convinced of the truth of the conclusion at issue. In the case of apparently paradoxical philosophical claims like the present doctrine, this in turn requires that some other, independent arguments or lines of thought led the thinker to adopt them already previously. We have seen that Berkeley was in the grip of pertinent philosophical pictures (Chapter 4), reconstructed how lines of thought motivated by these pictures lead to the standard sense-datum doctrine (Chapter 3.5), and considered a moment ago the oft-repeated explicit argument that took Berkeley from the standard to the strong doctrine.

Already earlier, we encountered another potentially pertinent line of thought, which proceeds from the kind of spontaneous reinterpretation philosophers are prone to place on picture-driven claims they find intuitively compelling in spite of open conflicts with facts or definitions they accept. Defective sublimation of the fundamental claim that ‘ideas’ of various sorts ‘exist in the mind’ may lead to the conclusion that the tangible things we see and think of are ideas (Chapter 2.1): Like Locke, Berkeley seeks to provide an innocuous interpretation of talk of things as ‘existing in the mind’ or ‘in the understanding’, which frees such talk from all spatial implications:

[W]hen I speak of objects as existing in the mind . . . I would not be understood in the gross literal sense, as when bodies are said to exist in a place . . . My meaning is only that the mind comprehends or perceives them. (3D 250)





To say that something ‘exists in the mind’ simply is to say that the mind—or, resolving the metonymy, that the subject, with the use of his mind— ‘perceives’ something, namely in the conventional metaphorical sense in which perception terms apply to intellectual activities and achievements including ‘comprehending’ or understanding things, and thinking about them. That something ‘exists in my mind’ simply means that I understand, remember, imagine or otherwise ‘think of’ it (cp. EHU I.ii.5). Hence the trees and houses we think of can be said to ‘exist in the mind’. When thinkers under the spell of perceptual pictures employ this explanation, they are prone to unwittingly continue all the same to presuppose in further reasoning that the things which ‘exist in the mind’ exist in some private and immaterial space of perception (Chapter 2.1). When a thinker does this, he will leap from the present conclusion that the trees and houses we think of exist in the mind to the further conclusion that those trees and houses exist in that private and immaterial space of inner perception whose population is by common philosophical custom referred to as ‘ideas’. In this way, houses and trees and other perfectly tangible objects of thought may come to be identified with ideas in people’s minds.

Berkeley’s further explanations are consistent with these steps. He takes his above explanation to be in line with the ‘common custom’ of philosophers who ‘speak of the immediate objects of the understanding as things existing in the mind’ (3D 250)—thus implying that the things understood or thought of are immediate objects of the understanding—and explains elsewhere that when he ‘speak[s] of tangible ideas, I take the word “idea” for any the immediate object of sense or understanding’ (NTV 45). Quite possibly, Berkeley first placed a ‘non-spatial’ interpretation on talk of things ‘existing in the mind’, then unwittingly lapsed from these good intentions, and thereby came to identify sensible objects like houses and trees with ideas in the mind.

To sum up: In the Master Argument, Berkeley either explicitly assumes or tacitly presupposes the strong sense-datum doctrine, the very doctrine the argument is meant to support. Different picture-driven lines of thought we may plausibly attribute to him independently lead to this doctrine. Presumably, therefore, Berkeley’s supposedly clinching argument represents a case of strong belief bias: Once intuitively compelling lines of thought had taken him to the core doctrine, he unwittingly assumed or presupposed it in an argument for it which struck him as most forceful. Even if Berkeley thought it clearly superior to all the classical arguments for the sense-datum doctrine, his Master Argument is every bit as unsound, for essentially the same reasons.





4. THE ROAD TO IDEALISM: RECONCILIATION-PROBLEMS AND THEIR ‘SOLUTIONS’




Picture-driven lines of thought lead thinkers to paradoxical claims they find compelling and adopt in the absence of warrant. Once found compelling, these claims are presupposed in further reasoning and in the interpretation of ordinary talk and familiar facts; as a result they may be firmly maintained on the basis of arguments that presuppose them and of bogus ‘evidence’ that appears to support them only when interpreted in their light. The conflicts, real or apparent, of such intuitively compelling but unwarranted claims and common sense, or among each other, create distinctive kinds of philosophical ‘problems’: philosophical paradoxes and reconciliation problems (Chapters 2.3 and 3.4). Philosophers try to solve such problems by constructing theories which are to refute or reconcile what appears to be at odds; typically, these theories are based on lines of thought motivated by the same pictures that created the ‘problems’ to which they respond (Chapter 3.5).

This account is further confirmed by Berkeley’s ascent to idealism. We have seen that Berkeley was in the grip of pertinent philosophical pictures and came to endorse the sense-datum doctrine in their grip (Chapter 4). We have also seen that Berkeley’s first strategy for establishing this doctrine (Chapter 4.2), the arguments implementing this strategy (Chapters 4.5–4.6 and 5.1–5.2), and the ‘Master Argument’ which supersedes these arguments all presuppose or assume what they are to establish. The doctrine Berkeley thus comes to firmly maintain on utterly specious grounds appears to conflict with various convictions he is unwilling or unable to shed. The struggle with the problems raised by such conflicts leads Berkeley to the metaphysical theory he is most famous for: his idealism.

Recall (from the previous section) the ‘easy reflection’ (cp. 3D 212) that takes us from the standard to the strong version of the sense-datum doctrine:

You allow the things immediately perceived by sense to exist nowhere without the mind: but there is nothing perceived by sense, which is not perceived immediately: therefore there is nothing sensible that exists without the mind. (3D 215, cp. 3D 203)





This apparent implication of the standard sense-datum doctrine appears to contradict flat-out our common-sense conviction that we see and feel houses and trees and other such real things in our environment. Those convinced of the truth of the philosophical doctrine will hence worry how the common-sense conviction can be maintained at the same time:

How is it possible for us to perceive real objects, given that all we can immediately perceive by sense are ideas in our minds?





In one shape or another, this has been the guiding problem of the philosophy or perception, at least from the 18th to the mid-20th century. The major philosophical theories of perception sought to address it in different ways: Different kinds of direct realism address the problem by refuting the sense-datum doctrine that raises it or at any rate arguing that we do perceive real physical objects immediately or directly. Different kinds of indirect realism, phenomenalism and idealism attempt in different ways to reconcile the doctrine with the conviction that we perceive real objects (if, perhaps, not directly).

The subsequently most popular position is a brand of indirect realism which later became known as ‘representationalism’. In Berkeley’s Dialogues, Hylas introduces it in response to the ‘easy reflection’ above and puts it thus: While ideas alone can be perceived immediately, ‘real things or external objects [are] perceived by the mediation of ideas, which are their images and representations’ (3D 203, cp. PHK 8). To spell it out more fully: We can perceive real or external objects ‘mediately’ by sense, in the sense that we can immediately perceive in our minds ideas which (i) are caused by, and (ii) resemble those objects. In this way we perceive by sense things we do not perceive immediately (so the second premise of the above argument is false), and these things are external objects.

Berkeley, however, thinks that we cannot make sense of either half—(i) or (ii)—of the crucial suggestion: The modern ‘way of accounting for our sensations or ideas’ by referring to the causal action of physical objects on our organs of sense and nerves (3D 208) does not even begin to render it intelligible how ‘sensations of sound or colour’ could possibly ‘be the effect’ of ‘a motion in the nerves’ (3D 210). Hence the suggestion that ideas are caused by external objects remains unintelligible. Second, Berkeley finds it impossible to ‘understand how anything but an idea can be like an idea’ (3D 206, cp. PHK 8), namely due to an argument which presupposes that only ideas in the mind can be ‘viewed’: ‘Two things cannot be said to be alike or unlike till they have been compared. Comparing is the viewing two ideas together and marking what they agree in and what they disagree in. The mind can compare nothing but its own ideas’ (PC 378, cp. PC 51). Hence an idea can be said to be like (or unlike) only another idea and the notion that ideas resemble external objects is unintelligible. Therefore it makes no sense to suggest that we see or feel real objects in our environment ‘by the mediation of ideas which are their images and representations’. So we still face the problem of reconciling the sense-datum doctrine with the common-sense conviction that we see and feel real objects.

In his Principles, Berkeley moots—though no more—the main idea of what later became known as ‘phenomenalism’ (PHK 3, 58): A proper analysis of their meaning is bound to reveal that statements about the sort of real objects we take ourselves to perceive actually boil down to statements about what ideas we do or would perceive in our minds if certain other conditions obtained (if we were at a different place or time, etc.). In the Dialogues, however, he offers another bold new solution, also already mooted in the Principles (PHK 6, cp. PHK 48). This solution is simple and elegant—and merely adds one step to the line of thought that appears to bring the doctrine into open conflict with common sense. He grants its conclusion, that ‘there is nothing sensible that exists without the mind’ (3D 215). On the other hand, he agrees with common sense that ‘the real things are those very things I see and feel, and perceive by my senses’ (3D 219, cp. 3D 210–211). Berkeley simply draws the conclusion these two apparently incompatible claims jointly entail: If the houses, mountains and rivers we perceive by sense are ideas in the mind, and the houses, mountains and rivers we see or feel are the real things that make up our world, then the real things that make up our world are ideas which exist in the mind. By ‘putting together’ the philosophical doctrine about perception with the common-sense conviction, one obtains the core of Berkeley’s position (cp. 3D 262). With those two premises, idealism is only a syllogism away: The real things are not represented by, but identical with ideas perceived in the mind.

This beautifully simple and apparently straightforward solution to the first problem raises another. Ideas can exist only in a mind (3D 214, 215). That something exists in a mind simply means that it is perceived there with or by a mind (3D 250, cp. Chapter 2.1). But the real things I see and feel ‘depend not on my thought, and have an existence distinct from being perceived by me’ (3D 212). The tree I see still stands in the garden when I close my eyes (cp. PHK 45). So:

How is it possible for the real things to be identical with ideas, if they exist regardless of whether I perceive them in my mind?



Berkeley’s solution again appears straightforward: If the real things are ideas, but have an existence distinct from being perceived by me, in my mind, then they must exist in some other mind; the real things are ideas which exist in ‘an infinite omnipresent spirit who contains and supports’ them (3D 212), presumably as a substance supports accidents (pace PHK 49), a ‘spirit’ being a ‘thinking substance’ (3D 231). Even where and when no finite spirit perceives them (cp. 3D 230–231), these ideas exist in the mind of God (3D 212), ‘whose will it is they should be exhibited to me’ (3D 214). This solution simultaneously appears to overcome the major obstacle to understanding how ideas can be produced in us: While the early modern ‘way of accounting for our sensations or ideas’ failed to render intelligible how physical bodies should cause ideas in our mind, ‘it is very conceivable that [ideas] should . . . be produced by a spirit; since this is no more than I daily experience myself, inasmuch as I . . . by an act of my will can form a great variety of them, and raise them up in my imagination’ (3D 214). In other words: ‘That a being endowed with knowledge and will, should produce or exhibit ideas, is easily understood’ (3D 242), quite simply because each of us produces ideas whenever we imagine something, call up a memory, or form a belief.

Alas, this response papers over the conflict between the pertinent claims and convictions without reconciling them. Consider the most careful statement of the solution in the Three Dialogues:

[T]he things I perceive are my own ideas . . . [T]hese ideas or things by me perceived, either themselves or their archetypes, exist independently of my mind . . . They must therefore exist in some other mind, whose will it is they should be exhibited to me. (3D 214, my italics)



The philosophical conclusion to be reconciled with common sense was that the things I perceive through my senses are ideas which I perceive, and which exist, in my mind: They are ‘my own ideas’. Hence these ideas do not exist independently of my mind. Therefore Berkeley has to re-introduce the notion of ‘archetypes’ of ideas, of things represented by ideas: Such archetypes, but not my own ideas can exist independently and out of my mind. Since only an idea can represent or resemble another idea, these archetypes are ideas which exist independently of my mind; being ideas, they must exist in some other mind. They exist in God’s mind. But then the things which exist independently of my mind are not the things I perceive by my senses. The latter are ideas in my mind; the former are ideas in God’s mind which are similar to but distinct from them.

The present suggestion also fails to bring us any closer to a solution to the problem about the production of ideas: The daily exercise of our powers of imagination, memory, and reflection may provide us with some sort of understanding of how a ‘being endowed with knowledge and will’ can ‘form ideas’ in his own mind. But it does not even begin to explain how one such being should produce ideas in the mind of another such being or ‘exhibit’ ideas to him. The model afforded by our daily experience may provide us with some sort of understanding of how God forms in His mind the ideas that are the archetypes of the ideas in our minds. But it does not even begin to let us understand how He should work the trick of ‘exhibiting’ to us the archetypes in His mind or producing ideas resembling them in our minds.

Berkeley’s metaphysical idealism comprises three main claims: The real things that make up our world are (combinations of) ideas. These ideas, or their archetypes, exist in the mind of God. And there is no other substance than mind or spirit, finite or infinite—there is no such thing as matter. We now need to turn to this third claim. It appears to follow from the conclusion that the sensible qualities are sensations or ideas and the decision to regard such qualities and their combinations as the real things: ‘In the common sense of the word matter’ it signifies ‘an extended, solid, figured, moveable substance existing without the mind’ (3D 225, cp. 3D 216). In other words: ‘matter’, in the standard philosophical sense of the period, is a substance distinct from the mind, in which the accidents of extension, solidity, shape, and motion exist or inhere. The arguments of the First Dialogue concluded that the sensible qualities of extension, solidity, figure and motion, that is, the extension, solidity, figure and motion we see or feel are ideas or sensations which exist only in the mind. If, then, ‘the real things are those very things I see and feel, and perceive by my senses’ (3D 219, cp. 3D 210–11), there is no real (invisible) extension different and distinct from the ‘visible extension’ we perceive by sight (cp. 3D 189) and no real (intangible) solidity in addition to the sensations of resistance we perceive by touch (cp. 3D 191): Extension, solidity, and the rest, only exist as sensations or ideas in the mind. Berkeley takes the Master Argument to have established the even stronger conclusion that the sensible things, which are combinations of these and further sensible qualities, cannot possibly exist without the mind. Hence there cannot possibly be a substance distinct from the mind in which extension, solidity, shape and motion exist or inhere, and it is ‘evident that no such substance can possibly exist’ (3D 216). There cannot be any such thing as ‘matter’ in the standard sense.

This does not exclude the existence of ‘matter’ in some other sense. Since ‘there is nothing sensible that exists without the mind’ however, this ‘matter’ will have to be ‘something that may be discovered by reason, and not by sense’ (3D 215). It will have to be something we need to posit outside our minds in order to explain how ideas of perception come to be produced in them. Accordingly, Berkley considers in turn the various roles matter could be assigned in different such explanations, in those that do not invoke Divine intervention and in such that do: He argues that we are unable to give consistent or substantive content to the notions of matter as efficient cause of ideas (3D 217), as God’s instrument in bringing about our ideas (3D 217–219), and as ‘occasion’ at ‘the presence whereof God excites ideas in our mind’ (3D 219–220). When we cannot assign it a role in the production of our ideas, however, we can only describe matter as some sort of ‘entity’ in general, and this description is utterly vacuous (3D 221–222). Finding us unable to form any conception of it, Berkeley concludes that ‘matter is impossible’ (3D 226).

All these arguments rest ultimately on the sense-datum doctrine, as defended by the unsound arguments of the First Dialogue: The crucial argument against the existence of ‘matter’ in the standard sense proceeds from the premise that the pertinent sensible qualities are ideas. So does the argument to refute the view that our ideas are produced ‘not by any act of will or spiritual efficiency, but by that kind of action which belongs to matter, viz. motion’ or impulse (3D 217, cp. Locke: EHU II.viii.11). The argument is this: Motion is a sensible quality. Sensible qualities are nothing but ideas. Ideas are ‘perfectly passive and inert, including nothing of action in them’. Therefore motion ‘is no action’ and we cannot ‘conceive any action besides volition’ (3D 217). Since my ideas of perception—in contrast to many of my other ideas—do not depend upon my own volition, they must be brought about by the will of some other, more powerful thinking being: God (3D 214). So the next arguments are to show that it is largely vacuous and incompatible with the attributes of God to suppose Him in need of any material or ‘unthinking’ substance to furnish instruments or occasions for the production of ideas in us.

To sum up: Berkeley’s idealism is a sustained attempt to reconcile the sense-datum doctrine of perception with various fundamental commonsense convictions (and with religious beliefs including his belief in Genesis, cp. 3D 250–256). This attempt assumes or presupposes the sense-datum doctrine at every major turn. The first core claim, that the real things making up our world are ideas, is to reconcile this doctrine with the commonsense conviction that we see and feel real things like houses and trees. The second core claim, that these ideas exist in the mind of God, is meant (but fails) to reconcile the previous claim with the fact that these things exist independently of my mind, when I do not perceive them. And the core arguments for the third claim, that there is no such thing as matter, proceed from the assumption that the elementary sensible objects, viz. sensible qualities, are ideas in the mind. Berkeley’s metaphysical theory is squarely based on the doctrine whose accommodation is its prime concern.

To put it in more general terms: Berkeley’s metaphysical theory is an attempt to solve reconciliation problems generated, first, by an ultimately picture-motivated philosophical doctrine and, second, by the ‘solution’ to that ‘problem’. Though most highly influential, the initial problem is ill-motivated: Berkeley supports the sense-datum doctrine that raises it only by unsound argument which presupposes the very claim it is meant to establish. The first core claim of idealism, which is to solve the ‘problem’ thus raised, is obtained by adding just one step to the reasoning that brought the latter to a head. This ‘solution’ raises new problems of the same sort, as it conflicts with other pieces of common sense. These further problems are papered over rather than solved by the further claims that make up Berkeley’s metaphysical theory. Berkeley’s guiding problems and main theory thus follow the same pattern as Locke’s problems and theories about ‘secondary qualities’ (Chapter 3.4): Where picture-motivated assumptions that raise ill-motivated problems are the basis of the theories that are to solve the ‘problems’, thinkers multiply their superfluous difficulties and end up endorsing merely apparent solutions to merely illusory problems.





5. ABSURD DOCTRINES




In this and the previous chapter, we went through all the arguments for the sense-datum doctrine—classical and more idiosyncratic—that make up its first systematic defence, and examined the consequences Berkeley derived from this influential doctrine. Again and again, we found that the arguments at issue tacitly presuppose or even explicitly assume this very doctrine. All of them are unsound. A consequence of this is that Berkeley (and all those who champion the same doctrine on the same grounds) lack all warrant for maintaining it. Indeed, Berkeley’s belief in all his core doctrines is absurd, in a strict sense of this occasionally over-used term, to which we shall now build up.

We are dealing here with a cluster of subject-relative notions of purely epistemic appraisal (the differences between which we may ignore for present purposes): You may have warrant to hold a belief which it is not reasonable for me to have; and you may be justified in assuming something it would be paranoid of me to even suspect when ignorant of all the relevant facts. What is warranted or unwarranted is not what a person believes but his believing it—or, in slightly misleading technical terms, not the proposition believed but a particular person’s propositional attitude towards it. Such warrant is independent of truth: Our belief that p may be very well founded (we may have excellent reasons to believe that p) and yet it needn’t be the case that p; conversely, it may be utterly unreasonable for me to believe that p even when it happens to be the case. These ‘purely epistemic’ terms apply quite generally to those of the so-called ‘propositional attitudes’ which (unlike, say, ‘wishing’) imply some sort and degree of belief: suspecting, fearing, expecting and hoping as well as stating, assuming and hypothesising, among others. Different attitudes require evidence or argument of different strength: What warrants a suspicion or a hypothesis need not warrant a belief or an assumption. And, whatever their object, warrant, justification and reasonableness are gradable notions: A suspicion, hypothesis or belief may be more or less warranted, justified or reasonable.

The notion of ‘absurdity’ is used to mark the bottom end of this scale: When we call a belief or claim ‘absurd’, we dismiss it as too unreasonable—for us—to merit even brief examination or discussion. We then ordinarily say that ‘everything speaks against and nothing speaks for’ the belief. But of course we do not consider ‘everything’ (all the potentially pertinent facts); rather, we say this when we take a significant number of known facts to be incompatible with the truth of the claim. If this is the case, we are justified in regarding the claim as false and it is utterly unreasonable for us to believe it true, unless we have at the same time some specific and at least prima facie sound reasons to do so. (If the reasons we can come up with fail to be even prima facie sound, we ought to have seen through them and our belief is absurd anyway.) We then say that ‘nothing speaks for’ the belief when we are unaware of any such reasons. So someone’s belief is absurd or, to be more precise,

it is absurd for a subject to believe that p iff he is actually entitled to regard p as false in the absence of specific and at least prima facie sound reasons but does not have any such reasons.



As a non-philosophical first example,4 consider a pathologically jealous wife who comes to believe that her affectionate and devoted husband has betrayed her, and does so on no other grounds than a transparently slanderous denunciation she sees through immediately: She knows perfectly well that her husband is affectionate and devoted to her, and this known fact renders it unlikely that her belief is true. Her belief is thus unreasonable unless supported by reasons that look sound at least at first sight. But her sole reason for it is a slanderous denunciation that she sees through immediately. So the belief she cannot help maintain even so is absurd.

How much evidence or how strong an argument a subject has to provide to render a belief justified depends to a large extent upon whether this belief is part of, compatible or inconsistent with what we may call ‘received wisdom’, namely the body of beliefs generally accepted as common knowledge. Where a belief goes beyond or even against such received wisdom, the proponent is required to produce significant or even extremely strong evidence or argument. But where a belief is part of received wisdom, a subject need not know any argument or evidence himself; it is enough for him to point out that he learned this at school, read it in a textbook, or that ‘everybody knows’, and his belief will be accepted as warranted. Such received wisdom is of course not immune to challenge. But until forcefully challenged the beliefs that make it up are accepted as warranted and a subject need not be able to provide any reasons supporting the belief to be entitled to hold it. The moment we realise a belief p is inconsistent with a body of such received wisdom, we are therefore entitled to conclude that it is not the case that p and it is unreasonable of us to believe that p—until we come to know at least prima facie sound reasons which support the conclusion that p.

The biggest body of received wisdom is common sense. Beliefs which are ‘paradoxical’ in the down-to-earth sense of being inconsistent with commonsense convictions are therefore candidates for absurdity: In the absence of at least prima facie sound specific reasons, we are entitled to regard them as false, and those who hold such a belief without having such reasons hold an absurd belief. We thus obtain a philosophically important sufficient condition:

It is absurd for a subject to believe that p if p is inconsistent with common-sense convictions and he has no specific and at least prima facie sound reasons to believe that p.





To defend a paradoxical belief against such a charge of absurdity, a thinker needs to provide such reasons—or to show, alternatively, that his belief is in fact compatible with the common-sense convictions with which it appeared to be inconsistent.

As Berkeley is aware, common sense maintains that ‘those things [we] immediately perceive are the real things’ (3D 262): Of course only philosophers use the term “immediate perception”, but we certainly believe that most of the time we perceive physical objects like houses and mountains, and do so without the intervention of any other thing (unless we look into a mirror or see a reflection in water or in a dark window). Similarly, it is common sense that when we use our eyes or fingers we see and feel, in as strict or loose a sense of “see” and “feel” as you please, physical things rather than mental images and sensations, respectively. On all of its versions, the sense-datum doctrine of perception is inconsistent with common-sense convictions.

We have just seen that Berkeley adopts the first two core claims of his idealism in an unsuccessful attempt to reconcile the two (Section 4). But adding these two claims to the sense-datum doctrine, we found, does not add up to a whole consistent with the common-sense convictions he wants to honour. Hence the sense-datum doctrine remains paradoxical, and his belief in it is absurd unless he has specific and at least prima facie sound reasons to support it. To render belief in this genuinely paradoxical doctrine reasonable, it is therefore not enough for Berkeley to point out advantageous consequences of his position (PHK 85ff., 3D 257–58), such as that idealism refutes scepticism—of a kind only generated by the sense-datum doctrine, as it happens (3D 227–230). Given the doctrine’s actual conflict with common sense, he has to adduce arguments which specifically support it. But none of his arguments specifically for this doctrine is even prima facie sound: They either explicitly assume the doctrine from the start (Chapters 4.5–4.6, 5.1, 5.3) or openly rely on a patently unsound principle of inference, the Phenomenal Principle, which only appears plausible to those already in the grip of the doctrine (Chapter 5.2). What gets stronger and stronger as argument gets piled on argument is not the case for maintaining the paradoxical doctrine—no such case is being built up—but the case for crediting its proponents with belief bias and dismissing their reasons. Berkeley’s belief in the sense-datum doctrine is absurd in the strict sense explained: It is absurd for him to maintain this doctrine. Of course he is not alone in this: Many champions of sense-data relied exclusively on some of the arguments Berkeley states, plus the ‘argument from illusion’ which we shall find to be no less unsound (Chapters 6.1 and 8.2). We can therefore conclude that this popular doctrine was as absurd for many of its proponents to maintain as for Berkeley. (In fact, it has, to the best of my knowledge, been absurd for any of its champions to date.)

The core claims of Berkeley’s idealism are no less absurd: First, Berkeley does not manage to render them consistent with the common-sense convictions he wants to honour (Section 4); they are at least as paradoxical as the sense-datum doctrine. Second, Berkeley derives them in a straightforward manner from this doctrine. That is, he bases them on arguments which openly assume an absurdity and therefore are not even prima facie sound. Both the popular sense-datum doctrine and the core claims of his less popular brand of idealism are absurd for him to maintain.

This is no coincidence but a characteristic result of non-intentional reasoning under the spell of philosophical pictures: When we excessively assimilate abstract phenomena to simple concrete models, against our better knowledge, we will typically obtain a claim that is inconsistent with the common sense of which this better knowledge is a part. The paradoxical claim will typically be supported by nothing but the non-intentional inference that, once spelled out, is patently unsound by the thinker’s own lights, since it presupposes an analogy or similarity which the thinker knows not to obtain. We have encountered some examples, including:





To think about something is to perceive some thing somewhere in us, through an inner sense. (Chapter 1.3)

The things perceived in the mind are in the mind. (Chapter 2.1)

A quality is a thing that can only exist in other objects. (Chapter 3.1)





All these picture-driven conclusions satisfy the philosophically important sufficient condition of absurdity: They are at odds with facts the thinkers acknowledge or with definitions they champion, i.e., with parts of the ‘received wisdom’. Through sublimating explanations and reconciliatory theorising, philosophers who find them attractive then address this apparent defect, like Berkeley, by trying to show that these conclusions and further conclusions derived from them are in fact consistent with common sense. Where these efforts fail and also no at least prima facie sound argument is forthcoming in the conclusions’ support, these conclusions are and remain absurd.

Sublimating explanations tend to fail: They either merely paper over the conflict with common sense (in particular when they remain purely metaphorical) or are but a fig leaf behind which sordid business is carried on as usual (when these explanations get ignored even by their own authors). When sublimation leads a thinker to explicitly maintain a paradoxical belief without further argument added to the initial non-intentional inference, this belief will hence be absurd. We have encountered several beliefs which were widely shared and arguably formed by picture-driven leaps and their sublimation. They include:





Locke’s belief that there are ideas perceived in minds (Chapter 1.3),

Locke’s belief, shared by Boyle and Galileo, that the only qualities to really exist in a sensible body are the qualities of the insensible particles that make it up (Chapter 3.2),

Berkeley’s belief that qualities do not exist each of them apart by itself but blended together, several in the same object (Chapter 4.4).



When such absurd claims are presupposed in further reasoning, the conclusions obtained will frequently—though not invariably—be as inconsistent with common sense as the presuppositions they rely on, while the reasoning relying on those presuppositions will be utterly unsound by the lights of the very thinker who engages in it. Examples encountered include:





the widely shared belief that the mind is a private realm (Chapter 2.1),

the belief shared by Galileo, Boyle and Locke that colour, taste, smell and sound are sensations in us (Chapter 3.3),

Locke’s and Berkeley’s belief that whenever we see or hear, taste or smell, anything, we perceive ideas that have come into our minds (Chapters 3.5 and 4.2).





These subsequent conclusions occasioned—unsuccessful—efforts to support them by further argument or show them consistent with common sense (or both). We have considered Locke’s theories of secondary qualities and of external sensation, as well as Berkeley’s idealism, and seen that all of them fail to reconcile the pertinent beliefs with the common-sense convictions with which they appear to be at odds (Chapters 3.4–3,5 and 5.4). The justificatory efforts fared no better, as the arguments adduced presupposed either the very doctrine they were meant to support or the absurd claims from which the doctrine had been inferred at an earlier stage of the inferential avalanche that is the pictorial process (Chapters 3.3–3.5, 4 and 5). This process systematically generates absurd beliefs. When under the spell of philosophical pictures, thinkers trade in absurdities and do so en gros, not en détail.





6 Philosophical Delusions


Ayer Reinvents ‘Perceptions’




Processes of non-intentional reasoning may lead even the most competent thinkers to adopt paradoxical philosophical claims, which belief-bias effects may lead them to maintain in the continued absence of warrant. The clash of these unwarranted—and typically absurd (Chapter 5.5)—claims with common sense or science then gives rise to ill-motivated problems, met with philosophical theories built on the very claims that conjured up the problems. Up to now, we have documented this development for two eminent early modern thinkers: Locke and Berkeley. Even when their importance is unreservedly acknowledged, there may be a temptation to think that centuries of intellectual progress separate us from those thinkers and protect us from their misfortunes. In particular, there may be a temptation to think that, if nothing else, the rigour and subtlety of 20th-century analytic philosophy will have neutralised the influences of non-intentional reasoning and belief bias. To find out whether this thought is true or overly complacent, we now advance from the 17th to the 20th century and turn from classical to logical empiricism, namely from the first systematic defence of the sense-datum doctrine, by Berkeley, to the doctrine’s most sophisticated development, by A.J. Ayer. We will find that plus ça change, plus c’est pareil: Just like the early modern defence, the sophisticated analytic development of the doctrine presupposes everything it is to show, and more.

In picture-driven reflection, as in belief bias, we encountered a curious persistence of assumptions that kept cropping up where they should not: picture-driven claims were tacitly presupposed even once they had been explicitly rejected, and thinkers unwittingly relied upon certain claims in the course of the very arguments adduced to support them. Ayer’s work is marked by a curious persistence of a similar kind: Ayer famously rejects the classical ‘factual’ version of the sense-datum doctrine and re-develops the doctrine as the recommendation of a new terminology (Section 1)—but keeps presupposing the doctrine’s rejected version in further reasoning, which leads him into a series of philosophical problems and to the theory of phenomenalism that is to solve them (Section 2). Indeed, he presupposes the classical version of the doctrine already in the course of the ‘linguistic’ re-evaluation that leads to its explicit rejection (Section 3). This makes for some striking parallels to a textbook case of delusion (Section 4). After taking on a tough problem from the philosophy of mental health and defining the concept of ‘delusion’ (Section 5), we will see that Ayer’s curious attachment to the classical sense-datum doctrine is constitutive of a delusion. Indeed, we will see that the processes of non-intentional reasoning analysed in the previous chapters systematically generate beliefs that satisfy a strict definition of ‘delusion’ and are constitutive of a kind of intellectual altitude sickness (Section 6). These findings imply the need for therapy in philosophy (Section 7).





1. INTRODUCING SENSE-DATA:

AYER’S LINGUISTIC EVALUATION




Ayer bases his case for the sense-datum doctrine exclusively on the argument from illusion, which builds on an intuition we can easily reproduce: Place a round silver coin on an otherwise empty table. Then view it from the side, three steps or so back. The round coin now looks elliptical to you. But you now see something that not merely looks elliptical but is elliptical: You see an elliptical silvery patch, a spot of colour that actually is elliptical. (Just look!) When you (as we would ordinarily say) stare at the round coin, this elliptical silvery spot is staring right back at you. Since it is elliptical, this spot cannot be identical with the physical coin, which of course remains round. The elliptical spot you see must be a non-physical object of perception, a ‘perception’ or ‘sense-datum’.

The arguments from illusion that proceed from this intuition descend from the ‘arguments from relativity’ we have considered earlier in their Berkeleyan guise (Chapter 5.2). However, they rely on stronger assumptions: Where Berkeley’s argument stopped short of assuming that when something looks small from a mile’s distance it actually is rather big, and merely asserted that the appearance grows larger and larger as you approach, the present arguments proceed from such assumptions as that the coin actually is round when it looks elliptical. Arguments from relativity merely assume that appearances are relative to the position and condition of the perceiver; arguments from illusion assume that some such appearances are an ‘illusion’ in the sense that things look or otherwise appear F while actually having the different property F’, namely another determinate property falling under the same determinable. At the same time the arguments from illusion are similar to those from relativity in employing a phenomenal principle like the Principle P Berkeley relied on (Chapter 5.2).
