But since we now explicitly assume the existence of material objects which fail to be F when they look F, we do not want to assume, as Principle P did, that all objects of perception have a sensible quality F when they look or otherwise appear F. Rather, we want to say more cautiously that there are such objects of perception (if in addition to others): Whenever we perceive something that looks or otherwise appears F to us, there are objects of perception which have the sensible quality F (the second premise below).

Properly spelled out, the present argument is this:1





In some cases of perception, physical objects appear to possess sensible qualities that they do not actually possess. (When you look at a round coin from the side, it looks elliptical.)

Whenever something appears to a subject to possess a sensible quality, there is something of which the subject is aware which does possess that quality. (When something looks elliptical to you, there is an elliptical spot of which you are aware.) Hence:

In some cases of perception there is something of which the subject is aware which possesses sensible qualities which the physical object the subject is purportedly perceiving does not possess. (There is a silvery spot which is elliptical, while the coin is not.)





Whence it follows, by Leibniz’s Law, that in perception we are sometimes aware of something other than physical objects, namely of immaterial ‘sense-data’. But in the ‘delusive’ cases the argument starts out from, our visual experience is qualitatively so similar to the experience we have in those ‘veridical’ cases in which things do appear to us the way they actually are, that we must be aware of the same kind of thing in both. Whence it is finally inferred that in perception we are always aware of immaterial sense-data.

This argument turns on the non-standard use of terms. As any dictionary reminds us, “aware of” is commonly used to mean ‘having knowledge or understanding of’. Like the claim that someone knows something, the claim that he is aware of something entails that certain facts obtain; and like the attribution of knowledge, it entails no claim about the ‘experience’ the subject ‘enjoys’: When aware of a problem, risk, opportunity, etc., we know that there is a problem or risk, etc., and may be aware of it throughout, even when we do not think of it the whole time. Similarly, when you assert that ‘Smith is aware of the noise Jones’ lawn-mower makes’, you imply that Jones’ lawn-mower is in fact noisy, and that Smith knows this; and your assertion need not even suggest that there is something to be heard at the time: ‘Well aware of the noise Jones’ lawn-mower makes, Smith closed the windows already the moment Jones approached his tool shed.’ Even when there is a noise to be heard, someone who does not hear it may be said to be aware of it: ‘The invigilator moved the examination to another hall, because he was aware of the distracting noise’ is true also in case the almost deaf invigilator was alerted to the noise only by a written note. Accordingly, ‘There is an elliptical patch of which you are aware’ would commonly be understood to express a claim not about any ‘experience’ you enjoy ‘in perception’, but about facts you know and became aware of ‘through perception’ or otherwise: There is in fact something elliptical lying around to be seen or felt, and you know this (because you see or feel it, or, e.g., someone tells you).

On this reading, the notorious Phenomenal Principle—(2) above—is clearly false: When the round coin looks elliptical, there is nothing elliptical lying around for me to be aware of. But, equally clearly, its proponents do not want to talk about our knowledge of things actually lying around, competing for space with coins or sticks. They give the term “aware” a new use, if they do not resort to the new phrases “directly aware” or “directly perceive” from the start. Typically, neither innovation is explained in any detail. What gives reason for concern is of course not the novelty as such, but the lack of explanation.

Unlike many other sense-datum theorists, Ayer is aware of the novelty, and seeks to remedy the lack, by means of his famous ‘linguistic evaluation’ of the argument from illusion, in the opening chapter of his Foundations of Empirical Knowledge (henceforward: FEK): Properly spelled out, the Phenomenal Principle emerges as a rule of language that introduces the new phrases. Ayer treats “x is directly aware of y” as synonymous with “x directly perceives y”, which he presents as a new, third, sense of perception-verbs that philosophers introduce in addition to two established senses of those verbs. Ayer infers the existence of these two senses from the fact that different perception-reports are equally legitimate: ‘For example, a man will say that he sees a distant star which has an extension greater than that of the earth; but if he is asked to describe what it is that he is actually seeing, he may say that it is a silvery speck no bigger than a sixpence’ (FEK 22). In this case, says Ayer, there need be no contradiction, as the man may use the verb “to see” in two different senses:

In one sense [A], the sense in which the man can say truly that he sees the star, it is [i] necessary that what is seen should really exist, but [ii] not necessary that it should have the qualities it appears to have. In another sense [B], which is that in which the man can say truly that what he sees is no bigger than a sixpence, it is [ii] not possible that anything should seem to have qualities that it does not really have, but [i] also not necessary that what is seen should really exist. (FEK 23)





‘What the advocates of the sense-datum theory have done’—namely, by upholding the Phenomenal Principle which is plainly false when interpreted in accordance with the rules of ordinary language—is ‘to decide’ to introduce a further, third sense of verbs of perception, which combines feature (i) of Sense A with feature (ii) of Sense B: They ‘define [“direct perception” or] “direct awareness” in such a way that if someone is directly aware of [or perceives] an object x, it follows that x exists and that it really has whatever properties it is appearing to have’ (FEK 61). ‘At the same time’, they thus ‘decide to apply’ these words in this sense ‘to delusive as well as to veridical experiences’ (FEK 24). ‘The word “sense-datum” [then] is to stand [quite simply] for any object of which it is conceivable that someone should [perceive it in this new sense, i.e.] be directly aware’ (FEK 59).

This new sense of perception terms is related to the two new notions we have seen Berkeley introduced (Chapter 4.1). According to an intuitive line of thought, everything that is ‘directly perceived’ in Ayer’s new sense, will be ‘perceived immediately’ and ‘strictly speaking by sense’, in Berkeley’s terms: That we ‘directly perceive’ something, in the present new sense, implies that the thing is F when it looks or otherwise appears F; and we are to apply this verb in all cases of perception. So whenever something looks silvery to me, at any rate the thing I directly perceive actually is silvery. I can just see that the object of direct perception has this colour and do not have to infer this; I can perceive it by sense, strictly speaking, without making any inference. Second, the things I can be said to ‘perceive directly’ are said to exist— and have the properties they appear to have—also when my experience is delusive and no object with the qualities things look to me to have exist in my physical environment. Presumably, the objects of direct perception then exist in my mind, instead, where I perceive them immediately. If we further assume that what we directly perceive are always objects of the same kind, we will conclude that what we perceive directly are always ideas which we immediately perceive in our minds—and through whose intervention we perhaps perceive objects outside our mind, whose properties we need to infer from those of our ideas.

At any rate at first glance, however, Ayer appears to deploy his new terms in a novel way which does not allow us to draw these classical conclusions. For a start, he accepts the argument from illusion only in this ‘linguistic’ form, which explicitly phrases the Phenomenal Principle—(2) below—as a rule introducing a new sense of perception-verbs (cp. FEK 24–25):





When we see [in sense A] a straight stick partially immersed in water (where it looks bent), the stick appears to us to have a property it does not really possess (as it appears bent rather than straight).

Let there be a sense of “to perceive” and its cognates in which, whenever a subject perceives anything in any sense [A or B], there actually is something that is being perceived, that really has the properties it appears to have.

By (1) and (2) and Leibniz’s Law: When we [in sense A] see the stick-in-water, there actually is something other than the stick, the physical object, that is being perceived (in the new sense of that verb).

Apply the term “sense-datum” to whatever objects are being perceived in the new sense of the verb.

By (3) and (4): When we [in sense A] see the stick in water, we perceive (in the new sense) a sense-datum but no physical object.





Once its first half is thus recast, the argument no longer presents a paradox. It no longer even appears to bring out a contradiction in our common beliefs: Between the conclusion that, in one (new) sense, we ‘perceive’ no physical object but a sense-datum and the assumption that we then ‘perceive’ a stick, in another (established) sense, there is no more of a contradiction than between the two statements of the star-watcher. Instead of establishing a paradoxical conclusion, the ‘argument’ merely illustrates the use of two new rules of language that introduce a new terminology:

The philosopher who says that he is seeing a sense-datum in a case where most people would say that they were seeing a material thing is not contradicting the received opinion on any question of fact . . . What he is doing is simply to recommend a new verbal usage. He is proposing to us that instead of speaking, for example, of seeing a straight stick which looks crooked . . . we should speak of seeing a sense-datum which really has the quality of being crooked, and which belongs to a straight stick. (FEK 25)



The generalisation from delusive to all cases of perception turns on the ‘contention that if [delusive and veridical] perceptions are not qualitatively distinguishable the objects perceived must be of the same kind’ (FEK 24–25). Crucially, Ayer accepts also this ‘contention’ only ‘as a rule of language’ (FEK 25): One and the same label should be applied to those objects. As the label “sense-datum” is to be applied to whatever is being ‘perceived’ in the new sense—(4) above—, the further rule actually is to use perception-verbs in this new sense, regardless of whether the perception is delusive or veridical. We may therefore note (even if Ayer does not) that, in the latter case, what we perceive in the new sense are physical objects, which then ‘really exist and have the properties they appear to have’. Thus, the new rules have us apply the label “sense-datum” also to physical objects: In some situations, the ‘sense-data’ we are said to ‘sense’ are material things. Therefore, Ayer is perfectly right to conclude his evaluation by stating that the final conclusion of the argument, ‘What we see, or otherwise directly experience, are never material things, but only sense-data’, does not express ‘a proposition about an empirical matter of fact’, viz., about which of two different kinds of thing we perceive, but rather a ‘resolution about the usage of words’ (FEK 28), viz., about which of two different sets of terms to use: To say what you perceive, always use the terminology of ‘direct perception’ and ‘sense-data’.

Thus properly understood, the so-called ‘argument from illusion’ is no argument at all: It is not even meant to establish the truth of any claim. Rather, it is a ‘procedure’ for introducing a new ‘terminology’ (FEK 25): ‘[T]he expressions “direct awareness” and “sense-datum” are to be regarded as correlative; and since each of them is being used in a special, technical sense, it is not satisfactory merely to define one in terms of the other. It is necessary first to employ some other method, such as the method of giving examples’ (FEK 61). Such examples are provided by the ‘delusive’ cases the ‘argument from illusion’ starts out from, where one is tempted to point in the direction of the (straight) stick or the (round) coin, and say, ‘Do you see the bent speck/the elliptical spot? That speck/ this spot is what I mean by a “sense-datum”, and your seeing it is a case of “direct perception”’. The first half of the ‘argument’ seeks to explain this in a less objectionable form; it explains the meaning of new technical terms by giving examples. The second half then motivates the recommendation that the new terms be applied in all cases of perception.

Ayer stresses that in endorsing the sense-datum ‘theory’ he endorses but this recommendation: ‘[T]he acceptance of this theory involves nothing more than a decision to use a technical language’ (FEK 57). Indeed, ‘this important point’ holds quite generally for ‘what philosophers call theories of perception’ (FEK 48). None of them puts into question anything we believe about matters of fact. Ayer’s evaluation thus illustrates a more general introductory remark of Ayer’s most acute critic, J.L. Austin: ‘[S]trange though the [sense-datum] doctrine looks, we are sometimes told to take it easy—really it’s just what we’ve all believed all along. (There’s the bit where you say it and the bit where you take it back.)’ (S&S 2). We now turn to the bit where Ayer reinstates what he here takes back.





2. THE PERSISTENCE OF IMPLICIT BELIEF:

AYER’S REAL VIEW




Ayer’s evaluation of the argument from illusion and the sense-datum ‘theory’ it seeks to establish appears, at first sight, to lend substance to the claims with which he introduces his own approach to ‘the problem of specifying the relationship of material things to sense-data’ (FEK 229):

There is, indeed, a sense in which it is correct to say that both sense-data and material things exist, inasmuch as sentences that are used to describe sense-data and sentences that are used to describe material things both very frequently express true propositions. But it would not be correct to infer from this that there really were both material things and sense-data, in the sense in which it can truly be said that there really are chairs as well as tables, or that there are tastes as well as sounds. For whereas, in these cases, the existential propositions refer to different empirical “facts”, this does not hold good in the case of sense-data and material things. (FEK 229)





Certainly—according to his evaluation, talk of sense-data was introduced to re-describe familiar facts about material things (e.g., that the straight stick looks crooked when immersed in water), indeed, introduced in such a way that material things sometimes are (being referred to as) ‘sense-data’. The empirical facts are exactly what we took them to be all along; the sense-datum theorists merely introduced a new way of talking about them that is useful ‘in philosophising about perception’ (FEK 26).

But, it quickly emerges, this is not at all what Ayer here means to claim: The reason why it is ‘not correct’ to say that ‘there really are both material things and sense-data’ is that, upon final analysis, ‘material things are nothing but collections of . . . sense-data’ (FEK 231)! Of course, Ayer assures the reader, material things are not literally constituted of immaterial sense-data ‘as a patchwork quilt consists of different coloured pieces of silk’ (FEK 232); rather, they are ‘logical constructions out of sense-data’ (FEK 237). Initially, ‘what is being claimed is simply that the propositions which are ordinarily expressed by sentences which refer to material things could also be expressed by sentences which referred exclusively to sense-data’ (FEK 232). But already in the two very next sentences Ayer sets out to show something far more ambitious:

As for the belief in the “unity” and “substantiality” of material things, I shall show that it may be correctly represented as involving no more than the attribution to visual and tactual sense-data of certain relations which do, in fact, obtain in our experience. And I shall show that it is only the contingent fact that there are these relations between sense-data that makes it profitable to describe the course of our experience in terms of the existence and behaviour of material things. (FEK 232)





I.e.: All we actually ‘describe’ when talking of material things is ‘the course of our experience’. And it is only due to the fortunate coincidence that the sense-data that make up this experience stand in certain relations, that it is even ‘possible for us successfully to employ the physical terminology that we do’ (FEK 243), i.e., to speak as if there were substantial things like sticks and stars—even though, in fact, our experiences are all there is.

Proceeding from an analysis of a much earlier part of the text (FEK 11–19), Austin arrived at the same conclusion, namely that ‘the apparent sophistication of Ayer’s ‘linguistic’ doctrine really rests squarely on the old Berkeleian . . . ontology of the ‘sensible manifold’’ (S&S 61). Ayer believes that all there is and all we can perceive (and talk about by means of our ‘physical terminology’) are sense-data. I.e.: Ayer upholds not only the very factual, paradoxical, claim about perception that, according to his evaluation, fails to be established by the ‘arguments’ philosophers typically adduce for their ‘theories of perception’. He also maintains an even stronger, ontological, claim that entails that doctrine about perception.

Far from lending the latter any support, however, the—rather quick-and- dirty—reasoning that leads Ayer to the ontological claim tacitly presupposes the sense-datum doctrine of perception. This reasoning turns on a semantic claim: Ayer believes that propositions that refer to material things are expressible in terms of sense-data. He does not provide what ‘would seem . . . the best way to justify’ this claim, namely ‘specimen translations’ of statements referring to material things into statements explicitly about sense-data (FEK 238). Indeed, he thinks that such translations are impossible, since ‘references to material things are vague in their application to phenomena and . . . the series of sense-data that they may be understood to specify are composed of infinite sets of terms’ (FEK 242). Even so, Ayer believes that ‘any proposition that refers to a material thing must somehow be expressible in terms of sense-data’, namely ‘if it is to be factually significant’ (FEK 231, my italics)—as it surely is. Ayer clearly assumes that propositions about material things have an ‘empirical’ or ‘factual significance’ precisely to the extent to which they are grounded in propositions about sense-data. Therefore the former somehow boil down to the latter, (facts about) material things really are (facts about) sense-data, and upon final analysis our experiences are all there actually is.

The crucial (semantic) assumption follows from a definition and an epistemological doctrine:

We say that a sentence is factually significant to any given person, if, and only if, he knows how to verify the proposition it purports to express—that is, if he knows what observations would lead him, under certain conditions, to accept the proposition as being true, or reject it as being false. 2





And the only observations that lead us to accept or reject propositions about material things are observations of sense-data; our evidence for those propositions ‘will always consist in the occurrence of . . . sense-data’ (FEK 231, cp. FEK 17).

The most influential motivation for accepting only ‘facts about our experience’ (FEK 17) as evidence is the quest for certainty. But, far from embracing it, Ayer explicitly rejects this quest, as based on a conceptual confusion: ‘The notion of certainty does not apply to propositions in which we express our perceptual judgements’ (FEK 45). So why does he maintain the epistemological doctrine, even so? The above passage continues: ‘Indeed, there is nothing else [but the occurrence of sense-data] in which one can legitimately suppose [our empirical evidence] to consist, once one has accepted the rule that the word “sense-datum” is to be used to stand for whatever is, in fact, observed’ (FEK 231). But of course Ayer had explicitly introduced the word “sense-datum” to stand for whatever is directly perceived. Thus, he manifestly makes a leap of thought that presupposes that only what is directly perceived is in fact observed. Failing to support his epistemological doctrine by any independent argument, Ayer here tacitly and (presumably) unwittingly relies on a ‘factual’ version of the sense-datum ‘theory’ of perception, of the sort he has explicitly rejected.

Arguably, the same thing happens again, e.g., when Ayer asserts without argument, as if it was entirely uncontroversial, that ‘facts about our experiences’ constitute ‘the evidence that is ordinarily thought [!] sufficient to establish’ assumptions about material things (FEK 17): Without noticing it, I submit, he conflates the standard explanation ‘Empirical evidence is the evidence accessible to our senses’ and his doctrine ‘Empirical evidence is the evidence constituted by facts about our experiences’. In unwittingly moving from the truism to the contentious doctrine, he presupposes that only facts about sense-data are accessible to our senses.

To sum up: In the end, Ayer sticks to everything he took back, and makes even stronger claims. He continues to rely on the ‘factual’ version of the sense-datum doctrine or ‘factual sense-datum doctrine’ for short, that is, on the very—paradoxical—doctrine that he knows full well not to be supported by the arguments he deems relevant. He presupposes this doctrine in leaping to an epistemological doctrine, from which he infers a semantic claim from which, in turn, he proceeds to an ontological conclusion. These are the three pillars of the philosophical theory known as ‘phenomenalism’: All empirical evidence is constituted by facts about sense-data; all claims about material things somehow boil down to claims about sense-data; upon final analysis, all there actually is are our experiences.

We credit people with beliefs not only on the basis of their statements (sincere or other) but, more generally, on the basis of their actions, verbal and other—where deeds may speak more loudly than words. These deeds include the inferences we make. Ayer thus continues to maintain the factual sense-datum doctrine as what we may call an ‘implicit belief’ and attribute to thinkers on the basis of sufficient conditions including this one:

A subject maintains the implicit belief that p if he presupposes that p in arguments or lines of thought he advances explicitly.





Once he has refuted the paradoxical factual version of the sense-datum doctrine as unwarranted, Ayer continues to maintain it as an implicit belief.

This belief is of the greatest consequence: It is not merely the foundation of Ayer’s phenomenalist theory but also generates the guiding problem this theory is to solve. Since all we actually perceive are immaterial sense-data, all our claims about the ‘stars’ and ‘sticks’, ‘coins’ and ‘tables’ we take to see around us must in fact boil down to a description of sense-data. But how is this possible?

[H]ow [is] it possible for us to describe our sense-data, which are conceived to be transitory and private, by the use of expressions which ostensibly refer to substances which are supposed to endure unperceived, to be endowed with causal properties, to be accessible to different senses and to different observers, and to stand to one another in the system of relations that constitutes their being in physical space? (FEK 244)





The bulk of Ayer’s Foundations is devoted to answering this question in a way that reconciles the doctrine that all we in fact perceive are transitory, private, and immaterial sense-data with the undeniable fact that we successfully communicate by means of terms which we take to refer to objects that are rather more stable, public, material and causally efficacious: After presenting his linguistic evaluation of the sense-datum doctrine (FEK Chapter 1), he explains the stability of physical things (in what sense things may be ‘supposed to endure unperceived’) (FEK Chapter 2), their publicity (how ‘the publicity of these material things . . . is compatible with the privacy of sense-data’) (FEK Chapter 3), their causal efficacy (‘what we mean by attributing causal properties to them’) (FEK Chapter 4), and their material or physical nature (how ‘we derive from successive sense-data the conception of a single, persistent material thing’) (FEK Chapter 5) (all quotes from Ayer’s own summary on FEK 244).

All of these problems are generated only by the sense-datum doctrine, and only on the factual reading Ayer initially sought to overcome: As long as we bear in mind that the initial definition of “sense-data” is frequently satisfied by material objects and that we can be said to ‘see’ or ‘feel’ such objects in the most familiar sense of these verbs even in the cases of delusive perception in which we ‘directly perceive’ no material object, there does not even appear to be a tension between the recommendation that we should always be said to ‘directly perceive’ only ‘sense-data’ and the common-sense convictions implicit in our ordinary talk of physical objects. So even after having ‘evaluated’ the sense-datum doctrine as amounting merely to the recommendation of a new terminology which puts none of our factual beliefs into question, Ayer continues to agonise about problems which the doctrine generates only on the factual reading he explicitly rejected—and proceeds to ‘solve’ them by means of a theory built on the same factual claim.





3. BELIEF BIAS: AYER’S EVALUATION REVISITED




In fact, Ayer takes the truth of the factual sense-datum doctrine for granted even in the course of the linguistic evaluation of the argument from illusion that has him reject this version or reading of the doctrine. As we have seen (in Section 1), Ayer’s reasoning proceeds from the distinction between two allegedly established uses of perception-verbs, which make for different descriptions of such cases of ‘qualitatively delusive perception’ as the ‘argument from illusion’ starts out from: Sense A, in which it is ‘[i] necessary that what is seen should really exist, but [ii] not necessary that it should have the qualities it appears to have’; and sense B, in which ‘it is [ii] not possible that anything should seem to have qualities that it does not really have, but [i] also not necessary that what is seen should really exist’ (FEK 23). This latter sense Ayer took the star-watcher to employ in saying ‘I see a small silvery speck’ when looking at a distant celestial body greater than the Earth (FEK 22).

But, first, Ayer explicitly assumes that there actually is a huge star to be seen and that the star-watcher sees it (in sense A). And, pace B(i), we would certainly not say that the man sees anything at all (in any sense), if nothing (no star, planet, satellite or plane etc.) was to be seen in the region of the sky he was looking at (cp. S&S 94-–95). Second, pace B(ii), we do not use the phrase “a small silvery speck” to describe anything as actually being small and silvery, when speaking of far-away things (rather than specks of grime or spots on a dress). In these situations, we use the idiom of ‘spots’, ‘specks’ and ‘patches’ to pick out or characterise the things we see in terms of how they look or appear to us, and of how well we can see them.

To return to Berkeley’s example (from NTV 44, Chapter 5.2), we might, on a country walk, ask our companion: ‘What is that faint, small, reddish spot over yonder? Is that the brick tower where our guidebook tells us to turn right?’ We resort to this idiom when we cannot discern of what kind the thing to be seen is; we say how it looks when we cannot (yet) tell what it is. We speak of a ‘small reddish spot’ when we cannot tell whether the thing we see over yonder is, say, a brick tower (or something else). What we refer to with those words is the thing we see (the tower, if that is what the spot turns out to be); we typically refer to it thus only as long as we cannot tell; and we then do not imply that the thing we see actually is small (instead of merely looking small to us, from here, now).

Accordingly, when gazing up at the night sky we say ‘I see a small silvery speck’ when we cannot tell whether what we see is a star, a planet or a satellite. But we do then claim that there really is some thing, made out of gas or silicone or whatnot, while we do not claim that this thing actually is small. Adhering, by assumption, to the established usage, the star-watcher uses “to see” in Ayer’s sense A, both when saying that he sees a huge star and when saying that he sees a small speck: He is using the expressions “a huge star” and “a small speck” to refer to one and the same, ‘really existing’, object of perception, viz., a celestial body. In one case, he says of what kind that object is, classifies it in terms of ‘qualities it really has’. In the other, he says how it looks to him. And in both cases it is, in Ayer’s slightly misleading words, ‘not necessary’ that the object ‘should have the qualities it appears to have’.

What matters for present purposes is not that Ayer’s analysis of our established usage is wrong, but that it is based on a leap of thought that presupposes a factual claim of the very kind Ayer’s ‘linguistic evaluation’ sought to overcome. Ayer takes the star-watcher to be using “to see” in two different senses, because he assumes that in saying ‘I see a silvery speck no bigger than a sixpence’ the man ‘say[s] truly that what he sees is no bigger than a sixpence’ (FEK 23). I.e.: Ayer thinks that the expression “small silvery speck” is being used to describe—correctly—something that is small (and silvery). Without any argument, he leaps to that idea from this description of the example: ‘a man will say that he sees a distant star . . . but if he is asked to describe what it is that he is actually seeing, he may say that it is a silvery speck no bigger than a sixpence’ (FEK 22). The latter assumption does not entail the conclusion by itself; in moving from the former to the latter, Ayer presupposes an immediate consequence of the Phenomenal Principle, understood as a factual claim:



(A) When he looks at a huge distant star which looks no bigger than a sixpence, the man says ‘I see a silvery speck no bigger than a sixpence’ to describe what it is that he is actually seeing.



(P*) When he looks at a huge distant star which looks no bigger than a sixpence, the man is actually seeing some thing (a speck or sense-datum) that really is no bigger than a sixpence. Hence:



(C) When he looks at a huge distant star which looks no bigger than a sixpence, the man says ‘I see a silvery speck no bigger than a sixpence’ to describe some thing that really is no bigger than a sixpence.



In declaring that the speaker here employs the perception-verb in sense B, Ayer leaps to the even stronger conclusion that the man means to describe some thing as really being no bigger than a sixpence. It is, Ayer seems to think, perfectly obvious to the man that what he is seeing is no bigger than a sixpence. But at the same time, Ayer seems to suppose, the—plain—man would say that the object of perception that fits the given description, the speck, merely ‘appears to exist’ or, at any rate, that it does ‘not really exist’ in the sky (where there is a huge star). For sense B is framed to accommodate precisely this verdict. To sum up: At the heart of the first step of Ayer’s reasoning lies a leap of thought that relies on the Phenomenal Principle, on the factual reading Ayer sought to overcome: Whenever we perceive anything that appears F to us, we actually perceive some thing (or: there is some object of perception) that really has that sensible quality.

The crucial next step, the introduction of the new sense of perception-verbs, proceeds from the re-combination of features taken from the two supposedly established senses of perception-verbs previously distinguished: Bii (where the object of perception ‘really has the qualities it appears to have’) and Ai (where it ‘really exists’). But it involves more than their mere combination. For the mere adoption of a perception-verb with these two features would have the plain man say that we ‘directly perceive’ something only in cases of veridical perception: Only in these, we would ordinarily say, does there ‘really exist’ some thing that ‘really has the qualities it seems to have’. It is therefore crucial for the argument that the sense-datum theorists also, second, ‘decide . . . to apply the word “see”’ and its cognates in this new sense ‘to delusive as well as to veridical experiences’ (FEK 24). But of course they do not believe that their ‘decision’ creates an object that really is bent, in addition to the stick-in-water that merely looks bent. Clearly, therefore, when they ‘decide to apply’ the word in the new sense in the cases from which, indeed, their reasoning starts out, they already assume that this object, which actually is crooked, ‘really exists’. This is of course the very assumption the plain man, a moment ago, was taken not to share. If, like Ayer, we go along with this ‘decision’ without further argument, we simply presuppose, tacitly, an admittedly controversial ontological assumption. Thus, Ayer relates with approval:

Having adopted it [the new sense of perception-verbs], they [the sense-datum theorists] cannot say, in the case of delusive perception, that what is experienced is a material thing; for [a] either the requisite material thing does not exist, or else it has not got the requisite property. And so they say that [b] it [the thing they can say is experienced or perceived, in the new sense] is a sense-datum. (FEK 24)





—and not one word is added to explain why they should then be able to say that anything is ‘experienced’, in the new sense. In thus moving, without further argument, from (a) on to (b), Ayer simply presupposes that there always exists a thing which ‘has got the requisite property’—an immaterial thing, when no material thing obliges. In other words, he presupposes the first conclusion the argument from illusion is meant to establish, and presupposes it on the factual reading he seeks to overcome: At any rate in the cases of delusive perception, we perceive immaterial objects which are what other things merely appeared to be, for short: ‘sense-data’.

The third and final step of the ‘linguistic evaluation’ is to provide reasons to adopt the new sense of perception-verbs that the sense-datum theorists have introduced with their ‘decision’:

[1] [S]ince in philosophising about perception our main object is to analyse the relationship of our sense-experiences to the propositions we put forward concerning material things, it is useful for us to have a terminology that enables us to refer to the contents of our experiences independently of the material things they are taken to present. And this the sense-datum language provides. [2] It also has the advantage of laying down an unambiguous convention for the use of words that stand for modes of perception, and so freeing us from the verbal problems that develop . . . out of the ambiguous use of such words in ordinary speech. (FEK 26)





Strikingly, these reasons—the only ones Ayer adduces—are defeated by his previous points: What sort of terminology does (1) request? Presumably, Ayer seeks a terminology which enables us to describe our experiences without implying any statement about material things. But such an ‘idiom’ we apparently have all along: the supposedly well-established sense B of “to see” and its cognates which Ayer explained just three pages before (FEK 23). Alternatively, he might want an idiom that enables us to describe our experiences without using any words by means of which we commonly take ourselves to refer to material things. But only one page up the sense- datum theorist had ‘recommended’ that ‘we should speak . . . of seeing a sense-datum which really has the qualities that are characteristic of the appearance of an oasis’ (FEK 25). And the noun “oasis” is commonly used to refer to material things (in Ayer’s loose sense of the latter term—and of “to refer”). So the new terminology is either superfluous or fails to meet the stated need.

The second reason seems no better. Just two pages up, Ayer noted that the ‘ambiguity’ he now wants to avoid ‘is not ordinarily a source of confusion to us, because we are able to tell from the context what is the sense in which such words are intended to be understood’ (FEK 24). The only people he points out as having been ‘misled’ by these ambiguities are ‘philosophers’ who have made ‘use of the argument from illusion’ (FEK 24), namely one or more of the ‘misuses’ (FEK 28) he goes on to discuss at length (FEK 28–57). But the proponents of this argument have willy-nilly introduced the new terminology. This terminology does not seem much of a help to the few people who, according to Ayer, were ‘misled’ by the different uses of perception verbs ‘in ordinary speech’. And, arguably, these few were misled into their ‘verbal problems’ rather by the new than by any well-established uses of perception verbs. What is more, Ayer had argued a mere three pages up that either of the two ‘established’ senses he distinguished is individually sufficient to describe all empirical facts (FEK 23). If the ambiguity is to be avoided, after all, we can restrict ourselves to one of these—to sense B, if we also want to steer clear of all implications about matters material. On Ayer’s own premises, the new sense is clearly not called for.

Ayer’s explicit reasons are, in other words, remarkably poor in the light of ‘important points’ he takes care to stress practically in the same breath. When sincere, people typically adduce reasons with this defect only when they took a decision for reasons they cannot identify or admit to themselves, and therefore start to confabulate when pressed for reasons.3 In the previous step, Ayer had presupposed that, at least sometimes, we actually perceive immaterial objects which really have the properties other things merely appear to have. I submit he now endorses the introduction of the terminology of ‘sense-data’ and ‘direct perception’ quite simply because it provides a handy way of referring to or picking out those immaterial objects and formulating true statements about them. Since this presupposes the truth of a factual claim Ayer explicitly rejects, he could not admit this reason to others or himself, and was reduced to confabulating.

To recap: Ayer’s famous ‘linguistic evaluation’ of the argument from illusion is to show that its crucial assumption and conclusion, the Phenomenal Principle and the sense-datum doctrine, are defensible merely as rules of language which introduce new terms or tell us to use a new terminology. But already in setting out the established usage, in introducing the new terminology and in arguing for its adoption Ayer presupposes the principle and the core part of the doctrine in all their traditional ‘factual’ splendour. Interpretation in line with the (factual) sense-datum doctrine renders the—otherwise patently wrong—Phenomenal Principle intuitively compelling (cp. Chapter 5.2). Arguably, therefore, Ayer cannot help tacitly presupposing the factual sense-datum doctrine throughout the three main steps of the ‘linguistic evaluation’ that leads him to explicitly reject this paradoxical claim.

Ayer’s ‘linguistic’ re-explication of the sense-datum doctrine and the Phenomenal Principle thus suffers the same fate as Locke’s and Berkeley’s sublimating explanations of how talk of ‘ideas existing in the mind’ is to be properly understood (Chapter 2.1): All these explanations are immediately ‘overridden’; that is, in their reasoning thinkers presuppose the claims that attract them, on the initial intuitive understanding that the explicit explanations repudiate, and cannot help doing so even in the very passages in which they develop or give those explanations.—Recall how Locke explains that “idea” simply stands for whatever one may think of (people, objects, events, problems, etc.) and then treats ideas as objects located in a private space of inner perception, in the very next sentence (EHU I.i.8, see Chapter 2.1).—Where a proposition is systematically presupposed in a thinker’s stated reasoning, he maintains an implicit belief in it (Section 2). Where a thinker seems to explicitly state this belief, then explains that these statements are to be understood differently, but cannot help overriding these explanations immediately and in the very passages in which he gives them, such an implicit belief manifestly biases the thinker’s reasoning with, and interpretation of statements in the same way in which we found firmly maintained explicit beliefs to bias reasoning and the interpretation of ordinary and philosophical statements in exemplary cases of belief bias (Chapters 4.5–4.6, 5.1–5.2).

Where all the arguments adduced to support a belief are marred by strong belief bias and rely from the start on the claim they are to establish, the adoption of this belief must be due to something other than the stated arguments which already take it for granted. All the familiar arguments for the sense-datum doctrine (Chapters 5.1–5.2, 6.1) and Ayer’s new ‘linguistic’ argument (which does not even purport to support this factual doctrine anyway) rely on it. At least two processes of non-intentional reasoning which are characteristic of philosophical reflection can account for the adoption of this doctrine independently of all stated argument. We have already examined pertinent picture-driven reasoning (Chapter 3.5). Later we will encounter an independent process which involves seductive verbal fallacies of a kind we are particularly prone to make in philosophical reflection and then are prone to make systematically (Chapter 8). This process can have us adopt the Phenomenal Principle (Chapter 8.2) and the sense-datum doctrine (Chapter 8.3), on their traditional factual readings and independently of all explicit argument.

Presumably, Ayer came to adopt these two propositions through one or more processes of non-intentional reasoning, and then, in a display of strong belief bias, presupposed these propositions in his interpretation of ordinary talk and further reasoning—even once he had explicitly rejected them. Arguably, non-intentional reasoning and strong belief bias are to be found not only in early modern thought but also in sophisticated contributions to 20th-century analytic philosophy.





4. HALLMARKS OF DELUSION:

AYER’S IMPLICIT BELIEF RECONSIDERED




These processes drive Ayer to adopt and maintain an implicit belief in the factual sense-datum doctrine. This belief is in two ways independent of all reasons stated in its support: Ayer adopts it independently of all stated arguments, which are either rejected by him or are meant to support a different claim, while all of them rely on the doctrine. He then continues to maintain his implicit belief, namely to presuppose this doctrine, even once he has rejected all arguments adduced for it and replaced them by a line of argument that does not even purport to support the paradoxical factual doctrine. In a nutshell, non-intentional reasoning and belief bias drive Ayer to maintain an implicit belief that is in two ways independent of all reasons stated in its support: It precedes their consideration and survives their rejection.

This implicit belief is absurd. This term, you recall (from Chapter 5.5), marks a great discrepancy between a proponent’s obligation, and his ability to provide evidence or argument in support of a claim or belief, a discrepancy that is so great that we dismiss his claim as too unreasonable—for us—to merit even brief examination or discussion. The unmet obligation may arise from an apparent conflict with bits of generally received wisdom or facts at least the proponent is familiar with and accepts. One case which is philosophically particularly pertinent is captured by this sufficient condition:

It is absurd for a subject to believe that p if p is inconsistent with common-sense convictions and he has no specific and at least prima facie sound reasons to believe that p.





Ayer’s implicit belief in the factual sense-datum doctrine is in the same epistemic boat as the explicit belief that our previous protagonist, Berkeley, maintained in it. Both beliefs satisfy the present condition: The doctrine at issue is paradoxical, and while Berkeley lacked pertinent reasons because all his arguments failed to be at least prima facie sound, Ayer endorses no reasons that purport to support the paradoxical factual doctrine.

But even so he continues to maintain, and act on, an implicit belief in this very doctrine, understood as a factual claim that is at odds with common sense (Section 2). Indeed, he even relies on this claim in the course of the very ‘evaluation’ that is meant to show it unwarranted (Section 3). From the very beginning to the very end of his endeavours, Ayer is completely in the grip of this absurd belief, which he is unable to shed; even once he has forcefully criticized his sole argument for it as supporting no such factual claim, he cannot help maintaining an implicit belief in its truth.

Persistent absurd beliefs—if on different topics—are quite familiar to practitioners of another subject. Here is a case related by Sigmund Freud:

I made the acquaintance of a well-preserved lady of fifty-three, friendly and simple in her nature, who told me the following story without any reluctance. She lived in the country, most happily married, with her husband, who was at the head of a large factory. She could not give enough praise to her husband’s affectionate solicitude. It had been a love-match thirty years ago, and since then there had never been any trouble, discord or cause for jealousy . . . A year before, she had received an anonymous letter accusing her excellent husband of a love affair with a young girl; and the incredible—and to herself unintelligible—result was that she immediately believed it, and since then her happiness has been destroyed. The course of events, in greater detail, was something like this. She had a housemaid with whom she used, perhaps too often, to have intimate talks. This girl pursued another one with a positively malicious hostility because she had done so much better for herself in life, though she was of no higher origin. Instead of going into service, this other girl had managed to get a commercial training, had entered the factory and . . . was promoted to a good position. The girl who had made less of a success in life was of course ready to repeat all kinds of bad things of her former schoolmate. One day our lady had a conversation with the housemaid about a gentleman who had been staying with them, who was well known not to be living with his wife but to be having an affair with another woman. She did not know how it happened, but she suddenly said: ‘The most dreadful thing that could happen to me would be if I were to learn that my dear husband was having an affair too.’ The next day she received an anonymous letter by post which, as though by magic, gave her this very information, written in a disguised hand. She decided, probably rightly, that the letter was the work of the malicious housemaid, since it specified as her husband’s mistress the girl whom the servant pursued with her hatred. But although she at once saw through the intrigue and had seen enough instances where she lived of how little credence such cowardly denunciations deserved, what happened was that the letter immediately prostrated her. She became terribly excited, sent for her husband at once and reproached him violently. Her husband laughed the accusation off and did the best possible thing. He brought the family doctor . . . who made efforts to soothe the unfortunate lady. The further conduct of both of them was also entirely sensible. The housemaid was dismissed, but the alleged rival was not. Since then the patient had repeatedly been pacified to the point of no longer believing the content of the anonymous letter, but never thoroughly and never for long. It was enough for her to hear the young lady’s name mentioned or to meet her in the street and a fresh attack of distrust, pain, and reproaches would burst out in her. This, then, is the case history of this excellent woman.4





Freud points out the following as the ‘essential characteristics’ of the case:

It does, indeed, happen that elderly gentlemen have love affairs with young girls. But there is something else about it which is absurd and hard to understand. [1] The patient had no other reason at all for believing that her affectionate and loyal husband belonged to this otherwise not so rare class of husbands except what was asserted in the anonymous letter. [2] She knew that this document had no evidential value and she was able to give a satisfying explanation of its origin. She ought therefore to have been able to tell herself that she had no ground whatever for her jealousy, [3] and she did tell herself so. [4] But in spite of this she suffered as much as if she regarded this jealousy as completely justified. Ideas of this kind, which are inaccessible to logical arguments based on reality, are by general agreement described as delusions. The good lady, then, was suffering from delusions of jealousy.5



Ayer’s case shares these four features. At the risk of belabouring the obvious:





Ayer has got no other reason than the argument from illusion in its various versions to believe that we perceive only immaterial sense-data.

Ayer knows that the argument does not warrant this conclusion, and he knows this because he can give a satisfying analysis of the argument which reveals that it establishes no such surprising matter of fact—just as Freud’s patient knows that her only evidence, the letter, does not warrant the conclusion that her affectionate husband is cheating on her, because she can give a satisfying account of its provenance which reveals that it warrants no such conclusion. So, just as the lady should be able to tell herself that she has got no reason for her jealous suspicion, Ayer should be able to tell himself that there is no reason to believe that we perceive only immaterial sense-data.

Actually, Ayer does tell himself (and his readers) quite explicitly, in evaluating the argument as establishing no unfamiliar matter of fact but merely introducing a new terminology.

But in spite of this he continues to implicitly believe that all we actually perceive are immaterial sense-data: He goes on to presuppose this claim in the most consequential reasoning, that is, he continues to reason as if he regarded this claim as completely justified—just as the lady continues to suffer as if she regarded her jealous suspicion as completely justified.





Later research into delusional thinking revealed that such suffering is the result of what we called implicit belief: Regardless of whether or not they explicitly accept their delusional belief as justified, subjects cannot help presupposing it in the interpretation of life situations and utterances, and responding to them accordingly, both emotionally and behaviourally.6 Arguably, the lady’s persistent belief is implicit like Ayer’s (as she is seized by pangs of jealousy and bursts out in reproaches against her husband whenever she meets her alleged rival in the street and takes herself to confront her husband’s lover—even though she herself rejected her belief in the allegation as ‘unintelligible’).

In view of features (1) and (2) and their conflict with facts or commonsense beliefs their proponents accept, the pertinent beliefs of the Austrian lady and the British philosopher, respectively, are absurd in the sense defined (in Chapter 5.5). But even once the insufficiency of their reasons has been pointed out, namely—(3)—by themselves, they—(4)—continue to implicitly maintain their beliefs. Both Ayer and the lady have an absurd belief that is strongly persistent.

A subject’s belief is strongly persistent iff the subject maintains it, or would maintain it, even in the face of criticism which shows that, even though the belief is in need of strong argumentative or evidential support, the subject has no sound reasons for it.





The degree of conviction with which a belief is thus maintained may change significantly over time, and may change swiftly. An implicit belief maintained in this manner may make itself felt only intermittently, as it gets presupposed in a subject’s reasoning, and thus affects his feelings and actions, only in specific situations. (Freud’s ‘patient had repeatedly been pacified to the point of no longer believing the content of the anonymous letter, but never thoroughly and never for long.’) We have thus encountered the two hallmarks of delusional beliefs, which definitions of “delusion” advert to directly or indirectly (see below): They are absurd and strongly persistent, in the senses explained.

A more fundamental property of delusions, which is hardly ever pinned down succinctly, emerges from a further similarity between Ayer and the Austrian lady. Freud asks his readers to note

that the patient herself positively provoked the anonymous letter, which now gave support to her delusion, by informing the scheming housemaid on the previous day that it would cause her the greatest unhappiness if her husband had a love affair with a young girl. In this way she first put the notion of sending the anonymous letter into the housemaid’s head. Thus the delusion acquires a certain independence of the letter; it had been present already in the patient as a fear—or . . . wish.7





If this conjecture is correct, the lady’s belief is in two ways independent from the pertinent evidence: Another propositional attitude with the same content precedes its consideration, and the belief survives its rejection, namely the insight that the evidence does not warrant the belief—point (3) above. We have found that Ayer’s belief in the factual sense-datum doctrine is two-way independent from argument, in an even more straightforward manner: The belief itself precedes all pertinent arguments (Section 3) and survives their rejection (Section 2)—or, more precisely, the insight that, properly re-developed, these arguments clearly fail to warrant the factual doctrine.

This forcefully brings out what we generally infer from the strong persistence of beliefs which are absurd: that they are not under the subjects’ rational control. Of course we cannot adopt or shed beliefs at will; we have only a limited kind of control over them. But we ought to actually believe that p (and not merely entertain, e.g., the fleeting thought or mild suspicion that p) only if we have some evidence or argument and are justified to believe that p: Ideally, we ought to adopt a belief when, and only when, we have warrant for doing so, and ought to shed a belief the moment we lack warrant for maintaining it. Our conception of an autonomous rational subject does not demand that we actually live up to this ideal all the time. But it requires that we be able to live up to it in each specific case, i.e., that we have ‘rational control’ over our beliefs, at least in this modest sense:





A subject has rational control over the belief that p iff

he is able to adopt the assumption that p and employ it in his reasoning, when he is justified in believing that p, and

he is able to reject the assumption that p and disregard it in his reasoning, when he lacks all warrant for believing that p.





Both Ayer and the Austrian lady continue to maintain their pertinent belief implicitly, even once they have realised—and said explicitly—that there is no justification whatsoever to adopt and maintain it. Clearly, they are unable to shed a belief they are not justified to maintain. To the extent to which the pertinent beliefs already precede the generation or construction of the—patently unsound—evidence or arguments subsequently adduced for them, our protagonists adopt these unreasonable beliefs already prior to having even the semblance of a justification for them, and manifest also an inability to resist these beliefs when having no warrant to adopt them. In two respects, they lack rational control over the particular beliefs at issue. We take people to be unable to shed an absurd belief when they continue to maintain such a belief even though they have told themselves that the pertinent reasons are specious and their belief unreasonable. We also attribute that inability to them when they stick to their beliefs even though others have pointed this out to them: Where I have no even prima facie sound reasons for a belief in need of strong evidential or argumentative support, I plainly ought to give it up the moment my attention is drawn to the need for such support—e.g. to the conflict of my belief with common sense or facts I accept—and to the flimsiness of my reasons. If I do not then abandon my belief, you will judge me unable to shed it. We thus infer lack of rational control over a particular belief from the facts that it is absurd and that the subject maintains it even once he has been shown to have no even prima facie sound reasons for this belief.

To sum up: We have encountered two hallmarks of delusion, viz. absurdity and strong persistence, and a fundamental property we may infer from these two hallmarks, viz. lack of rational control. And we have found that Ayer’s implicit belief in the factual sense-datum doctrine shares all three with a textbook case of delusion.





5. THE CONCEPT OF DELUSION




Contrary to common prejudice, people with delusional beliefs may be capable of reasoned argument and sensible behaviour. While some people who have delusions also suffer from deficits in cognitive skills, delusions are typically not associated with such deficits even in those exemplary cases that attract the attention of the mental health services: Experimental work on psychotic patients established a ‘paradoxical lack of association’8 between delusions and gross cognitive deficits.9 Less fundamental cognitive skills deficits10 were found only in association with some specific conditions involving delusions, such as the apparent inability to understand negative events as the result of situational features rather than personal actions, which was established for paranoid patients.11 The only deficit in cognitive skills that has been experimentally documented for a wide range of medical conditions involving delusions (viz. psychotic conditions) is a deficit in ‘theory of mind skills’, that is, in the ability to understand others and infer their beliefs, desires and attitudes in everyday situations;12 and this deficit was found to be limited to periods of acute illness and unrelated to IQ.13 For our purposes we may keep in mind that, even in the exemplary cases studied by medical researchers, delusions typically are not associated with deficits in cognitive skills which would be relevant in philosophical reflection, and that they are not necessarily associated with any cognitive skills deficit at all.

The fact that Ayer continues to display a high degree of intellectual competence in addressing the problems raised by the factual sense-datum doctrine therefore does not in any way exclude the hypothesis suggested by the important similarities between his belief in this doctrine and the jealous delusion of Freud’s patient: the hypothesis that Ayer’s belief might be a delusion. To explore this at first sight outlandish hypothesis, we need to tackle a major challenge in the philosophy of mental health: the problem of getting clear on meaning and import of this notoriously ill-understood term.14

For a start, we need to dispel common misconceptions, some of which are conveniently summed up by the rightly much-criticised ‘official definition’ in the most recent edition of the American Psychiatric Association’s Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders (DSM):

delusion: A false belief based on incorrect inference about external reality that is firmly sustained despite what almost everyone else believes and despite what constitutes incontrovertible and obvious proof or evidence to the contrary.15





Almost every single element of this ‘definition’ is incompatible with the actual application of the term in diagnostic practice. Already a literature review from 1940 commented on how frequently it has been noticed that delusions may be true ‘accidentally’.16 If the Austrian lady’s devoted husband had, against all probability, been seduced by his wife’s alleged rival, succumbed under unusual circumstances known neither to his wife nor her scheming maid, and covered everything up perfectly, his wife would still have had only utterly unsound reasons for her jealousy and her belief would still have been unreasonable—and delusional.17 The term “delusion” implies an epistemic, not an alethic defect—utter lack of warrant, not lack of truth. Second, many delusions can indeed be understood as conclusions of ‘incorrect inferences’ proceeding, e.g., from everyday observations (‘That CIA spy keeps track of me’, referring to a man seen waiting each morning at the same bus stop); but the pertinent premises include observations of one’s own thoughts and feelings (as in delusions of thought insertion or withdrawal).18 Third, delusions need not be ‘firmly sustained’: Conviction in them may vary significantly from day to day19—like our patient’s belief in the denunciation, which abated intermittently. The point is, rather, that when they are being maintained, they are unresponsive even to sound criticism. Fourth, there often is no conflict between the delusional belief and the beliefs of ‘almost everyone else’, quite simply because most people have no opinion either way about whether, say, Mr. Smith is betraying his wife. And, fifth, psychiatrists and clinical psychologists are hardly ever in possession of ‘incontrovertible and obvious proof or evidence to the contrary’ (say, incontrovertibly establishing Mr. Smith’s loyalty or the non-existence of extra-terrestrials) when crediting someone with a delusion: Rather more frequently, they elicit what ‘proof or evidence’ the patient can provide to support his belief and regard it as delusional in case these reasons do not stand up to even cursory scrutiny.

If we prune the ‘official definition’ of these misconceptions, we are left with the two hallmarks we have already encountered: The first clause is false, and deleted. Upon correction, the second clause merely tells us that delusional beliefs are formed by inferences from truth-apt premises, and can therefore be assessed for truth, for soundness of the inferences leading to them and, therefore, for warrant.20 The remaining clauses imply a certain outcome of such assessment. Properly corrected, they imply absurdity and strong persistence: Where—(4)—other people do have an opinion on the matter at issue and ‘almost everyone else’ rejects the belief, this creates a strong need for argumentative or evidential support, which may also arise from patent tension with less widely known facts familiar to the subject. Where—(5)—the subject is unable to provide evidence or arguments that stand up at least to cursory scrutiny (all pertinent inferences are patently unsound), the belief is absurd. If—(3)—even once this point has been made, the subject maintains the belief continuously or intermittently, explicitly or implicitly, his belief is strongly persistent, in the sense explained (in the previous section).

The DSM attempts to give ‘operational definitions’ of the terms employed in psychiatric diagnosis. Such definitions are to specify operations which allow us to verify pertinent attributions (where both “operations” and “verify” are used in everyday, not behaviourist senses). The DSM defini-tion of “delusion” quoted above, for example, suggests the psychiatrist confront a subject with ‘incontrovertible and obvious proof or evidence’ against his belief and determine whether the subject continues to maintain the belief despite this. Both ‘absurdity’ and ‘strong persistence’ are amenable to operational definition of this kind: By questioning the subject and ourselves, we can find out whether a belief he holds is at odds with facts he accepts or with a relevant body of ‘received wisdom’, and, if so, whether he is able to support the belief with specific and at least prima facie sound reasons. Once we have thus established that a belief is absurd, we can refute those flimsy reasons in discussion with the subject and verify through questioning and reasoning tasks whether he continues to explicitly maintain or implicitly presuppose the belief, in further reasoning.

‘Delusionality’ has come to be regarded as a gradable property:21 Beliefs are more or less delusional according to the extent to which they display various gradable characteristics.22 An operational definition of the notion should therefore invoke but gradable properties or characteristics. Absurdity (discrepancy between the strength of the argumentative or evidential support required and provided, respectively) and strong persistence both are such properties (where strong persistence can be graded in several dimensions: extent and stability of explicit maintenance and level of confidence, extent and stability of implicit presupposition, extent and strength of emotional and behavioural impact). We thus obtain a straightforward definition of “delusionality”, which is ‘operational’ in the sense the DSM definitions aspire to:

A belief is delusional to the extent to which it is absurd and strongly persistent.



Many operational definitions are to capture and ‘operationalise’ notions previously introduced by other explanations, and are adequate just in case they manage to do so. Definitions of ‘delusion’ are no exception. The term entered the technical vocabulary of psychiatry not from ordinary English but as a translation from German, the language of most of the founding fathers of that subject: It translates the term “Wahnidee”. Devoid of the gentler connotations of deception, this term unequivocally implies mental illness and stands for pathological ideas and beliefs. A ‘delusion’ is, quite simply a ‘pathological belief’.23 To get clear on the adequacy of the above definition, we need to spell out the latter notion.

In the most relevant sense of the word, “pathological” means ‘caused by or evidencing a mentally disturbed condition’ or, to be more accurate, ‘caused by a mental disease or evidencing mental illness’.24 In the fourth book of his Republic,25 Plato mooted a conception of mental health that still shapes our intuitive understanding of what it is to be ‘mentally ill’: Plato envisaged a state of mental balance in which the ‘rational part of the soul’ controls the ‘spirited’ and ‘appetitive’ parts, and suggested that this state is constitutive of a kind of ‘health’: the ‘well-being of the soul’. He thus mooted the ideal of a balanced, rational agent who can master his feelings and his impulses to act sufficiently well to be truly a ‘master of himself’ and autonomous in the sense, roughly, of not being a slave to unreasonable passions or impulses. On an intuitive understanding, a person is ‘mentally healthy’ to the extent to which he enjoys this kind of autonomy and ‘mentally ill’ to the extent to which he lacks it. I.e.:

To be mentally ill is to lack rational control over—some of—one’s feelings and actions.





Any action or emotion over which we lack rational control will ‘evidence mental illness’ and hence qualify as ‘pathological’; with the present defi- nition, we can immediately infer the pathology of an emotion or action from the subject’s lack of rational control over it. We shall presently see that within the currently dominant paradigm of psychotherapy only emotions and actions over which a subject lacks rational control are treated as ‘pathological’. We thus obtain a straightforward bi-conditional:

A subject’s feeling or action is pathological iff the subject lacks rational control over it.



Control is something we may but need not exercise when we have it. A subject has rational control over a feeling like the fear of failing an exam just in case he possesses a certain ability to resist or espouse it, which he may but need not exercise: just in case he is able to resist the feeling when it is clearly unwarranted, to shed it when it clearly is no longer warranted and—conversely—to espouse it when it is amply justified. Lack of such control may take different forms: the various exaggerated fears of failure and rejection frequently involved in depression, the aerophobic’s inability to resist the fear of flying that unfailingly seizes him the moment he sets out to travel (no matter with how reputable an airline), or—conversely— the inability to feel fear in objectively dangerous situations that may be displayed by psychopaths.

A subject has rational control over an action just in case he is able to resist the impulse to perform it when it is utterly unreasonable, to break it off the moment it becomes clearly unreasonable, and to perform it when it is clearly called for (and the requisite physical movements are possible). A person lacks such control, for example, when suffering from an obsessive-compulsive disorder or from depression: when he is overwhelmed by the urge to wash his hands all the time even when he is at work (and has a job—e.g. as a construction worker—where he can ill afford to do so) or when the lethargy characteristic of depression prevents him from launching the application for a much-needed and suitable job. Another kind of example is provided by the paranoid man who, when hearing a car horn in front of his house, leaps to the wild conclusion that killers have come for him and runs away.

More often than not, lack of rational control over feelings and actions involves no impairment of cognitive skills: The phobic usually has no trouble realising that his fear of flying is unreasonable, the depressed patient is typically able to appreciate the need to perform an action which he cannot get himself to do even so, and Freud’s deluded patient managed to determine— and explicitly tell herself—that her jealousy and reproaches were unfounded. In a further range of different cases (which include dementia), cognitive skills are impaired, and these cognitive deficits, in turn, impair rational control over a wide range of feelings and actions. (Dementia patients, e.g., tend to ‘act confused’ whenever confronted with unfamiliar surroundings or people.)

The understanding of ‘mental illness’ or ‘pathology’ we have thus obtained is implicit in a clinical criterion commonly used to assess whether a person is in need of psychotherapy: Clinicians seek to ‘ease severe emotional distress (depression, anxiety . . . ) and disturbed behaviour (self-harm, avoidance)’,26 that is, to solve what they call ‘emotional and behavioural problems’. A person is regarded as mentally ill if, and only if, he has such problems: ‘Mental illness [is] the psychological state of someone who has emotional or behavioural problems serious enough to require psychiatric intervention’.27 Emotions, actions, and omissions are treated as ‘pathological’ just in case they are constitutive of such problems.

In first approximation, a behavioural problem ‘may be either doing something that the individual strongly wants not to do . . . or not doing something that the person strongly wants to do.’28 This is to cover cases such as that of the paranoid man who hears the noise of a car horn, immediately leaps to the conclusion that ‘they have come for me, to kill me’ and runs from his flat.29 But of course the man does want to run away, given that he actually (if unreasonably) believes he is about to be attacked. The lack of autonomy from which he suffers does not consist in an inability to desist from doing something he does not want to do. Rather, it consists in his inability to resist a behavioural impulse—viz. to run away—which is utterly unreasonable, since it is motivated and justified solely by an utterly unwarranted thought (‘They have come for me, to kill me’) to which the subject unreasonably lends credence. His lack of autonomy consists in lack of rational control over this action (and others, motivated by similar thoughts).

The notion of emotional problems, too, is often used differently than it is explained: Psychotherapists frequently say they draw the distinction between pathological emotional disturbance requiring therapy and non-pathological distress in terms of severity.30 But non-pathological grief, for example, may be even more severely distressing than the emotions involved in depression, which are deemed pathological. Accordingly, clinicians actually consider whether the emotion at issue is excessive, that is, considerably stronger than warranted by events: ‘if an individual is not excessively upset by [e.g.] losing a job, then it is not in this sense a psychological problem.’31 And of course this psychological problem only amounts to a significant emotional problem in need of therapeutic treatment in case the subject is unable to shed or weaken this unwarranted or unwarrantedly strong emotion himself. The emotional problems psychotherapists seek to solve are constituted by emotions which are not under the rational control of the subject.

To sum up, we have defined ‘mental illness’ and explicated the ‘pathology’ of actions and emotions in terms of lack of rational control. These explanations capture the intuitive understanding of these notions that actually guides clinical practice. They also avoid the various problems besetting the major competing proposal, viz. the explication of mental pathology in terms of harmful dysfunction that has been a focus of debate.32 The explanation of ‘pathology’ we have obtained thus seems satisfactory. It can be straightforwardly extended from feelings like fear to other propositional attitudes. This yields some straightforward definitions:

A subject’s belief or other propositional attitude is pathological to the extent to which the subject lacks rational control over it.



A delusion is a pathological belief. A subject’s belief is delusional to the extent to which it is pathological. Hence:



A subject’s belief is delusional to the extent to which the subject lacks rational control over it.





Above (in Section 4), we have defined ‘rational control over beliefs’ and have seen that the absurdity and strong persistence of a belief jointly entail that the subject lacks such control over it. The operational definition of delusions as strongly persistent absurd beliefs can hence be used as a heuristic for identifying delusions in the initial sense of ‘pathological beliefs’.





6. AN INTELLECTUAL ALTITUDE SICKNESS




We found that Ayer’s implicit belief in the ‘factual’ sense-datum doctrine is absurd and strongly persistent; we provided significant evidence which warrants the conclusion that this belief of his is in two ways independent of his stated reasons and not under Ayer’s rational control (Sections 2–4). Ayer’s continued implicit belief in the sense-datum doctrine—the ‘factual’ original rather than the ‘linguistic’ version obtained by re-evaluation—provides us with the first example of a philosophical delusion.

The particular way in which Ayer comes to develop and maintain his present delusional belief is quite idiosyncratic. But the formation of a philosophical delusion as such is no freak occurrence; it is not, as it were, the intellectual equivalent to the birth of Siamese twins. Rather, the very process we have studied in this book, the pictorial process, systematically leads to the formation and implicit maintenance of delusional beliefs. The pictorial process has thinkers leap to picture-driven conclusions at odds with familiar facts, sublimate them and maintain them explicitly or implicitly in further reasoning, which presupposes them, in their ‘raw’ or sublimated shape, from the start and throughout (belief bias). Above (in Chapter 5.5), we have seen that picture-driven inferences and their sublimation systematically lead thinkers to form and maintain absurd beliefs. Whenever such a belief is also strongly persistent, the thinker lacks rational control over it and maintains it as a matter of delusion.

This will happen when strong belief bias has us maintain beliefs picture-driven reasoning had us adopt. We can infer lack of rational control over the pertinent beliefs in at least two cases. First, a thinker clearly is unable to disregard the absurd assumption that p in his further reasoning, when he unwittingly continues to presuppose that p even once he has explicitly rejected the absurd claim. Frequently, thinkers explicitly reject the absurd conclusions of picture-driven inferences, namely through the act of sublimation: They reject these conclusions out of hand when spontaneously explaining that their words are to be understood in some other sense. But, even so, thinkers go on to unwittingly presuppose those picture-driven claims precisely in the ‘raw’ initial shape they reject: The thinkers we examined continue to presuppose that to think something is to perceive something somewhere in us, through an inner sense, that the things perceived in the mind are located in the mind, that a quality is a thing that must exist somewhere in other objects, etc. (Chapters 2.1–2.2 and 3.2). The picture-driven claims that thus creep back into the thoughts of philosophers who explicitly reject them constitute implicit beliefs which are not under the rational control of the thinkers who have them. Precisely some of the most fundamental philosophical beliefs we have considered are maintained as a matter of delusion.

Second, we can infer more restricted lack of rational control when a belief is explicitly endorsed but keeps creeping in also in reasoning in which the thinker clearly does not want to rely on it, as in the arguments he adduces to support that very belief. Thus, when we find that all of Berkeley’s arguments for the sense-datum doctrine, including those he regarded as decisive, rely on that paradoxical doctrine directly or indirectly, we can infer not only that his belief in this doctrine is absurd but also that it is not entirely under his rational control and to that extent delusional. Like Ayer’s implicit belief in the sense-datum doctrine, also Berkeley’s explicit belief in this doctrine is constitutive of a delusion.
