We have seen that his belief is ultimately due to picture driven reasoning (Chapters 3.5 and 4.2–4.6) and can now conclude more generally: Wherever picture-driven reasoning culminates in strong belief bias that has us presuppose its paradoxical conclusions in all further reasoning, including the very arguments we adduce in their support, thinkers lack rational control over these conclusions and maintain them as a matter of delusion.

The conflict of such philosophical delusions with familiar facts, common-sense convictions or among each other gives rise to a wide range of philosophical problems. These in turn occasion much toil and some worry. Worries engendered by such ill-motivated problems are constitutive of ‘emotional problems’; pointless efforts to solve such problems and, e.g., to reconcile the pertinent facts with the delusions with which they appear to be at odds, are constitutive of ‘behavioural problems’:

A subject has emotional problems iff he experiences unwarranted and distressing or otherwise disabling emotions.33



An emotion is disabling iff it prevents the subject from doing things he would otherwise want to do or drives him to do things he would otherwise refrain from.



A subject has behavioural problems to the extent to which his behaviour is unreasonable (or unjustifiable) and fails to be autonomous.



A subject’s behaviour fails to be autonomous in case he does things he would not want to do if not in a certain disabling condition that is not under his direct control, and in case he refrains from doing things that he would want to do if not in such a condition.





Apparently Wittgenstein had emotional problems of this kind: He stressed again and again the disquiet or uneasiness ultimately engendered by wrong analogies and the resultant ‘disorder in our concepts’ and (misleading) expressions; indeed, he made it his chief aim to remove this kind of disquiet (Chapter 9.1). While few other philosophers find philosophical problems disquieting enough to qualify as having such emotional problems, many do have behavioural problems: Few of us want to waste precious research time on efforts to show otherwise unproblematic convictions consistent with utterly unwarranted claims with which they appear to be at odds. Where we make such efforts only because the unwarranted claims are rendered compelling by non-intentional inferences, and these are engendered by adherence to philosophical pictures, we thus do something we would not want to do, if not under the spell of philosophical pictures—the presently relevant ‘disabling condition’.

A subject counts as ‘mentally ill’ to the extent to which he lacks rational control over his beliefs or actions (Section 5). To the extent to which he has emotional or behavioural problems in the sense explained, a subject lacks such control. When thinkers are under the spell of philosophical pictures, thereby come to formulate ill-motivated philosophical problems, and find these ‘problems’ worrying or make otherwise pointless efforts to solve them, they hence qualify as ‘ill’. As we shall presently see, the autonomous cognitive processes that make us qualify by engendering such problems, are constitutive of a kind of disease: a ‘disease of the understanding’, to borrow Wittgenstein’s phrase (from CV 50), namely a kind of intellectual altitude sickness that can befall even the most competent thinkers the moment they try to scale the heights of abstract reflection with undue haste, and without due care or caution.

A disease is a condition of the individual that causes him to be ill. More precisely, it is a condition which the individual cannot control directly and which causes him to be ill or will cause him to be ill or to die, if untreated. The intuitive notion of a ‘disease’ is that of an ‘internal’ process causing ill health (or death), as opposed to rather more sudden causes like—external or internal—injuries. When we apply the notion in saying that someone suffers from a disease, we commonly assume that there is some underlying inner process that causes the complaints that make the person qualify as ill (complaints such as pain or physical disability, or serious emotional or behavioural problems); we posit, namely, a process that causes ‘signs’ and ‘symptoms’ which engender or include those complaints. The process in question can be either literally or metaphorically ‘internal’ or ‘inner’; it can be either a physiological or a mental process which the subject cannot directly control.34 Where such a process is known, a specific disease (e.g. what we commonly call ‘the flu’) gets defined in terms of the pertinent signs and symptoms (fever, weakness, etc.) and a particularly decisive condition of the individual that is an antecedent of those symptoms in that process (e.g. the presence of a virus in his body): Where the same symptoms are due to different such conditions, we are dealing with different diseases; in exemplary cases, both signs and symptoms and some causally decisive conditions serve to individuate the disease.

The distinction between ‘symptoms’ and ‘signs’ further structures the concept of ‘disease’. In clinicians’ terms, symptoms are ‘subjective evidence’ of the disease (such as pain or diarrhoea), to be discovered by questioning the patient, whereas signs are ‘objective evidence’ to be obtained independently (such as elevated glucose levels in urine, to be determined through lab tests). In the more technical sense of medical researchers, however, signs and symptoms are effects of an underlying causal process, where signs are more immediate effects than symptoms. In using these terms, researchers make three assumptions:35 The signs can be observed independently from the symptoms. While both signs and symptoms are over-determined in having many causal antecedents, signs have fewer antecedents than symptoms. Third, there are explanatory links between some of the signs and some of the symptoms. Ideally, as research progresses, ever more signs, ever further up an assumed causal-explanatory chain, are found to be correlated with and (partially) explanatory for the symptoms.36 The starting-point of such a research process may be the observation of a syndrome, that is, the observation of a cluster of symptoms which occur together significantly above chance level. Usually, they will be reliably correlated with antecedent signs, which further suggest that the co-occurrence of the symptoms is not by chance but due to one and the same underlying process.37 To the extent to which such a process is discovered, the definition of the syndrome in terms of co-occurring symptoms is developed into a ‘proper’ disease concept which defines the disease in terms both of symptoms and signs and of some causally decisive antecedent.

The currently most widely used classification of mental diseases or disorders, the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders, defines most such disorders as syndromes, though with reference only to symptoms and not to signs. The symptoms invoked include emotions and patterns of behaviour, having or displaying which amounts to having emotional or behavioural problems. Crucially for our present purposes, the symptoms also include beliefs which engender such problems. For example, delusional beliefs are the sole symptom of delusional disorder and qualify as symptoms of a disease because they may engender behavioural problems, for example when the subject acts on them and thereby does things he would have refrained from doing if not suffering from delusional disorder.38 (Upon hearing a car outside his house, a paranoid forms the belief ‘They have come to get me’ and runs to the garden, to hide from imaginary attackers.)39

In following up how philosophical pictures shape philosophical reflection, we have encountered, for each of the terms that enter into a proper disease concept, a phenomenon to which the term applies:





We have already seen that in philosophical reflection even the most competent thinkers may come to systematically adopt unwarranted beliefs (like the belief that only immaterial sense-data can be immediately perceived) or entertain unwarranted ideas (say, that thoughts must exist in an ethereal realm), that these beliefs and ideas apparently conflict with further convictions and thus give rise to ill-motivated problems (e.g. about the possibility of using terms purporting to refer to material object, or about the nature of thoughts and matters mental) and that pointless efforts to solve these ‘problems’ constitute— genuine—behavioural problems and may go with unwarranted disquiet amounting to an emotional problem.

Since they engender behavioural and emotional problems and thus make thinkers ‘ill’ in a strict sense of the term, these utterly unwarranted but intuitively compelling beliefs and ideas are symptoms of disease at least in the same sense as the delusional beliefs that are recognised as symptoms of delusional disorder.

These beliefs and ideas come to be espoused or entertained as the result of cognitive distortions, i.e., of recurrent (rather than one-off), characteristic (not idiosyncratic) and systematic (rather than unrelated) mistakes in non-intentional reasoning. The philosophical beliefs and ideas at issue are formed due to picture-driven leaps of thought (which presuppose the likes of R, K and M, cp. Chapters 1.3 and 3.1) and further leaps which presuppose conclusions thus inferred, directly or indirectly (cp. Chapters 2.1–2.2, 3.2–3.3 and 3.5). These systematic leaps are antecedents of the formation of the pertinent beliefs and ideas (symptoms). Whether a thinker makes leaps of this kind (i.e. leaps presupposing those assumptions) can be established without considering whether he holds one of the beliefs or entertains one of the ideas at issue, for example by testing what spontaneous inferences he makes from various related premises, to conclusions other than the particular belief to be explained. Like this belief, the leaps leading to it have antecedents in the shape of other leaps which lead, often from various different premises, to the tacit assumptions they presuppose; but as we advance towards the initial leaps—which presuppose only the likes of R and M—they rely on fewer and fewer leaps, whose presuppositions are obtained from fewer and fewer premises. Finally, reference to each of these antecedent leaps contributes to an explanation of the philosophical belief or idea at issue. To sum up, these systematic leaps of thought have the crucial characteristics of signs: They are independently ascertainable, have fewer explanatory antecedents than the ‘symptoms’ and are themselves (partially) explanatory antecedents of the latter.

These ‘sign-like’ cognitive distortions are part-and-parcel of a mental process which the subject cannot directly control, namely, of a cognitive process which occurs at the level of non-intentional reasoning and eventually leads to the formation of the ‘symptom-beliefs’: the pictorial process (Chapter 1.3). This autonomous process will drive the thinking at issue iff the thinker is under the spell of the pertinent philosophical pictures. This is the decisive condition of the individual that is an antecedent of those symptoms in that process. Being under the spell of, say, the perceptual picture of reflection is a condition of the kind used to individuate diseases. More generally, cognitive processes that are not under our direct control can be constitutive of diseases, when engendering emotional and behavioural problems.



That is, when, in the grip of philosophical pictures, we seriously worry about, or work on ill-motivated problems, we are ill. Certain philosophical beliefs and ideas which give rise to those problems are symptoms of a kind of disease; certain cognitive distortions are its signs; the pictorial process we have made out is pathological. Where it leads us to waste time and effort on ill-motivated problems, being under the spell of a philosophical picture is a disease, in the ‘proper’ sense explained. In fact, it thus qualifies as a disease in a stricter sense than the mental disorders defined in the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual, which specifies no signs (or equivalent) and suggests no inner process, mental or physical, to explain the symptoms. To be in the grip of a philosophical picture is to have a disease of the understanding, in a perfectly strict sense of the term ‘disease’.

The pertinent inner (or ‘inner’) processes are constitutive of diseases, and qualify as ‘pathological’, not in virtue of an abnormality in or of their course.40 Rather, they qualify in virtue of their effects or consequences: An inner process qualifies if it causes us pain or disability or related harms such as emotional or behavioural problems. Accordingly, this process may represent an actually quite normal response to certain environmental conditions, and some familiar diseases are such responses to extreme conditions to which people are not used. Take altitude sickness: It can befall even the most physically fit people when they are suddenly exposed to higher altitudes, without proper acclimatisation. It then is the more likely to befall us the higher we go (20% chance at 2000–2500 metres, 40–50% at 3000– 3500 metres, etc.).41 Its symptoms (headache, weakness, etc.) are caused by a process contingent on an environmental condition: on the shortage of oxygen at altitudes with ever lower atmospheric pressure. The resultant difficulties in coping with high altitudes reflect not so much individual shortcomings of particular climbers but limitations of our species, which has evolved in different habitats.

According to the analysis presented, being under the spell of philosophical pictures is a roughly analogous disease: a disease of the understanding which human thinkers are prone to develop when trying to scale the heights of abstract reflection, without proper precautions. We have hypothesised that thinkers are particularly prone to engage in non-intentional analogical reasoning which is unsound when they lack knowledge of relevant detail or context and when suitable (i.e. structurally analogous and familiar) models are available (Chapter 1.2). We further hypothesised that these conditions are often met in philosophical reflection, where thinkers frequently engage in general reasoning without sufficiently frequent reference to sufficiently well worked-out examples, use technical terms which are too coarsely defined, and often think about abstract phenomena for which structurally analogous familiar models are provided by the process of metaphorical extension (Chapter 1.2). If these hypotheses are correct, the cognitive process that causes the symptoms of the present disease of the understanding is contingent upon quasi-environmental conditions: upon the paucity of context and detail and upon the availability of suitable models, which are characteristic of much philosophical debate. When engaged in such debate, we then are the more likely to be in the grip of philosophical pictures the more, and the more swiftly, we ignore sufficiently concrete examples and contexts and ascend from the concrete and specific to the abstract and general. Being in the grip of philosophical pictures probably is, as it were, an intellectual altitude sickness.

As such, it reflects not individual cognitive deficits of particular thinkers but limitations of our species which has evolved in response to rather more practical challenges, addressed in rather richer contexts. The mistakes it has us make are natural in the contexts in which we are prone to make them. These natural mistakes then propagate and ramify at a later stage of the pertinent process, due to belief-bias effects which are well documented for the general population and a plethora of contexts: everyday, social, scientific etc.42 Arguably, the philosophical delusions generated at the several stages of the pictorial process reflect, quite simply, the cognitive endowment of human beings. They are a fact of our intellectual life, which we have to reckon with when we address challenges our species has not evolved to meet.





7. CONCLUSION AND OUTLOOK




The term “therapy” implies that there are diseases to be cured or symptoms of illness to be relieved. This implication cannot be cancelled simply by saying that the term is being used in a metaphorical sense. As we have seen (in Chapter 1.1), the metaphorical extension of terms escapes vacuity by preserving core inferential relations between several related terms. To escape vacuity, conceptions of therapy in philosophy hence have to identify a whole set of suitably related phenomena to which a whole set of related terms can be applied, metaphorically or literally: They have to identify a ‘disease’ which causes ‘signs’ or ‘symptoms’ which evidence ‘illness’, i.e., failure to meet a certain ideal of ‘health’. In this chapter, we have spelled out an influential ideal of mental health and spelled out when and in what ways philosophers may fall short of it and qualify as ‘ill’, in virtue of their professional pursuits. We have identified the symptoms that evidence this illness and traced their aetiology, via antecedent signs to the pathological condition constitutive of the pertinent disease: adherence to philosophical pictures. We have thus obtained a first substantive (non-vacuous) conception of therapeutic philosophy, that is, of a therapy which addresses problems arising specifically in and through philosophical reflection.

This substantive conception vindicates the call for something that is literally a therapy, in philosophy: The ideal of health of which we fall short when we agonise over picture-raised problems is the same ideal of rational autonomy of which people fall short when suffering from exemplary mental disorders: When falling short of it, philosophers are ill in a different way than, but in the same sense as people suffering from depression or psychosis. Their key symptoms are beliefs which literally qualify as delusions, and the autonomous cognitive process to which they are due satisfies a strict definition of the term “disease”.

In the next chapter, we will explain the need for therapy in philosophy and how we can meet it. But this much is clear already now: Therapy is required in philosophy in order to cure the disease we have made out, viz. our adherence to philosophical pictures, and to relieve us of its symptoms, namely of the philosophical delusions we form and maintain under the spell of philosophical pictures. Where these delusions are maintained explicitly, we have to reduce thinkers’ conviction in them; where they are maintained implicitly, we have to weaken the propensity unwittingly to presuppose them in further reasoning. To not only relieve the symptoms but cure the disease that causes them then is to weaken the underlying propensities to unwittingly assimilate various targets to particular models more closely than one knows to be appropriate (e.g. assimilate intellectual efforts to visual search in ways in which one knows the two to be different). Only once we have weakened the pertinent propensities are we able to give up the claims we have seen reason to reject, to abandon the ill-motivated but intuitively compelling ‘problems’ they raise, and to put an end to the kind of non-intentional reasoning that would otherwise have us conjure up new but equally bad ‘problems’ to take the place of the ones we managed to see through.





Part III

Therapeutic Philosophy





7 Two Turns


A New Vision of Philosophy




In the first part of this book we have explored how philosophical reflec-tion is shaped at several levels by philosophical pictures. More specifically, we have explored how such reflection is shaped by autonomous cognitive processes and effects that complement each other and make up what we called the ‘pictorial process’: We have seen that processes and effects of which we are hardly ever aware and which are not under our direct control may decisively shape the concepts philosophers use, the intuitions we feel compelled to honour, the swift arguments that take us from trite premises to earth-shaking conclusions (with—remarkably—few compelling steps) and the claims we confidently maintain as well as the problems thus raised and the theories proposed to solve these problems. In the second part of the book (chapters 4–6), we turned from intuitive reasoning to the deliberate and careful construction of philosophical arguments, and found that such arguments too may be shaped—and vitiated—by unsound non-intentional reasoning. We discovered that influential philosophical doctrines which are ‘supported’ by a familiar stock of carefully constructed arguments and which raise central philosophical problems have been maintained as a matter of delusion. Indeed, we found that the autonomous thought processes we have analysed systematically engender such philosophical delusions, whose clash with reality or among each other systematically gives rise to philosophical problems. We have thus established the need for therapy in philosophy, to liberate us from philosophical delusions and the bogus problems they have us imagine.

In this final part of the book, we will get clearer on the relevance of these findings, will try to gauge the extent of the need for such therapeutic philosophy, and will explore extant and fresh methods to put it into practice. In this chapter, we will see that the struggle with philosophical pictures and their off-spring is characteristic of philosophical work (Section 1) and still central to our subject today (Section 2). This warrants a twofold re-orientation of philosophical work: first, the ‘meta-philosophical turn’ which provides us with a fresh approach to philosophical problems belonging to that off-spring (Section 3) and, second, the ‘therapeutic turn’ which has us adopt new guiding aims for philosophical work (Section 4). Ideas borrowed from cognitive approaches in clinical psychology will finally let us see that these two ‘turns’ complement each other: The therapeutic aim is to put an end to the unwarranted toil and worry engendered by philosophical pictures and delusions. We can attain it by acquiring ‘meta-cognitive insight’ into the ways we think when doing philosophy: by coming to understand how philosophical reflection is shaped at several levels by philosophical pictures and, more generally, by autonomous cognitive processes which are unsound (Section 5). The kind of meta-philosophical analysis we have engaged in vindicates the project of therapeutic philosophy and provides the key to its successful practice. We will thus win through to a first therapeutic strategy (Section 6) and to a fresh vision of philosophy: the vision of philosophy as a therapeutic practice guided by the findings of a scientifically informed meta-philosophy, in something like the way in which the practice of cognitive psychotherapy is guided by the findings of clinical psychology.

Finally we will turn to extant paradigms of such therapeutic philosophy, due to J.L. Austin and Ludwig Wittgenstein. Their analysis will not only provide the vision with a pedigree but also lend it further substance: We will see that both thinkers took the two complementary turns we have vindicated. They adopted therapeutic aims and sought to attain them by coming to understand how philosophical doctrines and problems are shaped by autonomous thought processes of which we are largely unaware. Austin seeks to make us aware of ‘concealed motives’ for maintaining the sense-datum doctrine of perception. The analysis of the motives he exposes will lead us to identify a second autonomous cognitive process that is particularly likely to occur in philosophical reflection and may complement the pictorial process we have made out (Chapter 8). Wittgenstein seeks to grow aware of ‘urges to misunderstand’ which lead him to worry about problems that are bad by his own lights, and employs a subtle method of self-therapy that anticipates some guiding ideas of later cognitive therapy (Chapter 9). The analysis of his proceeding will provide us with a model of cognitive self-therapy that can guide us the moment we turn from the works of great dead men to our own philosophical worries, and try to apply the lessons we have learned by studying those works in efforts to dissolve and overcome the philosophical problems that worry us today.





1. DISTINCTIVELY PHILOSOPHICAL PROBLEMS




The caricature of the development of our subject that we employed already in the Introduction can help us bring out the philosophical relevance of our main findings so far. It is not entirely wrong to see philosophy in its present form as the result of a process through which scientific disciplines have emerged from within the community of thinkers called ‘philosophers’. It is also not entirely wrong to regard this community as initially having been made up of all thinkers engaged in intellectual pursuits that are neither immediately directed at any practical, social or political goal nor entirely devoid of intellectual rigour. To the extent to which a range of notions and concepts, methods and techniques for formulating and addressing problems of a particular kind came to be developed and generally accepted by a certain number of such thinkers, communities of specialists emerged, and with many new intellectual communities new disciplines were born (from geometry as philosophy’s arguably first, to psychology and linguistics as its latest off-spring), so that the new methods and techniques left philosophy along with their users.

Accordingly, much (though certainly not all) of the work commonly classified as philosophical is ‘purely theoretical’ and largely ‘speculative’: It is not geared towards any practical goals and does not follow or employ any well-developed body of methods or techniques for formulating and addressing pertinent problems. To the extent to which we lack explicit methods or techniques we can deliberately use to guide our reflection, we will be particularly prone to unwittingly fall back on various kinds of non-intentional reasoning. If we also lack practical goals which would relate our efforts to sufficiently rich contexts, we are particularly prone to engage in non-intentional analogical reasoning with its characteristic mistakes. In much of the work commonly classified as ‘philosophical’, therefore, conditions obtain which favour the intellectual altitude sickness we have made out, viz. adherence to philosophical pictures, and thus facilitate the generation of absurd paradoxical beliefs which we cannot help presupposing in further such reasoning. The formation of such delusions in philosophy is no accident but due to characteristics that can be traced back to distinctive features of the subject.

There is no reason to believe that the process of the emergence of new disciplines has left philosophy now (or at any earlier point in time) with one homogeneous class of problems, all of one and the same kind. Arguably, we may encounter problems of very different kinds when joining philosophical debates. All the same, the conflict of paradoxical, and often delusional, claims with common-sense convictions, or among each other, generates problems of a characteristic kind, which are distinctively philosophical in character. We have analysed in detail how picture-raised paradoxes systematically give rise to philosophical problems (Chapters 2.3, 3.4 and 5.4). The problems thus raised include the core problems of the modern philosophies of mind and perception, respectively, and many problems whose roots in paradox have hitherto escaped notice. These paradoxes and the problems they engender can claim to be seminal and distinctive for philosophy: As we shall now see, they provide a distinctive pre-scientific (and self-reinforcing) motivation for engaging in philosophical reflection at all (and persisting with it), and are commonly not claimed as their business by new disciplines which emerge from philosophy and deal with the topic the problem or paradox is about.

There tends to be an apparent ‘tension’ between the conclusions we leap to in the grip of philosophical pictures and common-sense convictions and perfectly familiar facts: Together with the latter, those conclusions— delusions and consequences inferred from them—appear to entail a contradiction or to have otherwise unacceptable implications. To those in the grip of the pertinent pictures, who do not question the picture-motivated conclusions, it will therefore seem that the common-sense convictions just cannot be true and that the familiar facts simply cannot obtain. Without necessarily being able to spell out the tension, they then wonder how what is plainly the case is as much as possible. In each of the three problem-clusters we considered, the first problems (that engendered others) were of this kind.

In the grip of the part-whole picture of qualities, thinkers inferred from the delusional idea that qualities must exist somewhere in the bodies to which they belong that colours and tastes, smells and warmth do not really inhere in physical bodies (Chapter 3.2). This conclusion appears to fly in the face of the most obvious and familiar facts, confronting thinkers with a conflict they cannot gloss over through sublimation: Just move about without closing your eyes and holding your nose or ears, and you come across a myriad of things that look red or white, are silver or gold, feel warm or are cold, smell sweet or sound loud. So how is this possible? That is (Chapter 3.4): Where are the colours we see and the heat we feel, if they do not really inhere in the bodies that make up our physical environment? And how can we attribute them to such bodies?

Similarly, in the grip of perceptual pictures we form and maintain the— delusional—belief that to think about or remember something or feel a pain is to perceive something somewhere in us, and infer that thoughts, memory images and pains must exist somewhere in us, for us to see or feel or otherwise have them (Chapters 1.3 and 2.1–2.3). So where are, say, the memory images we call up every day? They are not in our physical environment, surely not in our bodies either (competing for space with lumps of grey matter), and we do not countenance the idea of some ethereal realm (and how could we look into that, even if it existed?). So how is it possible for me to see the memory image I now call up—or to feel pains (which do not compete for space with any part of our body, either)?

Some such problems have intuitively compelling solutions which engender, in turn, problems of the same kind. Take the idea—which at any rate Berkeley (arguably along with Galileo, Boyle and Locke) maintains as a matter of delusion (Chapters 4–5)—that the colours we see and the heat we feel, the sounds we hear, the odours we smell and the tastes we enjoy all exist—only—in our minds: They are sensations or perceptions in us; and such ‘inner’ and private entities are all we can ever perceive directly or immediately (chapters 4.1–4.2). But, surely, the table I see and feel when I sit at it and the beef stew I smell and taste as I bite into chunks of meat are perfectly public and physical objects and stuffs. Hence the question (Chapter 5.4): How is it possible for me to see, feel, smell and taste such real things?

In all these cases, a philosophical claim we find intuitively compelling appears to imply that a certain familiar fact cannot obtain: that it cannot be the case that p (that physical things cannot really be coloured in the way we ordinarily judge them to be, that we cannot have the memory images or pains we clearly enjoy or suffer, or that we cannot perceive by sense the things in our physical environment). When we are unable or unwilling to give up either our conviction that p or our philosophical claim, we are disquieted by the apparent inconsistency in our beliefs. We may then think of specific examples: that the coin I now look at is silver, that I can still see grandmother’s Christmas tree ‘before my inner eye’, or that I see and smell the lunch on my plate. The moment we forcefully remind ourselves in this—or any other—way that it plainly is a fact that p, disquiet may give way to a characteristic sense of wonder: On the one hand, we find the fact peculiarly remarkable (and marvel, e.g., at the fact that I now have a memory image ‘before my inner eye’); on the other hand, we are puzzled how what is plainly the case is as much as possible, and ask: ‘How is it (possible) that . . . ?’1

According to Plato, such a sense of wonder in the face of familiar facts is the initial motivation for engaging in philosophical reflection.2 It certainly constitutes a distinctive motivation, different from that of exemplary scientific (or proto-scientific) explanations of familiar facts: Efforts to explain, e.g., why apples fall from trees are not motivated by lines of thought which suggest that apples cannot fall from trees. Rather, we observe that some things (including apples) fall straight to the ground when nothing supports them, whereas others (like pollen) do not, and want to know ‘how it is’ that apples fall (and pollen does not)—though it may take a man of genius not to remain content with the platitudinous answer that apples fall straight because they are heavy (and pollen does not because they are light). Even when they lead us to ask questions using the same form of words, we can hence distinguish scientific curiosity about why things happen the way they do from a characteristic sense of wonder in the face of the mere fact that certain things happen or are the way they are (that I have this memory image, etc.), a sense of wonder fuelled by the impression of a tension between these facts and intuitive insights. This sense of wonder and the disquieting impression that fuels it constitute a distinctively philosophical motivation for asking questions of the ‘How is it that . . . ?’-variety. Since few academic philosophers today share Plato’s sense of wonder, let’s focus on the underlying impression of a tension, which we shall presently see drives crucial philosophical efforts to the present day. Let’s call it the ‘proto-Platonic motivation’.

Due to the phenomena we have examined in this book, this motivation arises systematically and is self-reinforcing: In purely theoretical and largely speculative reflection, human thinkers are prone to unwittingly lapse into non-intentional analogical reasoning. Such reasoning systematically takes us to paradoxical conclusions we are unaware of inferring but find intuitively compelling. They are at odds with familiar facts which thus appear impossible or somehow problematic. This impression and the potentially resulting sense of wonder in the face of what we full well know to be the case constitute a powerful motivation for engaging in more deliberate reflection about familiar facts. This pre-scientific motivation for intellectual endeavours may drive us also when we are otherwise content with platitudinous answers to the question of why familiar things are the way they are or happen the way they do (‘Apples fall because they are heavy’). It has thinkers engage in further reflection which is purely theoretical and largely speculative and thus favours more non-intentional reasoning, generating further paradoxical intuitions. These produce further tensions, with other intuitions, facts and common sense, and ever more specific challenges to the consistency of thinkers’ beliefs. Processes of non-intentional reasoning thus provide us with a distinctive pre-scientific and self-reinforcing motivation for intellectual efforts, stirred by these challenges and aimed at defusing the tensions in our beliefs.

These efforts are more commonly made by philosophers than scientists. To understand why, let’s return to our caricature of philosophy’s development (at the beginning of this section): We can think of the philosophical community as a mixed bunch, comprising thinkers with different motivations, including the scientific and proto-Platonic motivations we considered. At various points in history, thinkers with primarily scientific motivations develop new concepts and methods and apply them to frame and address specific problems of a sort of particular interest to them. To the extent to which they deliberately employ their increasingly refined new methods and to the extent to which they relate theoretical problems to specific cases and ever more contexts of application, they are less than before subject to conditions favouring non-intentional analogical reasoning. Accordingly, these practitioners of an emerging new discipline will be less prone to lapse into such reasoning than the remaining members of the community they are leaving. Also, thinkers with a primarily scientific motivation accord less importance to what intuitions they do have: Those who first and foremost want to find out how things are and why they are so, or what happens and why it does, will at some point simply give up even the most intuitive idea if it is inconsistent with important facts and findings, without feeling the need to make further efforts to ‘explain it away’, let alone to show that it is, after all, compatible with the facts and findings with which it appears to be odds. Briefly: The more a scientific discipline emerging from philosophy goes on to mature in its methods and application, its practitioners will become less prone to picture-motivated intuitions and less willing to address reconciliation- or accommodation-problems raised by what intuitions of this sort they might still have. In this sense, these problems are distinctively philosophical in character.

To sum up, philosophical pictures systematically engender distinctively philosophical problems which provide a distinctive pre-scientific—and self-reinforcing—motivation for intellectual efforts. In this book, we have analysed efforts made in early modern times and in pre-war analytic philosophy. Let’s now have at least a brief look at more recent work from postwar analytic philosophy, to see whether it is reasonable to hypothesise that the struggle with philosophical pictures and their off-spring is an enterprise which continues to the present day.





2. THE ONGOING STRUGGLE WITH PICTURES: FUNCTIONALISM




The peculiar pre-scientific motivation we have identified is not incompatible with but is independent of scientific curiosity: A researcher may be moved by both but can have one motivation without the other. Few philosophers explicitly state the motivation of their work. But we can frequently infer what moves a thinker to address a ‘How is it that . . . ?’-question or related problems, namely from the kind of efforts he makes in response. While few modern academic philosophers share much of Plato’s sense of wonder, we can infer that much philosophical theorising to this day addresses the underlying impression of a tension and is primarily or even exclusively motivated by the desire to restore consistency to the theorists’ beliefs, in the face of specific intuitive challenges. This proto-Platonic motivation usually takes the shape of wanting to integrate the pertinent philosophical intuitions (which thinkers often cleave to tenaciously) into one’s further convictions (which get modified more readily), in view of disquieting tensions apparent between them. Occasionally it leads to efforts to explain away those intuitions. (Scientific theories explain things, philosophical theories explain things away. They explain away either certain intuitions or common-sense convictions or the impression of a tension between them.)

Whether the proto-Platonic eclipses the scientific motivation can be inferred from the extent to which the theory gets developed. Scientists build theories on or around theoretical assumptions, try to accommodate various empirical observations and experimental findings, and often find it quite puzzling how all of them fit together. Precisely because their paramount aim is to explain why certain things happen or are the way they are, however, scientists do not merely puzzle about how their theoretical assumptions can be rendered consistent with the known facts but make sustained efforts to develop those assumptions to a point where they can be employed to derive predictive and explanatory hypotheses, and to derive and test these hypotheses. To the extent to which thinkers are driven only by the proto-Platonic motivation, by contrast, they will remain content with either explaining away problematic intuitions or arguing that they are consistent with each other and with familiar facts and ways of speaking; that is, they will develop their theory no further than necessary to render plausible either the rejection of certain intuitions or the assumption that they are consistent with the rest.

This is exactly what we find in the case of many philosophical theories (including those we have examined in this book): Frequently they are developed no further than their proponents think necessary to render plausible the assumption that the pertinent facts and intuitions are consistent (or, alternatively, the rejection of intuitions which are inconsistent with the rest); in particular, nothing further is done to show that the theories are actually correct. Where this happens, we can either condemn philosophers as slackers who fail to do a proper job—or follow up an intriguing hypothesis: Regardless of the self-image they explicitly maintain, many philosophers are perhaps less interested in finding out how things actually are (or why they are so) than in doing exactly what they are doing; arguably, they primarily seek to restore consistency to their beliefs, in view of disquieting tensions apparent between them. In other words: Where philosophical theories persistently remain underdeveloped, it is plausible to infer that their proponents have the proto-Platonic motivation and that this motivation is so dominant that the thinkers lose interest in the development of the theory the moment this motivation has been met to their satisfaction.

As a case in point, consider analytic functionalism, a major version of the currently dominant philosophical theory of the mind. With slight simplification, we can say that this theory is built around two apparently incompatible intuitions and owes its persuasive force largely to the impression that it reconciles them with each other and with the materialist world-view. The first intuition already motivated J.J.C. Smart’s move from behaviourism to the psycho-physical identity theory (objections to which gave rise to analytic functionalism):

[I]t does seem to me as though, when a person says ‘I have an afterimage’, he is making a genuine report, and that when he says ‘I have a pain’, he is doing more than ‘replace pain-behaviour’, and that ‘this more’ is not just to say that he is in distress.3





This paraphrases a time-honoured philosophical intuition about sensations and occurrent ‘mental states’ as one about their verbal expressions: A ‘genuine report’ is one that reports what we actually perceive, and the intuition is that



(I1) When we have an after-image, pain or thought, we perceive something, namely somewhere in us (cp. conclusion C1 in Chapter 1.3).



Since our materialist world-view does not leave any space for nonphysical correlates of brain events, we would then like to think—like Smart—that the after-images, pains, etc. are events in our brains. This conclusion, however, appears to be inconsistent with various conceptual facts (like T2 in chapter 1.3) and linguistic intuitions which reflect them: We attribute sensations like pain to many different kinds of creatures, with radically different brains, in which we cannot reasonably assume the same events to go on when those creatures have pain. Indeed, an influential thought-experiment made popular by Hillary Putnam4 reveals even on its most cautious analysis that



(I2) We are prepared to attribute pains, thoughts, beliefs and other mental states to alien creatures which we suppose to be physically rather different from us (and, in particular, to have so completely different brains that they cannot be in the same brain-states as us).

Analytic functionalism accommodates intuitions I1 and I2 by suggesting that when we credit people (or animals) with a pain, thought, belief, etc., we refer to an inner state (presumably a brain state), which we characterise, however, purely in terms of its ‘functional’ or causal relations:



(AF) That someone is in a certain mental state (pain, belief, etc.) is to say that he is in a state which, given various other mental states, is caused by certain sensory or environmental input and which, together with various other mental states, causes certain behaviour.



In different kinds of organisms, with different kinds of brains, different physical states may stand in the requisite relations and ‘realize’ the same, say, pain-state. When we feel a pain, we are aware of a state literally ‘in’ us (a state of a part of our brain), and when the Martian feels a pain he feels a state literally ‘in’ him, but there need be no physiological similarity whatsoever between human and Martian brains.

At the heart of this theory is the claim (AF) about the meaning of our ordinary mental terms. The proof of this pudding lies in the eating: To show that the claim is true, we need to actually spell out analyses of particular mental terms, which define them by reference to the causal relations of the mental states at issue. In 1972 David Lewis proposed a method for constructing such definitions of mental kinds, which soon became—and still is—accepted as ‘canonical’:5 We have to assemble the sort of generalisations about causes and effects of mental states that common sense accepts as platitudinous, and thus spell out a ‘folk theory’ of the mind. Then we have to conjoin all these generalisations, replace all mental terms (“belief”, “desire”, “pain”, etc.) in them with variables and bind these with existential quantifiers. The result is a very long sentence which constitutes an implicit definition of all our mental terms (i.e., of the ‘theoretical terms’ of our folk theory). To show that analytic functionalism is correct, we need to construct such a definition and show it consistent with the linguistic intuitions of competent speakers about how to describe particular cases. Once philosophers have obtained valid definitions, neuroscientists can ascertain whether states of our brain stand in the right causal relations to ‘realize’ the mental states we ordinarily attribute to each other. If they do, the existence of beliefs and their ilk has been shown compatible with our materialist world-view.

Even though issues of the greatest philosophical importance thus hang on it, none of this work—none of it, not even a preparatory compilation of potentially pertinent generalisations about selected mental states—has been done even today, more than thirty-five years after Lewis proposed the ‘canonical’ method. Whereas cognitive linguists started to compile substantive metaphor databases to facilitate further work on conceptual metaphors, within a few years of that research programme’s inception, there are still no databases of folk-theoretical generalisations of the sort analytic functionalism takes us to employ every day. Since these generalisations are regarded as parts of implicit definitions of our ordinary mental terms, whose use we master, and since most analytic philosophers have regarded such conceptual analysis as a central part of their brief, this is highly remarkable. (The popular idea that the functional characterisation of one mental state has to refer to all others—and is therefore too daunting an enterprise to even attempt—is obviously false, and should equally obviously lead those who even so regard it as true to abandon analytic functionalism as an unfeasible research programme; it is clearly more of an excuse than an explanation for this curious inaction.)

In fact, this replicates the attitude of the previous generation of philosophers to the then dominant theory of the mind. Logical behaviourism addressed the intuitively compelling ideas we obtain by sublimating picture-driven intuitions—like I1—to the effect that having sensations, thoughts, beliefs, etc. involves perceiving something immaterial, in a nonphysical space (cp. C2 in Chapter 1.3). It did not seek to accommodate these intuitions but to explain them away, to show that the so-called ‘mental phenomena’ are or involve nothing above and beyond such physical phenomena as a materialist world-view can recognise. To this end, logical behaviourists proposed analyses of the meaning of our mental vocabulary, according to which attributions of mental states boil down to attributions of behavioural dispositions. The most defensible analysis maintains that when we say, e.g., that a subject has a headache, we are saying how he would behave under various circumstances (if offered an aspirin, if subjected to loud music, etc.). Indefinitely many counter-factual conditionals are true of a subject in a particular mental state, and it is not possible to give a comprehensive list of them. But this need not prevent one from defending the proposed analysis of meaning by actually spelling out at least a few dozen such conditionals each, for at least a few dozen sensations, beliefs, desires, etc., and showing that competent speakers are willing to countenance their attribution to a subject just in case various such (counter-factual) conditionals are true of him. But during the thirty (!) years in which this theory of the mind was dominant in analytic philosophy, no theorist made any sustained effort to produce such model analyses. Like the analytical functionalists who followed them, logical behaviourists remained throughout content with avowedly simplistic examples merely meant to ‘convey the rough idea’.

Not only philosophers of mind remain forever content with rough outlines and mere projects. Consider philosophers of language who appeared to pursue a straightforwardly explanatory programme: who tried to explain speakers’ competence with new sentences they have never encountered before by hypothesising that they have ‘tacit knowledge’ of a compositional theory of meaning for their language.6 Such ‘knowledge’ is to be attributed to speakers just in case a successful causal-explanatory account of the psychological or neurological processes involved in the exercise of their linguistic competence has a structure that ‘mirrors’ the structure of the theory of meaning at issue. Philosophy’s contribution to this explanatory endeavour was to consist in the construction of suitable compositional theories of meaning. None of the philosophers who thus explained ‘how the theory of meaning can be a philosophical project’ went on to actually execute this project and develop a compositional semantic theory, say, for their mother tongue or any significant part of it.7 This attitude is strikingly different from that of researchers in artificial intelligence like, say, Terry Winograd, who actually provided workable language comprehension pro-grammes for well-defined parts of our language, in little over ten years of their research programme’s inception.8

At least to some extent, this difference is arguably due to a difference in guiding interests and underlying motivation. To return to analytic functionalism, its proponents have been anything but idle. But instead of doing what on their account needs to be done to prove that their theory is actually correct, they made sustained efforts to reconcile the theory with various powerful philosophical intuitions that appear to be incompatible with it. One is the intuition that we can ‘introspect’ at least our occurrent mental states (sensations, thoughts, etc.), at least in the sense that we can recognise them ‘directly’, without engaging in any inference (say, working out their causal relations). Another intuition has it that experiential states (like sensations, perceptions and emotions) are at least partially defined or individuated by the way it is like to have them: At least part of what makes an inner state a pain is what it feels to be in it, not that it is caused by injuries and causes moaning; an inner state might well have these (or various other) causes and effects without feeling the way it does and being a pain. Attempts to accommodate these and further intuitions have spawned an extensive literature.9

To sum up: The development of the major theories that have dominated analytic philosophy of mind for thirty and forty years respectively, logical behaviourism and analytic functionalism, consists largely in attempts to either explain away or accommodate philosophical intuitions which appear to be incompatible with each other or a certain world-view or familiar facts. It has not included sustained efforts to show that the theories are actually correct, even where generally accepted methods for doing so were available. It seems reasonable to infer that these theories’ proponents primarily tried, in different ways, to restore consistency to their beliefs, in the face of specific challenges posed by powerful intuitions—crucially including intuitions (like I1 above) that have been engendered by the perceptual pictures we have identified.

The only way to rigorously determine whether thinkers actually are in the grip of philosophical pictures and address problems raised only through picture-motivated reasoning is to conduct case-studies of the kind presented in previous chapters. In their course, we established not only that four lastingly influential early modern thinkers were in the grip of philosophical pictures and addressed problems raised in that grip. We also encountered prima facie evidence for adherence to pictures on a rather large scale: Thinkers are likely to adhere to philosophical pictures wherever their reflections are couched in terms introduced as the result of picture-motivated leaps (Chapter 2.5). We have found this to be the case for the key terms of the terminologies of “substances” and “accidents” (Chapter 3.1), “minds” and “ideas” (Chapter 1.3), and “perceptions” and “sense-data” (Chapter 3.5, cp. 8.2). These three terminologies pre-structure practically all Scholastic and 17th-century metaphysics, practically all early modern philosophy of mind, and modern philosophy of perception until the mid-20th century, respectively. While considerably loftier, the present considerations render it reasonable to engage in similar case-studies on yet more recent and current philosophical work, and to follow up the working hypothesis that, through the ages and to the present day, many, and many consequential, efforts in philosophy have been motivated by the struggle with philosophical pictures and the problems they engender; arguably, this struggle is both distinctive of, and (still) central to philosophy.

Some members of the philosophical community are driven by scientific motivations and engage in endeavours which sometimes develop into new autonomous disciplines. We can distinguish these ‘proto-scientific’ from more distinctively philosophical endeavours which have a distinctive pre-or non-scientific motivation and address problems that remain within the purview of the subject, no matter what new sciences emerge from it and take accretions to that core with them. For better or worse, such endeavours can be said to constitute the essence of philosophy. We have seen (in Section 1) that the struggle with picture-raised problems is of this kind and found reason to suspect that it plays a central role to this day. If that is so, it constitutes the essence of at any rate a part of philosophy. Our heterogeneous subject may include more than one class of such endeavours. The now common distinction between ‘theoretical philosophy’ (those areas of philosophy that cluster around epistemology and metaphysics, and the philosophies of mind and language) and ‘practical philosophy’ (comprising ethics, moral and political philosophy, as well as aesthetics) may imperfectly reflect the existence of two such classes: the concern with such normative questions as how we should lead our lives, treat each other and organise our communities, and the concern with problems of the distinctive kind we have considered here. The struggle with philosophical pictures and their off-spring may well constitute the essence of theoretical philosophy.

In view of the normative or exhortatory ring with which statements about the ‘nature’ or ‘essence’ of philosophy are frequently endorsed, it may bear stating that we are now advancing an empirical hypothesis about the problems addressed by philosophers and the motivation with which they address them, as evident from the efforts they make in response. This hypothesis allows us to understand how philosophy can be a distinctive and in some ways autonomous intellectual enterprise without having what it manifestly lacks, namely a subject-area of its own or some generally accepted body of method to set it apart. More importantly, this hypothesis is open to empirical confirmation and disconfirmation, namely through further case-studies of the kind we have undertaken. If it is confirmed, we will see that, as a brute matter of fact, theoretical philosophy as we know it is essentially a struggle with philosophical pictures.





3. THE META-PHILOSOPHICAL TURN




Whether or not this sweeping claim will be borne out, picture-raised problems certainly form an extensive and important class of philosophical problems. The struggle with them commonly takes the form of the construction of philosophical theories. The first responses to the conflicts between picture- motivated ideas and familiar facts are often spontaneous rather than deliberate: Thinkers spontaneously offer sublimating glosses which paper them over. The moment spontaneous reinterpretation can no longer gloss over these conflicts and they become salient, however, philosophers formulate paradoxes or reconciliation problems and construct theories to resolve the underlying tensions in their beliefs. These theories are typically developed in the grip of the same philosophical pictures that led to the problems they are meant to solve; that is, these theories tend to be developed under the spell of the very pictures that led thinkers to adopt the intuitively compelling claims that appear to be at odds with familiar facts and ways of speaking and thus raise the problems at issue. These theories are ill-founded to the extent to which they are built on these typically absurd and frequently delusional claims, pointless to the extent to which they are meant to solve problems which are generated only by such claims, and pernicious both to the extent to which they lead to further absurd claims which raise further ill-motivated problems, and to the extent to which they lull sincere thinkers into confidently maintaining those absurdities. These points were illustrated most forcefully by Berkeley’s swift ascent from a delusional doctrine of perception to an absurd metaphysical system which promised to reconcile that doctrine with common sense and, to this end, reduced the entire world to ideas and minds to house them (Chapters 4–5). While this particular system remained unpopular and did not migrate into men’s minds, it nicely illustrates that at any rate the philosophical theories devised in the grip of philosophical pictures, in response to picture-raised problems, ‘are but so many stage-plays, representing worlds of their own creation’ (to borrow words from Bacon).10

We have developed an arguably more reasonable and fruitful approach to the problems we raise in the grip of philosophical pictures: the approach of diagnostic analysis guided by novel meta-philosophical findings and theories (first outlined in Chapters 2.4–2.5). This new approach implies a radical reorientation of philosophical work. We now need to ascend from the detail to the big picture implied, from the trees we have studied to the wood we may otherwise miss for them.

The approach builds on two basic insights: Many philosophical questions are motivated by the impression of some sort of difficulty, often about familiar facts. Frequently, such impressions are due to picture-driven reasoning which is unsound by the proponent’s own lights, and the difficulty is illusory. Where this is the case, we can resolve the problem our question poses, by reconstructing the pertinent reasoning and showing that it is unsound: Where this reasoning is fallacious root-and-branch, we thus come to see that we have no right to believe there is such a difficulty as we imagined, that our problem is illusory. In response to such a problem, we accordingly ask, first, for the philosophical motivation for formulating it and, second, about the cognitive processes that systematically have us go wrong in philosophical reflection, and which may have misled us in formulating the question at issue:





Why do we ask this question, formulate this problem?

What cognitive processes systematically have us go wrong (i) in philosophical reflection in general and (ii) on that topic, in particular?





In many cases, pursuit of these two questions will allow us to elicit, develop and refute what philosophical motivation we had for raising the problem. We thus come to resolve philosophical problems by turning to meta-philosophical questions about how philosophers reason about the topic at issue and came to conceive of those problems and questions about it. This turn from the topic the philosophical question is about to the meta-philosophical question of how philosophers reason about that topic and came to ask that question represents a radical reorientation of philosophical work. Let us call it the ‘meta-philosophical turn’.

This turn represents as fundamental a reorientation of philosophical work as the ‘linguistic turn’ of early analytic philosophy, where—speaking very roughly indeed—philosophers sought to resolve philosophical problems by turning their attention from the matters of non-linguistic fact these problems appeared to be about, to questions about the meaning of the linguistic terms used to talk about those matters. The ultimate motivation of our new turn is the same: We turn away from the same issues of perennial philosophical disagreement, fired by the same hope of replacing manifestly unproductive by potentially more helpful questions. But we turn to an entirely new set of questions. (In their pursuit, however, we may also find ‘linguistic’ questions highly relevant: Wherever we seek to expose biased interpretations of linguistic expressions, the proper analysis of their actual meaning and established use is essential—e.g. when we reconstruct the third stage of the pictorial process, or other relevant processes like those we will consider in the next chapter.)

At first glance, the meta-philosophical turn may appear outright mad. After all, no historian will arrive at a correct answer to a historical question by examining his own motivation for pursuing it. This may make him aware of blinkers, ideological and other, which obstruct his quest for an answer, but will not by itself provide that answer. Nor will a physicist succeed in explaining any phenomenon by getting clear on what got him interested in it. And while both may benefit from an account of vicious cognitive processes shaping their reflections, this may help them to spot mistakes they have made in the past and avoid similar mistakes in the future, but will not by itself lead them to an answer to their questions about, say, the financial situation of the Prussian crown at the height of the Seven Years’ War or the conductivity of metals at low temperatures. So why should a philosopher be able to resolve the problems that worry him by examining his motivation for asking his questions and the cognitive processes that drive his reflections?

The answer is of course provided by old insights we have rediscovered and new findings we have made, about the genesis of (many) philosophical problems and the nature of (much) philosophical reflection: To put it in the language of Bacon and Berkeley, we have re-discovered that some of the difficulties which have worried philosophers are entirely of our own making, that under the spell of various idols we first raise ‘a dust, and then complain, we cannot see.’11 We have examined philosophical problems which are particularly central to and distinctive of the subject, and found that they are not raised by matters of fact but are generated only by unsound reasoning issuing in philosophical paradoxes. Therefore, they are to be resolved not by acquiring new knowledge about the topic at hand but by insight into their nature and genesis: by showing that they are generated only by certain paradoxical lines of thought, that these lines of thought are unsound by the proponent’s own lights, and that he has no right to maintain the conclusions that raise the problems. In many cases, the key to the resolution of a philosophical problem is a proper understanding of its nature and genesis.

This is illustrated by our first chapters’ treatment of the ontological mind-body problem in its seminal form (see, in particular, Chapter 2.3). We found that the struggle with a set of perceptual pictures has us introduce the ‘mind’ as an inner space of perception, conceive of this space as a nonphysical and private realm, interpret talk of thoughts and beliefs, memories and sensations as referring to things perceived in some such realm, and in this way has us wonder where in the world these things exist. We saw that we do not solve this problem by acquiring new knowledge about our thoughts and beliefs, memories and sensations, say knowledge about how and where these things are ‘perceived’ (or ‘realised’). Rather, we saw that we can dissolve the problem by becoming aware of the lines of thought that, in philosophical reflection, led us to imagine that these things have to be somewhere when we have them, by exposing in these lines of thought the moves that are unsound by our own lights, and by becoming aware of our propensity to unwittingly make such moves, against better knowledge. In the next chapter, we will develop an analogous treatment of problems raised by the sense-datum doctrine of perception. We can dissolve such problems through systematic diagnostic analysis, a new kind of historical, plain method: by retracing the steps that led us to formulate the problem, and exposing the fallacies they contain.

We have found that the first and most decisive of these steps are not made in deliberate reasoning and explicit philosophical argument, which often simply presuppose the most consequential ideas from the very start. The most fundamental of these ideas tend to be maintained without any argument whatsoever. Others get presupposed more or less directly by the arguments (if any) that are subsequently constructed to support them. With still pardonable exaggeration we can say that all the most important moves and mistakes have already been made when the deliberate construction of explicit arguments gets underway. We hence need to focus less on these arguments than on what precedes them: on our non-intentional reasoning and the plethora of inferences we spontaneously make without realising that we are making any substantive step; we need to turn our attention to those intuitively compelling or ‘natural’ steps which appear too trivial to merit closer examination, let alone argumentative support.

The motor of most creative thinking in philosophy, as elsewhere, is analogical reasoning, non-intentional and deliberate, sound and unsound. (To be ‘creative’, thinking has to engender novel but not necessarily good ideas.) Accordingly, we found the deductive arguments we encountered to be the icing on cakes filled with conclusions of analogical reasoning, metaphorical and other. So it seems we should turn our attention from deductive argument and inductive inference (which plays a rather minor role in philosophy, anyway) to analogical reasoning (which philosophers have explored rather less well). To sum up, the turn, at the strategic level, from philosophical problems to meta-philosophical questions about their nature and genesis and about the ways in which philosophers think about the pertinent topics, this meta-philosophical turn promises to be productive if accompanied, at the tactical level, by shifts of attention from controlled to non-intentional reasoning, and from deductive and inductive to analogical inference.

These moves take us to the project of a cognitive epistemology of philosophy. An ‘epistemology of’ a given subject is a normatively relevant study of how its practitioners build up and maintain a body of real or supposed knowledge: Such study seeks to help them assess what right or reason they have for maintaining what they do, by identifying and evaluating some of the relevant processes and principles of belief-formation and justification. Such epistemological study can go significantly beyond the sort of careful scrutiny with which conscientious researchers in any subject retrace their steps every now and then. It can make a highly pertinent contribution, in particular, when it proceeds from an analysis of factors or processes that shape belief-formation and justification in a discipline but lie outside that discipline’s purview.

The cognitive epistemology of philosophy analyses how philosophical reflection is shaped by non-intentional reasoning and spontaneous (non-controlled) inferences, which we are hardly ever aware of but get to know about through psychological experiments. With a focus on non-intentional analogical reasoning and its pit-falls, we have analysed, in particular, how philosophical intuitions, claims, questions, problems, arguments and theories are shaped by experimentally documented cognitive distortions: recurrent (rather than one-off), systematic (rather than unrelated) and characteristic (rather than idiosyncratic) mistakes in non-intentional reasoning and spontaneous inference. Such analysis puts us into a position to reconstruct the steps of philosophical reasoning even where traditional forms of philosophical analysis have nothing to go on, for example, where thinkers maintain substantive claims in the complete absence of argument (like the claims that make up the classical conception of the mind) or on the basis of ‘arguments’ sporting huge gaps which cannot be filled by invoking only their stated views (like Galileo’s argument from conceivability).

The cognitive epistemology of philosophy is an empirical study undertaken for philosophical purposes: with the foremost aim not of formulating any laws of nature (psychology) but of exposing, understanding and correcting fallacies in philosophical reasoning. This can either help us to resolve philosophical problems or prevent us from falling into error when engaged in speculative theorising. The immediate fruits of such study are accounts or theories of how cognitive distortions shape philosophical reflection about a certain topic (like our account of how picture-driven intuitions shaped thinking about cognition and sensation and led to a new concept of ‘the mind’). These, in turn, facilitate the diagnostic analysis of various philosophical problems in the area, which resolves them by showing that they arise only through unsound reasoning (like our treatment of the seminal ontological mind-body problem, in Chapter 2.3). They also facilitate the critical analysis of speculative theorising in which we are particularly prone to fall prey to such distortions. This includes not only distinctively philosophical theorising devoted to the above-mentioned problems but also proto-scientific theorising (like Boyle’s account of the qualities of bodies, cp. Chapter 3.2). (Thus those who stand to gain from a cognitive epistemology of our subject also include those philosophical theorists who have a primarily scientific motivation.)

There are at least two complementary approaches to engaging in such philosophically rewarding empirical study: Experimental work of a kind already undertaken in cognitive psychology can establish the existence or occurrence of certain kinds of cognitive distortions, under conditions which obtain also in much philosophical reflection (see Chapter 1.2). To find out how these distortions shape actual philosophical reflection, however, we must turn to philosophical texts which record such reflection and its results, typically in the shape that thinkers’ sustained efforts and corrections gave them, leaving only those distortions that manifestly overpower them and are of lasting consequence.

In the first part of this book, we have developed a methodology which employs philosophical texts as empirical evidence, first, to identify occurrences of experimentally documented cognitive distortions in the author’s reasoning and, second, to reconstruct how these distortions shape—and vitiate—his claims, arguments, problems and theories. In the central part of this book, we have used this new methodology in a series of case-studies on Locke’s, Berkeley’s and Ayer’s work on sense-perception. Through such a series of case-studies on philosophical works about the same range of problems or topics, we can build up to a meta-philosophical theory about the non-intentional reasoning that raises those problems and the patterns of such reasoning that permeate much philosophical reflection about the given topic. Such theories provide a basis for the diagnostic resolution of the pertinent problems (and insight into errors one needs to guard against when engaging in speculative theorising about the topic).

This approach, the findings it is built on and the results it yields endow the study of the history of our subject with a new kind of philosophical relevance: Such study can facilitate the resolution of philosophical problems, not by extracting from old sources important philosophical insights about the topic at issue but by identifying pernicious cognitive processes that drive (and vitiate) philosophical reflection about it, which engenders those problems. These meta-philosophical insights we can then apply— everyone for himself—to the analysis of our own thinking about the same problems or their descendants. In particular where the problems that worry us have emerged from the philosophical tradition (as will most often be the case), we are then likely to find that our own thinking involves fallacies of the same kind that led already our predecessors to imagine difficulties where there are none.

Where philosophy boils down to a struggle with philosophical pictures and their off-spring, its primary motivation is so radically different from those of scientific inquiry that it is a mistake to even try to turn it into a science (Section 1). But the struggle can be guided by science: The resolution of the philosophical problems we raise in the grip of philosophical pictures can be based on a study of their nature and genesis which is scientifically informed, empirically grounded and suitably rigorous. The conception of philosophy as a struggle with philosophical pictures thus has us give up the age-old ambition of placing the discipline on the secure path of a science. But, at the same time, the project of a cognitive epistemology of philosophy allows us to realise a closely related ambition: the ambition to provide the philosophical struggle with sound guidance from a discipline that can aspire to proceed on that path and put an end to complaints engendered by dust we have kicked up ourselves.

The struggle with philosophical pictures and their off-spring is a distinctively philosophical enterprise (Section 1). To the extent to which it serves this enterprise, this new discipline of cognitive epistemology is distinctively philosophical in character. While building on concepts and findings from different branches of cognitive science, including cognitive linguistics and cognitive and social psychology, the cognitive epistemology of philosophy is not ‘just psychology’; it is a new way of doing philosophy that builds on old (meta-)philosophical insights and fresh scientific findings.





4. THE THERAPEUTIC TURN




The principal result of our first exercises in such cognitive epistemology is the identification of the complementary workings of different autonomous cognitive processes, namely non-intentional analogical reasoning and belief-bias effects, which together with spontaneous reinterpretation (sublimation) make up what we have called the ‘pictorial process’. This autonomous cognitive process has us systematically form and maintain paradoxical beliefs in the absence of warrant. Their clash with other beliefs gives rise to ill-motivated but disquieting ‘problems’ which we typically try to solve through pointless and ill-founded theories whose construction is driven by the same process that had us imagine the problems. These find-ings vindicate the adoption of radically new aims for philosophical work: the adoption of aims that are constitutive of therapy, in the strict, literal sense in which the term applies to exemplary psychotherapies.

Philosophers frequently think of therapy ‘in the usual sense’ as necessarily opposed to argument and assume that the use of non-argumentative methods must set therapeutic intervention apart from rational discourse.12 But this is not so. Even exemplary psychotherapies like cognitive therapy 13 and rational emotive therapy14 for depression crucially involve argument and the assessment of evidence (to establish, for instance, whether the depressed patient really is as utterly inept as he thinks). Therapy may proceed by argument; therapeutic dialogue may be a form of rational discourse. What sets cognitive and rational therapy apart from other forms of rational discourse are not the (merely supposedly non-argumentative) means employed, but the ends pursued and the problems addressed—which are, anyway, the only things that the very different forms of psychotherapy have in common. According to standard explanations of the terms, a procedure qualifies as ‘therapeutic’ to the extent to which it is designed to cure illness or relieve the symptoms of disease.15 Psychotherapies are designed to cure mental illness, that is, to remedy a lack of rational control over one’s feelings and actions which materialises as a series of emotional or behavioural problems, i.e. emotions and behaviours which are frequently conceptualised as ‘symptoms’ of mental disorders or diseases. According to a common clinical criterion, a subject qualifies as in need of therapy when he has significant emotional or behavioural problems.16 Conversely, psychotherapy is, quite simply, a treatment that primarily seeks to solve or ease such problems and prevent their future recurrence.

Therapeutic procedures may seek to achieve this aim by realising other, instrumental, goals. Cognitive therapy, for instance, seeks to achieve its aim by improving the subject’s self-understanding in various ways and acquiring shared insight into the cognitive and other psychological processes that shape his thoughts and feelings (see below). But what makes it qualify as a ‘therapy’ is that it subordinates these intellectual or cognitive goals to the guiding aim of solving emotional and behavioural problems and organises a variety of efforts towards achieving this primary aim.
