The autonomous cognitive process we have identified gives rise to paradoxical beliefs which engender unwarranted feelings of disquiet and the pointless construction of ill-founded theories. Those beliefs frequently qualify as ‘delusions’ and the feelings and efforts they engender as ‘emotional’ and ‘behavioural problems’ (Chapter 6.6). While few philosophers have strong feelings of disquiet, behavioural problems are not uncommon. You recall:

A subject has behavioural problems to the extent to which his behaviour is unreasonable and fails to be autonomous.



A subject’s behaviour fails to be autonomous in case he does things he would not want to do if not in a certain disabling condition that is not under his direct control, and in case he refrains from doing things that he would want to do if not in such a condition.





We may have such a problem when we are under the spell of philosophical pictures (the presently relevant disabling condition), when picture-driven reasoning has us formulate ill-motivated problems and when we construct otherwise pointless philosophical theories that are to solve these ‘problems’. These philosophically pointless efforts constitute a behavioural problem if, and only if, we would not want to waste time and effort on attempts to solve the ‘problems’ at issue if we were not under the spell of the pertinent pictures and saw through the problems they have us imagine. Hence a career-minded academic philosopher who does not care whether the problems he works on are well- or ill-motivated, as long as work on them is fashionable and easily publishable, will not have a behavioural problem when working on picture-raised and ill-motivated problems: He would want to do the same work even if he was not under the spell of the pertinent pictures and fully realised how ill-motivated his philosophical problems are. Behavioural problems of this kind are the privilege of intellectually sincere thinkers.

When the philosophical problems we work on are raised only by the clash between delusions and other beliefs, or among each other, we imagine a problem where there is none—or, at any rate, where we have no right to believe there is one. As we have seen (in Section 1), philosophical questions are frequently motivated by the impression that there is some kind of difficulty or problem about familiar facts, and this impression is frequently created by, and only by, a paradoxical claim we find intuitively compelling when engaged in philosophical reflection: When we use our five senses, we immediately perceive only immaterial sense-data. So how is it possible for us to see houses and trees (Chapter 5) or to communicate by means of terms—like “house” and “tree”—which purport to refer to public material objects (Chapter 6)? Only mechanical properties really exist in physical bodies. So where are the colours we see, and how is it possible for us to describe such bodies as coloured (Chapter 3)? And so on and so forth. All these philosophical questions are motivated by absurd beliefs frequently maintained as a matter of delusion, and only these beliefs create the impression of the difficulty to be overcome.

These problems are as imaginary as the intellectual problem of a man who is in the grip of paranoid delusions and wonders how it is possible that his wife apparently fails to notice the spies who surround them. (Perhaps she is part of the plot?) But just as this imaginary problem causes the poor man emotional and behavioural problems which are very real—his behaviour towards his wife gets ever more hostile—the difficulties we merely imagine under the spell of philosophical pictures give rise to genuine emotional or behavioural problems. And just as the poor man’s emotional and behavioural problems are the only ‘paranoia-related’ problems that are real, the philosopher’s emotional or behavioural problems are the only ‘picture-related’ problems that really confront him. We have seen reason to suspect that an extensive and central part of theoretical philosophy is driven by a specifically philosophical motivation ultimately due to picture-driven reasoning (Section 1), that this central part of philosophy consists in a struggle with philosophical pictures and their off-spring, and addresses only ‘picture-related’ problems (Section 2). If this is correct, emotional and behavioural problems are the only problems we actually confront, and do not merely imagine, in a core part of our subject. In this part of our subject, the only problems we can reasonably try to solve are the emotional and behavioural problems constituted by unreasonable philosophical toil and worries.

If we do not wish to waste our lives on merely imaginary problems, we may respond to this conclusion in either of two ways. We can accept the specifically philosophical motivation we have identified (in Section 1) as the strongest force driving us, acknowledge the struggle with philosophical pictures and their off-spring as our main concern, and make it our main aim to put an end to the unwarranted intellectual disquiet and the pointless intellectual efforts thus engendered and, eventually, to cure the disease of the understanding that brings about those symptoms, i.e., to liberate ourselves from the spell of philosophical pictures. The adoption of this primary aim is constitutive of therapy. It amounts to a major reorientation of philosophical work. Let’s call it the therapeutic turn: To take the therapeutic turn is to explicitly adopt as primary aim of one’s philosophical work the aim of solving emotional or behavioural problems or of curing the diseases that cause them.

Alternatively, we can disown the specifically philosophical motivation we have identified, decide not to waste a thought on the very possibility of any familiar fact, not allow ourselves any Platonic marvelling but only scientific curiosity and turn from the familiar problems of philosophical tradition to topically related scientific questions. If we do not retrain in a different discipline but try to contribute through the kind of abstract theorising characteristic of philosophy, we will probably continue to be under the spell of philosophical pictures and remain prone to the cognitive distortions they engender. These will systematically hinder our efforts to contribute sound argument to the scientific debate we wish to join. To put ourselves into a position to make a worthwhile contribution, we hence first have to break or weaken the hold the pertinent pictures have on us. We have to cure in ourselves the intellectual altitude sickness we have identified. Even if we do not want to take the therapeutic turn, we need to make therapeutic efforts.

Up to now, we have adopted the perspective of the intellectual craftsman who seeks to contribute to the solution of some problems, who therefore turns either to emotional or behavioural problems or to scientific questions when he finds traditional philosophical problems to be ill-motivated, and who needs to make therapeutic efforts to contribute to problems or questions of either kind. But there is of course also a deeper, ethical, motivation for trying to cure diseases of the understanding from which we find we suffer: We aspire to being masters of ourselves. When we find that in pure reflection we are at the mercy of deeply entrenched habits of unsound inference rooted in autonomous cognitive processes, we may therefore wish to wrest from them control over our own thoughts, to weaken those ingrained habits and to modify those autonomous processes, in order to gain as much rational control over our beliefs and as much intellectual autonomy as possible—not only to increase our ability to attain further goals, intellectual or other, but also, quite simply, as an end in itself.

Our meta-philosophical findings thus vindicate a two-fold reorientation of philosophical work, at the level of approach and aims. The meta-philosophical turn leads us to a new approach to philosophical problems. The therapeutic turn has us adopt new aims for philosophical work. We now turn to a framework that allows us to harness the new approach to the new aims: Some guiding ideas from cognitive therapy will provide us with a helpful framework for the practice of therapeutic philosophy, the kind of therapy in philosophy which seeks, first and foremost, to solve emotional and behavioural problems that arise in and through philosophical reflection and to cure the diseases of the understanding that give rise to those problems.





5. A FRAMEWORK FOR THERAPEUTIC PHILOSOPHY




The disease of the understanding we face is adherence to philosophical pictures. It engenders unwarranted toil and worry by having us form delusional beliefs and ideas which clash with common sense or other convictions. Since they are supported by autonomous cognitive processes which are not under our direct control and are maintained as a matter of delusion, the mere identification and refutation of these beliefs is not going to make us shed them: Even if we do not continue to maintain them explicitly, we will be unable to stop them from creeping back into our reasoning, as tacit presuppositions. As long as this happens, tensions in our beliefs will persist and motivate philosophical efforts which we would reject as pointless if we were not in the grip of the pertinent delusions and the philosophical pictures that engender them. To overcome picture-raised philosophical problems and put an end to the toil and worry they engender, we have to do more than merely refute the pertinent beliefs.

To meet this at first sight daunting challenge we may borrow ideas from cognitive approaches in clinical psychology. Cognitive-behavioural therapies addressing delusions began to emerge about fifteen to twenty years ago and have proved quite effective.17 The main aim of cognitive-behavioural therapy for psychotic conditions is to reduce the emotional and behavioural distress due to delusional beliefs.18 Once formed, these beliefs permeate subjects’ thinking through (perfectly normal) belief-bias effects; biased interpretation of everyday situations in the light of delusional beliefs then systematically generates bogus evidence that appears to support them.19 (Hearing a car horn outside, the paranoid man leaps to the conclusion that ‘they have come to get me’ and flees in distress through the back door, apparently with fresh evidence of the ongoing conspiracy against him.) To put an end to such unreasonable worries and actions, therapists seek to break such ‘belief maintenance cycles’, i.e., seek to weaken the delusional beliefs and the propensity to interpret things in their light, ideally until the subject explicitly rejects the beliefs and no longer presupposes them in interpretation and reasoning. To weaken these beliefs and propensities, an approach of cognitive-behavioural therapy proceeds in four conceptually distinct but practically overlapping steps.20

(1) The therapist elicits and challenges what evidence the subject is able to adduce for the belief targeted. Most, if not all, of this evidence is constituted by biased interpretations of specific events (like the sounding of the car horn), namely interpretations in the light of the delusional belief at issue. The therapist explains how belief-bias effects commonly shape everybody’s interpretation of situations and helps the subject identify the beliefs that may have guided him in this way. Going through the evidence for these beliefs, the therapist then suggests, for each item of evidence offered, a more reasonable interpretation of the situation adverted to. The exercise is to make the subject wary of his spontaneous interpretations of events, to bring out that these interpretations owe more to his own beliefs than to any salient features of the situations in question and show that he has little or no warrant for the belief thus ‘supported’ (which may of course be true, for all that). 21

(2) Once this has prepared the subject for the suggestion that he holds the pertinent beliefs for another, other than rational motivation, the therapist seeks to identify such a motivation. According to some theories of paranoia, for example, delusional beliefs formed in this condition have a psychological function and serve to ‘defend against’ negative self-evaluations, that is, prevent the subject from being completely overwhelmed by feelings of inadequacy and insignificance (e.g. by making him feel the focus of many conspirators’ attention). 22 Thus the therapist seeks to explain to the patient how he may have come to form and maintain certain beliefs in the complete absence of warrant, due to a-rational psychological processes, cognitive and other, over which subjects have no direct control.23 Together, these first two steps provide the subject with a new, alternative framework for understanding his situation: He comes to understand that psychological processes, cognitive and other, which are not under our direct control and of which we are typically unaware, may systematically push people to form and maintain beliefs in the complete absence of warrant. Such processes may be systematically unsound and surround us with imaginary threats and challenges, successes and failures, which those very processes have us regard as ever more real.

(3) To enable the subject to soberly explore whether his initial or this new understanding of his situation is accurate (whether he is really pursued by conspirators or whether psychological processes of which he is unaware drive him to imagine a conspiracy where there is none), the therapist then seeks to weaken the psychological motivation identified. He may, e.g., try to improve the subject’s self-esteem through a variety of exercises.24 These moves, and only these moves, put the subject into a position to accept and assimilate the refutation of his delusional beliefs: To do so, he needs a new, alternative framework for understanding how he could form and maintain the beliefs in the complete absence of warrant, and needs to be liberated from psychological pressures to cleave to them, come what may.

(4) Once the ground has been thus prepared for a convincing refutation of the targeted beliefs, the therapist discusses with the subject what observations or tests would establish their falsity to the latter’s satisfaction and prods him to make such observations and tests (including ‘behavioural tests’ which establish to the subject’s satisfaction either that he is able to do things he believed beyond his pale or that he is able to cope with failure he believed would devastate him). 25 This approach has been found to significantly weaken delusional beliefs and the propensity to presuppose them and interpret everyday situations in their light. More specifically, it has been found to weaken them to a point where the subject becomes able to distance himself from them not only in word but also in deed and feeling, so that they no longer cause sustained distress and no longer influence his actions.

Apart from the identification and removal of pertinent psychological motivations, the key move in this kind of cognitive therapy consists in conveying ‘meta-cognitive insight’, 26 namely general insight into the a-rational and autonomous psychological processes, cognitive and other, which have the subject systematically form and maintain troubling beliefs in the absence of warrant. The key move, in other words, is to help the subject acquire a proper understanding of why and how he comes to imagine, e.g., a conspiracy where there is none and to surround himself with threats of his mind’s making.

Where delusional beliefs do not have any distinct, non-cognitive, psychological motivation and are mainly due to a-rational and autonomous cognitive processes, it is reasonable to assume that they can be successfully modified by conveying such meta-cognitive insight. Where we have no specifically psychological problems to contend with, the key to overcoming philosophical delusions lies in improving the thinker’s self-understanding: We can overcome such delusions by coming to understand how unsound cognitive processes of which we are hardly ever aware shape our philosophical reflections about the topic at issue and systematically push us to maintain wild claims and imagine difficulties where there are none. Thinkers can overcome their philosophical delusions by improving their self-understanding.

In the next chapters, we shall see that two therapeutic philosophers, J.L. Austin and Ludwig Wittgenstein, explicitly adopted primarily therapeutic aims for their work and sought to achieve them by gaining and conveying relevant meta-cognitive insight: by exposing ‘concealed motives’ for espousing a philosophical delusion, motives of which its champions are unaware (Austin), or by identifying ‘urges to misunderstand’ which are not under our direct control (Wittgenstein). In the previous chapters, we have built up to meta-cognitive insights of this kind, in a more systematic manner, on a larger scale, and with the help of concepts and findings not yet available to these philosophers. We have identified an autonomous cognitive process of crucial importance, the pictorial process (Chapter 1.3), traced how it shapes philosophical reflection at several levels, and devised the approach of systematic diagnostic analysis that lets us understand how this process systematically shapes our philosophical reflection and leads us to maintain several related philosophical claims and to raise several related philosophical problems (Chapters 2.4–2.5). We now see that this approach, which we initially introduced only to facilitate economies of scale, actually provides us with exactly the kind of meta-cognitive insight we need to weaken delusional philosophical beliefs and solve the emotional and behavioural problems they engender.





6. A THERAPEUTIC STRATEGY




Cognitive therapists found initial engagement with those in need of therapy one of the biggest challenges facing them.27 Many philosophers will feel hostile to the new account of philosophical reflection and, in particular, the suggestions that such reflection is profoundly shaped by inferences thinkers are typically not aware of making, that many philosophical problems are ultimately due to mistakes in such non-intentional reasoning, and that these problems are to be resolved by coming to understand their nature and genesis—rather than by acquiring new knowledge about the topic they are about. To get even sincere thinkers to soberly listen to these suggestions and follow them up with an open mind, we clearly need to make some such moves as cognitive therapists who devote much effort to ‘de-stigmatising’ and ‘normalising’ the phenomena they address, e.g., by pointing out that various famous and successful people were affected by them.28 Thus, in addition to stressing the ‘normality’ of non-intentional analogical inferences and belief-bias effects, the present book has adapted this particular move and developed its new account of philosophical reasoning by analysing classical works of thinkers whose lasting philosophical importance cannot reasonably be questioned (Locke and Berkeley), before turning to a rightly highly respected academic philosopher (A.J. Ayer) whose stature and working environment are perhaps more similar to those of current readers.

The first step of a viable therapeutic strategy thus consists in a systematic diagnostic analysis of the classical texts from which the philosophical problems that interest us are ultimately derived. This analysis shows us that the authors at issue were under the spell of certain philosophical pictures and reconstructs how non-intentional inferences driven by these pictures have shaped their philosophical reflections. We thus acquire general insight into the way in which unsound processes of non-intentional reasoning can lead competent thinkers to accept unsound intuitions, maintain unwarranted claims and raise ill-motivated problems. There is significantly less variation between these philosophical intuitions, claims and problems than there is between the delusions of psychotic patients who share the same diagnosis. The specific content of the latter is to a considerable extent determined by the personal fates and fortunes of the individual patients—even when two men have the delusion that their wives are unfaithful, their delusions will still be about different women and reinforced by different bogus evidence. The philosophical claims and questions that interest us, by contrast, are advanced with pretty similar content by many different thinkers and tend to be based on a repertoire of intuitions or arguments shared by many different philosophers, often across different generations. The analysis of classical texts can therefore reveal not only what unsound cognitive processes may in general shape philosophical reflection but can also show us how such processes may shape particular intuitions, arguments, claims and questions which we share ourselves.

The insight that philosophical intuitions may be systematically due to cognitive distortions rather than intellectual competencies may make us wary of the claims we find intuitively compelling in philosophical reflection. We may then suspend our trust not only in the particular intuitions reconstructed but also in all other philosophical intuitions we have not yet examined in detail (which may, for all we know at this point, be due to the same unsound processes, if to different philosophical pictures). When we do so, we should also suspend our belief in the claims apparently supported by those intuitions as well as our work on philosophical questions and problems that might be motivated by them, until we have found out whether we are ourselves under the spell of philosophical pictures or victim to other unsound processes of non-intentional inference.

To determine whether a thinker is under the spell of a given philosophical picture, we need to determine whether inferences driven by that picture contribute to the best available explanation of paradoxical intuitions and gaps in his argument (Chapter 1.2). These are comparatively easy to identify where the intuitions and arguments at issue are couched in concepts in which we are not at home ourselves—as in the early modern texts from which we proceeded in this book. The concepts’ novelty—to us—significantly reduces the likelihood of the relevant non-intentional analogical inferences being triggered in us, even when we are under the spell of the pertinent philosophical pictures. But the moment we turn from historical case-studies to our own case, the relevant intuitions will not strike us as paradoxical, as picture-driven inferences or belief bias will lead us to conflate these intuitive claims with the distinct truisms or common-sense-convictions from which we in fact infer them. The same phenomena will have us mistake argumentative gaps that are actually quite wild for trivial or obvious steps. To identify the relevant explananda in our own work, we will have to make an extra effort.

This effort involves different kinds of ordinary language analysis, pioneered by G.E. Moore and J.L. Austin, respectively:29 We may have to get clear on what the philosophical claims we find intuitively compelling come down to in plain English. And we certainly need to determine, through careful analysis of ordinary pre-philosophical talk, whether we assert or imply anything of the sort in such talk about the pertinent topic. This allows us to determine whether the claims we initially regarded as truisms or pieces of common sense really qualify as such—or go beyond common sense or are even at odds with it. A case in point is our analysis of pre-philosophical talk of ‘the mind’ (Chapters 1.4–1.5), which revealed that in such talk we do not use the term to refer to any space or organ of inner perception but employ it either as a metaphor-marker or as an integral part of idiomatic expressions which do not imply the existence of any literally or figuratively ‘inner’ space or organ of perception. (For instance, “I see the solution in my mind” means the same as “I see the solution” in the familiar metaphorical sense of that verb, namely that I managed to think of the solution; and “to keep in mind” simply means ‘to remember in one’s thinking’ and does not imply that anything is kept at any location.) Thus the apparently commonsensical intuition—I1 (in Chapter 1)—that there are in us spaces and organs of inner perception called ‘minds’ turned out to be a paradoxical intuition which cannot be explained as the result of the exercise of ordinary linguistic skills or common sense but of picture-driven inference.

Such ordinary language analysis will often require us to think of different situations in which we would actually use the expressions at issue, of the different purposes to which they are then put and the different implications they are then taken to carry, depending upon differences in the details of the context. Lack of awareness of relevant detail and context is one of the three conditions we hypothesised to facilitate non-intentional analogical reasoning (Chapter 1.2). By remedying this lack, this Austinian kind of ordinary language analysis can be expected to reduce a bit our propensity to engage in such reasoning, and to put picture-driven interpretations on familiar facts and ways of speaking. Ordinary language analysis can thus establish that certain intuitively compelling claims are in fact paradoxical and at the same time improve our ability to make and accept such findings.

Paradoxical claims are utterly unwarranted, viz. absurd, when (and, of course, only when) they are not supported by at least prima facie sound argument (Chapter 5.5). Where such a claim’s proponents fail either to produce or (like Ayer) to accept any argument adduced in its support, this exposure of its paradoxical nature is enough to show it absurd. Where arguments are produced and accepted for a paradoxical claim, ordinary language analysis can be used in conjunction with logical analysis of these arguments to assess their soundness. It will thus contribute to showing the claim unwarranted, where all the arguments for it are found unsound—as when we found all the classical arguments for the sense-datum doctrine to be circular, or almost circular (Chapter 5).

The insights gained through systematic diagnostic analysis of classical texts provide us with a new framework for making sense of the disconcerting finding that we, competent thinkers, maintain unwarranted claims in the absence of all argument or on the basis of arguments which are circular or almost circular: Unsound cognitive processes which are experimentally documented but of which we are typically unaware may lead us to do just this. We may come to accept and assimilate the refutation of these intuitively compelling ideas through systematic diagnostic analysis of our own work, which shows that this hypothesis actually applies to us: The moment we have, one by one, worked through a whole series of our philosophical intuitions, including perhaps new intuitions generated for the purposes of this inquiry, and the moment we have found, again and again, that our intuitions can be best explained by assuming the occurrence of unsound non-intentional inferences which are independently documented, we will come to form and accept a realistic self-image: The moment we engage in overly abstract reflection, unsound cognitive processes which are not under our direct control and of which we are typically unaware, systematically have us form, and maintain or presuppose, paradoxical beliefs, whose con-flict with other convictions, or among each other, gives rise to a characteristic class of ill-motivated philosophical problems. Conceptualising these processes as constitutive of a kind of intellectual altitude sickness may further help us to distance ourselves from, and disown, these intuitions and intuitive leaps in arguments, and the ill-motivated problems into which they lead us.

The successful use of partially self-gained meta-cognitive insight in cognitive therapy of psychosis warrants the hypothesis that the strategy outlined may significantly weaken our belief, both explicit and implicit, in intuitively compelling philosophical claims, even where these are maintained as a matter of delusion. It should allow us to simultaneously dissolve picture-driven problems by exposing and refuting the assumptions that raise them, and cure the underlying intellectual altitude sickness, namely weaken the spell of the philosophical pictures that render these assumptions intuitive. In brief, the present strategy should let us dissolve particularly tenacious philosophical problems in a way that enables us to actually let them go. We will now further develop this approach by developing ideas first mooted by J.L. Austin (Chapter 8) and Ludwig Wittgenstein (Chapter 9).

To sum up, we have seen that the struggle with processes of non-intentional reasoning and their deliverances, with philosophical pictures and their off-spring, is a particularly central and distinctive part of philosophy. We have found that a proper understanding of how these processes shape philosophical reflection and have us raise philosophical problems is the key to turning a frequently futile struggle into an effective response: It is the key to refuting unwarranted claims and overcoming them even when maintained as a matter of delusion—as will frequently be the case. We have developed first concepts and methods of a cognitive epistemology, which may allow us to gain such a proper understanding. This understanding also vindicates the adoption of new, therapeutic, aims for philosophical work: First and foremost, we should not seek to solve philosophical problems but to cure in us the diseases of the intellect that have us imagine such problems where there are none and to put an end to intellectual pursuits which are largely pointless.

We have thus won through to a new conception of philosophy as it is actually practiced in an important part of the subject and to a fresh vision of philosophy as we ought to practice the subject. The conception is that of philosophy as a—commonly futile—struggle with philosophical pictures and the delusions they engender. The vision is that of a therapy that allows us to put an end to this struggle and to regain or increase our intellectual autonomy: a therapy that is guided by the findings and theories of a scientifically informed cognitive epistemology, in something like the way in which cognitive psychotherapy is guided by findings and theories from various branches of psychology.





8 Linguistic Analysis as Therapy


Austin on ‘Perceptions’




J.L. Austin’s lecture series Sense and Sensibilia is not only the most thorough response to the subject of our last case-study, A.J. Ayer’s Foundations of Empirical Knowledge. It is also one of the most forceful attacks on the sense-datum doctrine of perception in the history of our subject. At the same time, it is a deeply perplexing text. In many respects it is a model of clarity. The points made are individually straightforward, the structure of argument is neither convoluted nor elaborate; the language is plain and devoid of technicalities. But, even so, the text notoriously puzzles its readers and gives rise to two questions that appear to have perplexed even the editor who reconstructed the text from Austin’s lecture notes: ‘Though not an obscure text, it is certainly a rather strange one: what is it for? What does it do?’1 That is: What are Austin’s aims? What is his approach? In his later, primarily ‘positive’ or constructive papers, Austin is often thought to have proceeded to establish philosophical claims by addressing the question, what we should ordinarily say when. Indeed, this approach became associated with his name. We shall see, however, that, at least in these early, primarily ‘negative’ or critical lectures, his aims are therapeutic, and his methods are geared towards acquisition of meta-cognitive insight that allows us to attain those therapeutic aims. In other words, we will see that, in Sense and Sensibilia (henceforward: S&S), Austin took the two turns we have outlined in the previous chapter: the therapeutic turn which had him adopt new aims, and the meta-philosophical turn which led him to a novel approach.

In this chapter, we will establish this claim and thus start providing our fresh vision of philosophy with a pedigree (Section 1). Building on Austin’s ideas, we will come to expose another unsound process of non-intentional inference, independent from the pictorial process we have focused on so far, which may shape philosophical reflection just as systematically and with equally unhelpful results. This process involves seductive verbal fallacies of kinds we are particularly prone to make in philosophical reflection and are then prone to make systematically, against our better knowledge: the unwitting transfer of ordinary implications (Section 2), which may be facilitated by philosophically motivated but unwitting linguistic innovation (Section 3). Independently from all explicit argument, this process may lead thinkers to endorse the Phenomenal Principle (Section 2) and the sense-datum doctrine often derived from it (Section 3). Finally we will sum up how ordinary language analysis can contribute to endeavours in therapeutic philosophy—without running afoul of the familiar objections to ordinary language philosophy, which have led to its perhaps premature demise (Section 4).





1. AUSTIN’S AIMS AND APPROACH




Sense and Sensibilia is a critical examination of the doctrine that we never see or otherwise perceive, or anyhow never directly perceive, material objects but only sense-data (S&S 2). Austin approaches this familiar topic with novel aims and an apparently outrageous approach. He is clear on what he wants to do—and what not:

I am not, then—and this is a point to be clear about from the beginning—going to maintain that we ought to be ‘realists’, to embrace, that is, the doctrine that we do perceive material things (or objects). This doctrine would be no less scholastic and erroneous than its antithesis. The question, do we perceive material things or sense-data, no doubt looks very simple—too simple—but is entirely misleading . . . So we are not to look for an answer to the question . . . What we have above all to do is, negatively, to rid us of such illusions as ‘the argument from illusion’ [for the sense-datum doctrine] . . . —an operation which leaves us, in a sense, just where we began. In a sense—but actually we may hope to learn something positive in the way of a technique for dissolving philosophical worries (some kinds of philosophical worry, not the whole of philosophy). (S&S 3–5)





I.e.: Austin’s chief aim is not to establish any general philosophical claim or theory, but to dissolve the philosophical worry of those who ‘find’ the sense-datum doctrine ‘disturbing’ (S&S 3). Second, he wants to ‘reduce a bit’ the ‘constant repetition of assertions that are . . . not even faintly sensible’ (S&S 5), in particular, the repetition of answers to the ‘entirely misleading’ question, what kind of thing we perceive: He wants to get the philosophers he addresses to give up looking for an answer to it (S&S 4). Finally, he also hopes to learn something ‘about the meanings of some English words . . . which, besides being philosophically very slippery are in their own right interesting’ (S&S 5).

Austin addresses philosophers disturbed by a doctrine they find compelling even though it is, he judges, ‘not even faintly sensible’ for them to maintain. The worries he addresses are hence, in Austin’s judgement, utterly unwarranted. Austin’s chief aim thus is to put an end to unwarranted feelings of intellectual unease and disquiet, that is, to solve a specific emotional problem (in the sense defined in Chapter 6.6). His second aim is that of solving what we called a ‘behavioural problem’: He wants to put an end to attempts to answer an entirely misleading question and accommodate a not even faintly sensible doctrine that purports to answer that question. Question and answer are, Austin thinks, motivated by ‘a wide variety of concealed motives’ (S&S 5). Since Austin accuses nobody of insincerity, these are not motives philosophers conceal but motives concealed from them, motives of which they are unaware. At least as long we are unaware of such concealed motives, they are arguably not under our direct control. In this case they are constitutive of the kind of ‘disabling condition’ that turns unreasonable efforts thus motivated into ‘behavioural problems’, in the strict sense defined (in Chapter 6.6). An enterprise is therapeutic if its chief—though not necessarily sole—aim is to solve emotional or behavioural problems (Chapter 7.4). In Sense and Sensibilia, Austin is squarely engaged in what we called ‘therapeutic philosophy’ and seeks, first and foremost, to solve emotional and behavioural problems that arise in and through philosophical reflection.

In pursuit of his therapeutic aims, Austin employs a striking approach: Instead of examining the stated arguments explicitly adduced to support the disturbing doctrine, he proposes to proceed by ‘exposing a wide variety of concealed motives’ (S&S 5). Above, we have seen that such motives must indeed be more relevant than the arguments stated in the doctrine’s support: All the classical arguments for the sense-datum doctrine simply presuppose or assume from the start either the doctrine itself or equally paradoxical assumptions motivated by it (Chapters 5.1–5.2). Indeed, some of their ablest proponents were prepared to ‘reckon [these arguments] for nothing’ (Berkeley: 3D 200) or reject them as failing to support any factual doctrine (Ayer: FEK 24–25). But, even so, they went on to maintain the doctrine, explicitly (e.g. Berkeley) or implicitly (e.g. Ayer), on the basis of arguments which are patently defective, as they either assume the doctrine (Berkeley, Chapter 5.3) or do not even purport to support it (like Ayer’s ‘purely linguistic’ arguments, Chapters 6.1–6.2).

At least to some extent, Austin is aware of these facts: Mentioning Berkeley, Hume, Russell and Ayer, he judges that the philosophers ‘who have been most adept at working’ the argument from illusion ‘have all themselves felt [their argument] to be somehow spurious’ (S&S 4). And while we established the persistence of Ayer’s belief in the factual sense-datum doctrine he had explicitly rejected, by considering the problems Ayer addresses in the bulk of Foundations (Chapter 6.2), Austin infers from his own analysis of an earlier passage (FEK 11–17) that ‘the apparent sophistication of Ayer’s ‘linguistic’ doctrine really rests squarely on the old Berkeleian . . . ontology of the ‘sensible manifold’’ (S&S 61, cp. 106)—and that Ayer continues to maintain the sense-datum doctrine on the very factual reading he takes to be unsupported by the arguments he accepts for it. Hence it is perfectly reasonable, for us and for Austin, to turn to concealed motives which may push thinkers to maintain the paradoxical doctrine independently from the stated arguments.

In the bulk of his lectures, Austin seeks to ‘unpick, one by one, a mass of seductive (mainly verbal) fallacies’ that may constitute such motives (S&S 4–5), namely fallacies that may lead one to pursue the question, what kind of thing we perceive, and to accept part or all of the sense-datum doctrine that purports to answer it. The most fully spelled out account of such a motivating fallacy that Austin gives is that of a conflation between “illusion” and “delusion” which, he claims, is apt to ‘edge us a little closer towards the position’ of the sense-datum theorist (S&S 25). At first sight, this conflation (if it is made at all) appears to be philosophically irrelevant: After all, it is only in the name or title of the ‘argument from illusion’, but not in the statement of the argument itself, that there is the implication that it proceeds from cases of illusion—which are also described as cases of ‘delusive perception’ (though not necessarily in the course of the argument itself). By discussing this conflation, Austin thus seems to fail to engage with the actual argument (which might as well be renamed, or employ the term “non-veridical” instead of “delusive”). But since Austin seeks to expose fallacies that may lead thinkers to accept part or all of the sense-datum doctrine independently of the arguments stated in its support, these fallacies need not be committed in the course of the argument itself but may rather surface in the surrounding text, including its name and the description the proponent gives elsewhere of the cases from which it proceeds. In three steps, Austin explains why the conflation of “illusion” and “delusion” is a fallacy, why we may commit it, and how it may suggest to us that we perceive something immaterial in those crucial cases, say, when looking at a straight stick that is partially immersed in water and looks bent (S&S 22–25).

First, Austin shows that there are important differences between illusions and delusions, that the concepts of illusion and delusion are not at all the same. To do so, he points out some genuine examples of illusion and delusion, respectively: optical illusions like the Müller-Lyer illusion or the familiar phenomenon of rapidly rotating wheels that look as if they were turning slowly in the other direction; as opposed to delusions of persecution or of grandeur, which ‘are primarily a matter of grossly disordered beliefs . . . and may well have nothing to do with perception’ (S&S 23). These examples he then uses to bring out what are for present purposes ‘the most important differences’ between the two, first and foremost: ‘The term ‘an illusion’ (in a perceptual context) does not suggest that something totally unreal is conjured up—on the contrary, there just is the arrangement of lines on the page . . . [or] the rotating wheels; whereas the term ‘delusion’ does suggest something totally unreal, not really there at all’ (S&S 23).

Second, Austin provides those who found the ‘argument from illusion’ persuasive with reasons to believe that they actually confused the two concepts: He brings out that, and why, this confusion is seductive and points out that it surfaces in the argument from illusion. As he explains, it is easy to confuse ‘illusions’ and ‘delusions’ not only because ‘these terms are often used loosely’, but also because ‘people may have, without making this explicit, different views or theories about the facts of some cases’ (S&S 24), so that one and the same case is described in both terms—e.g., Ayer’s example of the ‘delusive perception’ of a mirage: Some (like Ayer) tend to think mirages are visions conjured up by the crazed brains of thirsty and exhausted travellers (cp. delusion); many others explain mirages as cases of atmospheric refraction (illusion). The confusion thus rendered seductive actually crops up in the ‘argument from illusion’: In its title and its talk of ‘delusive perceptions’, ‘there are two clear implications—(a) that all the cases cited in the argument are cases of illusions; and (b) that illusion and delusion are the same thing’ (S&S 22).

Third, Austin explains how this confusion may have been effective as a motive for endorsing the idea at issue. This might make those who previously acknowledged that they shared the confusion further acknowledge it as one of their motives for endorsing that idea. Austin explains: ‘So long as it is being suggested that the cases paraded for our attention are cases of illusion, there is the implication (from the ordinary use of the word) that there really is something there that we perceive’ (S&S 25). Whereas when we call these cases ‘delusive’, ‘there comes in the very different suggestion of something being conjured up, something unreal or at any rate ‘immaterial’. These two implications taken together may then subtly insinuate that in the cases cited there really is something that we are perceiving, but that this is an immaterial something’ (S&S 25), that, in Ayer’s words, ‘even in the case where what we see is not the real quality of a material thing . . . we are still seeing something . . . [but something that] is not part of any material thing’ (FEK 4).

Having thus exposed a ‘seductive (mainly verbal) fallacy’ as a concealed motive for endorsing this idea, Austin needs to remove the motive by showing up the fallacy for what it is. Already the opening step of the exposure showed that, pace (b), illusions are not to be conflated with delusions. In the sequel (S&S 26–27), Austin examines briefly ‘some of the other cases Ayer lists’ (refraction, perspective, reflections), to show that, pace (a), none of them can be described as a case of illusion. This argument involves two assumptions: (i) Someone qualifies as being ‘under an illusion’ only if he is ‘taken in’; and (ii) something deserves to be termed an ‘illusion’ only if most people are under an illusion when confronted with it. Both assumptions are in line not only with common usage but also with the explicit definitions of “illusion” given by the philosophers Austin targets, such as this defini-tion from Price: ‘An illusory sense-datum of sight or touch is a sense-datum which is such that we tend to take it to be part of the surface of a material object, but if we take it so we are wrong’. 2 Indeed, but for the reference to ‘parts of surfaces’, this definition would capture no other idea than the one the present two assumptions articulate. Perhaps for this reason, Austin goes on to show that this reference should be dropped, as it prevents the definition from fitting exemplary cases of illusion (such as the Müller-Lyer illusion or the Headless Woman), which it is surely meant to accommodate (S&S 27–28). The relevant upshot is that it is presumably from assumptions (i) and (ii) that the philosophers addressed meant to proceed to (a). Austin’s argument against the latter thus engages with his addressees: He exposed and refuted fallacies that constituted (one of) their concealed motives for adopting the specific doctrine that we perceive only immaterial sense-data in cases of ‘delusive perception’.

Thus Austin does not merely seek to show this doctrine unwarranted but tries to explain what led competent thinkers to find it compelling enough to endorse it prior to or independently from the arguments offered in its support. To do so, he does not just point out fallacies that would lead us to endorse the doctrine but also explains why it is natural and tempting for people to make these fallacies and provides evidence that the proponents of the argument from illusion actually made them. We can further develop this approach by looking for specifically philosophical fallacies: We can look for fallacies which we are particularly prone to make in philosophical reflection, and are then prone to make recurrently or systematically.

We will now proceed from exegetical to philosophical development of Austin’s ideas, and proceed from some acute observations of his to an account of two such specifically philosophical fallacies and the processes of non-intentional inference in which they are embedded: We will consider how the unwitting transfer of the implications an expression has in one context, to its use in another context, can motivate the two assumptions on which the argument from illusion is built, including the Phenomenal Principle (Section 2). Then we will consider how the characteristically philosophical quest for generality can facilitate the unwitting transfer of ordinary implications of one term, to certain other terms, and how this transfer can independently motivate that arguments’ conclusion, the sense-datum doctrine in its full splendour (Section 3).





2. UNWITTING TRANSFER OF IMPLICATIONS:

THE PHENOMENAL PRINCIPLE




Different processes of non-intentional reasoning may work together, and in conjunction with a variety of well-documented cognitive distortions. Austin mentions a number of such distortions that tend to recur in philosophical reflection: ‘over-simplification, schematization, and constant obsessive repetition of the same small range of jejune ‘examples’ are . . . far too common to be dismissed as an occasional weakness of philosophers . . . It is essential, here as elsewhere, to abandon old habits of Gleichschaltung, the deeply ingrained worship of tidy-looking dichotomies’ (S&S 3). Together with the pictorial process we have reconstructed (in Chapters 1–2), this latter habit of dichotomous thought has momentous consequences. The moment they have the picture-driven intuitions that have us postulate in us spaces of inner perception called ‘minds’, thinkers are prone to embrace a dichotomy of two realms of perception: We are surrounded by a physical environment in which we perceive public and material objects through our eyes and other external sense-organs; at the same time, we harbour within us a mind in which we perceive private and immaterial things through an inner sense. Everything we can be said to ‘perceive’ has to be perceived either in that external or in this internal space of perception. Whenever thinkers feel compelled to conclude that some object of perception cannot populate the physical space that surrounds us, they therefore infer that it has to be located in the internal space of our mind, instead. Whatever is perceived, but not perceived around us, must be perceived within us.

Such inferences swiftly extended the scope of inner perception. In particular, they had early modern thinkers conclude that we must perceive ideas in our mind not only when we use our wits but also when we use our eyes or ears, palates or noses. Above (in Chapters 1–2), we have reconstructed how non-intentional reasoning driven by perceptual pictures of various intellectual activities and achievements (but also of sensation) led early modern thinkers to take for granted that we perceive ideas or sensations in our mind whenever we think (or have pains). We have also reconstructed how non-intentional reasoning driven by another philosophical picture, the mereological picture of qualities built into the substance/accident-terminology of the period (Chapter 3.1), led early modern philosophers to believe that colours and sounds, tastes and smells do not really exist in the physical bodies we commonly credit with such ‘secondary qualities’ (Chapter 3.2). By the pattern of dichotomous reasoning we just considered, they concluded that since we manifestly see colours, hear sounds, enjoy tastes and smell odours, and these colours etc. manifestly exist, they must be perceived, and exist, in the mind (Chapter 3.3): The colours (etc.) we see (etc.) must be ideas or sensations which physical bodies outside us cause within us when we direct our organs of sense at them (Chapters 3.4–3.5). From its very inception, the modern mind thus served as an asylum for objects of perception evicted from the physical world.

Such dichotomous reasoning is also at the heart of the more recently popular argument from illusion: With the Phenomenal Principle we infer from such truisms as that a round coin looks elliptical when viewed from the side, that we then are, in perception, aware of something, a silvery patch, which actually is elliptical. We thus come to posit a patch of colour that is elliptical, as an object of perception. By Leibniz’s Law, this object is distinct from the—round—coin looked at. Of course it is not identical with any other physical object in our environment, either. In its most popular version, the argument concludes without further ado that the distinct object of perception is the kind of immaterial and private object that is allegedly perceived in the mind. The moment we find it compelling to posit an object of perception that appears to have no place in our physical environment, we infer it must be perceived in our mind, instead. Conversely, it is because we take the existence of this inner asylum or alternative space of perception for granted all along that we have no qualms positing objects of perception which appear to have no place in our physical environment.

What has us posit such objects, in the cases at issue, is the Phenomenal Principle: The champions of the argument from illusion tend to accept without argument, as an obvious truism, that whenever something appears to a subject to possess a certain quality, there is something of which the subject is aware which does possess that quality. We have already noted that this claim is plainly false, when interpreted in line with the ordinary English that most champions of the argument think they then speak (Chapter 6.1): As any dictionary reminds us, “aware of” is commonly used to mean ‘having knowledge or understanding of’. Like the claim that someone knows something, the claim that he is aware of something entails that certain facts obtain; and like the attribution of knowledge, it entails no claim about the ‘experience’ the subject ‘enjoys’. Accordingly, ‘There is something that is elliptical of which you are aware’ would commonly be understood to imply that there is in fact something elliptical lying around to be seen or felt, and that you know this (Chapter 6.1). But of course there need not be anything elliptical lying around to be seen when something looks elliptical to us, and you know that there is nothing elliptical—but only a round coin—lying around to be seen or felt in the case from which the argument from illusion proceeds. So why do we find it intuitively compelling to go along with the Phenomenal Principle and conclude that we then see something, some patch of colour, which is elliptical and therefore distinct from the round coin on the table?

This may be due to another kind of non-intentional inference that may complement the non-intentional analogical inferences we have focused on so far and can be plausibly hypothesised to recur in philosophical reflection: the unwitting transfer of ordinary implications. While he did not use these terms, Austin seems to have spotted the general phenomenon and some of the pertinent inferences. He notes that linguistic expressions carry different implications in different contexts: ‘How [a word] is intended and taken will depend on further facts about the occasion of utterance; the words themselves imply nothing either way’ (S&S 40). But even though ‘what is meant and what can be inferred (if anything) can be decided only by examining the full circumstances in which the words are used’, philosophers are prone merely ‘to examine the words themselves’ (S&S 41). As a result, they may unwittingly credit their words with implications they only have in other contexts and draw unsound inferences. Earlier, Austin suggested that the inadvertent mixing of the implications of different words—such as “illusion” and “delusion”—can facilitate the ready acceptance of false claims (S&S 25, cp. Section 1). The same thing may happen when we inadvertently mix the implications one and the same word has in different contexts.

Let’s develop this idea. A variety of factors can make us lose sight of the relevant contextual features: When philosophising, we are prone to generalise quickly and extensively and to proceed by abstract argument developed without reference to specific cases. These propensities make us ignore or neglect those contextual features that would determine what implications our words should be taken to have. Where we come to ignore or neglect these features, we may spontaneously and unwittingly fall back on patterns of inference that exploit those implications that our words have in contexts which are particularly dominant or common, and therefore shape our habits of spontaneous or ‘automatic’ inference. When speaking in a less common context to whose features we are oblivious, we may spontaneously leap to conclusions licensed by the implications our words have in such more common or dominant contexts—regardless or whether or not they have them in the context at issue. We may thus unwittingly transfer the implications our words have in dominant contexts also to further contexts of utterance in which they do not have them. Such unwitting transfer of implications has us leap to unwarranted conclusions and may even have us adopt conclusions that are patently false.

This may account for our ready acceptance of the Phenomenal Principle. When we grant that we are aware of, or see, an elliptical silvery patch when viewing a round coin sideways, we regard this as an obvious fact stated in ordinary English. In ordinary English, we sometimes use talk of ‘patches’ and ‘spots’ of colour in order to pick out physical objects by the way they look to us (from here, now). We typically do this when we cannot tell (from here, now) what kind of object it is we are seeing: On a mountain tour, we might point down into the valley and ask, ‘What is the small reddish spot over there? Might that be our car?’ As we approach it, we can say that ‘the spot grows bigger’ and that it finally ‘turns out to be our car’ (or another physical object). Here spot-talk is used throughout to refer to physical objects: The ‘small reddish spot’ is a physical object that looks small and reddish (to the speaker, at the time of utterance). When we use the phrase thus, there is no implication that we see anything that does not merely look small and reddish (from here, now) but actually possesses these two sensible qualities. We can hence use these phrases when we know full well that nothing actually answering to the description is around to be seen, and speak of an ‘elliptical silvery patch’ when we full well know that the only thing to be seen on the table is a round object which merely looks elliptical to us now—the phrase “the elliptical silvery speck” is then used to refer to that round physical object; the speck is the coin.

The dominant use of adjectives like “elliptical”, “small” and “silvery” is of course very different. First and foremost, we use these adjectives to attribute those shapes, sizes and colours to whatever the nouns thus quali-fied stand for. When I say ‘I have to remove a small speck of grime from the table’, I clearly imply, and you may infer, that there is something—the speck of grime—that actually is small. The moment we ignore or fail to think of the less frequent use that allows us to employ the phrase “elliptical silvery patch” in setting out the argument from illusion, we will unwittingly fall back on the pattern of inference that is licensed by this implication from the dominant use of the relevant adjectives, and leap to the conclusion that there then is something, a silvery patch, that actually is elliptical. It is when we make the special use previously discussed and then unwittingly transfer to it the implications of the dominant use of the pertinent adjectives that we go along with the Phenomenal Principle and find it natural and compelling to first speak of our seeing an elliptical silvery patch and then infer that there is something we see with our eyes, this patch, which is elliptical and therefore distinct from the round coin on the table—or any other physical object in our environment.

This conclusion is of course false. For sure, we commonly see with our eyes many things which we would hesitate to describe as ‘physical objects’: rainbows, images in mirrors, pictures on the movie screen, etc. But no such non-physical object of perception is around to be seen in the case at issue, on the table we are meant to look at. Nothing elliptical and distinct from the coin is around to be seen then and there, by you and me. Arguably, we readily accept the conclusion only because we accept all along the picture-driven conception of an alternative private realm of perception, in which we can place the elliptical object of perception we have now posited. Indeed, the common formulation of the Phenomenal Principle suggests that the latter is placed in the mind immediately, rather than as the result of an extra inference: When philosophers endorse that assumption about what we are ‘aware of in perception’, they typically think of ‘experiences’ we ‘enjoy’ in perception as the things we are ‘aware of’, and take ‘experiences’ to be things we behold in the privacy of our mind (rather than joint trips, public encounters or other ‘experiences’ in the pre-philosophical sense of the term).

To sum up, the habit of dichotomous thinking, the picture-driven conception of the mind as a space of inner perception, and the unwitting transfer of implications from a dominant to a quite special use of adjectives may work together and have us go along with the Phenomenal Principle. The assumption on which the first half of the argument from illusion is built is rendered intuitively compelling by different kinds of non-intentional inference that are particularly likely to occur the moment thinkers lose themselves in overly general or abstract reflection—as frequently happens in philosophy.

The unwitting transfer of ordinary implications might also facilitate acceptance of the assumption on which the second half of the argument from illusion rests. This assumption too is wild and, even so, typically maintained without argument: There is, we are told, no qualitative difference between ‘delusive’ and ‘veridical perceptions’. With the further assumption that qualitatively identical perceptions must be of the same kind of object, this allows the argument to generalise: If immaterial sense-data are what we are aware of, in perception, in the ‘delusive’ cases, they are what we are thus aware of in all cases of perception. Austin refutes both assumptions (S&S 44–54) and breaks the first up into these assumptions about the specific cases on which arguments from illusion are typically based: Seeing a round coin from the side, so that it looks bent, is just like seeing an elliptical piece of silver. Seeing a straight stick partially immersed in water, so that it looks bent, is just like seeing a bent stick. Etc. These assumptions are patently false. As Austin points out, ‘it is simply not true to say . . . that seeing a stick refracted in water is exactly like seeing a bent stick’ (S&S 49): Of course the straight stick only looks bent when partially immersed in water. But then it does not look like a bent stick partially immersed in water—which would, in these circumstances, look a different shape. So the most we can say is that a straight stick partially immersed in water looks like a bent stick that is not immersed in water. But then the two experiences are not alike at all: In one case, the stick is immersed in water, in the other it is not, and this makes for a quite noticeable difference, since water is not invisible (cp. S&S 53).

We might be led to accept these otherwise patently false assumptions by a confusion of two different ways in which the expressions “like”, “looks like” and “looks” may be meant and taken (S&S 40). Consider the sentence “They look like ants.” When I say this of some small animals scurrying around at my feet, I mean that they look exactly like ants, that going by their looks I think them ants (or would do so, had I not been warned of similar insects populating the area). When I say this of people I watch from a high tower, however, I mean no such thing. Rather, I then mean that people seen from this great distance look rather like ants—as big as ants— seen from the sort of distance at which one normally sees ants—say, about six feet (S&S 40–41). Mutatis mutandis, the same goes for “They look small”: In some contexts, I imply that going by their looks I think they are small, in others I do not. These two ways in which “looks (like)” may be meant and taken are easy to confuse in philosophising, where thinkers tend not to examine the full circumstances in which the words are used. When discussing the ‘delusive’ perception of the stick partially immersed in water we may thus fail to ‘remember . . . what sort of situation we are dealing with’ (cp. S&S 42) and spontaneously take the perfectly true sentence “The stick looks bent” to imply that the stick looks exactly like, and might well be mistaken for, a bent stick. In this way, the spontaneous transfer of implications “looks” has in dominant or prominent contexts, to a rarer context, may contribute to our acceptance of an assumption we would see through the moment we bothered to attend to the ‘highly significant attendant circumstances’ of the special case at issue (cp. S&S 54).

To sum up: By developing ideas mooted by Austin, we have identified a second kind of spontaneous or non-intentional inference which is particularly likely to occur under conditions frequently met in philosophical reflection and which may complement the non-intentional analogical inferences we have explored previously: the unwitting transfer of implications an expression ordinarily has in a dominant use or context, to another use or context in which it does not have them. Such unwitting transfer of implications can facilitate ready acceptance of the two dubious assumptions on which the successive main steps of the argument from illusion rely.





3. UNWITTING LINGUISTIC INNOVATION:

THE SENSE-DATUM DOCTRINE




We may also unwittingly transfer ordinary implications of one term onto another term, which does not have them. Austin suggested that this may happen where people hold different views about a case without acknowledging their difference of view, and thus come to apply different descriptions or terms (say, “illusion” and “delusion”), with different implications, to the same case (say, mirages), and regard the terms as equivalent (Section 1).

Earlier, we identified another, specifically philosophical, motivation for such spontaneous transfer of implications (Chapter 4.1): Wanting to argue at a very general level, philosophers often give a general or generic use to familiar terms which ordinarily do not have it. Without noticing the novelty, philosophers of perception thus started to use the verb “to perceive” as shorthand, to talk at one go about seeing, hearing, tasting, smelling and feeling—without realising that “to perceive” has (in several constructions) implications of recognition and knowledge which “to see” and its cognates do not share (in the same constructions). You recall: I can be said to ‘see’ Fred in the crowd, even when I fail to recognise my former school-mate. The explosion of the secret ammunitions depot can be ‘heard’ by anyone within ten miles, not only by the few soldiers who know what the loud bang is. Etc. By contrast, it is unidiomatic to say ‘I perceived Fred straightaway but recognised him only later’: The ordinary implication of ‘S perceives x’ is that the perceiving subject recognises x and knows who or what it is he sees or hears. When we start to use “to perceive” as a generic term for seeing, hearing, etc. but continue to leap to conclusions licensed only by the ordinary implications it does not share with “to see” and its cognates, we unwittingly transfer those epistemic implications to the latter. (If x sees y, x perceives y. If x perceives y, then x recognises y. Therefore: If x sees y, then x recognises y.) Accordingly, many philosophers of perception have regarded seeing and hearing, in the ordinary senses of these terms, as epistemic achievements, involving recognition or knowledge of what is seen or heard. In this way, unwitting linguistic innovation can lead to equally unwitting transfer of ordinary implications.

This transfer turns seeing and hearing into epistemic achievements which require a knowledge that can be acquired either through the mere use of the senses or only with the help of reason and inference. This conception motivated Berkeley’s introduction of a new ‘strict’ sense of perception-verbs, which singles out the contribution the senses make to these epistemic achievements: ‘In truth and strictness’ we see or hear or otherwise perceive by sense, rather than reason, only what we perceive without making an inference (Chapter 4.1). This imposes two requirements on seeing and its cognates: To qualify ‘in truth and strictness’ as seeing an F, a subject has to know that it is an F he is seeing, and has to acquire this knowledge without any inference, automatic or other. Ordinary English fails to impose either of these two requirements; in particular, it does not even require possession of the kind of knowledge at issue: The visitor to the aviary can be said to ‘see’ the Hammerkop in front of him already prior to looking at the sign that indicates the species he never heard of before. Berkeley and others thus change the topic, from seeing to completely different achievements, while imagining they are merely rendering ordinary talk more ‘strict’ or precise. Unwitting linguistic innovation involving unwitting transfer of ordinary implications may motivate deliberate linguistic innovation which looks less radical than it is.

When engaged in philosophical reflection about a given topic, we may unwittingly press various different, if related, terms into new, more general or generic uses. Thus, philosophers who want to speak and reason at one go about all five ‘modes of perception’ often also want to speak and reason at one go about how the things we see, hear, smell, taste or feel look or sound or smell or taste or feel to us. They then usually take the verbs “to seem” and “to appear” for the job, without being aware of putting them to any new use. They thus take for granted that also in ordinary parlance the two verbs mean pretty much the same, namely something like “to look” but more general, namely ‘to look-or-sound-or-taste-or smell-or-feel’ to a subject.

This, however, is quite wrong: As Austin brings out, the ‘root ideas behind the uses’ of these three expressions in ordinary language are quite distinct: “Looks” is used quite simply to comment on the looks of things. “Appears” is used with reference to certain special circumstances, whereas “seems” makes an implicit reference to certain (less than conclusive) evidence. Austin brings this out by considering three sets of examples (S&S 36–37):





He looks guilty—he has the look of a guilty man.

He appears guilty—‘I quite agree that, when he’s prevaricating over all those searching questions about what he did with the money, he appears guilty, but most of the time his demeanour (not just ‘his looks’) is innocence itself.’

He seems guilty—‘On the evidence we have heard so far, he certainly seems guilty.’ [The evidence of course suggests that he is guilty, not that he seems guilty. And it is less than conclusive; otherwise, I would have said less cautiously, ‘He is guilty.’]

‘The hill looks steep’—it has the look of a steep hill.

‘The hill appears steep’—when you look at it from down here.

‘The hill seems steep’—to judge by the fact that we have had to change gear twice.

‘She looks chic’—straightforward enough.

‘She appears (to be) chic’—in unsophisticated, provincial circles.

‘She seems (to be) chic’—from these photographs, from what they have told me about her, etc.



When we start to use “appears” and “seems” as generic terms for ‘looks-or-sounds-or-smells-or-tastes-or-feels’ but, failing to notice the novelty, continue to leap to conclusions licensed by their ordinary implications, we will hence again unwittingly transfer ordinary implications from supposedly generic to a supposedly merely more specific term which does not have them: Given the new generic sense of “seems”, “X looks F” entails “X seems F”. “X seems F” ordinarily implies that there is some less than conclusive evidence that suggests that X is F. Accordingly, we may in philosophical reflection take “X looks F” to imply that there is some such less than conclusive evidence. Similarly: Given the new sense of “appears”, “X looks F” implies “X appears F”. “X appears F” ordinarily implies that X looks F under certain special circumstances and suggests the possibility that in fact it is not F. Accordingly, we may, in philosophical reflection, take “X looks F” to imply that it does so under certain special circumstances and to suggest the possibility that it is not in fact F.

Such transfer of ordinary implications may be simultaneously facilitated by the philosophical failing we considered in the previous section, viz. by philosophers’ not uncommon failure to pay sufficient attention to the specific circumstances of terms’ use. Such attention is particularly necessary where terms share implications in some, but not all contexts. The use of “seems”, for instance, often involves implicit reference to certain evidence, but not always (‘The next three days seemed like one long nightmare’), while the use of “looks” usually does not involve such reference, but occasionally does. For example, ‘that somebody looks ill may be the evidence on which we could also remark that he seems to be ill’ (S&S 38). But “looks” is not used to refer to any evidence ‘either when the way something looks is wholly inadequate evidence (it would be rash to say that her jewellery seems to be genuine just because it looks genuine); or when the way something looks is wholly conclusive (what more must she do to be chic than to look chic?); or, for that matter, when something’s really being such-and-such is not in question at all (‘He looks like his father’—but no one is going to say that he seems to be his father)’ (S&S 38). Therefore ‘just what is meant and what can be inferred (if anything) can be decided only by examining the full circumstances in which the word is used’ (S&S 41). The moment we pay insufficient attention to these circumstances or reason from insufficiently developed examples which leave those circumstances unspecified, we are therefore prone to treat these different expressions as more or less interchangeable—like Ayer, who puts his terms exactly the wrong way ‘round when indicating the case from which his argument from illusion proceeds: ‘A stick that normally appears straight looks bent when it is seen in water’ (FEK 3). When we do this, we will take these terms to always share certain implications which they in fact have in common only in specific circumstances.

These unwitting transfers of ordinary implications may lead us to apparently paradoxical conclusions and render these conclusions intuitively plausible prior to all explicit argument. In the present case, it may lead us to endorse, first, an unduly sceptical conclusion and, second, the sense-datum doctrine of perception. Suppose we take “X looks F” to imply (i) that there is some less than conclusive evidence that X is F and (ii) that it looks F only under certain special circumstances (with some suggestion that in fact it is not F). Then we will take the looks of F to be potentially deceptive in any situation in which we apply it: Even under the most favourable circumstances, we may not assume that X actually is F when it looks F; even then we have only less than conclusive evidence that X is F and cannot know that the thing in front of us is an F by simply having a look there and then. We always have to work this out, perhaps—as many philosophers, again including Ayer, suggest—on the basis of how the thing looks (and feels) under various different conditions (cp. FEK 267–270).

A moment ago, we have considered a similar transfer of ordinary implications, namely from “perceiving” to “seeing” and “hearing”. This had us take for granted that to see or hear an F we have to know that it is an F, and led us on to say that we strictly speaking see or hear an F only if this knowledge is acquired by exercising these senses there and then, that we strictly speaking perceive an F by sense only when we can acquire this knowledge without having to work anything out by reason. The present conclusion thus implies that we can never, strictly speaking, see or hear an F or perceive by sense any physical object (under this or any other name or description). All we can, strictly speaking, perceive by sense, are the looks or appearances of physical objects, which may constitute less than conclusive evidence for those objects’ being what they seem to be. (Dichotomous thinking may then have us conclude that since our physical environment only contains the physical objects that appear to us thus but not the appearances that are what we strictly speaking perceive by sense, these appearances must be perceived in that other space of perception, our mind.) To sum up: By unwittingly giving first “perceive” and then “seem” and “appear” new generic uses, while inadvertently transferring their ordinary implications to supposedly merely more specific terms, we may arrive at a version of the sense-datum doctrine of perception.

Since we have unwittingly burdened “seeing” and “hearing” with epistemic implications they do not have in ordinary language, the conclusion that we do not, strictly speaking, see or hear physical objects or events, is quite compatible with the common-sense conviction that we see or hear coins, sticks, coaches and explosions, in the ordinary sense of those verbs. But when we fail to notice the fundamental and unwitting linguistic innovation that burdens “seeing” etc. with implications of knowledge and recognition, we will interpret the conclusion as being at odds with this piece of common sense. Thus unwitting linguistic innovation going with equally unwitting transfer of ordinary implications may lead us to a claim that strikes us as both intuitively compelling and paradoxical.

We have thus identified a second process of non-intentional inference, distinct from the pictorial process with its analogical inferences, which is facilitated by common and characteristic features of philosophical reflec-tion and can render apparently paradoxical claims intuitively compelling independently from, and already prior to, explicit argument: The quest for generality may have us engage in unwitting linguistic innovation, as we put familiar terms to more general or generic uses whose novelty we fail to realise. This and insufficient attention to relevant context may have us transfer ordinary implications from some terms or statements to others, which do not have them. We have reconstructed a sequence of such non-analogical non-intentional inferences that provides an alternative route to the sense-datum doctrine, namely an alternative to the classical early modern route which proceeds through picture-driven inferences and via the view that only primary qualities really exist in physical bodies.

This alternative route may have led Ayer to adopt his terminology of ‘direct perception’ and the factual sense-datum doctrine he could not shed. To define ‘direct perception’, Ayer stipulates that ‘S directly perceives x’ entails (i) that x really exists and (ii) that it really has whatever properties it appears (to S) to have (FEK 61), and that all things that can be directly perceived are to be called ‘sense-data’ (FEK 59) (cp. Chapter 6.1). After noting the paucity of the reasons Ayer gives for introducing the first of these terms (which we examined in Chapter 6.3), Austin suggests that Ayer’s ‘real motive’ for its introduction is the desire to produce statements (presumably, perceptual judgements) which are incorrigible (S&S 103): It is tempting to think that, if the sense-data I directly perceive are guaranteed to exist and be what they appear, then I cannot go wrong in my perceptual judgements, as long as I content myself with judgements about sense-data.
