But Ayer explicitly rejects at any rate the most influential motivation for seeking incorrigible statements, namely the quest for certainty (FEK 45, cp. Chapter 6.2). Possibly, he rather sought, like Berkeley, to single out those things which we can perceive by using only our senses and not our reason. Possibly, he too transferred the epistemic implications of “to perceive” to “to see” and its cognates, assumed that when things must be what they appear we can recognise them by just having a look, whereas we must work out what they are when appearances may be deceptive, and leaped to the conclusion that ‘in truth and strictness’ we perceive by sense, and without automatic or other inference, only those things which are guaranteed to be elliptical when they look elliptical, i.e., to which the distinction between being F and merely looking F does not apply: the elliptical silvery patches he resolved to call ‘sense-data’.3

This would be consistent with the fact that he presupposes in core passages that only what is directly perceived is in fact observed (e.g. FEK 231, cp. Chapter 6.2)—and not inferred. Other passages suggest that Ayer also conflated “appears” and “looks” (e.g. FEK 3), and establish that he maintained the apparent implication that we always have to work out what material objects we are seeing—and hence cannot ever perceive such objects directly (e.g. FEK 267–270). The sequence of non-intentional inferences we have outlined might well account for Ayer’s adoption of, and his strikingly persistent implicit belief in, the sense-datum doctrine. I have to leave it to the reader to follow up this hypothesis, with Ayer’s text in hand.





4. PROSPECTS FOR ORDINARY LANGUAGE ANALYSIS




By their friends and foes alike, ordinary language philosophers have frequently, and often rightly, been credited with the aim of showing philosophical claims false or meaningless by showing they are inconsistent with common sense, as implicit in ordinary language, or by demonstrating that they involve deviations from ordinary use.4 However, many common-sense convictions have been refuted by scientific inquiry, many others still stand to be refuted thus, and ordinary language ‘can everywhere be supplemented and improved upon and superseded’—as Austin himself pointed out. 5 This objection appears particularly forceful in the case of Sense and Sensibilia, which addresses a philosopher who develops his arguments and theories in technical language explicitly introduced for the purpose. Even philosophers who admired the brilliance of ordinary language analysis at its best have therefore taken a rather dim view of its ultimate philosophical relevance.6 This raises what is presumably the central methodological puzzle about ordinary language analysis: How can such analysis lead not only to insight about pre-philosophical talk but contribute to the resolution of philosophical problems?

In this book, we have developed a new conception of the nature and genesis of an extensive and important class of philosophical problems. This conception warrants a new approach to the problems at issue, within which ordinary language analysis can be put to novel uses. These uses do not require us to assume that common-sense convictions must be true or that philosophers’ utterances must be meaningless the moment they deviate from established usage. And these uses are apt to contribute significantly to resolving and overcoming philosophical problems.

At the end of the previous chapter, we saw how ordinary language analysis can contribute to the dissolution of ill-motivated problems, that is, to refuting our reasons for raising them and showing we are justified in dropping their pursuit. It can contribute by exposing the paradoxical character of intuitively compelling assumptions that raise those problems. From this character we must not infer that these claims are false or meaningless. But we may infer that their proponents are under an obligation to support them with explanation or argument, and that they are unwarranted when their proponents fail to provide or accept any relevant explanation or argument—as is frequently the case (Chapter 7.6).

In this chapter, we have seen how ordinary language analysis can make a second major contribution and help us to actually overcome what we have seen reason to abandon. Such analysis can help us reconstruct unsound processes of non-intentional inference that may complement the kind of non-intentional analogical reasoning we have previously considered. Where those reconstructions offer the best available explanation of why certain philosophers maintain certain claims—which may happen, for instance, where philosophers do not provide or accept any relevant arguments for claims in strong need of argumentative support—we may conclude that those processes actually drove these thinkers to maintain these claims and raise whatever ill-motivated problems the latter engender. Ordinary language analysis can thus complement the sort of diagnostic analysis we have developed in the bulk of this book, to uncover and reconstruct a wider range of non-intentional reasoning shaping philosophical reflection. In this way, it can help us acquire the kind of meta-cognitive insight or self-understanding that we have seen apt to weaken delusional beliefs and mitigate the underlying intellectual altitude sickness (Chapter 7.5). Ordinary language analysis can thus contribute to therapeutic efforts that liberate us from philosophical delusions and the groundless worries and pointless efforts these delusions engender by clashing with common sense, familiar facts, or among each other. By exposing some of the concealed motives that may stealthily shape our philosophical reflection, the analysis of ordinary language can help us to liberate ourselves from bad questions, including deeply disquieting questions that have mesmerised generations of philosophers.





9 Self-Reflection as Therapy


Wittgenstein on Understanding




Our new account of philosophical problems and reflection vindicates a two-fold reorientation of philosophical work: the ‘meta-philosophical turn’ which has us adopt a novel approach and the ‘therapeutic turn’ which has us pursue new ultimate aims (Chapter 7). The latter ‘turn’ motivates another reorientation of philosophical work, at the level of addressees. Where picture-driven reasoning or other ‘concealed motives’ of which we are unaware have us imagine philosophical problems where there are none, and worry and toil needlessly, the only thing we can reasonably do is put an end to such unreasonable worries and endeavours. We thus turn from attempts to solve philosophical problems which are ill-motivated or illusory to efforts to solve emotional and behavioural problems which are genuine. By and large, the personal problems that matter most to us are our own. The adoption, for philosophical work, of the new therapeutic aims of solving emotional or behavioural problems thus motivates, though it does not necessitate, the turn from a third- to a first-person focus: The moment we seriously turn to the practice of philosophy as therapy, we are likely to find ourselves the most worthwhile patients. First and foremost, therapeutic philosophy will be practiced as self-therapy.

We have seen that, by and large, the predicaments of one philosopher tend to resemble those of others: Our worries and efforts are as similar as the philosophical pictures that guide us, the intuitions they generate, and the problems and questions these intuitions engender. By solving the emotional and behavioural problems engendered by my intuitions I can hence show you not only what kind of thing you need to do to solve yours, in the way in which the successful self-therapy of a cognitive therapist suffering from depression can show another sufferer what kind of treatment might help him too. Rather, my efforts may sometimes demonstrate almost the exact treatment you too require. Self-therapy does not even get close to implying inter-subjective irrelevance.

But, even so, the turn from others to oneself is probably even harder for many philosophers to countenance than the two-fold reorientation of philosophical work we have advocated so far: One may well accept the approach of dissolving and overcoming ill-motivated problems by gaining meta-cognitive insight, and may even adopt as one’s paramount goal the therapeutic aims of solving the emotional and behavioural problems of those worried by such ‘problems’, but still balk at the idea that the approach should be applied, first and foremost, to oneself (rather than a philosophical opponent) and that one should, first and foremost, treat one’s own emotional and behavioural problems rather than help others with theirs. Here, more than anywhere else, we need to adapt and employ the kind of move cognitive therapists employ to facilitate engagement, and provide a respected model (cp. Chapter 7.6): To get as much as a fair hearing for the idea of philosophy as self-therapy, we arguably need to show that a philosopher whose work is widely accepted as important and relevant has practised philosophy in this way.

We therefore now turn to Ludwig Wittgenstein and, more specifically, the ‘chapter’ on understanding that forms a core part of his later main work, the Philosophical Investigations (henceforward: PI). We will argue that the later Wittgenstein advocated and practiced philosophy as therapy (Section 1), and was his own patient: We will identify a pertinent habit of non-intentional inference or ‘urge to misunderstand’ (Section 2) and show that Wittgenstein exposed this urge in his own thought, i.e., diagnosed himself with this ‘disease of the understanding’ (Section 3). Then we will reconstruct how this ‘disease’ led him to raise the problem about sudden understanding around which that ‘chapter’ of the Investigations revolves (Section 4). We will find that this problem fits Wittgenstein’s conception of nature and genesis of philosophical problems, and that this conception vindicates his adoption of a therapeutic aim (Section 5). Finally, we will consider how he pursues this aim in response to the problem about sudden understanding; we will find that Wittgenstein employs an approach which is geared towards the acquisition of meta-cognitive insight (Section 6). By showing that, why, and how, Wittgenstein took the therapeutic and the meta-philosophical turn, we will obtain a model for philosophical self-therapy. By considering the results of his self-analyses, we will also extend our case-studies on non-intentional reasoning in philosophy beyond members of the empiricist tradition, and from problems in metaphysics and the philosophies of mind and perception to problems in the philosophy of language: Non-intentional reasoning shapes philosophical reflection not only in the areas in which we have been moving up to now or in the one tradition of philosophy we have studied so far. It demonstrably occurs rather more widely.





1. WITTGENSTEIN’S THERAPEUTIC AIM




Wittgenstein’s Philosophical Investigations is perplexing in the same way as Austin’s Sense and Sensibilia, and perhaps even more so. While written in plain German, this text too has left its readers wondering what it is for, and what it does. For a start, it explicitly repudiates various conventional aims of philosophical work:

[W]e may not advance any kind of theory. There must not be anything hypothetical in our considerations. We must do away with all explanation, and description alone must take its place. (PI 109)





That is: Wittgenstein does not seek to construct any theories or offer any explanations (cp. PI 126). Nor is it his aim to establish the truth of any hypotheses or non-trivial or potentially controversial claims (cp. PI 128, 599).

These statements of non-intent are consistent with Wittgenstein’s actual proceeding. While commentators guided by more conventional ideas about the point and purpose of philosophical work tended to read substantive claims, or even philosophical theories, into various passages of the Investigations,1 an unbiased and closer look quickly reveals that Wittgenstein either did not endorse the claims attributed to him or did not pursue the pertinent investigations with the chief aim of establishing them—neither on a substantive nor on a truistic reading. Take the closely linked investigations of understanding and rule-following that lie at the heart of the Investigations (PI 138–242). This long stretch of text contains only four (!) remarks that even appear to state substantive philosophical claims, 2 and appear to state, between them, the grand total of three distinct claims, which we now consider in turn.

The first is the so-called ‘use theory of meaning’, i.e., the claim that the meaning of an expression consists in its use. Wittgenstein is usually taken to advance this claim already in an earlier passage, with the words: ‘For a large class of cases of the use of the word “meaning”—though not for all cases of its use, one can explain the word thus: the meaning of a word is its use in language’ (PI 43). This formulation is not only remarkably cautious (the italics are Wittgenstein’s), but also stops noticeably short of advancing any claim about ‘what the meaning of a word is’ or ‘consists in’. It is not ‘about word meaning’ but about one of different actual uses of the word “meaning”: It contents itself with the claim that in many (though not all) cases in which we speak of a word’s ‘meaning’, one can explain what we are saying by paraphrasing it as a statement about the word’s use in language. When the idea that ‘the meaning of a word is its use’ is taken up again in the investigations now at issue, in PI 138, it is not asserted but constitutes the antecedent of a conditional employed in setting up an objection Wittgenstein proceeds to attack. When taken up again, in PI 197, it is rewritten as a premise of the refuted objection. Accordingly (to take another famous, if apparently trivial, remark supposedly stating what the meaning of a word is), Wittgenstein does not assert that ‘the meaning of a word is what an explanation of its meaning explains’; rather, this sentence is merely being mentioned, enclosed in double quotes, and explicitly interpreted as expressing not a claim but this heuristic principle: ‘if you want to understand the use of the word “meaning”, look for what are called “explanations of meaning”’ (PI 560). There is no textual basis for attributing to him any answer, substantive or trivial, to the philosophical question ‘What is the meaning of a word?’ Indeed, there is every reason to believe that Wittgenstein rejected the very question as the expression of a groundless disquiet, in line with his general view that ‘[t]he first mistake we encounter in a philosophical investigation is . . . always the question itself’ (MS 165: 55).3

The next pertinent remark states: ‘In the sense in which there are processes (including mental processes) which are characteristic of understanding, understanding is not a mental process’ (PI 154d). Depending upon what the ‘sense’ adverted to is, this may well boil down to an entirely uncontroversial and trivial observation. More importantly, this and the third, apparently more substantive, pertinent remark—‘And hence also ‘obeying a rule’ is a practice’ (PI 202)—were inserted into the text considerably later than the discussion surrounding them: Paragraph 154d was included neither in the first nor the second typescript version of the surrounding discussion, but only inserted in handwriting into the latter typescript (TS 239). And section 202 was not included in the first version of the discussion from which its claim is inferred (the so-called ‘Intermediary Version’).4 This can be explained in a way consistent with Wittgenstein’s meta-philosophical statements: The present investigations at first did not contain these claims because these investigations are not directed at establishing their truth, substantive or trivial, but at different ends, and were subsequently included when Wittgenstein came to believe that their appreciation can contribute to attaining these different ends.

In line with the potentially unhelpful view that ‘[i]f this book is written as it should be, then everything I say must be easily understandable, indeed, trivial, but it should be difficult to understand why I say it’ (MS 117: 140–41), 5 the Investigations remain largely silent about what these different ends or aims might be.6 In his earlier Big Typescript, however, Wittgenstein clearly identifies a guiding aim. Here, in his most extensive discussion of philosophical method, he stresses again and again the disquiet or uneasiness (‘Beunruhigung’ or ‘Unruhe’: BT 409, 415, 416, 421, 431) engendered in us by wrong analogies (BT 409), ‘disorder in our concepts’ (BT 421) and (misleading) expressions (BT 416). Wittgenstein’s declared chief aim is the removal of such unwarranted disquiet:

As I do philosophy, its entire [!] task is to shape expression in such a way that certain disquietudes disappear. (BT 421)





He seeks freedom from those unwarranted but ‘deep disquietudes’ that arise through various forms of ‘misinterpretation’ (PI 111a2), so as to achieve a certain peace of mind:

Thoughts at peace. That is the [!] goal someone who philosophises longs for. (CV 50) 7





Since his proceeding in the investigations we are studying is consistent with his explicit rejection of more conventional aims, we should take these statements perfectly seriously.8

Unwarranted but distressing or otherwise disabling emotions are constitutive of emotional problems (Chapter 6.6). In other words, Wittgenstein’s chief goal is the therapeutic aim of solving emotional problems. Accordingly, he conceives of the problems he seeks to solve as consisting in the feelings of disquiet of particular subjects, rather than in the philosophical questions or puzzles they are disquieted by or worry about: 9 ‘The problems arising through a misinterpretation of our forms of language . . . are deep disquietudes’ (PI 111a1–111a2, my italics). Indeed, even more to the point: ‘What we [i.e., I—editorial plural] call “philosophical problems” are particular personal disquiets [besondere individuelle Beunruhigungen]’ (MS 115: 35, my italics).

Wittgenstein puts these emotional problems down to ‘diseases of the understanding’ and maintains that ‘the philosopher’ needs to cure such diseases in himself:

The philosopher is someone who has to cure in himself many diseases of the understanding, before he can arrive at the notions of common sense. (CV 50)





A disease, you recall (from Chapter 6.6), is a somatic or psychological process, cognitive or other, that is not under our direct control and makes us physically or mentally ill, for instance by bringing about emotional problems. Wittgenstein puts the emotional problems at issue down to ‘urges to misunderstand’: These disquietudes ‘aris[e] through a misinterpretation of our forms of language’ (PI 111a1) and these ‘philosophical problems’—i.e. personal disquiets—‘are to be solved . . . by mak[ing] us recognise [the workings of our language]: in despite of an urge to misunderstand them’ (PI 109a8). ‘Urges’ are propensities which are not under our direct control. When they bring about groundless disquiet, urges to misunderstand thus qualify as diseases. Arguably, they are constitutive of the ‘diseases of the understanding’ Wittgenstein thinks a philosopher has to cure in himself. We shall presently see that Wittgenstein attempts to cure intellectual diseases he diagnoses in himself. Arguably, he wants to put an end to certain feelings of disquiet that bother him, i.e., to solve his own emotional problems. To show that Wittgenstein was indeed engaged in self-therapy, we shall now develop his notion of ‘urges to misunderstand’ (Section 2) and show that he exposed such urges in his own thought (Section 3).





2. URGES TO MISUNDERSTAND




One of the ‘urges to misunderstand’ Wittgenstein struggles with at length is the inclination to posit conscious or inner events where there are none: ‘When we do philosophy, we should like to hypostasise [e.g.] feelings where there are none’ (PI 598). This urge materialises, for example, in leaps to unwarranted conclusions from sentences containing verbs or expressions like “reading”, “copying”, “writing from dictation”, “playing from a score”—the at first sight motley crew that Wittgenstein idiosyncratically lumps together under the label of “reading” (PI 156a). For all these activities there are characteristic forms of behaviour that may, however, be displayed without actually performing the activity. Thus, a pupil may carefully follow the text line by line with eyes and finger, while merely pretending to read out a text he has previously learnt by rote. Actual and merely apparent reading may involve exactly the same external behaviour, in the given situation. In abstract reflection, many people therefore find it intuitively compelling to conclude that the two must involve different inner processes (156g), and unquestioningly go along with leaps of thought that presuppose instances of

Schema I: If, and only if, someone actually V-ies, an inner (conscious or unconscious) process P goes on that makes the difference between actual and merely apparent V-ing.



Wittgenstein characterises the inclination to make such leaps by means of a comparison that brings out its most striking feature:

Just as Germanisms creep into the speech of a German who speaks English well although he does not first construct the German expression and then translate into English; just as this makes him speak English as if he were translating, ‘unconsciously’, from the German—so we often think as if our thinking were founded on a thought-schema:

as if we were translating from a more primitive mode of thought into ours. (PI 597)





That we ‘think as if our thinking were founded on a thought-schema’ means that we have a certain habit of thought or inference: We systematically make non-trivial leaps of thought that tacitly presuppose assumptions instancing always the same schema, like Schema I. The comparison brings out that we are not aware of relying on the assumptions presupposed, which may even be assumptions we explicitly reject. (In leaping to a conclusion, ‘we think as if we were inferring it, “unconsciously”, from an assumption’ that remains tacit and may ‘creep in’ against our will, just as the German constructions creep in, even though the speaker knows them to be out of place.) Hence these leaps, and the propensity to make them, are ‘autonomous’: The thinker is prone to make these leaps irrespective of whether or not he wants to rely on the assumptions they presuppose.

Wittgenstein points out that the propensity to make the pertinent leaps is context-sensitive: We make them when considering particular examples, with an abstract philosophical question in mind (PI 156e). Thus, it is not when considering an adult reader, but when thinking of a beginner who makes a conscious effort as he ‘reads the words by laboriously spelling them out’ (PI 156d), that ‘we shall be inclined’ to leap from this straightforward description to the conclusion that the subject’s reading essentially involves conscious mental acts. When asking ourselves what reading consists in, we then further leap from this specific to the general conclusion that reading ‘is a special conscious activity of mind’ (PI 156e) or, put in semantic guise, that ‘the one real criterion for anybody’s reading is the conscious act of reading’ (PI 159a1). This happens in abstract reflection; when confronted with specific cases, we are unwilling to treat any conscious event either as necessary or as sufficient condition of reading (PI 159–160).

Third, the propensity asserts itself even when we have no warrant whatsoever for the conclusions we leap to: Wittgenstein swiftly brings out that we have no warrant for the present conclusion, in either guise (see below, Section 6). Moreover, the moment we accept this fact, we leap to a related conclusion: Suppose ‘we . . . admit that—as far as concerns uttering any one of the printed words—the same thing may take place in the consciousness of the pupil who is ‘pretending’ to read, as in that of the practised reader who is ‘reading’ it’ (PI 156g1). Then we infer that an unconscious inner process must make the relevant difference:

Now we should of course like to say: What goes on in the practised reader and in a beginner [who is ‘pretending’ to read] when they utter the word cannot be the same. And if there is no difference in what they happen to be conscious of there must be one in the unconscious workings of their minds, or in the brain. (PI 156g3–156g4)





We should like to jump to this conclusion, even though we really do not know anything at all about such unconscious mechanisms (PI 158). The moment we appreciate this, we may then revert to the previous conclusion (PI 159a1). I.e.: In abstract reflection seizing on certain examples, we may shuttle between different unwarranted conclusions to which we leap in line with Schema I. The inclination to make such leaps of thought is a-rational (though not necessarily irrational): Their conclusions strike the thinker as intuitively compelling, regardless of what warrant he has for these claims.

To sum up: According to Wittgenstein’s analysis, the intellectual propensity at issue is autonomous, a-rational and context-sensitive. In these three respects, it is similar to exemplary urges, like the otherwise very different urges for food or sex: These urges are context-sensitive, as they lie dormant for certain periods and assert themselves in particular situations, frequently triggered by certain cues. They are autonomous in that they make themselves felt and influence our behaviour, whether or not we want them to. And they are a-rational in the sense that we feel the urge to act, and may succumb to it, whether or not the action is right by our lights. In view of these similarities, it is quite fitting to characterise autonomous cognitive habits or propensities like the one at issue as cognitive or intellectual ‘urges’. Let’s call the present urge, to make inferences in line with Schema I and posit inner events where there are none, the ‘mentalist urge’.

When we find given claims intuitively compelling, we tend to presuppose them in our interpretation of the observations we make and the talk we encounter—the well-documented phenomenon of belief bias we have already come across several times. Due to such bias, any a-rational intellectual urge that has us find unwarranted conclusions intuitively compelling will lead us to place unwarranted interpretations on observations and statements and thus to misunderstand what is going on and what is being said. The conclusions to which the mentalist urge has us leap will colour our interpretation of our observations of pertinent activities (like the laborious reading of the beginner) as well as our interpretation of talk about them. We will consequently misunderstand what is going on, for instance, when somebody reads, as well as what we say, and how we talk, about this (as when we conclude that we must seize on a conscious act as a criterion for reading). In the latter case, we can be said to ‘misunderstand the workings of our language’. But in examining the mentalist urge, Wittgenstein examines also various ‘non-linguistic’ conclusions to which it may have us leap (in conjunction with belief bias). The intellectual urges he seeks to expose and overcome are hence not just ‘urges to misunderstand the workings of our language’ (as he characterises his foes in PI 109a8) but are ‘urges to misunderstand’ a rather wider variety of things. The ‘urges to misunderstand’ he examines are, more generally, context-sensitive and autonomous habits or propensities to make a-rational leaps of thought.

Many of the phenomena of non-intentional reasoning we have examined in previous chapters satisfy this definition: Being under the spell of a philosophical picture amounts to having a context-sensitive and autonomous propensity to make certain a-rational leaps of thought (Chapter 1.2). The same goes for the various propensities to unwittingly transfer ordinary implications that we have examined in connection with Austin’s work (Chapter 8). It also applies to some further propensities that Austin commented on, like our tendency to unwittingly fall back on dichotomous patterns of inference, when engaged in abstract reflection.

Some of these more fundamental ‘urges to misunderstand’ can account for leaps of thought in line with the present Schema I. Recall the perceptual pictures of intellectual activities and achievements we considered at the outset (Chapter 1). Non-intentional inferences driven by these pictures render compelling the assumption that various intellectual activities and achievements essentially involve perceiving something in the mind, that is, a conscious ‘inner’ event. Thus, an inference driven by the perceptual picture of understanding could account for the first manifestation of the mentalist urge Wittgenstein considers: the propensity to leap to conclusions, which presuppose that when we understand something (like a number-series), we must be aware of some inner event or process (PI 153, see Section 4).

Another relevant source is philosophers’ widespread tendency to ignore context, which may have them presuppose that real and apparent V-ing have to be distinguished by reference to something that happens in the given or present situation—rather than anything that happens or might happen before or after. Dichotomous thinking may then have us leap from the observation that actual and merely apparent, say, reading may involve exactly the same external behaviour, in the given situation, to the conclusion that different internal events or processes must distinguish the two. The urge to misunderstand that Wittgenstein examines in his investigations of understanding and reading results, I submit, from the phenomena of non-intentional reasoning we have examined.





3. WITTGENSTEIN’S SELF-DIAGNOSIS




Wittgenstein does not seek to explain this urge; he merely seeks to expose it in a philosopher’s thought. In his investigation of ‘reading’ (PI 156–178), which interrupts the investigation of understanding, Wittgenstein examines how the mentalist urge has a philosopher imagine experiences where there are none. This philosopher is none other than Wittgenstein himself. He is his own guinea-pig, as he works here from the first-person perspective he advocates in characterising ‘the philosopher’ as ‘someone who has to cure in himself [!] many diseases of the understanding’ (CV 50), and which he explicitly acknowledges as his own: ‘Almost the whole time I am writing conversations with myself. Things I say to myself tête-à-tête’ (CV 88). The afterthought brings out what the otherwise pleonastic German formulation (‘Selbstgespräche mit mir selbst’, i.e. ‘soliloquies with myself’) makes clear from the start: Wittgenstein’s Investigations do not merely have the form of a philosophical soliloquy (with a, perhaps, fictitious protagonist), but record what their author actually told himself.

One of the ‘diseases of the understanding’ Wittgenstein finds ‘in himself’ by examining the things he is inclined to say to himself tête-à-tête is the present urge to misunderstand: He uses, rather than quotes, the words “I [!] should like to say” in introducing the different versions of the idea to which the mentalist urge has thinkers leap (e.g. PI 169c2, 173b11, 174a3, 176a4). He ‘would like to say’ (in the cautious sense presently explained) that when reading or copying, etc., we are having characteristic experiences that distinguish actual from merely apparent exercises of these activities— and frequently expresses this idea with idiosyncratic formulations that cannot credibly be put into anyone else’s mouth, e.g.: ‘I should like to say: “I experience the because”’ (PI 177a1). Indeed, Wittgenstein often addresses himself in the second person (‘you’), as one does in soliloquy, and various of the statements that in the Investigations occur in double quotes and without attribution to ‘me’ or ‘you’ or anyone else, are derived from manuscript sources in which they are referred to as what ‘I am inclined to say’.10 Most of the statements commentators commonly attribute to a fictitious interlocutor, and many of the statements typically regarded as impersonal items for discussion, in fact articulate things Wittgenstein is inclined to say and prone to find intuitively compelling.11

Wittgenstein’s attitude towards these intuitively compelling ideas differs radically, though, from that of other philosophers in the grip of the mentalist urge: He is aware of his predicament and therefore careful not to endorse the ideas that strike him as compelling. He would ‘like to say’ and believe these things merely in the sense that it comes naturally to him to say them and that they strike him as intuitively compelling. For he regards this inclination as just that—as an inclination or ‘temptation to use a particular kind of expression’ (PI 254a3), indeed, as a temptation to talk ‘nonsense’ (in the colloquial sense that covers both meaninglessness and absurdity). All the same, he attends to it carefully, in line with his exhortation: ‘Don’t for Heaven’s sake be afraid of talking nonsense. Only don’t fail to pay attention to your nonsense’ (CV 64). He pays attention even to patently nonsensical conclusions that strike him as tempting, in order to identify urges to misunderstand that, in different settings, may have him actually go along with absurd ideas he sees through less easily (as we shall see in the next section).

In the investigation of ‘reading’—in his liberal, ‘technical’ sense of the word which includes copying, writing from dictation, etc. (PI 156a)— Wittgenstein makes himself aware that he has (1) a context-sensitive propensity to be attracted by specific ideas (about reading, copying, etc.) for which (2) there really is no warrant. He achieves this through a series of ‘experiment[s]’ (PI 169c7): Addressing each activity in turn (reading: PI 169, copying a text: PI 173, copying a figure: PI 175), he first executes it, bearing in mind the question of what then happens (PI 170b1). Then he (1) takes note of what he is spontaneously inclined to say about experiences that then occur, of the precise expression he feels attracted to, and of the particular circumstances under which he is tempted to use it. This yields a ‘psychologically accurate account’ of his ‘temptation’ (PI 254a3). And (2) he checks what warrant there is for saying what he thus says, by considering what he can actually remember. This reveals the idea at hand is a ‘fiction’ (PI 166a2) or ‘imagination’ (PI 170c1) due to unwarranted leaps of thought.

Thus, after copying a doodle put down for the purpose (PI 175a1–2), he is spontaneously inclined to say that he felt a kind of influence of the original on his movements: ‘When I look back on the experience I have the feeling that what is essential about it is an ‘experience of being influenced’, of a connexion—as opposed to any mere simultaneity of phenomena’ (PI 176a1). But this impression is entirely unwarranted: ‘It’s as if I couldn’t believe that I merely looked, made such-and-such a face, and drew a line.— But don’t I remember anything else? No’ (PI 175b4–175b 5, cp. PI 176a2). He notes that certain formulations trigger the leap of thought: ‘And yet I feel as if there must have been something else; in particular when I say “guidance”, “influence”, and other such words to myself. “For surely”, I tell myself, “I was being guided.” Only then does the idea of that ethereal, intangible influence arise’ (PI 175b6–175b8).

This is a leap of thought presupposing an instance of (part of) Schema I:





(1) ‘I have actually copied the figure, was being guided by it.’

(I1) ‘If someone actually copies a figure, he has an experience of being influenced or guided, which experience makes the difference between actual copying and merely coincidental reproduction.’ Which entails:

(i) ‘I had an experience of being influenced’ (of being guided: cp. PI 172).



Wittgenstein feels inclined to leap to this conclusion, even though he reflec-tively rejects (I1): It is not an experience that makes the difference between actually being guided by, and merely coincidental reproduction of, the original, but rather the fact that he drew the second line down exactly parallel to it, rather than arbitrarily, and would have explained by reference to the original line why he drew the second the way he did (PI 177a3, cp. PI 169b5–169b6). I.e.: As all the leaps of thought Wittgenstein examines in this investigation, this leap is autonomous.

These leaps have him posit an experience where there is none—as the leap from (1) to (i)—or to radically misinterpret an experience he actually has. Wittgenstein elicits one such misinterpretation through another self-experiment: He first reads off a sentence and then pronounces it while following a line of mostly non-alphabetic signs, including: &8§‚ §‚?ß . . . (PI 169a2–169a3). ‘It does of course make a difference whether I say “i” when I see “i” or when I see “§”’ (PI 169c5). Spontaneously, ‘I should like to say: when I read I feel a kind of influence of the letters working on me—but I feel no influence from that series of arbitrary flourishes on what I say’ (PI 169c2). But, comparing the cases more carefully, Wittgenstein finds that this misinterprets the difference he can actually notice, which is this: ‘when I see the letter it is automatic for me to hear the sound “i” inwardly, it happens even against my will; and I pronounce the letter more effortlessly when I read it than when I am looking at “§”’ (PI 169c6). So he interprets this difference which he actually notices ‘as the difference between being influenced and not being influenced’ (PI 170a3) and thus comes to imagine an ‘experience of being influenced’ where he is not aware of anything that would fit the description (PI 170c).

Through these and similar experiments, with himself in the two-fold role of guinea-pig and experimenter, Wittgenstein elicits many autonomous leaps of thought (PI 165, 166, 169, 173, 175; cp. Section 6 below). He thus exposes his own urge to systematically imagine or misinterpret experiences, in leaps of thought that presuppose assumptions he explicitly rejects. He thus comes to diagnose himself with a specific disease of the understanding.





4. ILLUSORY PROBLEMS: SUDDEN UNDERSTANDING




When this urge has him imagine experiences where there are none, a particularly acute conflict with reality arises and generates the impression of a problem, at the very least in the form of unwarranted philosophical puzzlement. Thus, Wittgenstein initially feels puzzled by his ‘copying-experiment’: He is inclined to say he had an ‘experience of being influenced’. ‘But at the same time I should not be willing to call any experienced phenomenon [that I can actually remember] the “experience of being influenced”’ (PI 176a2). His inclination thus leaves him with the perplexing idea of an influence that is effective but ‘ethereal, intangible’ (PI 175b8).

A philosopher who is not merely inclined to say, but actually assumes, that a characteristic mental event is essential to the exercise of some activity, and is simultaneously unable to grow aware of any suitable event in performing it, will feel there is a tension between a philosophical assumption he makes and a fact he recognises. This will have him formulate the characteristic kind of problem we called a ‘reconciliation problem’: the problem of reconciling different assumptions in the face of an apparent tension between them (Chapter 2.3).

Wittgenstein develops a problem of this sort right before launching the investigation of reading (in PI 151–153). The problem is about understanding of number-series. While of course no activity, but an achievement, such understanding is akin to the activities discussed in that it is associated with characteristic forms of behaviour that may, however, be displayed without actual understanding. Thus, someone may correctly continue the series by a fluke, may utter the pertinent formula without understanding (of either the formula or the series), etc. Also here leaps of thought in line with Schema I may occur. The temptation to make them is particularly strong in the case of sudden understanding, signalled by the exclamation ‘Now I understand!’ For in such cases of understanding, in marked contrast to others, we typically are aware of some mental process (thinking of a pertinent formula, some mental arithmetic, etc.)—and may all too easily conclude that it makes the difference between genuine and merely apparent understanding. Accordingly, leaps to this mentalist conclusion about sudden understanding may occur even once one has appreciated that the attribution of understanding (of a number series) amounts to more or less the same as the attribution of a capacity or know-how (to continue the series), which point Wittgenstein appreciates in the immediately preceding sections (PI 146–150).

He then raises his problem about sudden understanding as a result of three leaps of thought. They differ from those he deliberately examines in his subsequent ‘experiments’ in that he initially finds them compelling and goes along with them. Even though he explicitly equates understanding with a capacity (PI 151b4), he immediately puts a ‘mentalist’ interpretation on the question posed by the next sentence, ‘What is it that makes its appearance here?’ (PI 151b5): He does not respond with a detailed or otherwise informative description of the capacity he explicitly assumes to make its appearance, but spontaneously interprets the question as one about an event: ‘What happened here?’ (PI 151b7), and immediately answers it by adducing some mental events we are typically aware of when suddenly understanding a series: thinking of a formula, etc. (PI 151b8ff.). In leaping from the first to the second question, Wittgenstein inadvertently presupposes that the understanding that makes its appearance here is a mental event. This translates into an assumption instancing Schema I:



(I2) If and only if someone actually understands, a mental event occurs that constitutes (real) understanding: It is inconceivable that the subject should not understand when it occurs.



The next two leaps presuppose this very assumption. The observation that



(2) Each of the events adduced may conceivably occur even when the subject does not understand (PI 152b2–152b4)



leads Wittgenstein to conclude immediately that



(ii) The mental process of understanding . . . seems to be hidden behind those coarser and therefore more readily visible accompaniments. (PI 153a1)



He tries to ‘get hold’ of this process, and fails to succeed (PI 153a1–153a2). This process is as ‘ethereal, intangible’ as the ‘experience of being influ-enced’ in copying. The conclusion thus brought home, that this process is ‘hidden’, then is at odds with the observation that



(3) ‘I said “Now I understand” because I understood’ (PI 153a5),



inadvertently interpreted—in the light of I2— as amounting to



(iii) ‘I said “Now I understand” because I was aware of the mental process that constitutes understanding.’



Whence ‘I am in a muddle’ (PI 153a7): Wittgenstein apparently faces the problem of reconciling his conclusion (ii) with the present observation.

In marked contrast to (ii) and (iii), (2) and (3) do not even appear to be at odds, when interpreted in line with our ordinary way of attributing understanding to others and to ourselves: We do not use the phrase “Now I understand” to report a mental state, but to signal that we can continue the series; and we and others judge whether the signal was rightly employed by considering how we continue that series (PI 180) and how we explain and justify our continuation of it. Accordingly, I may say ‘I said “Now I understand” because I understood (and not because I wanted to impress you)’ to state that I employed the signal sincerely, being confident that I could continue the series correctly and give an explanation to justify my choice of numbers (rather than hoping for luck in guessing). When rightly employing the signal, we will frequently think of such an explanation, usually a formula. But whether our utterance is correct does not depend upon whether any such mental event occurs. We may well say ‘Now I understand’ when nothing at all occurs in our minds (PI 179c). A fortiori, we may say this correctly when no mental event occurs that makes our failure to understand inconceivable: say, when we think of a formula that enables us (who we have learnt algebra and have used this kind of formula before) to continue the series correctly, but does not put a novice to algebra into that position (PI 179b). Properly understood, (3) does not even appear to be inconsistent with (2)—or with (ii), for that matter.

Thus, Wittgenstein (momentarily) feels there is a tension between assumptions that have to be honoured, only because he makes two moves (which he will swiftly correct): because he explicitly infers (ii) from (2) and implicitly interprets (3) as amounting to (iii). He has (as he will quickly realise) no warrant for either move: He is not aware of any such process as (ii) takes to be hidden and (iii) takes him to report, and he is not in possession of some more general grounds to support the tacit presupposition (I2), so as to turn his inadvertent leaps of thought into sound arguments. Indeed, he repudiates (I2), in equating understanding with a capacity. So he has no warrant for his assumption (ii), and none for regarding his other explicit assumption (3) as at odds with it. This illustrates at one go the two potentially complementary ways in which a reconciliation problem may be ill motivated: When raising the problem of reconciling different assumptions in the face of an apparent tension between them, we may lack warrant either for at least one of the assumptions to be reconciled, or for our impression of a tension between them—or, like Wittgenstein, for both. Like practically all the problems we have considered in this book, Wittgenstein’s problem is an ill-motivated reconciliation-problem, engendered by unsound inferences or leaps of thought which are not under the thinker’s direct control.





5. WITTGENSTEIN’S THERAPEUTIC TURN




A common response to such problems is the construction of a philosophical theory that is to reconcile what apparently is at odds. Many philosophical theories are explicitly meant to ‘reconcile’ different philosophical intuitions and salient facts, which the theory must ‘accommodate’. In the case of the present problem, this response would take the shape of the development of a ‘theory of hidden mental processes’ to solve the problem by explaining how it is possible for subjects to report that they understand something— even though the process of understanding is hidden. Such a theory would be in some ways similar to the ‘theory of tacit semantic knowledge’ actually proposed by Gareth Evans and others, in the early 1980s, which we briefly encountered above (Chapter 7.2).

Wittgenstein’s first decisive move towards an alternative response is to suspend belief in the things he is irresistibly inclined to say; he does not regard these intuitions as sources of insight:

Being unable—when we surrender ourselves to philosophical thought—to help saying such-and-such; being irresistibly inclined to say it—does not mean being forced into an assumption, or having an immediate perception or knowledge of a state of affairs. (PI 299)





Accordingly, he does not rush to ‘honour’ such philosophical intuitions by constructing a reconciliatory ‘theory’. As we already noted, he regards his inclinations to say this-or-that, and to find it compelling, as just that—as inclinations or temptations:

What we are ‘tempted to say’ in such a case is, of course, not philosophy; but it is its raw material. Thus, for example, what a mathematician is inclined to say about the objectivity and reality of mathematical facts [or a philosopher about processes of understanding], is not a philosophy of mathematics [or language], but something for philosophical treatment. (PI 254a4–254a5)





Prior to examination of the warrant for saying those things, it is a genuinely open question in which of two very different senses this ‘raw material’ ought to be ‘treated’: whether it should be elaborated into a theory of the matter or subject about which we are inclined to say this-or-that (a theory of understanding, say); or whether we should cure an urge to say things that are not even faintly sensible. This second, commonly neglected, possibility is explicitly adduced in the next sentence: ‘The philosopher’s treatment of a question is like the treatment of an illness’ (PI 255).

This detached attitude makes it possible for Wittgenstein to soberly determine the content of the claims that strike him as intuitively plausible or compelling, and to assess what warrant he has for maintaining them: ‘Now, ask yourself’, he enjoins himself, ‘what do you know about these things?’ (PI 158a7). Sincere answers to this question reveal systematic lack of warrant, if not of meaning, and allow him to conceptualise the problems he believed to confront him as arising from conflicts between intuitively compelling but unwarranted claims and other assumptions or convictions:

[The philosophical problem]12 A simile that has been absorbed into the forms of our language produces a false appearance, and this disquiets us. “But this isn’t how it is!”—we say. “Yet this is how it has to be!” (PI 112)





The disquieting problem is raised by the apparent conflict between how we are intuitively inclined to think that something has to be—a ‘false appearance’ like the intuitively compelling conclusion, (ii) above, that the mental process of understanding seems to be hidden—and the conviction or observation that this is not how it is.

Wittgenstein takes this problematic intuition to be produced by—presumably unsound—reasoning of which we are unaware: It is produced by a ‘simile . . . absorbed into the forms of our language’ (PI 112), and such similes are ‘at work in the unconscious’ (VW 69) or are at any rate not deliberately employed by the philosopher.13 More generally, Wittgenstein puts the problematic intuitions down to urges to misunderstand—a concept which covers also the urges to treat similes and metaphors more literally than we know we may that we have analysed in this book (Section 1).14 To put it in our terms, Wittgenstein thus takes autonomous habits of non-intentional reasoning to engender reconciliation problems which are ill-motivated. Indeed, he thinks such habits have us imagine difficulties where there are none: Only the ‘false appearances’ they produce create the disquieting impression that things cannot be how they actually are.

When the disquieting philosophical difficulties we take ourselves to face are merely imaginary, the only genuine problems we actually confront are the emotional problem constituted by our groundless disquiet and the behavioural problems constituted by what theoretical or other efforts we may make to solve those difficulties (cp. Chapter 7.4). Such emotional and behavioural problems are the only genuine problems that are engendered in this way, viz. through ‘misinterpretation of our forms of language’ guided by ‘similes absorbed into the forms of language’ (cp. PI 111a1). Since Wittgenstein distances himself from his philosophical intuitions, which he suspects are due to various urges to misunderstand, he does not make any efforts to reconcile them with his beliefs or to solve the difficulty they appear to raise, in any other way. The ‘misinterpretation’ at issue hence causes him no behavioural but only emotional problems, and he treats the latter as the only problems ‘arising through misinterpretation’: These problems are ‘deep disquietudes’ (PI 111a2).

Misinterpretation guided by similes absorbed into the forms of language is constitutive of just one kind of urge to misunderstand. The present argument applies more generally, wherever such an urge creates the disquieting impression that things cannot be how they actually are—say, that I cannot suddenly come to understand a number series without being aware of a strangely elusive mental process. In all these cases, the only genuine problems we face are our own behavioural and emotional problems—and only the latter, if, like Wittgenstein, we suspend our belief in the intuitively compelling ideas that create that disquieting impression.

Wittgenstein explicitly acknowledges the aim of solving such emotional problems as his paramount goal (Section 1). Above, we have seen reason to believe that his actual practice is consistent with this acknowledgement. We now see that the adoption of this new therapeutic aim is warranted by his conception of the nature and genesis of the philosophical ‘difficulties’ facing him, and that this conception actually applies, in its essentials, to a problem Wittgenstein is struggling with in a core part of his Investigations, viz. the ‘problem’ about sudden understanding. His adoption of a novel chief aim is appropriate in the face of at least some of the philosophical ‘problems’ he addresses and is vindicated by his—admittedly sketchy—conception of their nature and genesis.





6. WITTGENSTEIN’S THERAPEUTIC APPROACH




By itself, adopting the aim of putting an end to groundless worries does not push us beyond the demonstration of truth or falsity, and the assessment of warrant for the particular claims at issue. When worrying about an ill-motivated reconciliation problem, for instance, we may well put an end to our unwarranted disquiet, quite simply, by refuting the groundless ideas that give rise to the worrying problem: the unwarranted claims to be reconciled, and their presuppositions. In the case of the problem about sudden understanding (Section 4), we have to refute the conflicting conclusions (ii) and (iii), expose and refute the presupposition (I2), and thus return to the uncontroversial assumptions (2) and (3) from which we started—and which, properly understood, do not even appear to give rise to a contradiction, disquieting or other.

But where inferential urges which are autonomous and a-rational push us to leap from uncontroversial or common-sense assumptions to contradictory conclusions, we have to do more: ‘The philosopher . . . has to cure in himself many diseases of the understanding, before he can arrive at the notions of common sense’ (CV 50)—or return to them. The conclusions we leap to, when in the grip of such pathological urges, strike us as natural and compelling regardless of what warrant we have for them, and may continue to do so even in the face of their refutation. The present problem is a case in point. At first blush, we might think that the perplexity engendered by this ‘muddle’ is to be removed by refuting the idea that gives rise to it, viz., the idea that sudden understanding of a number-series involves a conscious mental event that constitutes understanding. This idea is to be shown false by considering which mental events we are, or can grow, aware of when coming to understand a number-series, and considering whether it is conceivable that the subject fails to understand when one of them occurs— which will reveal that no conscious event ‘constitutes’ understanding. But so far from ridding Wittgenstein of the problem, this finding—(2) above— leads him into it: He leaps from it to the conclusion (ii) that the conscious process of understanding is ‘hidden’. The ‘mentalist’ urge is overpowering: It has Wittgenstein cleave to an idea that has been shown false, by sensible standards he accepts. The idea—or, more accurately, the implicit belief (Chapter 6.2)—is delusional (Chapter 6.5). To rid himself of it and the disquiet it induces in him, Wittgenstein has to do more than merely refute it. He also needs to weaken the autonomous urge to misunderstand that has him cleave to the unwarranted idea and presuppose it, willy-nilly, in his further reasoning. He also has to cure in himself this disease of the understanding that has him cleave to his delusional idea, not only without, but against reason.

This is the key to a pretty, if neglected, exegetical puzzle. In response to his problem, Wittgenstein examines the idea that raised it by taking him from truism (2) to the disquieting conclusion (ii). He recasts both in linguistic terms and transforms the latter into ‘I employ the sentence “Now I understand” . . . as a description of a process occurring behind . . . that of saying [or thinking of] the formula’ (PI 154a). He then points out an alternative to the presupposition that a process has to back up the claim that I understand: Instead, it might be ‘particular circumstances which justify me in saying I can go on—when the formula occurs to me’ (PI 154b). This suggestion is reiterated (PI 155) and followed up: What entitles me to say I can go on when the formula occurs to me are such circumstances as that I know arithmetic and have used formulae of that kind before (179a–b; cp. Section 4). This refutes the idea presupposed in the crucial move to the disquieting conclusion and thereby the reasons for raising the problem, which is thus ‘dissolved’, in the sense we explained (in Chapter 2.4). So far, so straightforward. But, as the perceptive reader may have noticed, the sections that prepare (PI 154–155) and effect (PI 179ff.) this refutation are separated by a full twenty-three sections—devoted to the apparently unrelated topics of reading and copying (PI 156–178). Whence the puzzle: Why this digression?

From recent cognitive psychotherapies we have gleaned the insight that we can significantly weaken delusional beliefs and the emotions they engender, by gaining general meta-cognitive insight into the autonomous and a-rational cognitive processes that have us form and maintain them: by coming to understand not only how we came to form the particular belief at issue but how the pertinent processes shape our philosophical reflection quite generally and have us systematically maintain or presuppose unwarranted or even patently false claims (Chapter 7.5). The investigation of ‘reading’ is devoted to acquiring this kind of meta-cognitive insight, which allows a thinker to accept and assimilate the refutation of his delusional beliefs. Through this investigation, Wittgenstein makes himself aware of the fact that he has a certain general urge to misunderstand: a context-sensitive, a-rational and autonomous propensity to leap to unwarranted conclusions which are about various subtly related topics but are all variations on the same theme—the mentalist Schema I. To expose this general urge, he turns to the new topic of reading and makes three moves.

In abstract reflection in the grip of an urge to misunderstand, we tend to think that things ‘must be like this’, as we simply fail to think of possible alternatives. This failure makes it easy to overlook that we lack warrant for the conclusions and, indeed, that we are making a substantive move in leaping to them. As a preparatory move, Wittgenstein therefore first outlines an alternative to the ‘mentalist’ way of drawing the distinction between true and merely apparent reading (PI 157): We could draw it by reference to further responses to written signs, rather than to inner processes. He provides a context in which this proposal is attractive: If we trained people as ‘reading machines’, we would indeed care primarily about whether they consistently respond correctly to given signs. Then he derives from either alternative a consequence that can be tested against our ordinary way of speaking: When we use a certain inner process as a criterion of reading, we can say of a particular word that it was the first word the subject read; when we refer exclusively to his overt performance, we cannot say this. The fact that we do not commonly say this then suggests that we ordinarily draw the distinction between actual and apparent reading without reference to inner processes. While sorely inadequate as an analysis of the concept of reading (for which it has often been mistaken), Wittgenstein’s sketchy alternative does drive home that, so far from necessarily having to invoke inner processes to draw that distinction, we commonly do not do this. This alternative allows Wittgenstein to distance himself from the mentalist ideas on reading: In marked contrast to the above conclusion (ii), he merely takes note of his ‘temptation’ to endorse them, while careful not to do so.

Second, he elicits leaps of thought similar to the one that raised the initial problem, by ‘listening to the nonsense’ (CV 64) he is tempted to say, and gets clear on the context-sensitivity of this temptation to leap to conclusions by ‘giving a psychologically accurate’ account of it (PI 254a3): Wittgenstein considers contexts relevantly similar to the one in which his initial problem arose, lets himself say in response whatever strikes him as intuitively plausible, without self-censorship, and takes note of the precise wording that attracts him, and of the particular circumstances under which it strikes him thus. I.e.: He makes the sort of ‘experiment[s]’ (169c7) with himself in the dual role of subject and observer that we have considered above (in Section 3).

Third and finally, he refutes the tempting conclusions. The central part of the investigation of reading considers a sequence of thoughts in which each cedes only as much ground as made absolutely necessary by the refutation of the previous one:





The one real criterion for anybody’s reading is the conscious act of reading, the act of reading the sounds off from the letters. (PI 159a1)

Even if we do not seize on this conscious act or process as a criterion to distinguish genuine from merely apparent reading, still ‘reading is a quite particular process’ (PI 165a1), namely a conscious process: ‘reading is a particular experience’ (PI 165a7). We merely need to ‘read a page of print’ to ‘see that something special and highly characteristic is going on’ (PI 165a2).

Even if no special or characteristic trait I am conscious of is shared by all cases of reading, still ‘I feel [in various different ways perhaps] a kind of influence of the letters working on me, when I read’ (PI 169c2).





Wittgenstein refutes each of these claims in turn (in PI 159–160, 165–168 and 169–171, respectively). In view of the influential preconception that Wittgenstein proceeded exclusively by establishing conceptual facts and thus sought to expose philosophical ‘claims’ as meaningless (rather than ‘merely’ absurd) nonsense, it is worth noting that Wittgenstein shows all these claims to be, properly understood, false.

While (I) is ‘grammatical’, (II) and (III) are empirical, psychological claims. Accordingly, Wittgenstein refutes them in different ways: He elicits linguistic intuitions and their rationale, which are all at odds with (I). And he refutes (II) and (III) in various steps that crucially involve empirical observation and experiment (sic). We have already considered Wittgenstein’s treatment of (III) (Section 3). So let’s now focus on his treatment of (II), which he highlights as the most important of the three claims by using it, in the opening section of the discussion of reading (PI 156), as his example to introduce the notion that we are, in specific contexts, spontaneously inclined to leap to certain conclusions: When considering particular examples in the light of the philosophical question ‘What does reading consist in?’, ‘we shall be inclined to say: it is a special conscious activity of mind’ (PI 156e).

Wittgenstein now (in PI 165) returns to this question: ‘One could say, if you want to see what reading consists in, read, and attend to what happens’ (MS 152: 5, source of PI 165). He does this, i.e., actually reads a page of print, and finds: ‘I see printed words and say words out loud’ (PI 165a4)— and don’t know what else to say about ‘what happens’. But even so, in spite of having no warrant to say this, he finds it tempting to utter variations on the theme that ‘reading is a quite particular [conscious] process’ (PI 165a1): that ‘reading is a particular experience’ (PI 165a7), of which he ‘should like to say: “The words I utter come in a special way”’ (PI 165a9). This first reading exercise does not, of course, serve to find out what happens when we read so as to answer the question what reading consists in. Rather, it serves to expose a context-sensitive urge to misunderstand, namely, to elicit what Wittgenstein is, spontaneously and without warrant, inclined to say about reading, in specific actual cases, when considering that abstract question (cp. Section 2).

This first is followed by further exercises or ‘experiment[s]’ (PI 166a9). These serve, second, to get clear on the content of the things that sound so tempting in the abstract, namely on what they come down to in concrete terms in specific reading situations experimentally reproduced for the purpose. Further ‘experiments’ then serve, third, to refute the claims thus obtained, by showing them not meaningless, but false. Thus, Wittgenstein next reads the letter “A” (PI 166a4), writes a letter (PI 166a7), pronounces a sound while looking at a mere flourish (PI 166a11), and attends to the way in which the sounds and hand-movements ‘come’ in each case (PI 166a3). He does so to see whether he can identify any ‘special way’ in which they come. In each case, he finds he cannot (PI 166a6, 166a10, 166a11). This suggests (though it does not establish) that the empirical claim at issue is always false, namely the claim that when I read (and attend to what is happening) I can grow aware of a characteristic way in which the sounds ‘come’ to me, which is different from the way in which sounds ‘come’, e.g., when I make them up or something reminds me of them (PI 165a10–165a14). But, perhaps surprisingly, Wittgenstein then develops another interpretation of that phrase, and thus obtains a related claim he thinks is actually true of some cases of reading: The moment he does not look for characteristic sensations but rather takes the ‘situation’ or context of utterance into account as well, he can identify various differences between the way in which the sound of the familiar letter comes in actual reading and the sound he pronounces comes when he looks at the mere flourish (PI 166a13–166a17). And these differences allow us to say that when we read familiar letters in print the sounds come to us automatically, in a way they do not come when we look at unfamiliar marks (PI 166a18–166a20). When reading printed letters, the sounds do come to us ‘in a special way’. This much, properly understood, is not merely meaningful, but even true.

From this clarification, however, Wittgenstein proceeds to refute the generic proposition that attracts him: ‘Now what is there in the proposition that reading is ‘a quite particular process’? It presumably means that when we read one particular process takes place, which we recognize’ (PI 167a1– 167a2). And this proposition, Wittgenstein shows, is simply false. It is not false because we cannot grow aware of any potentially pertinent ‘mental process’ (PI 167a3)—‘there are of course many more or less characteristic sensations in reading a printed sentence; [and] it is not difficult to call such sensations to mind’ (PI 159a6). But the present proposition is false because all such processes or experiences occur only in some, rather than in all cases of reading: When we read a page of print, which is indeed a uniform experience (PI 167a4ff.), ‘our eye passes over printed lines differently from the way it passes over arbitrary pothooks and flourishes. (I am not speaking here of what can be established by observing the movements of the eyes of a reader [but about our experience].) The eye passes . . . with particular ease . . . and yet it doesn’t skid. And at the same time involuntary speech goes on in the imagination’ (PI 168a1–168a4); the sounds come automatically. ‘That is how it is when I read German and other languages, printed or written’ (PI 168a5), as I usually do. But it is not, as he seems to ascertain by further experiment (‘Compare . . . ’), when reading a text printed exclusively in capital letters (PI 168a8–168a9) or reading our script from right to left (PI 168a10), nor, as he suggests apparently without making the experiment, when turning Morse code into letters (PI 167a3). So we are not dealing with the same ‘mental process’ (PI 167a3) in all cases of reading: There is no one characteristic process which we recognise whenever we read.

To sum up, Wittgenstein experimentally reproduces ‘reading’ situations, for three purposes: to, first, elicit what things he is inclined to say in abstract reflection, spontaneously and without warrant, about what happens when he reads (what ‘experience’ he then has); second, to get clear on what content and import these things have in concrete terms (what kind of ‘experience’ they might advert to); and, third, to refute the claims thus obtained, by showing them false (no pertinent experience occurs sufficiently generally). In this way, he makes himself aware of a general urge to misunderstand that drives him to make leaps of thought which lead him to conclusions he finds compelling even though he has no warrant for them, and which in fact are simply false.

All three steps are in line with the striking precept from which our discussion of Wittgenstein’s approach proceeded: the precept that to take care of a philosophical problem we should advance no theories, hypotheses or explanations (PI 109), no uncontroversial claims (PI 128) and, in particular, no ‘new truths about the subject of the investigation’ (BT 416), here: understanding, reading, copying, etc. At none of those three stages does Wittgenstein advance an explanation or hypothesis. Rather, he allows himself to say what strikes him as compelling, and clarifies and refutes these things (like ‘When I read, the words come in a special way’), without adopting and advancing them at any stage as a hypothesis or explanation. And while he does establish in the process some facts about reading and the experiences it may involve, these facts (like the fact that when one reads letters in print, the eye passes with particular ease, yet does not skid) are familiar to any proficient reader—we merely need to be reminded of facts we did not think of in abstract reflection. By contrast, we do learn new facts, and can, perhaps, be said to obtain some sort of ‘explanation’, as the repeated execution of those three steps has us grow aware of a general urge to misunderstand that systematically drives us from truisms to perplexing conclusions. But these new facts are not about the subject of the philosophical investigation (reading or sudden understanding), but about our thinking about it; and we do not explain the phenomenon that perplexes us (say, sudden understanding), but come to understand why we came to find it perplexing. Improved self-understanding helps us see through self-made mystery. In the investigations we have examined, Wittgenstein gains such self-understanding through self-experimentation, through simple experiments with himself in the two-fold role of experimenter and subject.





7. BEYOND WITTGENSTEIN




We can move beyond such simple experiments, not only to more sophisticated philosophical self-experimentation (cp. Chapter 2.5) but also to scientific experiment. We may, in particular, turn to experimental work from those parts of cognitive science that are not excessively speculative or vitiated by the very phenomena of picture-driven reasoning that are, we suggested, liable to occur in speculative theorising—in philosophy and beyond. Since Wittgenstein’s day, experimental work in social and cognitive psychology has established the occurrence of a wide range of autonomous leaps of thought, including non-intentional analogical reasoning and several belief bias effects, which may shape philosophical reflection. In this book, we have developed ways and means for determining on the basis of evidence from philosophical texts whether, and how, such autonomous thought has shaped the philosophical reflections of particular thinkers (Chapters 1–2).

Where the pertinent inferences rely on tacit presuppositions the thinkers at issue explicitly reject, where those inferences thus are unsound by the thinkers’ own lights, such findings become a powerful tool of philosophical criticism—including self-criticism. We have found that a process in which non-intentional analogical reasoning works in conjunction with belief-bias effects may systematically affect philosophical reflection at several levels and shape philosophers’ intuitions, concepts, claims, problems and theories, arguments and argumentative strategies; this unsound ‘pictorial process’ may even determine what we accept as reason or evidence, and how strong a reason we think it is (Chapters 3–4). Several unsound processes of non-intentional and automatic inference may work together and lead even the most competent thinkers to form and maintain absurd claims, as a matter of delusion (Chapters 5–6 and 8). By reconstructing how these unsound processes stealthily shape the reflections of other philosophers and our own, we can refute part or all of our philosophical motivation for raising various philosophical problems, and thus solve or dissolve these problems (Chapter 2.4). At the same time, we can acquire the kind of general meta-cognitive insight into commonly hidden sources and channels of philosophical reflection that can weaken even delusional beliefs and allows us to give up the problems we have seen we should reject (Chapter 7).

To engage in such philosophical and therapeutic efforts and provide them with a firm foundation, we need to be sufficiently, but not excessively, wary of theories: We have seen ample reason to reject philosophical theories, in particular theories with a brief to ‘honour’ or ‘accommodate’ philosophical intuitions and ‘explain’ familiar facts in the light of apparent difficulties raised only by such intuitions.—All too often, such theories are based on the same unsound intuitions that raise the bogus problems they are meant to solve, and are both unwarranted and pointless.—But, at the same time, we have seen good reason to develop meta-philosophical theories which explain how competent thinkers come to go along with wild intuitions and gaps in arguments, and let us understand how our philosophical reflections are shaped by cognitive processes of which we are typically unaware. Such theories do not stand in any opposition to therapy; on the contrary, they may guide therapeutic efforts in philosophy in something like the way in which findings and theories from different branches of psychology guide efforts in cognitive psychotherapy.

We can develop such meta-philosophical theories through case-studies that examine how experimentally documented thought processes have shaped the philosophical reflections of actual thinkers, whose predicaments are sometimes more and sometimes less similar to our own, and sometimes exactly the same. Such theories not only let us dispel groundless intellectual disquiet (which does not seem to bother many philosophers as acutely as it bothered Wittgenstein). More importantly, they allow us to solve or dissolve tenacious philosophical problems, and wrest from autonomous habits of thought enough control over our reasoning to actually give up those problems and others like them, and start to think about the topics at issue with less undue interference. This might put an end to some bogus mystery and to intellectual efforts that are pointless by the lights of the very thinkers who are engaged in them. It might reduce the notorious disagreement among philosophers. And it can facilitate sound work on new problems, of a different kind.

This position was maintained by Boyle, who took for granted that we ordinarily apply colour-terms (also) to sensations in us (sic), voiced reservations about saying that colour ‘is a quality that resides in the body that is said to be coloured’ and preferred to say that colour is in ‘the light itself, which so modified strikes upon the organ of sight, and so causes that sensation which we [ordinarily] call colour’ (Boyle 1664/1999: 28).

4. This is an actual case, taken from Freud 1963: 248–50.

NOTES TO CHAPTER 9





1. Wittgenstein’s methodological remarks to the contrary were either simply ignored or rejected as expression of a methodological self-misunderstanding. See, e.g., Kenny 2004.

2. PI 138a3, repeated in PI 197a5; 154d and PI 202.

3. See Fischer 2006a.

4. See the ‘critical-genetic’ edition of the PI, Wittgenstein 2001b.

5. This note from early Fall 1938 presumably refers to the early version of the Investigations then nearing completion.

6. The most helpful statement we get is: ‘What is your aim in philosophy?—To show the fly the way out of the fly-bottle’ (PI 309).

7. From MS 127, 41v; dated 4.3.1944. This late remark provides evidence that Wittgenstein pursues the same chief aim in his BT (from 1933) and in the later PI (reworked and completed in 1944–46).

8. This goes very much against the grain of the secondary literature, where even most ‘therapeutic’ interpretations of Wittgenstein’s work regard it as the prime function of therapy to disentangle puzzles, confusions or conceptual difficulties, or improve our understanding of language or the relevant facts and their connections. The few clear exceptions include Farrell 1946, Peter-man 1992, Baker 2004 and Plant 2004. Fischer (2008: 80–82) explains how the account to be developed complements these extant interpretations.

9. This has been pointed out most clearly by Baker (2004: 212).

10. See Fischer 2004: 91-–101.

11. See Fischer 2004: 97–98 for a list of such statements from PI 138–197.

12. This initial, then deleted, heading of the remark in the final typescript (Wittgenstein 2001b: 810, fn.2) suggests that Wittgenstein regarded the structure and genesis indicated here as paradigmatic.

13. The longer early versions of PI 112 employ some of the same examples as VW 69–70, suggesting that the same kind of ‘similes’ are at issue in both passages. But this evidence is inconclusive, since the particular paragraph speaking of ‘a simile at work in the unconscious’ seems to be mislocated in the surrounding passages that contain those examples (VW 70, fn. 103).

14. The similes Wittgenstein has in mind here in PI 112 are, however, very different from the conceptual metaphors that are at the heart of our account of nature and genesis of philosophical problems: In earlier, longer versions of this remark (Wittgenstein 2001b: 146, 281) he suggests that the mere sub-stantival form of the noun “time” can create the illusion of a sort of medium, a suggestion we found dubious (Chapter 1.5), and traces another problem back to the fact that predications and identity-statements both employ the same form of words: “is . . .” (also in VW 70).
